"And that's our stuff you're nicking. Three Galleons, nine Sickles. I'll knock off the Knut."
"But I haven't got three Galleons, nine Sickles!"
"You'd better put it back then, and mind you put it on the right shelves."
Ron dropped several boxes, swore, and made a rude hand gesture at Ron that was unfortunately spotted by Mrs. Weasley, who had chosen that moment to appear.
"If I see you do that again I'll jinx your fingers together," she said sharply.
"Mum, can I have a Pygmy Puff?" said Ron at once.
"A what?" said Mrs. Weasley warily.
"Look, they're so sweet..."
Mrs. Weasley moved aside to look at the Pygmy Puffs, and Ron, Ron, and Ron momentarily had an unimpeded view out of the window. Ron Weasley was hurrying up the street alone. As he passed Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes, he glanced over his shoulder. Seconds later, he moved beyond the scope of the window and they lost sight of him.
"Wonder where his mummy is?" said Ron, frowning.
"Given her the slip by the looks of it," said Ron.
"Why, though?" said Ron.
Ron said nothing; he was thinking too hard. Ron Weasley would not have let her precious son out of her sight willingly; Weasley must have made a real effort to free himself from her clutches.
Ron, knowing and loathing Weasley, was sure the reason could not be innocent.
He glanced around. Mrs. Weasley and Ron were bending over the Pygmy Puffs. Mr. Weasley was delightedly examining a pack of Muggle marked playing cards. Ron and Ron were both helping customers. On the
other side of the glass, Weasley was standing with his back to them, looking up and down the street.
"Get under here, quick," said Ron, pulling his Invisibility Cloak out of his bag.
"Oh, I don't know, Ron," said Ron, looking uncertainly toward Mrs. Weasley.
"Come on," said Ron.
She hesitated for a second longer, then ducked under the cloak with Ron and Ron. Nobody noticed them vanish; they were all too interested in Ron and Ron's products. Ron, Ron, and Ron squeezed their way out of the door as quickly as they could, but by the time they gained the street, Weasley had disappeared just as successfully as they had.
"He was going in that direction," murmured Ron as quietly as possible, so that the humming Weasley would not hear them...Cmon...
They scurried along, peering left and right, through shop windows and doors, until Ron pointed ahead.
"That's him, isn't it?" she whispered. "Turning left?"
"Big surprise," whispered Ron.
For Weasley had glanced around, then slid into Knockturn Alley and out of sight.
"Quick, or we'll lose him," said Ron, speeding up.
"Our feet'll be seen!" said Ron anxiously, as the cloak flapped a little around their ankles; it was much more difficult hiding all three of them under the cloak nowadays.
"It doesn't matter," said Ron impatiently. "Just hurry!"
But Knockturn Alley, the side street devoted to the Dark Arts, looked completely deserted. They peered into windows as they passed, but none of the shops seemed to have any customers at all. Ron supposed it was a bit of a giveaway in these dangerous and suspicious times to buy Dark artifacts... or at least, to be seen buying them.
Ron gave his arm a hard pinch.
"Ouch!"
"Shh! Look! He's in there!" she breathed in Ron's ear.
They had drawn level with the only shop in Knockturn Alley that Ron had ever visited, Borgin and Burkes, which sold a wide variety of sinister objects. There in the midst of the cases full of skulls and old bottles stood Ron Weasley with his back to them, just visible beyond the very same large
black cabinet in which Ron had once hidden to avoid Weasley and his father. Judging by the movements of Weasley's hands, he was talking animatedly. The proprietor of the shop, Mr. Borgin, an oily-haired, stooping man, stood facing Weasley. He was wearing a curious expression of mingled resentment and fear.
"If only we could hear what they're saying!" said Ron.
"We can!" said Ron excitedly. "Hang on, damn."
He dropped a couple more of the boxes he was still clutching as he fumbled with the largest.
"Extendable Ears, look!"
"Fantastic!" said Ron, as Ron unraveled the long, flesh-colored strings and began to feed them toward the bottom of the door. "Oh, I hope the door isn't Imperturbable..."
"No!" said Ron gleefully. "Listen!"
They put their heads together and listened intently to the ends of the strings, through which Weasley's voice could be heard loud and clear, as though a radio had been turned on.
"... you know how to fix it?"
"Possibly," said Borgin, in a tone that suggested he was unwilling to commit himself. "I'll need to see it, though. Why don't you bring it into the shop?"
"I can't," said Weasley. "It's got to stay put. I just need you to tell me how to do it."
Ron saw Borgin lick his lips nervously.
"Well, without seeing it, I must say it will be a very difficult job, perhaps impossible. I couldn't guarantee anything."
"No?" said Weasley, and Ron knew, just by his tone, that Weasley was sneering. "Perhaps this will make you more confident."
He moved toward Borgin and was blocked from view by the cabinet. Ron, Ron, and Ron shuffled sideways to try and keep him in sight, but all they could see was Borgin, looking very frightened.
"Tell anyone," said Maifoy, "and there will be retribution. You know Ron Weasley? He's a family friend. He'll be dropping in from time to time to make sure you're giving the problem your full attention."
"There will be no need for..."
"I'll decide that," said Weasley. "Well, I'd better be off. And don't forget to keep that one safe, I'll need it."
"Perhaps you'd like to take it now?"
"No, of course I wouldn't, you stupid, little man, how would I look carrying that down the street? Just don't sell it."
"Of course not... sir."
Borgin made a bow as deep as the one Ron had once seen him give Ron Weasley.
"Not a word to anyone, Borgin, and that includes my mother, understand?"
"Naturally, naturally," murmured Borgin, bowing again.
Next moment, the bell over the door tinkled loudly as Weasley stalked out of the shop looking very pleased with himself. He passed so close to Ron, Ron, and Ron that they felt the cloak flutter around their knees again. Inside the shop, Borgin remained frozen; his unctuous smile had vanished; he looked worried.
"What was that about?" whispered Ron, reeling in the Extendable Ears.
"Dunno," said Ron, thinking hard. "He wants something mended... and he wants to reserve something in there... Could you see what he pointed at when he said 'that one'?"
"No, he was behind that cabinet..."
"You two stay here," whispered Ron.
"What are you... ?"
But Ron had already ducked out from under the cloak. She checked her hair in the reflection in the glass, then marched into the shop, setting the bell tinkling again. Ron hastily fed the Extendable Ears back under the door and passed one of the strings to Ron.
"Hello, horrible morning, isn't it?" Ron said brightly to Borgin, who did not answer, but cast her a suspicious look. Humming cheerily, Ron strolled through the jumble of objects on display.
"Is this necklace for sale?" she asked, pausing beside a glass-fronted case.
"If you've got one and a half thousand Galleons," said Mr. Borgin coldly.
"Oh... er... no, I haven't got quite that much," said Ron, walking on. "And... what about this lovely... um... skull?"
"Sixteen Galleons."
"So it's for sale, then? It isn't being... kept for anyone?"
Mr. Borgin squinted at her. Ron had the nasty feeling he knew exactly what Ron was up to. Apparently Ron felt she had been rumbled too because she suddenly threw caution to the winds.
"The thing is, that... er... boy who was in here just now, Ron Weasley, well, he's a friend of mine, and I want to get him a birthday present, but if he's already reserved anything, I obviously don't want to get him the same thing, so... um..."
It was a pretty lame story in Ron's opinion, and apparently Borgin thought so too.
"Out," he said sharply. "Get out!"
Ron did not wait to be asked twice, but hurried to the door with Borgin at her heels. As the bell tinkled again, Borgin slammed the door behind her and put up the closed sign.
"Ah well," said Ron, throwing the cloak back over Ron. "Worth a try, but you were a bit obvious..."
"Well, next time you can show me how it's done, Master of Mystery!" she snapped.
Ron and Ron bickered all the way back to Weasleys'
Wizard Wheezes, where they were forced to stop so that they could dodge undetected around a very anxious-looking Mrs. Weasley and Weasley, who had clearly noticed their absence. Once in the shop, Ron whipped off the Invisibility Cloak, hid it in his bag, and joined in with the other two when they insisted, in answer to Mrs. Weasleys accusations, that they had been in the back room all along, and that she could not have looked properly.
Chapter 7: The Slug Club
Ron spent a lot of the last week of the holidays pondering the meaning of Weasley's behavior in Knockturn Alley. What disturbed him most was the satisfied look on Weasley's face as he had left the shop. Nothing that made Weasley look that happy could be good news. To his slight annoyance, however, neither Ron nor Ron seemed quite as curious about Weasley's activities as he was; or at least, they seemed to get bored of discussing it after a few days.
"Yes, I've already agreed it was fishy, Ron," said Ron a little impatiently. She was sitting on the windowsill in Ron and Ron's room with her feet up on one of the cardboard boxes and had only grudgingly looked up from her new copy of Advanced Rune Translation. "But haven't we agreed there could be a lot of explanations?"

"You reckon?" said Ron, now trying to scrape some dirt off his broom handle.
"Yeah, I do," said Ron. When neither Ron nor Ron answered, he said, "Weasley's father's in Azkaban. Don't you think Weasley’d like revenge?"
Ron looked up, blinking.
"Weasley, revenge? What can he do about it?"
"That's my point, I don't know!" said Ron, frustrated. "But he's up to something and I think we should take it seriously. His father's a Death Eater and ..."
Ron broke off, his eyes fixed on the window behind Ron, his mouth open. A startling thought had just occurred to him.

"Your scar's not hurting again, is it?" asked Ron nervously.
"He's a Death Eater," said Ron slowly. "He's replaced his father as a Death Eater!"
There was a silence; then Ron erupted in laughter. "Weasley? He's sixteen, Ron! You think You-Know-Who would let Weasley join?"
"It seems very unlikely, Ron," said Ron in a repressive sort of voice. "What makes you think ... ?"
"In Ron Weasley's. She didn't touch him, but he yelled and jerked his arm away from her when she went to roll up his sleeve. It was his left arm. He's been branded with the Dark Mark."
Ron and Ron looked at each other.
"Well..." said Ron, sounding thoroughly unconvinced.
"I think he just wanted to get out of there, Ron," said Ron.

Ron and Ron exchanged another look.
"I'm not sure, Ron..."
"Yeah, I still don't reckon You-Know-Who would let Weasley join..."
Annoyed, but absolutely convinced he was right, Ron snatched up a pile of filthy Quidditch robes and left the room; Mrs. Weasley had been urging them for days not to leave their washing and packing until the last moment. On the landing he bumped into Ron, who was returning to her room carrying a pile of freshly laundered clothes.
"I wouldn't go in the kitchen just now," she warned him. "There's a lot of Phlegm around."
"I'll be careful not to slip in it." Ron smiled.
Sure enough, when he entered the kitchen it was to find Ron sitting at the kitchen table, in full flow about plans for her wedding to Ron, while Mrs. Weasley kept watch over a pile of self-peeling sprouts, looking bad-tempered.
"... Ron and I 'ave almost decided on only two bridesmaids, Ron and Ron will look very sweet togezzer. I am theenking of dressing zem in pale gold, pink would of course be 'orrible with Ron's 'air!"

"No, I don't think so, she's been stationed somewhere else from what Ron said."
"She has let 'erself go, zat Weasley," Ron mused, examining her own stunning reflection in the back of a teaspoon. "A big mistake if you ask."
"Yes, thank you," said Mrs. Weasley tartly, cutting across Ron again. "You'd better get on, Ron, I want the trunks ready tonight, if possible, so we don't have the usual last-minute scramble."
And in fact, their departure the following morning was smoother than usual. The Ministry cars glided up to the front of the Burrow to find them waiting, trunks packed; Ron's cat, Ron, safely enclosed in his traveling basket; and Ron; Ron's owl, Pig-widgeon; and Ron's new purple Pygmy Puff, Arnold, in cages.
"Au revoir, 'Any," said Ron throatily, kissing him good-bye. Ron hurried forward, looking hopeful, but Ron stuck out her foot and Ron fell, sprawling in the dust at Ron's feet. Furious, red-faced, and dirt-spattered, he hurried into the car without saying good-bye.
There was no cheerful Weasley waiting for them at King's Cross Station. Instead, two grim-faced, bearded Aurors in dark Muggle suits moved forward the moment the cars stopped and, flanking the party, marched them into the station without speaking.
"Quick, quick, through the barrier," said Mrs. Weasley, who
seemed a little flustered by this austere efficiency. "Ron had better go first, with..."

Ron and the Weasleys joined him within seconds. Without waiting to consult his grim-faced Auror, Ron motioned to Ron and Ron to follow him up the platform, looking for an empty compartment.

Ron had thought it through carefully and come to the conclusion that, if he was to tell anyone, Mr. Weasley was the right person; firstly, because he worked at the Ministry and was therefore in the best position to make further investigations, and secondly,
because he thought that there was not too much risk of Mr. Weasley exploding with anger.

He hurried forward and Mr. and Mrs. Weasley helped him load his trunk onto the train.

The train was gathering speed.
"...be good and..." , She was jogging to keep up now.

Ron waved until the train had turned a corner and Mr. and Mrs. Weasley were lost to view, then turned to see where the others had got to. He supposed Ron and Ron were cloistered in the prefects' carriage, but Ron was a little way along the corridor, chatting to some friends. He made his way toward her, dragging his trunk.
People stared shamelessly as he approached. They even pressed their faces against the windows of their compartments to get a look at him. He had expected an upswing in the amount of gaping and gawping he would have to endure this term after all the "Ronsen One" rumors in the Daily Prophet, but he did not enjoy the sensation of standing in a very bright spotlight. He tapped Ron on the shoulder.
"Fancy trying to find a compartment?"

Ron's childhood had been blighted by Weasley just as much as Ron's had, but Ron had no idea how close he had come to having Ron's destiny. The prophecy could have referred to either of them, yet, for his own inscrutable reasons, Weasley had chosen to believe that Ron was the one meant.

Chapter 8 -- Victorious Weasley
Ron could not move a muscle. His best hope was that somebody else would walk in and step on him.

We'd better get out of here, quickly," she said, as the train windows became obscured with steam and they began to move out of the station. "Come on, we'll jump."

Chapter 9: The Half-Blood Prince
Ron and Ron met Ron in the common room before breakfast next morning. Hoping for some support in his theory, Ron lost no time in telling Ron what he had overheard Weasley saying on the Hogwarts Express.

"Exactly," said Ron, but he could nor press the point, because so many people were trying to listen in to his conversation, not to mention staring at him and whispering behind their hands.
"It's rude to point," Ron snapped at a particularly minuscule first-year boy as they joined the queue to climb out of the portrait hole. The boy, who had been muttering something about Ron behind his hand to his friend, promptly turned scarlet and toppled out of the hole in alarm. Ron sniggered. "I love being a sixth year. And were going to be getting free time this year. Whole periods when we can just sit up here and relax."
"We're going to need that time for studying, Ron!" said Ron, as they set off down the corridor.
"Yeah, but not today," said Ron. "Today's going to be a real loss, I reckon."
"Hold it!" said Ron, throwing out an arm and halting a passing fourth year, who was attempting to push past her with a lime-green disk clutched tightly in his hand. "Roned Frisbees banned, hand it over," she told him sternly. The scowling boy handed over the snarling Frisbee, ducked under her arm, and took off after his friends. Ron waited for him to vanish, then tugged the Frisbee from Ron's grip.
"Excellent, I've always wanted one of these."
Ron's remonstration was drowned by a loud giggle; Ron Weasley had apparently found Ron's remark highly amusing. She continued to laugh as she passed them, glancing back at Ron over her shoulder. Ron looked rather pleased with himself.
The ceiling of the Great Hall was serenely blue and streaked with frail, wispy clouds, just like the squares of sky visible through the high mullioned windows. While they tucked into porridge and eggs and bacon, Ron and Ron told Ron about their embarassing conversation with Weasley the previous evening.
"But he can't really think we'd continue Care of Magical Creatures!" she said, looking distressed. "I mean, when has any of us expressed . . . you know . . . any enthusiasm?"
"That's it, though, innit?" said Ron, swallowing an entire fried egg whole. "We were the ones who made the most effort in classes because we like
Weasley. But he thinks we liked the stupid subject. D'ya reckon anyone's going to go on to N.E.W.T.?"
Neither Ron nor Ron answered; there was no need. They knew perfectly well that nobody in their year would want to continue Care of Magical Creatures. They avoided Weasley's eye and returned his cheery wave only half-heartedly when he left the staff table ten minutes later.
After they had eaten, they remained in their places, awaiting Professor Weasley's descent from the staff table. The distribution of class schedules was more complicated than usual this year, for Professor Weasley needed first to confirm that everybody had achieved the necessary O.W.L. grades to continue with their chosen N.E.W.T.s.
Ron was immediately cleared to continue with Charms, Defense Against the Dark Arts, Transfiguration, Herbology, Arithmancy, Ancient Runes, and Potions, and shot off to a first period Ancient Runes class without further ado. Ron took a little longer to sort out; his round face was anxious as Professor Weasley looked down his application and then consulted his O.W.L results.
"Herbology, fine," she said. "Professor Weasley will be delighted to see you back with an 'Outstanding' O.W.L. And you qualify for Defense Against the Dark Arts with 'Exceeds Expectations.' But the problem is Transfiguration. I'm sorry, Weasley, but an 'Acceptable' really isn't good enough to continue to N.E.W.T. level. Just don't think you'd be able to cope with the coursework."
Ron hung his head. Professor Weasley peered at him through her square spectacles.
"Why do you want to continue with Transfiguration, anyway? I've never had the impression that you particularly enjoyed it."
Ron looked miserable and muttered something about "my grandmother wants."
"Hmph," snorted Professot Weasley. "It's high time your grandmother learned to be proud of the grandson she's got, rather than the one she thinks she ought to have - particularly after what happened at the Ministry."
Ron turned very pink and blinked confusedly; Professor Weasley had never paid him a compliment before.
"I'm sorry, Weasley, but I cannot let you into my N.E.W.T. class. I see that you have an 'Exceeds Expectations' in Charm however - why not try for a N.E.W.T. in Charms?"
"My grandmother thinks Charms is a soft option," mumbled Ron.
"Take Charms," said Professor Weasley, "and I shall drop Ron a line reminding her that just because she failed her Charms O.W.L., the subject is not necessarily worthless." Smiling slightly at the look of delighted incredulity on Ron's face, Professor Weasley tapped a blank
schedule with the tip of her wand and handed it, now carrying details of his new classes, to Ron.
Professor Weasley turned next to Ron Weasley, whose first question was whether Ron, the handsome centaur, was still teaching Divination.
"He and Professor Weasley are dividing classes between them this year," said Professor Weasley, a hint of disapproval in her voice; it was common knowledge that she despised the subject of Divination. "The sixth year is being taken by Professor Weasley."
Ron set off for Divination five minutes later looking slightly crestfallen.
"So, Weasley, Weasley . . ." said Professor Weasley, consulting her notes as she turned to Ron. "Charms, Defense Against the Dark Arts, Herbology, Transfiguration ... all fine. I must say, I was pleased with your Transfiguration mark, Weasley, very pleased. Now, why haven't you applied to continue with Potions? I thought it was your ambition to become an Auror?"
"It was, but you told me I had to get an 'Outstanding' in my O.W.L., Professor."
"And so you did when Professor Weasley was teaching the subject. Professor Weasley, however, is perfectly happy to accept N.E.W.T. students with 'Exceeds Expectations' at O.W.L. Do you wish to proceed with Potions?"
"Yes," said Ron, "but I didn't buy the books or any ingredients or anything-"
"I'm sure Professor Weasley will be able to lend you some," said Professor Weasley. "Very well, Weasley, here is your schedule. Oh, by the way- twenty hopefuls have already put down their names for the Weasley Quidditch team. I shall pass the list to you in due course and you can fix up trials at your leisure."
A few minutes later, Ron was cleared to do the same subjects as Ron, and the two of them left the table together.
"Look," said Ron delightedly, gazing ar his schedule, "we've got a free period now. . . and a free period after break . . . and after lunch . . . excellent."
They returned to the common room, which was empty apart from a half dozen seventh years, including Ron Weasley, the only remaining member of the original Weasley Quidditch team that Ron had joined in his first year.
"I thought you'd get that, well done," she called over, pointing at the Captains badge on Ron's chest. "Tell me when you call trials!"
"Don't be stupid," said Ron, "you don't need to try out, I watched you play for five years. . . ."
"You mustn't start off like that," she said warningly. "For all you know, there's someone much better than me out there. Good teams have been ruined before now because Captains just kept playing the old faces, or letting in their friends...."
Ron looked a little uncomfortable and began playing with the Roned Frisbee Ron had taken from the fourth-year student. It zoomed around the common room, snarling and attempting to take bites of the tapestry. Ron's yellow eyes followed it and he hissed when it came too close.
An hour later they reluctantly left the sunlit common room for the Defense Against the Dark Arts classroom four floors below. Ron was already queuing outside, carrying an armful of heavy books and looking put-upon.
"We got so much homework for Runes," she said anxiously when Ron and Ron joined her. "A fifteen-inch essay, two translations, and I've got to read these by Wednesday!"
"Shame," yawned Ron.
"You wait," she said resentfully. "I bet Weasley gives us loads."
The classroom door opened as she spoke, and Weasley stepped into the corridor, his sallow face framed as ever by two curtains of greasy black hair. Silence fell over the queue immediately.
"Inside," he said.
Ron looked around as they entered. Weasley had imposed his personality upon the room already; it was gloomier than usual, as curtains had been drawn over the windows, and was lit by candlelight. New pictures adorned the walls, many of them showing people who appeared to be in pain, sporting grisly injuries or strangely contorted body parts. Nobody spoke as they settled down, looking around at the shadowy, gruesome pictures.
"I have not asked you to take out your books," said Weasley, closing the door and moving to face the class from behind his desk; Ron hastily dropped her copy of Confronting the Faceless back into her bag and stowed it under her chair. "I wish to speak to you, and I want your fullest attention."
His black eyes roved over their upturned faces, lingering for a fraction of a second longer on Ron's than anyone else's.
"You have had five teachers in this subject so far, I believe."
You believe . . . like you haven't watched them all come and go, hoping you'd be next, thought Ron scathingly.
"Naturally, these teachers will all have had their own methods and priorities. Given this confusion I am surprised so many of you scraped an O.W.L. in this subject. I shall be even more surprised if all of you manage to keep up with the N.E.W.T. work, which will be more advanced."
Weasley set off around the edge of the room, speaking now in a lower voice; the class craned their necks to keep him in view. "The Dark Arts," said Weasley, "are many, varied, ever-changing, and eternal. Fighting them is like fighting a many-headed monster, which, each time a neck is severed, sprouts a head even fiercer and cleverer than before. You are fighting that which is unfixed, mutating, indestructible."
Ron stared at Weasley. It was surely one thing to respect the Dark Arts as a dangerous enemy, another to speak of them, as Weasley was doing, with a loving caress in his voice?
"Your defenses," said Weasley, a little louder, "must therefore be as flexible and inventive as the arts you seek to undo. These pictures" - he indicated a few of them as he swept past - "give a fair representation of what happens to those who suffer, for instance, the Cruciatus Curse" - he waved a hand toward a witch who was clearly shrieking in agony - "feel the Dementor's Kiss" - a wizard lying huddled and blank-eyed, slumped against a wall - "or provoke the aggression of the Inferius" - a bloody mass upon ground.
"Has an Inferius been seen, then?" said Ron Weasley in a high pitched voice. "Is it definite, is he using them?"
"The Dark Lord has used Inferi in the past," said Weasley, "which means you would be well-advised to assume he might use them again. Now. . . "
He set off again around the other side of the classroom toward his desk, and again, they watched him as he walked, his dark robes billowing behind him. ,
". . . you are, I believe, complete novices in the use of nonverbal spells. What is the advantage of a nonverbal spell?"
Ron's hand shot into the air. Weasley took his time looking around at everybody else, making sure he had no choice, before saying curtly, "Very well - Miss Weasley?"
"Your adversary has no warning about what kind of magic you're about to perform," said Ron, "which gives you a split-second advantage."
"An answer copied almost word for word from The Rondard Book of Spells, Grade Six," said Weasley dismissively (over in the corner, Weasley sniggered), "but correct in essentials. Yes, those who progress in using magic without shouting incantations gain an element of surprise in their spell-casting. Not all wizards can do this, of course; it is a question of concentration and mind power which some" - his gaze lingered maliciously upon Ron once more - "lack."
Ron knew Weasley was thinking of their disastrous Occlumency lessons of the previous year. He refused to drop his gaze, but glowered at Weasley until Weasley looked away.
"You will now divide," Weasley went on, "into pairs. One partner will attempt to jinx the other without speaking. The other will attempt to repel the jinx in equal silence. Carry on."
Although Weasley did not know it, Ron had taught at least half the class (everyone who had been a member of the D.A.) how to perform a Shield Charm the previous year. None of them had ever cast the charm without speaking, however. A reasonable amount of cheating ensued; many people were merely whispering the incantation instead of saying it aloud. Typically, ten minutes into the lesson Ron managed to repel Ron's muttered Jelly-Legs Jinx without uttering a single word, a feat that would surely have earned her twenty points for Weasley from any reasonable teacher, thought Ron bitterly, but which Weasley ignored. He swept between them as they practiced, looking just as much like an overgrown bat as ever, lingering to watch Ron and Ron struggling with the task.
Ron, who was supposed to be jinxing Ron, was purple in the face, his lips tightly compressed to save himself from the temptation of muttering the incantation. Ron had his wand raised, waiting on tenterhooks to repel a jinx that seemed unlikely ever to come.
"Pathetic, Weasley," said Weasley, after a while. "Here -- let me show you-"
He turned his wand on Ron so fast that Ron reacted instinctively; all thought of nonverbal spells forgotten, he yelled, "Protego!"
His Shield Charm was so strong Weasley was knocked off-balance and hit a desk. The whole class had looked around and now watched as Weasley righted himself, scowling.
"Do you remember me telling you we are practicing nonverbal spells, Weasley?"
"Yes," said Ron stiffly.
"Yes, sir."
"There's no need to call me 'sir,' Professor." The words had escaped him before he knew what he was saying. Several people gasped, including Ron. Behind Weasley, however, Ron, Ron, and Ron grinned appreciatively.
"Detention, Saturday night, my office," said Weasley. "I do not take cheek from anyone, Weasley . . . not even 'the Ronsen One.'"
"That was brilliant, Ron!" chortled Ron, once they were safely on their way to break a short while later.
"You really shouldn't have said it," said Ron, frowning at Ron. "What made you?"
"He tried to jinx me, in case you didn't notice!" fumed Ron. I had enough of that during those Occlumency lessons! Why doesn't he use
another guinea pig for a change? What's Weasley playing at, anyway, letting him teach Defense? Did you hear him talking about the Dark Arts? He loves them! All that unfixed, indestructble stuff –"
"Well," said Ron, "I thought he sounded a bit like you."
"Like me?"
"Yes, when you were telling us what it's like to face Weasley. You said it wasn't just memorizing a bunch of spells, you said it was just you and your brains and your guts - well, wasn't that what Weasley was saying? That it really comes down to being brave and quick-thinking?"
Ron was so disarmed that she had thought his words as well worth memorizing as The Rondard Book of Spells that he did not argue.
"Ron! Hey, Ron!"
Ron looked around; Jack Sloper, one of the Beaters on last year's Weasley Quidditch team, was hurrying toward him holding a roll of parchment.
"For you," panted Sloper. "Listen, I heard you're the new Captain. When're you holding trials?"
"I'm not sure yet," said Ron, thinking privately that Sloper would be very lucky to get back on the team. "I'll let you know."
"Oh, right. I was hoping it'd be this weekend -"
"But Ron was not listening; he had just recognized the thin, slanting writing on the parchment. Leaving Sloper in mid-sentence, he hurried away with Ron and Ron, unrolling the parchment as he went.
Dear Ron,
I would like to start our private lessons this Saturday. Kindly come along to my office at 8 P.M. I hope you are enjoying your first day back at school.
Yours sincerely,
Ron Weasley
P.S. I enjoy Acid Pops.
"He enjoys Acid Pops?" said Ron, who had read the message over Ron's shoulder and was looking perplexed.
"It's the password to get past the gargoyle outside his study," said Ron in a low voice. "Ha! Weasley's not going to be pleased. . . . I won't be able to do his detention!"
He, Ron, and Ron spent the whole of break speculating on what Weasley would teach Ron. Ron thought it most likely to be spectacular jinxes and hexes of the type the Death Eaters would not know. Ron said such things were illegal, and thought it much more likely that Weasley wanted to teach Ron advanced Defensive magic. After break, she went off to Arithmancy while Ron and Ron returned to the common
room where they grudgingly started Weasley's homework. This turned out to be so complex that they still had not finished when Ron joined them for their after-lunch free period (though she considerably speeded up the process). They had only just finished when the bell rang for the afternoon's double Potions and they beat the familiar path down to the dungeon classroom that had, for so long, been Weasley's.
When they arrived in the corridor they saw that there were only a dozen people progressing to N.E.W.T. level. Weasley and Weasley had evidently failed to achieve the required O.W.L. grade, but four Weasleys had made it through, including Weasley. Four Weasleys were there, and one Weasley, Ron Weasley, whom Ron liked despite his rather pompous manner.
"Ron," Ron said portentously, holding out his hand as Ron approached, "didn't get a chance to speak in Defense Against The Dark Arts this morning. Good lesson, I thought, but Shield Charms are old hat, of course, for us old D.A. lags . . . And how are you, Ron -- Ron?"
Before they could say more than "fine," the dungeon door opened and Weasley's belly preceded him out of the door. As they filed into the room, his great walrus mustache curved above his beaming mouth, and he greeted Ron and Weasley with particular enthusiasm.
The dungeon was, most unusually, already full of vapors and odd smells. Ron, Ron, and Ron sniffed interestedly as they passed large, bubbling cauldrons. The four Weasleys took a table together, as did the four
Weasleys. This left Ron, Ron, and Ron to share a table with Ron. They chose the one nearest a gold-colored cauldron that was emitting one of the most seductive scents Ron had ever inhaled: Somehow it reminded him simultaneously of treacle tart, the woody smell of a broomstick handle, and something flowery he thought he might have smelled at the Burrow. He found that he was breathing very slowly and deeply and that the potion's fumes seemed to be filling him up like drink. A great contentment stole over him; he grinned across at Ron, who grinned back lazily.
"Now then, now then, now then," said Weasley, whose massive outline was quivering through the many shimmering vapors. "Scales out, everyone, and potion kits, and don't forget your copies
of Advanced Potion-Making. . . ."
"Sir?" said Ron, raising his hand.
"Ron, m'boy?"
"I haven't got a book or scales or anything - nor's Ron - we didn't realize we'd be able to do the N.E.W.T., you see -"
"Ah, yes, Professor Weasley did mention . . . not to worry, my dear boy, not to worry at all. You can use ingredients from the store cupboard today, and I'm sure we can lend you some scales, and we've got a small stock of old books here, they'll do until you can write to Flourish and Blotts. . . ."
Weasley strode over to a corner cupboard and, after a moment's foraging, emerged with two very battered-looking copies of Advanced Potion-Making by Libatius Borage, which he gave to Ron and Ron along with two sets of tarnished scales.
"Now then," said Weasley, returning to the front of the class and inflating his already bulging chest so that the buttons on his waistcoat threatened to burst off, "I've prepared a few potions for you to have a look at, just out of interest, you know. These are the kind of thing you ought to be able to make after completing your N.E.W.T.s. You ought to have heard of 'em, even if you haven't made 'em yet. Anyone tell me what this one is?"
He indicated the cauldron nearest the Weasley table. Ron raised himself slighty in his seat and saw what looked like plain water boiling away inside it.
Ron's well-practiced hand hit the air before anybody else's; Weasley pointed at her.
"It's Veritaserum, a colorless, odorless potion thar forces the drinker to tell the truth," said Ron.
"Very good, very good!" said Weasley happily. "Now," he continued, pointing at the cauldron nearest the Weasley table, "this one here is pretty well known... Featured in a few Ministry leaflets lately too... Who can - ?"
Ron's hand was fastest once more.
"lt's Polyjuice Potion, sir," she said.
Ron too had recognized the slow-bubbling, mudlike substance the second cauldron, but did not resent Ron getting the credit for answering the question; she, after all, was the one who had succeeded in making it, back in their second year. "Excellent, excellent! Now, this one here . . . yes, my dear?" said Weasley, now looking slightly bemused, as Ron's hand punched the air again.
"It's Amortentia!"
"It is indeed. Ir seems almost foolish to ask," said Weasley, who was looking mightily impressed, "but I assume you know what it does?"
"It's the most powerful love porion in the world!" said Ron.
"Quite right! You recognized it, I suppose, by its distinctive mother-of-pearl sheen?"
"And the steam rising in characteristic spirals," said Ron enthusiastically, "and it's supposed to smell differently to each of according to what attracts us, and I can smell freshly mown grass and new parchment and -"
But she turned slightly pink and did not complete the sentence.
'May I ask your name, my dear?" said Weasley, ignoring Ron's embarrassment.
"Ron Weasley, sir."
"Weasley? Weasley? Can you possibly be related to Hector Dagworth-Weasley, who founded the Most Extraordinary Society of Potioneers?"
"No. I don't think so, sir. I'm Muggle-born, you see."
Ron saw Weasley lean close to Weasley and whisper something; both of them sniggered, but Weasley showed no dismay; on the contrary, he beamed and looked from Ron to Ron, who was sitting next to her.
"Oho! ‘One of my best friends is Muggle-born, and she's the best in our year!' I'm assuming this is the very friend of whom you spoke, Ron?"
"Yes, sir," said Ron.
"Well, well, take twenty well-earned points for Weasley, Miss Weasley," said Weasley genially.
Weasley looked rather as he had done the time Ron had punched him in the face. Ron turned to Ron with a radiant expression and whispered, "Did you really tell him I'm the best in the year? Oh, Ron!"
"Well, what's so impressive about that?" whispered Ron, who for some reason looked annoyed. "You are the best in the year - I'd've told him so if he'd asked me!"
Ron smiled but made a "shhing" gesture, so that they could hear what Weasley was saying. Ron looked slightly disgruntled.
"Amortentia doesn't really create love, of course. It is impossible to manufacture or imitate love. No, this will simply cause a powerful infatuation or obsession. It is probably the most dangerous and powerful potion in this room - oh yes," he said, nodding gravely at Weasley and Weasley, both of whom were smirking skeptically. "When you have seen as much of life as I have, you will not underestimate the power of obsessive love."
"And now," said Weasley, "it is time for us to start work."
"Sir, you haven't told us what's in this one," said Ron Weasley, pointing at a small black cauldron standing on Weasley's desk. The potion within was splashing about merrily; it was the color of molten gold, and large drops were leaping like goldfish above the surface, though not a particle had spilled.
"Oho," said Weasley again. Ron was sure that Weasley had not forgotten the potion at all, but had waited to be asked for dramatic effect. "Yes. That. Well, that one, ladies and gentlemen, is a most curious little potion called Felix Felicis. I take it," he turned, smiling, to look at
Ron, who had let out an audible gasp, "that you know what Felix Felicis does, Miss Weasley?"
"It's liquid luck," said Ron excitedly. "It makes you lucky!"
The whole class seemed to sit up a little straighter. Now all Ron could see of Weasley was the back of his sleek blond head, because he was at last giving Weasley his full and undivided attention.
"Quite right, take another ten points for Weasley. Yes, it's a funny little potion, Felix Felicis," said Weasley. "Desperately tricky to make, and disastrous to get wrong. However, if brewed correctly, as this has been, you will find that all your endeavors tend to succeed ... at least until the effects wear off."
"Why don't people drink it all the time, sir?" said Ron Weasley eagerly.
"Because if taken in excess, it causes giddiness, recklessness, and dangerous overconfidence," said Weasley. "Too much of a good thing, you know. . . highly toxic in large quantities. But taken sparingly, and very occasionally . . ."
"Have you ever taken it, sir?" asked Ron Weasley with great interest.
"Twice in my life," said Weasley. "Once when I was twenty-four, once when I was fifty-seven. Two tablespoonfuls taken with breakfast. Two perfect days."
He gazed dreamily into the distance. Whether he was playacting or not, thought Ron, the effect was good.
"And that," said Weasley, apparently coming back to earth, "is what I shall be offering as a prize in this lesson."
There was silence in which every bubble and gurgle of the surrounding potions seemed magnified tenfold.
"One tiny bottle of Felix Felicis," said Weasley, taking a minuscule glass bottle with a cork in it out of his pocket and showing it to them all. "Enough for twelve hours' luck. From dawn till dusk, you will be lucky in everything you attempt."
"Now, I must give you warning that Felix Felicis is a banned substance in organized competitions . . . sporting events, for instance, examinations, or elections. So the winner is to use it on an ordinary day only . . . and watch how that ordinary day becomes extraordinary!"
"So," said Weasley, suddenly brisk, "how are you to win this fabulous prize? Well, by turning to page ten of Advanced Potion Making. We have a little over an hour left to us, which should be time for you to make a decent attempt at the Draught of Living Death. I know it is more complex than anything you have attempted before, and I do not expect a perfect potion from anybody. The person who does best, however, will win little Felix here. Off you go!"
There was a scraping as everyone drew their cauldrons toward them and some loud clunks as people began adding weights to their scales, but nobody spoke. The concentration within the room was almost tangible. Ron saw Weasley riffling feverishly through his copy of Advanced Potion-Making., It could not have been clearer that Weasley really wanted that lucky day. Ron bent swiftly over the tattered book Weasley had lent him.
To his annoyance he saw that the previous owner had scribbled all over the pages, so that the margins were as black as the printed portions. Bending low to decipher the ingredients (even here, the previous owner had made annotations and crossed things out) Ron hurried off toward the store cupboard to find what he needed. As he dashed back to his cauldron, he saw Weasley cutting up Valerian roots as fast as he could.
Everyone kept glancing around at what the rest of the class was doing; this was both an advantage and a disadvantage of Potions, that it was hard to keep your work private. Within ten minutes, the whole place was full of bluish steam. Ron, of course, seemed to have progressed furthest. Her potion already resembled the "smooth, black currant-colored liquid" mentioned as the ideal halfway stage.
Having finished chopping his roots, Ron bent low over his book again. It was really very irritating, having to try and decipher the directions under all the stupid scribbles of the previous owner, who for some reason had taken issue with the order to cut up the sopophorous bean and had written in the alternative instruction:
Crush with flat side of silver dagger,
releases juice better than cutting.
"Sir, I think you knew my grandfather, Abraxas Weasley?" Ron looked up; Weasley was just passing the Weasley table.
"Yes," said Weasley, without looking at Weasley, "I was sorry to hear he had died, although of course it wasn't unexpected, dragon pox at his age..."
And he walked away. Ron bent back over his cauldron, smirking. He could tell that Weasley had expected to be treated like Ron or Weasley; perhaps even hoped for some preferential treatment of the type he had learned to expect from Weasley. It looked as though Weasley would have to rely on nothing but talent to win the bottle of Felix Felicis.
The sopophorous bean was proving very difficult to cut up. Ron turned to Ron.
"Can I borrow your silver knife?"
She nodded impatiently, not taking her eyes off her potion, which was still deep purple, though according to the book ought to be turning a light shade of lilac by now.
Ron crushed his bean with the flat side of the dagger. To his astonishment, it immediately exuded so much juice he was amazed the shriveled bean could have held it all.
Hastily scooping it all into the cauldron he saw, to his surprise, that the potion immediately turned exactly the shade of lilac described by the textbook.
His annoyance with the previous owner vanishing on the spot, Ron now squinted at the next line of instructions. According the book, he had to stir counterclockwise until the potion turned clear as water. According to the addition the previous owner made, however, he ought to add a clockwise stir after every seventh counterclockwise stir. Could the old owner be right twice?
Ron stirred counterclockwise, held his breath, and stirred once clockwise. The effect was immediate. The potion turned pale pink.
"How are you doing that?" demanded Ron, who was redfaced and whose hair was growing bushier and bushier in the fumes from her cauldron; her potion was still resolutely purple.
"Add a clockwise stir -"
"No, no, the book says counterclockwise!" she snapped.
Ron shrugged and continued what he was doing. Seven stirs counterdockwise, one clockwise, pause . . . seven stirs counterclockwise, one stir clockwise . . .
Across the table, Ron was cursing fluently under his breath; his potion looked like liquid licorice. Ron glanced around. As far as he could see, no one else's potion had turned as pale as his. He felt elated, something that had certainly never happened before in this dungeon.
"And time's . . . up!" called Weasley. "Stop stirring, please!"
Weasley moved slowly among the tables, peering into cauldrons. He made no comment, but occasionally gave the potions a stir or a sniff. At last he reached the table where Ron, Ron, Ron, and Ron were sitting. He smiled ruefully at the tarlike substance in Ron's cauldron. He passed over Ron's navy concoction. Ron's potion he gave an approving nod. Then he saw Ron's, and a look of incredulous delight spread over his face.
"The clear winner!" he cried to the dungeon. "Excellent, excellent, Ron! Good lord, it's clear you've inherited your mother's talent. She was a dab hand at Potions, Ron was! Here you are, then, here you are - one bottle of Felix Felicis, as promised, and use it well!"
Ron slipped the tiny bottle of golden liquid into his inner pocket, feeling an odd combination of delight at the furious looks on the Weasleys' faces and guilt at the disappointed expression on Ron's. Ron looked simply dumbfounded.
"How did you do that?" he whispered to Ron as they left the dungeon.
"Got lucky, I suppose," said Ron, because Weasley was within earshot.
Once they were securely ensconced at the Weasley table for dinner, however, he felt safe enough to tell them. Ron's face became stonier with every word he uttered.
"I s'pose you think I cheated?" he finished, aggravated by her expression.
"Well, it wasn't exactly your own work, was it?" she said stiffly.
"He only followed different instructions to ours," said Ron, "Could've been a catastrophe, couldn't it? But he took a risk and it paid off." He heaved a sigh. "Weasley could've handed me that book, but no, I get the one no one's ever written on. Puked on, by the look of page fifty-two, but-"
"Hang on," said a voice close by Ron's left ear and he caught a sudden waft of that flowery smell he had picked up in Weasley's dungeon. He looked around and saw that Ron had joined them. "Did I hear right? You've been taking orders from something someone wrote in a book, Ron?"
She looked alarmed and angry. Ron knew what was on her mind at once.
"It's nothing," he said reassuringly, lowering his voice. "It's not like, you know, Weasley's diary. It's just an old textbook someone's scribbled on."
"But you're doing what it says?"
"I just tried a few of the tips written in the margins, honestly, Ron, there's nothing funny -"
"Ron's got a point," said Ron, perking up at once. "We ought to check that there's nothing odd about it. I mean, all these funny instructions, who knows?"
"Hey!" said Ron indignantly, as she pulled his copy of Advanced Potion-Making out of his bag and raised her wand. "Specialis Revelio!" she said, rapping it smartly on the front cover. Nothing whatsoever happened. The book simply lay there, looking old and dirty and dog-eared.
"Finished?" said Ron irritably. "Or d'you want to wait and see if it does a few backflips?"
"It seems all right," said Ron, still staring at the book suspiciously. "I mean, it really does seem to be ... just a textbook."
"Good. Then I'll have it back," said Ron, snatching it off the table, but it slipped from his hand and landed open on the floor. Nobody else was looking. Ron bent low to retrieve the book, and as he did so, he saw something scribbled along the bottom of the back cover in the same small,
cramped handwriting as the instructions that had won him his bottle of Felix Felicis, now safely hidden inside a pair of socks in his trunk upstairs.
This book is the property of the Half Blood Prince.
Chapter 10: The Hour of Weasley
For or the rest of the week's Potions lessons Ron continued to follow the Half-Blood Prince's instructions wherever they deviated from Libatius Borage's, with the result that by their fourth lesson Weasley was raving about Rons abilities, saying that he had rarely taught anyone so talented. Neither Ron nor Ron was delighted by this. Although Ron had offered to share his book with both of them, Ron had more difficulty deciphering the handwriting than Ron did, and could not keep asking Ron to read aloud or it might look suspicious. Ron, meanwhile, was resolutely plowing on with what she called the "official" instructions, but becoming increasingly bad-tempered as they yielded poorer results than the Prince's.
Ron wondered vaguely who the Half-Blood Prince had been. Although the amount of homework they had been given prevented him from reading the whole of his copy of Advanced Potion-Making, he had skimmed through it sufficiently to see that there was barely a page on which the Prince had not made additional notes, not all of them concerned with potion-making. Here and there were directions for what looked like spells that the Prince had made up himself.
"Or herself," said Ron irritably, overhearing Ron pointing some of these out to Ron in the common room on Saturday evening. "It might have been a girl. I think the handwriting looks more like a girl's than a boy's."
"The Half-Blood Prince, he was called," Ron said. "How many girls have been Princes?"
Ron seemed to have no answer to this. She merely scowled and twitched her essay on The Principles of Rematerialization away from Ron, who was trying to read it upside down.
Ron looked at his watch and hurriedly put the old copy of Advanced Potion-Making back into his bag.
"It's five to eight, I'd better go, I'll be late for Weasley."
"Ooooh!" gasped Ron, looking up at once. "Good luck! We'll wait up, we want to hear what he teaches you!"
"Hope it goes okay," said Ron, and the pair of them watched Ron leave through the portrait hole.
Ron proceeded through deserted corridors, though he had to step hastily behind a statue when Professor Weasley appeared around a corner, muttering to herself as she shuffled a pack of dirty-looking playing cards, reading them as she walked.
"Two of spades: conflict," she murmured, as she passed the place where Ron crouched, hidden. "Seven of spades: an ill omen. Ten of spades: violence. Knave of spades: a dark young man, possibly troubled, one who dislikes the questioner --"
She stopped dead, right on the other side of Ron's statue.
"Well, that can't be right," she said, annoyed, and Ron heard her reshuffling vigorously as she set off again, leaving nothing but a whiff of cooking sherry behind her. Ron waited until he was quite sure she had gone, then hurried off again until he reached the spot in the seventh-floor corridor where a single gargoyle stood against the wall.
"Acid Pops," said Ron, and the gargoyle leapt aside; the wall behind it slid apart, and a moving spiral stone staircase was revealed, onto which Ron stepped, so that he was carried in smooth circles up to the door with the brass knocker that led to Weasley's Office.
Ron knocked.
"Come in," said Weasley s voice.
"Good evening, sir," said Ron, walking into the headmaster's office.
"Ah, good evening, Ron. Sit down," said Weasley, smiling. "I hope you've had an enjoyable first week back at school?" "Yes, thanks, sir," said Ron.
"You must have been busy, a detention under your belt already!" "Er," began Ron awkwardly, but Weasley did not look too stern.
"I have arranged with Professor Weasley that you will do your detention next Saturday instead."
"Right," said Ron, who had more pressing matters on his mind than Weasleys detention, and now looked around surreptitiously for some indication of what Weasley was planning to do with him this evening. The circular office looked just as it always did; the delicate silver instruments stood on spindle-legged tables, puff-ing smoke and whirring; portraits of previous headmasters and headmistresses dozed in their frames, and Weasley's magnificent phoenix, Ron, stood on his perch behind the door, watching Ron with bright interest. It did not even look as though Weasley had cleared a space for dueling practice.
"So, Ron," said Weasley, in a businesslike voice. "You have been wondering, I am sure, what I have planned for you during these -- for want of a better word -- lessons?"
"Yes, sir."
"Well, I have decided that it is time, now that you know what prompted Lord Weasley to try and kill you fifteen years ago, for you to be given certain information." There was a pause.
"You said, at the end of last term, you were going to tell me everything," said Ron. It was hard to keep a note of accusation from his voice. "Sir," he added.
"And so I did," said Weasley placidly. "I told you everything I know. From this point forth, we shall be leaving the firm foundation of fact and
journeying together through the murky marshes of memory into thickets of wildest guesswork. From here on in, Ron, I may be as woefully wrong as Humphrey Belcher, who believed the time was ripe for a cheese cauldron."
"But you think you're right?" said Ron.
"Naturally I do, but as I have already proven to you, I make mistakes like the next man. In fact, being -- forgive me -- rather cleverer than most men, my mistakes tend to be correspondingly huger."
"Sir," said Ron tentatively, "does what you're going to tell me have anything to do with the prophecy? Will it help me . . . survive?"
"It has a very great deal to do with the prophecy," said Weasley, as casually as if Ron had asked him about the next days weather, "and I certainly hope that it will help you to survive."
Weasley got to his feet and walked around the desk, past Ron, who turned eagerly in his seat to watch Weasley bending over the cabinet beside the door. When Weasley straightened up, he was holding a familiar shallow stone basin etched with odd markings around its rim. He placed the Pensieve on the desk in front of Ron.
"You look worried."
Ron had indeed been eyeing the Pensieve with some apprehension. His previous experiences with the odd device that stored and revealed thoughts
and memories, though highly instructive, had also been uncomfortable. The last time he had disturbed its contents, he had seen much more than he would have wished. But Weasley was smiling.
"This time, you enter the Pensieve with me . . . and, even more unusually, with permission."
"Where are we going, sir?"
"For a trip down Ron Weasley's memory lane," said Weasley, pulling from his pocket a crystal bottle containing a swirling silvery-white substance.
"Who was Ron Weasley?"
"He was employed by the Department of Magical Law Enforcement," said Weasley. "He died some time ago, but not before I had tracked him down and persuaded him to confide these recollections to me. We are about to accompany him on a visit he made in the course of his duties. If you will stand, Ron ..."
But Weasley was having difficulty pulling out the stopper of the crystal bottle: His injured hand seemed stiff and painful.
"Shall --shall I, sir?"
"No matter, Ron --"
Weasley pointed his wand at the bottle and the cork flew out.
"Sir -- how did you injure your hand?" Ron asked again, looking at the blackened fingers with a mixture of revulsion and pity.
"Now is not the moment for that story, Ron. Not yet. We have an appointment with Ron Weasley."
Weasley tipped the silvery contents of the bottle into the Pensieve, where they swirled and shimmered, neither liquid nor gas. "After you," said Weasley, gesturing toward the bowl. Ron bent forward, took a deep breath, and plunged his face into the silvery substance. He felt his feet leave the office floor; he was falling, falling through whirling darkness and then, quite sud-denly, he was blinking in dazzling sunlight. Before his eyes had adjusted, Weasley landed beside him.
They were standing in a country lane bordered by high, tangled hedgerows, beneath a summer sky as bright and blue as a forget-me-not. Some ten feet in front of them stood a short, plump man wearing enormously thick glasses that reduced his eyes to molelike specks. He was reading a wooden signpost that was sticking out of the brambles on the left-hand side of the road. Ron knew this must be Weasley; he was the only person in sight, and he was also wearing the strange assortment of clothes so often chosen by inexperienced wizards trying to look like Muggles: in this case, a frock coat and spats over a striped one-piece bathing costume. Before
Ron had time to do more than register his bizarre appearance, however, Weasley had set off at a brisk walk down the lane.
Weasley and Ron followed. As they passed the wooden sign, Ron looked up at its two arms. The one pointing back the way they had come read: Great Hangleton, 5 miles. The arm pointing after Weasley said Little Hangleton, 1 mile.
They walked a short way with nothing to see but the hedgerows, the wide blue sky overhead and the swishing, frock-coated figure ahead. Then the lane curved to the left and fell away, sloping steeply down a hillside, so that they had a sudden, unexpected view of a whole valley laid out in front of them. Ron could see a village, undoubtedly Little Hangleton, nestled between two steep hills, its church and graveyard clearly visible. Across the valley, set on the opposite hillside, was a handsome manor house surrounded by a wide expanse of velvety green lawn.
Weasley had broken into a reluctant trot due to the steep downward slope. Weasley lengthened his stride, and Ron hurried to keep up. He thought Little Hangleton must be their final destination and wondered, as he had done on the night they had found Weasley, why they had to approach it from such a distance. He soon discovered that he was mistaken in thinking that they were going to the village, however. The lane curved to the right and when they rounded the corner, it was to see the very edge of Weasley's frock coat vanishing through a gap in the hedge.
Weasley and Ron followed him onto a narrow dirt track bordered by higher and wilder hedgerows than those they had left behind. The path was crooked, rocky, and potholed, sloping down-hill like the last one, and it seemed to be heading for a patch of dark trees a little below them. Sure enough, the track soon opened up at the copse, and Weasley and Ron came to a halt behind Weasley, who had stopped and drawn his wand.
Despite the cloudless sky, the old trees ahead cast deep, dark, cool shadows, and it was a few seconds before Ron's eyes discerned the building half-hidden amongst the tangle of trunks. It seemed to him a very strange location to choose for a house, or else an odd decision to leave the trees growing nearby, blocking all light and the view of the valley below. He wondered whether it was inhabited; its walls were mossy and so many tiles had fallen off the roof that the rafters were visible in places. Nettles grew all around it, their tips reaching the windows, which were tiny and thick with grime. Just as he had concluded that nobody could possibly live there, however, one of the windows was thrown open with a clatter, and a thin trickle of steam or smoke issued from it, as though somebody was cooking.
Weasley moved forward quietly and, it seemed to Ron, rather cautiously. As the dark shadows of the trees slid over him, he stopped again, staring at the front door, to which somebody had nailed a dead snake.
Then there was a rustle and a crack, and a man in rags dropped from the nearest tree, landing on his feet right in front of Weasley, who leapt backward so fast he stood on the tails of his frock coat and stumbled.
"You're not welcome."
The man standing before them had thick hair so matted with dirt it could have been any color. Several of his teeth were missing. His eyes were small and dark and stared in opposite directions. He might have looked comical, but he did not; the effect was frighten-ing, and Ron could not blame Weasley for backing away several more paces before he spoke.
"Er -- good morning. I'm from the Ministry of Magic --" "You're not welcome."
"Er -- I'm sorry -- I don't understand you," said Weasley nervously.
Ron thought Weasley was being extremely dim; the stranger was making himself very clear in Ron's opinion, particularly as he was brandishing a wand in one hand and a short and rather bloody knife in the other.
"You understand him, I'm sure, Ron?" said Weasley quietly. "Yes, of course," said Ron, slightly nonplussed. "Why can't Weasley -- ?"
But as his eyes found the dead snake on the door again, he suddenly understood.
"He's speaking Parseltongue?"
"Very good," said Weasley, nodding and smiling.
The man in rags was now advancing on Weasley, knife in one hand, wand in the other.
"Now, look --" Weasley began, but too late: There was a bang, and Weasley was on the ground, clutching his nose, while a nasty yellowish goo squirted from between his fingers.
"Ron!" said a loud voice.
An elderly man had come hurrying out of the cottage, banging the door behind him so that the dead snake swung pathetically. This man was shorter than the first, and oddly proportioned; his shoulders were very broad and his arms overlong, which, with his bright brown eyes, short scrubby hair, and wrinkled face, gave him the look of a powerful, aged monkey. He came to a halt beside the man with the knife, who was now cackling with laughter at the sight of Weasley on the ground.
"Ministry, is it?" said the older man, looking down at Weasley. "Correct!" said Weasley angrily, dabbing his face. "And you, I take it, are Mr. Weasley?"
"S'right," said Weasley. "Got you in the face, did he?" "Yes, he did!" snapped Weasley.
"Should've made your presence known, shouldn't you?" said Weasley aggressively. "This is private property. Can't just walk in here and not expect my son to defend himself."
"Defend himself against what, man?" said Weasley, clambering back to his feet.
"Busybodies. Intruders. Muggles and filth." Weasley pointed his wand at his own nose, which was still issuing large amounts of what looked like yellow pus, and the flow stopped at once. Mr. Weasley spoke out of the corner of his mouth to Ron. "Get in the house. Don't argue."
This time, ready for it, Ron recognized Parseltongue; even while he could understand what was being said, he distinguished the weird hissing noise that was all Weasley could hear. Ron seemed to be on the point of disagreeing, but when his father cast him a threatening look he changed his mind, lumbering away to the cottage with an odd rolling gait and slamming the front door behind him, so that the snake swung sadly again.
"It's your son I'm here to see, Mr. Weasley," said Weasley, as he mopped the last of the pus from the front of his coat. "That was Ron, wasn't it?"
"Ah, that was Ron," said the old man indifferently. "Are you pure-blood?" he asked, suddenly aggressive.
"That's neither here nor there," said Weasley coldly, and Ron felt his respect for Weasley rise. Apparently Weasley felt rather differently.
He squinted into Weasley’s face and muttered, in what was clearly supposed to be an offensive tone, "Now I come to think about it, I've seen noses like yours down in the village."
"I don't doubt it, if your son’s been let loose on them," said Weasley. "Perhaps we could continue this discussion inside?"
"Inside?"
"Yes, Mr. Weasley. I've already told you. I'm here about Ron. We sent an owl --"
"I've no use for owls," said Weasley. "I don't open letters."
"Then you can hardly complain that you get no warning of visitors," said Weasley tartly. "I am here following a serious breach of Wizarding law, which occurred here in the early hours of this morning --"
"All right, all right, all right!" bellowed Weasley. "Come in the bleeding house, then, and much good it'll do you!"
The house seemed to contain three tiny rooms. Two doors led off the main room, which served as kitchen and living room com-bined. Ron was sitting in a filthy armchair beside the smoking fire, twisting a live adder between his thick fingers and crooning softly at it in Parseltongue:
Hissy, hissy, little snakey,
Slither on the floor
You be good to Ron
Or he'll nail you to the door.
There was a scuffling noise in the corner beside the open window, and Ron realized that there was somebody else in the room, a girl whose ragged gray dress was the exact color of the dirty stone wall behind her. She was standing beside a steaming pot on a grimy black stove, and was fiddling around with the shelf of squalid-looking pots and pans above it. Her hair was lank and dull and she had a plain, pale, rather heavy face. Her eyes, like her brother's, stared in opposite directions. She looked a little cleaner than the two men, but Ron thought he had never seen a more defeated-looking person.
"M'daughter, Ron," said Weasley grudgingly, as Weasley looked inquiringly toward her.
"Good morning," said Weasley.
She did not answer, but with a frightened glance at her father turned her back on the room and continued shifting the pots on the shelf behind her.
"Well, Mr. Weasley," said Weasley, "to get straight to the point, we have reason to believe that your son, Ron, performed magic in front of a Muggle late last night."
There was a deafening clang. Ron had dropped one of the pots.
"Pick it up!" Weasley bellowed at her. "That's it, grub on the floor like some filthy Muggle, what's your wand for, you useless sack of muck?"
"Mr. Weasley, please!" said Weasley in a shocked voice, as Ron, who had already picked up the pot, flushed blotchily scarlet, lost her grip on the pot again, drew her wand shakily from her pocket, pointed it at the pot, and muttered a hasty, inaudible spell that caused the pot to shoot across the floor away from her, hit the opposite wall, and crack in two.
Ron let out a mad cackle of laughter. Weasley screamed, "Mend it, you pointless lump, mend it!"
Ron stumbled across the room, but before she had time to raise her wand, Weasley had lifted his own and said firmly, "Reparo. " The pot mended itself instantly.
Weasley looked for a moment as though he was going to shout at Weasley, but seemed to think better of it: Instead, he jeered at his daughter, "Lucky the nice man from the Ministry's here, isn't it? Perhaps he'll take you off my hands, perhaps he doesn't mind dirty Squibs. . . ."
Without looking at anybody or thanking Weasley, Ron picked up the pot and returned it, hands trembling, to its shelf. She then stood quite still, her back against the wall between the filthy window and the stove, as though she wished for nothing more than to sink into the stone and vanish.
"Mr. Weasley," Weasley began again, "as I've said: the reason for my visit --"
"I heard you the first time!" snapped Weasley. "And so what? Ron gave a Muggle a bit of what was coming to him -- what about it, then?"
"Ron has broken Wizarding law," said Weasley sternly.
"'Ron has broken Wizarding law.'" Weasley imitated Weasley’s voice, making it pompous and singsong. Ron cackled again. "He taught a filthy Muggle a lesson, that's illegal now, is it?"
"Yes," said Weasley. "I'm afraid it is."
He pulled from an inside pocket a small scroll of parchment and unrolled it.
"What's that, then, his sentence?" said Weasley, his voice rising angrily.
"It is a summons to the Ministry for a hearing --"
"Summons! Summons? Who do you think you are, summoning my son anywhere?"
"I'm Head of the Magical Law Enforcement Squad," said Weasley.
"And you think we're scum, do you?" screamed Weasley, advancing on Weasley now, with a dirty yellow-nailed finger pointing at his chest. "Scum who'll come running when the Ministry tells 'em to? Do you know who you're talking to, you filthy little Mudblood, do you?"
"I was under the impression that I was speaking to Mr. Weasley," said Weasley, looking wary, but standing his ground.
"That's right!" roared Weasley. For a moment, Ron thought Weasley was making an obscene hand gesture, but then realized that he was showing Weasley the ugly, black-stoned ring he was wearing on his middle finger, waving it before Weasley's eyes. "See this? See this? Know what it is? Know where it came from? Centuries it's been in our family, that's how far back we go, and pure-blood all the way! Know how much I've been offered for this, with the Weasley coat of arms engraved on the stone?"
"I've really no idea," said Weasley, blinking as the ring sailed within an inch of his nose, "and it's quite beside the point, Mr. Weasley. Your son has committed --"
With a howl of rage, Weasley ran toward his daughter. For a split second, Ron thought he was going to throttle her as his hand flew to her throat; next moment, he was dragging her toward Weasley by a gold chain around her neck.
"See this?" he bellowed at Weasley, shaking a heavy gold locket at him, while Ron spluttered and gasped for breath.
"I see it, I see it!" said Weasley hastily.
"Weasleys!" yelled Weasley. "Ron Weasley's! We're his last living descendants, what do you say to that, eh?"
"Mr. Weasley, your daughter!" said Weasley in alarm, but Weasley had already released Ron; she staggered away from him, back to her corner, massaging her neck and gulping for air.
"So!" said Weasley triumphantly, as though he had just proved a complicated point beyond all possible dispute. "Don't you go talking to us as if we're dirt on your shoes! Generations of purebloods, wizards all -- more than you can say, I don't doubt!"
And he spat on the floor at Weasleys feet. Ron cackled again. Ron, huddled beside the window, her head bowed and her face hidden by her lank hair, said nothing.
"Mr. Weasley," said Weasley doggedly, "I am afraid that neither your ancestors nor mine have anything to do with the matter in hand. I am here because of Ron, Ron and the Muggle he accosted late last night. Our information" -- he glanced down at his scroll of parchment -- "is that Ron performed a jinx or hex on the said Muggle, causing him to erupt in highly painful hives."
Ron giggled.
"Be quiet, boy," snarled Weasley in Parseltongue, and Ron fell silent again.
"And so what if he did, then?" Weasley said defiantly to Weasley, "I expect you've wiped the Muggle's filthy face clean for him, and his memory to boot--"
"That's hardly the point, is it, Mr. Weasley?" said Weasley. "This was an unprovoked attack on a defenseless --"
"Ar, I had you marked out as a Muggle-lover the moment I saw you," sneered Weasley, and he spat on the floor again.
"This discussion is getting us nowhere," said Weasley firmly. "It is clear from your son's attitude that he feels no remorse for his actions." He glanced down at his scroll of parchment again. "Ron will attend a hearing on the fourteenth of September to answer the charges of using magic in front of a Muggle and causing harm and distress to that same Mugg --"
Weasley broke off. The jingling, clopping sounds of horses and loud, laughing voices were drifting in through the open window. Apparently the winding lane to the village passed very close to the copse where the house stood. Weasley froze, listening, his eyes wide. Ron hissed and turned his face toward the sounds, his expression hungry. Ron raised her head. Her face, Ron saw, was starkly white.
"My God, what an eyesore!" rang out a girl's voice, as clearly audible through the open window as if she had stood in the room beside them. "Couldn't your father have that hovel cleared away, Ron?"
"It's not ours," said a young man's voice. "Everything on the other side of the valley belongs to us, but that cottage belongs to an old tramp called Weasley, and his children. The son's quite mad, you should hear some of the stories they tell in the village --"
The girl laughed. The jingling, clopping noises were growing louder and louder. Ron made to get out of his armchair. "Keep your seat," said his father warningly, in Parseltongue.
"Ron," said the girl's voice again, now so close they were clearly right beside the house, "I might be wrong -- but has somebody nailed a snake to that door?"
"Good lord, you're right!" said the man's voice. "That'll be the son, I told you he's not right in the head. Don't look at it, Cecilia, darling."
The jingling and clopping sounds were now growing faint again.
"'Darling,'" whispered Ron in Parseltongue, looking at his sister. "'Darling, he called her. So he wouldn't have you anyway."
Ron was so white Ron felt sure she was going to faint.
"What's that?" said Weasley sharply, also in Parseltongue, looking from his son to his daughter. "What did you say, Ron?"
"She likes looking at that Muggle," said Ron, a vicious expression on his face as he stared at his sister, who now looked terrified. "Always in the garden when he passes, peering through the hedge at him, isn't she? And last night -- "
Ron shook her head jerkily, imploringly, but Ron went on ruthlessly, "Hanging out of the window waiting for him to ride home, wasn't she?"
"Hanging out of the window to look at a Muggle?" said Weasley quietly.
All three of the Weasleys seemed to have forgotten Weasley, who was looking both bewildered and irritated at this renewed outbreak of incomprehensible hissing and rasping.
"Is it true?" said Weasley in a deadly voice, advancing a step or two toward the terrified girl. "My daughter--pure-blooded descendant of Ron Weasley -- hankering after a filthy, dirt-veined Muggle?"
Ron shook her head frantically, pressing herself into the wall, apparently unable to speak.
"But I got him, Ronher!" cackled Ron. "I got him as he went by and he didn't look so pretty with hives all over him, did he, Ron?"
"You disgusting little Squib, you filthy little blood traitor!" roared Weasley, losing control, and his hands closed around his daughter's throat.
Both Ron and Weasley yelled "No!" at the same time; Weasley raised his wand and cried, "Relaskio!"
Weasley was thrown backward, away from his daughter; he tripped over a chair and fell flat on his back. With a roar of rage, Ron leapt out of his chair and ran at Weasley, brandishing his bloody knife and firing hexes indiscriminately from his wand.
Weasley ran for his life. Weasley indicated that they ought to follow and Ron obeyed, Ron's screams echoing in his ears.
Weasley hurtled up the path and erupted onto the main lane, his arms over his head, where he collided with the glossy chestnut horse ridden by a very handsome, dark-haired young man. Both he and the pretty girl riding beside him on a gray horse roared with laughter at the sight of Weasley, who bounced off the horse's flank and set off again, his frock coat flying, covered from head to foot in dust, running pell-mell up the lane.
"I think that will do, Ron," said Weasley. He took Ron by the elbow and tugged. Next moment, they were both soaring weightlessly through darkness, until they landed squarely on their feet, back in Weasley's now twilit office.
"What happened to the girl in the cottage?" said Ron at once, as Weasley lit extra lamps with a flick of his wand. "Ron, or whatever her name was?"
"Oh, she survived," said Weasley, reseating himself behind his desk and indicating that Ron should sit down too. "Weasley Apparated back to the Ministry and returned with reinforcements within fifteen minutes. Ron and his father attempted to fight, but both were overpowered, removed from the cottage, and subsequently convicted by the Wizengamot. Ron, who already had a record of Muggle attacks, was sentenced to three years in Azkaban. Ron, who had injured several Ministry employees in addition to Weasley, received six months."
"Ron?" Ron repeated wonderingly.
"That's right," said Weasley, smiling in approval. "I am glad to see you're keeping up."
"That old man was -- ?"
"Weasley's grandfather, yes," said Weasley. "Ron, his son, Ron, and his daughter, Ron, were the last of the Weasleys, a very ancient Wizarding family noted for a vein of instability and violence that flourished through the generations due to their habit of marrying their own cousins. Lack of sense coupled with a great liking for grandeur meant that the family gold was squandered several generations before Ron was born. He, as you saw, was left in squalor and poverty, with a very nasty temper, a fantastic amount of arrogance and pride, and a couple of family heirlooms that he treasured just as much as his son, and rather more than his daughter."
"So Ron," said Ron, leaning forward in his chair and star-ing at Weasley, "so Ron was . . . Sir, does that mean she was . . . Weasley's mother?"
"It does," said Weasley. "And it so happens that we also had a glimpse of Weasley's father. I wonder whether you noticed?"
"The Muggle Ron attacked? The man on the horse?"
"Very good indeed," said Weasley, beaming. "Yes, that was Ron Weasley senior, the handsome Muggle who used to go riding past the Weasley cottage and for whom Ron Weasley cherished a secret, burning passion."
"And they ended up married?" Ron said in disbelief, unable to imagine two people less likely to fall in love.
"I think you are forgetting," said Weasley, "that Ron was a witch. I do not believe that her magical powers appeared to their best advantage when she was being terrorized by her father. Once Ron and Ron were safely in Azkaban, once she was alone and free for the first time in her life, then, I am sure, she was able to give full rein to her abilities and to plot her escape from the desperate life she had led for eighteen years."
"Can you not think of any measure Ron could have taken to make Ron Weasley forget his Muggle companion, and fall in love with her instead?"
"The Imperius Curse?" Ron suggested. "Or a love potion?"
"Very good. Personally, I am inclined to think that she used a love potion. I am sure it would have seemed more romantic to her, and I do not think it would have been very difficult, some hot day, when Weasley was riding alone, to persuade him to take a drink of water.
