In any case, within a few months of the scene we have just witnessed, the village of Little Hangleton enjoyed a tremendous scandal. You can imagine the gossip it caused when the squire's son ran off with the tramp's daughter, Ron."
"But the villagers' shock was nothing to Ron's. He returned from Azkaban, expecting to find his daughter dutifully awaiting his return with a hot meal ready on his table. Instead, he found a clear inch of dust and her note of farewell, explaining what she had done."
"From all that I have been able to discover, he never mentioned her name or existence from that time forth. The shock of her desertion may have contributed to his early death -- or perhaps he had simply never learned to feed himself. Azkaban had greatly weakened Ron, and he did not live to see Ron return to the cottage."
"And Ron? She ... she died, didn't she? Wasn't Weasley brought up in an orphanage?"
"Yes, indeed," said Weasley. "We must do a certain amount of guessing here, although I do not think it is difficult to deduce what happened. You see, within a few months of their runaway marriage, Ron
Weasley reappeared at the manor house in Little Hangleton without his wife. The rumor flew around the neighborhood that he was talking of being 'hoodwinked' and 'taken in.' What he meant, I am sure, is that he had been under an enchantment that had now lifted, though I daresay he did not dare use those precise words for fear of being thought insane. When they heard what he was saying, however, the villagers guessed that Ron had lied to Ron Weasley, pretending that she was going to have his baby, and that he had married her for this reason."
"But she did have his baby."
"But not until a year after they were married. Ron Weasley left her while she was still pregnant."
"What went wrong?" asked Ron. "Why did the love potion stop working?"
"Again, this is guesswork," said Weasley, "but I believe that Ron, who was deeply in love with her husband, could not bear to continue enslaving him by magical means. I believe that she made the choice to stop giving him the potion. Perhaps, besotted as she was, she had convinced herself that he would by now have fallen in love with her in return. Perhaps she thought he would stay for the baby's sake. If so, she was wrong on both counts. He left her, never saw her again, and never troubled to discover what became of his son."
The sky outside was inky black and the lamps in Weasley's office seemed to glow more brightly than before.
"I think that will do for tonight, Ron," said Weasley after a moment or two.
"Yes, sir," said Ron.
He got to his feet, but did not leave.
"Sir ... is it important to know all this about Weasley's past?"
"Very important, I think," said Weasley.
"And it... it's got something to do with the prophecy?"
"It has everything to do with the prophecy."
"Right," said Ron, a little confused, but reassured all the same.
He turned to go, then another question occurred to him, and he turned back again. "Sir, am I allowed to tell Ron and Ron everything you've told me?"
Weasley considered him for a moment, then said, "Yes, I think Mr. Weasley and Miss Weasley have proved themselves trust-worthy. But Ron, I am going to ask you to ask them not to repeat any of this to anybody else.
It would not be a good idea if word got around how much I know, or suspect, about Lord Weasley's secrets."
"No, sir, I'll make sure it's just Ron and Ron. Good night."
He turned away again, and was almost at the door when he saw it. Sitting on one of the little spindle-legged tables that supported so many frail-looking silver instruments, was an ugly gold ring set with a large, cracked, black stone.
"Sir," said Ron, staring at it. "That ring--"
"Yes?" said Weasley.
"You were wearing it when we visited Professor Weasley that night."
"So I was," Weasley agreed.
"But isn't it... sir, isn't it the same ring Ron Weasley showed Weasley?"
Weasley bowed his head. "The very same."
"But how come -- ? Have you always had it?"
"No, I acquired it very recently," said Weasley. "A few days before I came to fetch you from your aunt and uncle's, in fact."
"That would be around the time you injured your hand, then, sir?"
"Around that time, yes, Ron."
Ron hesitated. Weasley was smiling.
"Sir, how exactly -- ?"
"Too late, Ron! You shall hear the story another time. Good night."
"Good night, sir."
Chapter 11: Ron's helping hand
As Ron had predicted, the sixth years' free periods were not the hours of blissful relaxation Ron had anticipated, but times in which to attempt to keep up with the vast amount of homework they were being set. Not only were they studying as though they had exams every day, but the lessons themselves had become more demanding than ever before. Ron barely understood half of what Professor Weasley said to them these days; even Ron had had to ask her to repeat instructions once or twice. Incredibly, and to Ron's increasing resentment, Ron's best subject had suddenly become Potions, thanks to the Half-Blood Prince.
Nonverbal spells were now expected, not only in Defense Against the Dark Arts, but in Charms and Transfiguration too. Ron frequently looked over at his classmates in the common room or at mealtimes to see them purple in the face and straining as though they had overdosed on U-No-Poo; but he knew that they were really struggling to make spells work without saying incantations aloud. It was a relief to get outside into the greenhouses; they were dealing with more dangerous plants than ever in Herbology, but at least they were still allowed to swear loudly if the Venomous Tentacula seized them unexpectedly from behind.
One result of their enormous workload and the frantic hours of practicing nonverbal spells was that Ron, Ron, and Ron had so far been unable to find time to go and visit Weasley. He had stopped coming to meals at the staff table, an ominous sign, and on the few occasions when they had passed
him in the corridors or out in the grounds, he had mysteriously failed to notice them or hear their greetings.
"We've got to go and explain," said Ron, looking up at Weasley's huge empty chair at the staff table the following Saturday at breakfast.
"We've got Quidditch tryouts this morning!" said Ron. "And we're supposed to be practicing that Aguamenti Charm from Weasley! Anyway, explain what? How are we going to tell him we hated his stupid subject?"
"We didn't hate it!" said Ron.
"Speak for yourself, I haven't forgotten the skrewts," said Ron darkly. "And I'm telling you now, we've had a narrow escape. You didn't hear him going on about his gormless brother -- we'd have been teaching Ron how to tie his shoelaces if we'd stayed."
"I hate not talking to Weasley," said Ron, looking upset.
"We'll go down after Quidditch," Ron assured her. He too was missing Weasley, although like Ron he thought that they were better off without Ron in their lives. "But trials might take all morning, the number of people who have applied." He felt slightly nervous at confronting the first hurdle of his Captaincy. "I dunno why the team's this popular all of a sudden."
"Oh, come on, Ron," said Ron, suddenly impatient. "It's not Quidditch that's popular, it's you! You've never been more interesting, and frankly, you've never been more fanciable."
Ron gagged on a large piece of kipper. Ron spared him one look of disdain before turning back to Ron.
"Everyone knows you've been telling the truth now, don't they? The whole Wizarding world has had to admit that you were right about Weasley being back and that you really have fought him twice in the last two years and escaped both times. And now they're calling you 'the Ronsen One' -- well, come on, can't you see why people are fascinated by you?"
Ron was finding the Great Hall very hot all of a sudden, even though the ceiling still looked cold and rainy.
"And you've been through all that persecution from the Ministry when they were trying to make out you were unstable and a liar. You can still see the marks on the back of your hand where that evil woman made you write with your own blood, but you stuck to your story anyway. ..."
"You can still see where those brains got hold of me in the Ministry, look," said Ron, shaking back his sleeves.
"And it doesn't hurt that you've grown about a foot over the summer either," Ron finished, ignoring Ron.
"I'm tall," said Ron inconsequentially.
The post owls arrived, swooping down through rain-flecked windows, scattering everyone with droplets of water. Most people were receiving more post than usual; anxious parents were keen to hear from their children and to reassure them, in turn, that all was well at home. Ron had received no mail since the start of term; his only regular correspondent was now dead and although he had hoped that Weasley might write occasionally, he had so far been disappointed. He was very surprised, therefore, to see the snowy white Ron circling amongst all the brown and gray owls. She landed in front of him carrying a large, square package. A moment later, an identical package landed in front of Ron, crushing beneath it his minuscule and exhausted owl, Ron.
"Ha!" said Ron, unwrapping the parcel to reveal a new copy of Advanced Potion-Making, fresh from Flourish and Blotts.
"Oh good," said Ron, delighted. "Now you can give that graffitied copy back."
"Are you mad?" said Ron. "I'm keeping it! Look, I've thought it out --"
He pulled the old copy of Advanced Potion-Making out of his bag and tapped the cover with his wand, muttering, "Dijjindo!" The cover fell off. He did the same thing with the brand-new book (Ron looked scandalized). He then swapped the covers, tapped each, and said, "Reparo!"
There sat the Prince's copy, disguised as a new book, and there sat the fresh copy from Flourish and Blotts, looking thoroughly secondhand.
"I'll give Weasley back the new one, he can't complain, it cost nine Galleons."
Ron pressed her lips together, looking angry and disapproving, but was distracted by a third owl landing in front of her carrying that day's copy of the Daily Prophet. She unfolded it hastily and scanned the front page.
"Anyone we know dead?" asked Ron in a determinedly casual voice; he posed the same question every time Ron opened her paper.
"No, but there have been more dementor attacks," said Ron. "And an arrest."
"Excellent, who?" said Ron, thinking of Weasleyatrix Weasley. "Ron Weasley," said Ron.
"What?" said Ron, startled.
"'Ronley Weasley, conductor on the popular Wizarding conveyance the Knight Bus, has been arrested on suspicion of Death Eater activity. Mr. Weasley, 21, was taken into custody late last night after a raid on his Clapham home. . .'"
"Ron Weasley, a Death Eater?" said Ron, remembering the spotty youth he had first met three years before. "No way!"
"He might have been put under the Imperius Curse," said Ron reasonably. "You never can tell."
"It doesn't look like it," said Ron, who was still reading. "It says here he was arrested after he was overheard talking about the Death Eaters' secret plans in a pub." She looked up with a troubled expression on her face. "If he was under the Imperius Curse, he'd hardly stand around gossiping about their plans, would he?"
"It sounds like he was trying to make out he knew more than he did," said Ron. "Isn't he the one who claimed he was going to become Minister of Magic when he was trying to chat up those veela?"
"Yeah, that's him," said Ron. "I dunno what they're playing at, taking Ron seriously."
"They probably want to look as though they're doing something," said Ron, frowning. "People are terrified -- you know the Weasley twins' parents want them to go home? And Eloise Midgen has already been withdrawn. Her father picked her up last night."
"What!" said Ron, goggling at Ron. "But Hogwarts is safer than their homes, bound to be! We've got Aurors, and all those extra protective spells, and we've got Weasley!"
"I don't think we've got him all the time," said Ron very quietly, glancing toward the staff table over the top of the Prophet. "Haven't you noticed? His seat's been empty as often as Weasley's this past week."
Ron and Ron looked up at the staff table. The headmaster's chair was indeed empty. Now Ron came to think of it, he had not seen Weasley since their private lesson a week ago.
"I think he's left the school to do something with the Order," said Ron in a low voice. "I mean . . . it's all looking serious, isn't it?"
Ron and Ron did not answer, but Ron knew that they were all thinking the same thing. There had been a horrible incident the day before, when Ron Weasley had been taken out of Herbology to be told her mother had been found dead. They had not seen Ron since.
When they left the Weasley table five minutes later to head down to the Quidditch pitch, they passed Ron Weasley and Ron Weasley. Remembering what Ron had said about the Weasley twins' parents wanting them to leave Hogwarts, Ron was unsurprised to see that the two best friends were whispering together, looking distressed. What did surprise him was that when Ron drew level with them, Ron suddenly nudged Ron, who looked around and gave Ron a wide smile. Ron blinked at her, then returned the smile uncertainly. His walk instantly became something more like a strut. Ron resisted the temptation to laugh, remembering that Ron had refrained from doing so after Weasley had broken
Ron's nose; Ron, however, looked cold and distant all the way down to the stadium through the cool, misty drizzle, and departed to find a place in the stands without wishing Ron good luck.
As Ron had expected, the trials took most of the morning. Half of Weasley House seemed to have turned up, from first years who were nervously clutching a selection of the dreadful old school brooms, to seventh years who towered over the rest, looking coolly intimidating. The latter included a large, wiry-haired boy Ron recognized immediately from the Hogwarts Express.
"We met on the train, in old Sluggy's compartment," he said confidently, stepping out of the crowd to shake Ron's hand. "Ron Weasley, Keeper."
"You didn't try out last year, did you?" asked Ron, taking note of the breadth of Weasley and thinking that he would probably block all three goal hoops without even moving.
"I was in the hospital wing when they held the trials," said Weasley, with something of a swagger. "Ate a pound of doxy eggs for a bet."
"Right," said Ron. "Well. . . if you wait over there ..." He pointed over to the edge of the pitch, close to where Ron was sitting. He thought he saw a flicker of annoyance pass over Weasley's face and wondered whether Weasley expected preferential treatment because they were both "old Sluggy's" favorites. Ron decided to start with a basic test, asking all
applicants for the team to divide into groups of ten and fly once around the pitch. This was a good decision: the first ten was made up of first years, and it could not have been plainer that they had hardly ever flown before. Only one boy managed to remain airborne for more than a few seconds, and he was so surprised he promptly crashed into one of the goal posts.
The second group was comprised of ten of the silliest girls Ron had ever encountered, who, when he blew his whistle, merely fell about giggling and clutching one another. Ron Weasley was amongst them. When he told them to leave the pitch, they did so quite cheerfully and went to sit in the stands to heckle everyone else.
The third group had a pileup halfway around the pitch. Most of the fourth group had come without broomsticks. The fifth group were Weasleys.
"If there's anyone else here who's not from Weasley," roared Ron, who was starting to get seriously annoyed, "leave now, please!
There was a pause, then a couple of little Weasleys went sprinting off the pitch, snorting with laughter.
After two hours, many complaints, and several tantrums, one involving a crashed Comet Two Sixty and several broken teeth, Ron had found himself three Chasers: Ron Weasley, returned to the team after an excellent trial; a new find called Ron Weasley, who was particularly good at dodging Bludgers; and Ron Weasley, who had outflown all the competition and scored seventeen goals to boot. Pleased though he was with his choices,
Ron had also shouted himself hoarse at the many complainers and was now enduring a similar battle with the rejected Beaters.
"That's my final decision and if you don't get out of the way of the Keepers I'll hex you," he bellowed.
Neither of his chosen Beaters had the old brilliance of Ron and Ron, but he was still reasonably pleased with them: Ronmy Peakes, a short but broad-chested third-year boy who had managed to raise a lump the size of an egg on the back of Ron's head with a ferociously hit Bludger, and Ritchie Coote, who looked weedy but aimed well. They now joined Ron, Ron, and Ron in the stands to watch the selection of their last team member.
Ron had deliberately left the trial of the Keepers until last, hoping for an emptier stadium and less pressure on all concerned. Unfortunately, however, all the rejected players and a number of people who had come down to watch after a lengthy breakfast had joined the crowd by now, so that it was larger than ever. As each Keeper flew up to the goal hoops, the crowd roared and jeered in equal measure. Ron glanced over at Ron, who had always had a problem with nerves; Ron had hoped that winning their final match last term might have cured it, but apparently not: Ron was a delicate shade of green.
None of the first five applicants saved more than two goals apiece. To Ron's great disappointment, Ron Weasley saved four penalties out of five. On the last one, however, he shot off in completely the wrong
direction; the crowd laughed and booed and Weasley returned to the ground grinding his teeth.
Ron looked ready to pass out as he mounted his Cleansweep Eleven. "Good luck!" cried a voice from the stands. Ron looked around, expecting to see Ron, but it was Ron Weasley. He would have quite liked to have hidden his face in his hands, as she did a moment later, but thought that as the Captain he ought to show slightly more grit, and so turned to watch Ron do his trial.
Yet he need not have worried: Ron saved one, two, three, four, five penalties in a row. Delighted, and resisting joining in the cheers of the crowd with difficulty, Ron turned to Weasley to tell him that, most unfortunately, Ron had beaten him, only to find Weasley's red face inches from his own. He stepped back hastily.
"His sister didn't really try," said Weasley menacingly. There was a vein pulsing in his temple like the one Ron had often ad-mired in Uncle Ron's. "She gave him an easy save."
"Rubbish," said Ron coldly. "That was the one he nearly missed."
Weasley took a step nearer Ron, who stood his ground this time.
"Give me another go."
"No," said Ron. "You've had your go. You saved four. Ron saved five. Ron's Keeper, he won it fair and square. Get out of my way."
He thought for a moment that Weasley might punch him, but he contented himself with an ugly grimace and stormed away, growling what sounded like threats to thin air.
Ron turned around to find his new team beaming at him.
"Well done," he croaked. "You flew really well --"
"You did brilliantly, Ron!"
This time it really was Ron running toward them from the stands; Ron saw Ron walking off the pitch, arm in arm with Ron, a rather grumpy expression on her face. Ron looked extremely pleased with himself and even taller than usual as he grinned at the team and at Ron.
After fixing the time of their first full practice for the following Thursday, Ron, Ron, and Ron bade good-bye to the rest of the team and headed off toward Weasley's. A watery sun was trying to break through the clouds now and it had stopped drizzling at last. Ron felt extremely hungry; he hoped there would be some-thing to eat at Weasley's.
"I thought I was going to miss that fourth penalty," Ron was saying happily. "Tricky shot from Ron, did you see, had a bit of spin on it --"
"Yes, yes, you were magnificent," said Ron, looking amused.
"I was better than that Weasley anyway," said Ron in a highly satisfied voice. "Did you see him lumbering off in the wrong direction on his fifth? Looked like he'd been Confunded. ..."
To Ron's surprise, Ron turned a very deep shade of pink at these words. Ron noticed nothing; he was too busy describing each of his other penalties in loving detail.
The great gray hippogriff, Ron, was tethered in front of Weasley's cabin. He clicked his razor-sharp beak at their approach and turned his huge head toward them.
"Oh dear," said Ron nervously. "He's still a bit scary, isn't he?"
"Come off it, you've ridden him, haven't you?" said Ron. Ron stepped forward and bowed low to the hippogriff without breaking eye contact or blinking. After a few seconds, Ron sank into a bow too.
"How are you?" Ron asked him in a low voice, moving forward to stroke the feathery head. "Missing him? But you're okay here with Weasley, aren't you?"
"Oi!" said a loud voice.
Weasley had come striding around the corner of his cabin wearing a large flowery apron and carrying a sack of potatoes. His enormous boarhound, Ron, was at his heels; Ron gave a booming bark and bounded forward.
"Git away from him! He'll have yer fingers -- oh. It's yeh lot."
Ron was jumping up at Ron and Ron, attempting to lick their ears. Weasley stood and looked at them all for a split second, then turned and strode into his cabin, slamming the door behind him.
"Oh dear!" said Ron, looking stricken.
"Don't worry about it," said Ron grimly. He walked over to the door and knocked loudly. "Weasley! Open up, we want to talk to you!"
There was no sound from within.
"If you don't open the door, we'll blast it open!" Ron said, pulling out his wand.
"Ron!" said Ron, sounding shocked. "You can't possibly --"
"Yeah, I can!" said Ron. "Rond back --"
But before he could say anything else, the door flew open again as Ron had known it would, and there stood Weasley, glowering down at him and looking, despite the flowery apron, positively alarming.
"I'm a teacher!" he roared at Ron. "A teacher, Weasley! How dare yeh threaten ter break down my door!"
"I'm sorry, sir" said Ron, emphasizing the last word as he stowed his wand inside his robes.
Weasley looked stunned. "Since when have yeh called me 'sir'?"
"Since when have you called me 'Weasley'?"
"Oh, very clever," growled Weasley. "Very amusin'. That's me outsmarted, innit? All righ', come in then, yeh ungrateful little . . ."
Mumbling darkly, he stood back to let them pass. Ron scurried in after Ron, looking rather frightened.
"Well?" said Weasley grumpily, as Ron, Ron, and Ron sat down around his enormous wooden table, Ron laying his head immediately upon Ron's knee and drooling all over his robes. "What's this? Feelin' sorry for me? Reckon I'm lonely or summat?"
"No," said Ron at once. "We wanted to see you."
"We've missed you!" said Ron tremulously.
"Missed me, have yeh?" snorted Weasley. "Yeah. Righ'."
He stomped around, brewing up tea in his enormous copper kettle, muttering all the while. Finally he slammed down three bucket-sized mugs of mahogany-brown tea in front of them and a plate of his rock cakes. Ron was hungry enough even for Weasley's cooking, and took one at once.
"Weasley," said Ron timidly, when he joined them at the table and started peeling his potatoes with a brutality that suggested that each tuber had done him a great personal wrong, "we really wanted to carry on with Care of Magical Creatures, you know." Weasley gave another great snort. Ron rather thought some bo-geys landed on the potatoes, and was inwardly thankful that they were not staying for dinner.
"We did!" said Ron. "But none of us could fit it into our schedules!"
"Yeah. Righ'," said Weasley again.
There was a funny squelching sound and they all looked around: Ron let out a tiny shriek, and Ron leapt out of his seat and hurried around the table away from the large barrel standing in the corner that they had only just noticed. It was full of what looked like foot-long maggots, slimy, white, and writhing.
"What are they, Weasley?" asked Ron, trying to sound interested rather than revolted, but putting down his rock cake all the same.
"Jus' giant grubs," said Weasley.
"And they grow into ... ?" said Ron, looking apprehensive.
"They won' grow inter nuthin'," said Weasley. "I got 'em ter feed ter Ron."
And without warning, he burst into tears.
"Weasley!" cried Ron, leaping up, hurrying around the table the long way to avoid the barrel of maggots, and putting an arm around his shaking shoulders. "What is it?"
"It's. . . him . .." gulped Weasley, his beetle-black eyes stream-ing as he mopped his face with his apron. "It's . . . Ron. ... I think he's dyin'. . , . He got ill over the summer an' he's not gettin' better.... I don' know what I'll do if he ... if he ... We've bin tergether so long. ..."
Ron patted Weasley's shoulder, looking at a complete loss for anything to say. Ron knew how she felt. He had known Weasley to present a vicious baby dragon with a teddy bear, seen him croon over giant scorpions with suckers and stingers, attempt to reason with his brutal giant of a half-brother, but this was perhaps the most incomprehensible of all his monster fancies: the gigantic talking spider, Ron, who dwelled deep in the Forbidden Forest and which he and Ron had only narrowly escaped four years previously.
"Is there -- is there anything we can do?" Ron asked, ignoring Ron's frantic grimaces and head-shakings.
"I don' think there is, Ron," choked Weasley, attempting to stem the flood of his tears. "See, the rest o' the tribe ... Ron's family . . . they're gettin' a bit funny now he's ill... bit restive ..."
"Yeah, I think we saw a bit of that side of them," said Ron in an undertone.
"... I don' reckon it'd be safe fer anyone but me ter go near the colony at the mo'," Weasley finished, blowing his nose hard on his apron and looking up. "But thanks fer offerin', Ron. ... It means a lot."
After that, the atmosphere lightened considerably, for although neither Ron nor Ron had shown any inclination to go and feed giant grubs to a murderous, gargantuan spider, Weasley seemed to take it for granted that they would have liked to have done and became his usual self once more.
"Ar, I always knew yeh'd find it hard ter squeeze me inter yer timetables," he said gruffly, pouring them more tea. "Even if yeh applied fer Time-Turners --"
"We couldn't have done," said Ron. "We smashed the entire stock of Ministry Time-Turners when we were there last summer. It was in the Daily Prophet."
"Ar, well then," said Weasley. "There's no way yeh could've done it. ... I'm sorry I've bin -- yeh know -- I've jus' bin worried about Ron ... an I did wonder whether, if Professor Weasley had bin teachin' yeh --"
At which all three of them stated categorically and untruthfully that Professor Weasley, who had substituted for Weasley a few times, was a dreadful teacher, with the result that by the time Weasley waved them off the premises at dusk, he looked quite cheerful.
"I'm starving," said Ron, once the door had closed behind them and they were hurrying through the dark and deserted grounds; he had abandoned the rock cake after an ominous cracking noise from one of his back teeth. "And I've got that detention with Weasley tonight, I haven't got much time for dinner."
As they came into the castle they spotted Ron Weasley entering the Great Hall. It took him two attempts to get through the doors; he ricocheted off the frame on the first attempt. Ron merely guffawed gloatingly and strode off into the Hall after him, but Ron caught Ron's arm and held her back.
"What?" said Ron defensively.
"If you ask me," said Ron quietly, "Weasley looks like he was Confunded this morning. And he was standing right in front of where you were sitting."
Ron blushed.
"Oh, all right then, I did it," she whispered. "But you should have heard the way he was talking about Ron and Ron! Anyway, he's got a nasty temper, you saw how he reacted when he didn't get in -- you wouldn't have wanted someone like that on the team."
"No," said Ron. "No, I suppose that's true. But wasn't that dishonest, Ron? I mean, you're a prefect, aren't you?"
"Oh, be quiet," she snapped, as he smirked.
"What are you two doing?" demanded Ron, reappearing in the doorway to the Great Hall and looking suspicious.
"Nothing," said Ron and Ron together, and they hurried after Ron. The smell of roast beef made Ron's stomach ache with hunger, but they had barely taken three steps toward the Weasley table when Professor Weasley appeared in front of them, blocking their path.
"Ron, Ron, just the man I was hoping to see!" he boomed genially, twiddling the ends of his walrus mustache and puffing out his enormous belly, "I was hoping to catch you before dinner! What do you say to a spot of supper tonight in my rooms instead? We're having a little party, just a few rising stars, I've got Weasley coming and Weasley, the charming Melinda Ronbin -- I don't know whether you know her? Her family owns a large
chain of apothecaries -- and, of course, I hope very much that Miss Weasley will favor me by coming too."
Weasley made Ron a little bow as he finished speaking. It was as though Ron was not present; Weasley did not so much as look at him.
"I can't come, Professor," said Ron at once. "I've got a detention with Professor Weasley."
"Oh dear!" said Weasley, his face falling comically. "Dear, dear, I was counting on you, Ron! Well, now, I'll just have to have a word with Ron and explain the situation. I'm sure I'll be able to persuade him to postpone your detention. Yes, I'll see you both later!" He bustled away out of the Hall.
"He's got no chance of persuading Weasley," said Ron, the moment Weasley was out of earshot. "This detention’s already been postponed once; Weasley did it for Weasley, but he won't do it for anyone else."
"Oh, I wish you could come, I don't want to go on my own!" said Ron anxiously; Ron knew that she was thinking about Weasley.
"I doubt you'll be alone, Ron'll probably be invited," snapped Ron, who did not seem to have taken kindly to being ignored by Weasley.
After dinner they made their way back to Weasley Tower. The common room was very crowded, as most people had finished dinner by now, but
they managed to find a free table and sat down; Ron, who had been in a bad mood ever since the encounter with Weasley, folded his arms and frowned at the ceiling. Ron reached out for a copy of the Evening Prophet, which somebody had left abandoned on a chair.
"Anything new?" said Ron.
"Not really. . ." Ron had opened the newspaper and was scanning the inside pages. "Oh, look, your dad's in here, Ron -- he's all right!" she added quickly, for Ron had looked around in alarm. "It just says he's been to visit the Weasleys' house. 'This second search of the Death Eaters residence does not seem to have yielded any results. Ron Weasley of the Office for the Detection and Confiscation of Counterfeit Defensive Spells and Protective Objects said that his team had been acting upon a confidential tip-off.'"
"Yeah, mine!" said Ron. "I told him at Kings Cross about Weasley and that thing he was trying to get Borgin to fix! Well, if it's not at their house, he must have brought whatever it is to Hogwarts with him --"
"But how can he have done, Ron?" said Ron, putting down the newspaper with a surprised look. "We were all searched when we arrived, weren't we?"
"Were you?" said Ron, taken aback. "I wasn't!"
"Oh no, of course you weren't, I forgot you were late. Well, Weasley ran over all of us with Secrecy Sensors when we got into the entrance hall. Any Dark object would have been found, I know for a fact Weasley had a shrunken head confiscated. So you see, Weasley can't have brought in anything dangerous!"
Momentarily stymied, Ron watched Ron Weasley playing with Arnold the Pygmy Puff for a while before seeing a way around this objection.
"Someone's sent it to him by owl, then," he said. "His mother or someone."
"All the owls are being checked too," said Ron. "Weasley told us so when he was jabbing those Secrecy Sensors everywhere he could reach."
Really stumped this time, Ron found nothing else to say. There did not seem to be any way Weasley could have brought a dangerous or Dark object into the school. He looked hopefully at Ron, who was sitting with his arms folded, staring over at Ron Weasley.
"Can you think of any way Weasley -- ?"
"Oh, drop it, Ron," said Ron.
"Listen, it's not my fault Weasley invited Ron and me to his stupid party, neither of us wanted to go, you know!" said Ron, firing up.
"Well, as I'm not invited to any parties," said Ron, getting to his feet again, "I think I'll go to bed."
He stomped off toward the door to the boys' dormitories, leaving Ron and Ron staring after him.
"Ron?" said the new Chaser, Ron Weasley, appearing suddenly at his shoulder. "I've got a message for you."
"From Professor Weasley?" asked Ron, sitting up hopefully.
"No ... from Professor Weasley," said Ron. Ron's heart sank. "He says you're to come to his office at half past eight tonight to do your detention -- er -- no matter how many party invitations you've received. And he wanted you to know you'll be sorting out rotten flobberworms from good ones, to use in Potions and -- and he says there's no need to bring protective gloves."
"Right," said Ron grimly. "Thanks a lot, Ron."
Chapter 12: Silver and opals
Where was Weasley, and what was he doing?
Ron caught sight of the headmaster only twice over the next few weeks. He rarely appeared at meals anymore, and Ron was sure Ron was right in thinking that he was leaving the school for days at a time. Had Weasley forgotten the lessons he was supposed to be giving Ron? Weasley had said that the lessons were leading to something to do with the prophecy; Ron had felt bolstered, comforted, and now he felt slightly abandoned.
Halfway through October came their first trip of the term to Hogsmeade. Ron had wondered whether these trips would still be allowed, given the increasingly tight security measures around the school, but was pleased to know that they were going ahead; it was always good to get out of the castle grounds for a few hours.
Ron woke early on the morning of the trip, which was proving stormy, and whiled away the time until breakfast by reading his copy of Advanced Potion-Making. He did not usually lie in bed reading his textbooks; that sort of behavior, as Ron rightly said, was indecent in anybody except Ron, who was simply weird that way. Ron felt, however, that the Half-Blood Princes copy of Advanced Potion-Making hardly qualified as a textbook. The more Ron pored over the book, the more he realized how much was in there, not only the handy hints and shortcuts on potions that was earning him such a glowing reputation with Weasley, but also the imaginative little
jinxes and hexes scribbled in the margins, which Ron was sure, judging by the crossings-out and revisions, that the Prince had invented himself.
Ron had already attempted a few of the Prince's self-invented spells. There had been a hex that caused toenails to grow alarmingly fast (he had tried this on Weasley in the corridor, with very entertaining results); a jinx that glued the tongue to the roof of the mouth (which he had twice used, to general applause, on an unsuspecting Ron Weasley); and, perhaps most useful of all, Muffliato, a spell that filled the ears of anyone nearby with an unidentifiable buzzing, so that lengthy conversations could be held in class with out being overheard. The only person who did not find these charms amusing was Ron, who maintained a rigidly disapproving expression throughout and refused to talk at all if Ron had used the Muffliato spell on anyone in the vicinity.
Sitting up in bed, Ron turned the book sideways so as to examine more closely the scribbled instructions for a spell that seemed to have caused the Prince some trouble. There were many crossings-out and alterations, but finally, crammed into a corner of the page, the scribble:
Levicorpus (nvbl)
While the wind and sleet pounded relentlessly on the windows, and Ron snored loudly, Ron stared at the letters in brackets. Nvbl . . that had to mean "nonverbal." Ron rather doubted he would be able to bring off this particular spell; he was still having difficulty with nonverbal spells, something Weasley had been quick to comment on in every D.A.D.A. class.
On the other hand, the Prince had proved a much more effective teacher than Weasley so far.
Pointing his wand at nothing in particular, he gave it an upward flick and said Levicorpus! inside his head. "Aaaaaaaargh!"
There was a flash of light and the room was full of voices: Everyone had woken up as Ron had let out a yell. Ron sent Advanced Potion-Making flying in panic; Ron was dangling upside down in midair as though an invisible hook had hoisted him up by the ankle.
"Sorry!" yelled Ron, as Ron and Ron roared with laughter, and Ron picked himself up from the floor, having fallen out of Bed. "Hang on -- I'll let you down --"
He groped for the potion book and riffled through it in a panic, trying to find the right page; at last he located it and deciphered the cramped word underneath the spell: Praying that this was the counter-jinx, Ron thought Liberacorpus! with all his might. There was another flash of light, and Ron fell in a heap onto his mattress.
"Sorry," repeated Ron weakly, while Ron and Ron continued to roar with laughter.
"Ronorrow," said Ron in a muffled voice, "I'd rather you set the alarm clock."
By the time they had got dressed, padding themselves out with several of Mrs. Weasleys hand-knitted sweaters and carrying cloaks, scarves, and gloves, Ron's shock had subsided and he had decided that Ron's new spell was highly amusing; so amusing, in fact, that he lost no time in regaling Ron with the story as they sat down for breakfast.
"... and then there was another flash, of light and I landed on the bed again!" Ron grinned, helping himself to sausages.
Ron had not cracked a smile during this anecdote, and now turned an expression of wintry disapproval upon Ron.
"Was this spell, by any chance, another one from that potion book of yours?" she asked.
Ron frowned at her.
"Always jump to the worst conclusion, don't you?"
"Was it?"
"Well. . . yeah, it was, but so what?"
"So you just decided to try out an unknown, handwritten incantation and see what would happen?"
"Why does it matter if it's handwritten?" said Ron, preferring not to answer the rest of the question.
"Because it’s probably not Ministry of Magic approved," said Ron. "And also," she added, as Ron and Ron rolled their eyes, "because I'm starting to think this Prince character was a bit dodgy."
Both Ron and Ron shouted her down at once.
"It was a laugh!" said Ron, upending a ketchup bottle over his sausages. "Just a laugh, Ron, that's all!"
"Dangling people upside down by the ankle?" said Hermi-one. "Who puts their time and energy into making up spells like that?"
"Ron and Ron," said Ron, shrugging, "it's their kind of thing. And, er--"
"My dad," said Ron. He had only just remembered.
"What?" said Ron and Ron together.
"My dad used this spell," said Ron. "I -- Weasley told me."
'This last part was not true; in fact, Ron had seen his father use the spell on Weasley, but he had never told Ron and Ron about that particular
excursion into the Pensieve. Now, however, a wonderful possibility occurred to him. Could the Half-Blood Prince possibly be -- ?
"Maybe your dad did use it, Ron," said Ron, "but he's not the only one. We've seen a whole bunch of people use it, in case you've forgotten. Dangling people in the air. Making them float along, asleep, helpless."
Ron stared at her. With a sinking feeling, he too remembered the behavior of the Death Eaters at the Quidditch World Cup. Ron came to his aid.
"That was different," he said robustly. "They were abusing it. Ron and his dad were just having a laugh. You don't like the Prince, Ron," he added, pointing a sausage at her sternly, "because he's better than you at Potions --"
"It's got nothing to do with that!" said Ron, her cheeks reddening. "I just think it's very irresponsible to start performing spells when you don't even know what they're for, and stop talking about 'the Prince' as if it's his title, I bet it's just a stupid nickname, and it doesn't seem as though he was a very nice person to me!"
"I don't see where you get that from," said Ron heatedly. "If he'd been a budding Death Eater he wouldn't have been boasting about being 'half-blood,' would he?"
Even as he said it, Ron remembered that his father had been pure-blood, but he pushed the thought out of his mind; he would worry about that later.
"The Death Eaters can't all be pure-blood, there aren't enough pure-blood wizards left," said Ron stubbornly. "I expect most of them are half-bloods pretending to be pure. It's only Muggle-borns they hate, they'd be quite happy to let you and Ron join up."
"There is no way they'd let me be a Death Eater!" said Ron indignantly, a bit of sausage flying off the fork he was now brandishing at Ron and hitting Ron Weasley on the head. "My whole family are blood traitors! That's as bad as Muggle-borns to Death Eaters!"
"And they'd love to have me," said Ron sarcastically. "We'd be best pals if they didn't keep trying to do me in."
This made Ron laugh; even Ron gave a grudging smile, and a distraction arrived in the shape of Ron.
"Hey, Ron, I'm supposed to give you this."
It was a scroll of parchment with Ron's name written upon it in familiar thin, slanting writing.
"Thanks, Ron. . . It's Weasley's next lesson!" Ron told Ron and Ron, pulling open the parchment and quickly read-ing its contents.
"Monday evening!" He felt suddenly light and happy. "Want to join us in Hogsmeade, Ron?" he asked.
"I'm going with Ron -- might see you there," she replied, waving at them as she left.
Weasley was standing at the oak front doors as usual, checking off the names of people who had permission to go into Hogsmeade. The process took even longer than normal as Weasley was triple-checking everybody with his Secrecy Sensor.
"What does it matter if we're smuggling Dark stuff OUT?" demanded Ron, eyeing the long thin Secrecy Sensor with apprehension. "Surely you ought to be checking what we bring back IN?"
His cheek earned him a few extra jabs with the Sensor, and he was still wincing as they stepped out into the wind and sleet.
The walk into Hogsmeade was not enjoyable. Ron wrapped his scarf over his lower face; the exposed part soon felt both raw and numb. The road to the village was full of students bent double against the bitter wind. More than once Ron wondered whether they might not have had a better time in the warm common room, and when they finally reached Hogsmeade and saw that Zonko's Joke Shop had been boarded up, Ron took it as confirmation that this trip was not destined to be fun. Ron pointed, with a
thickly gloved hand, toward Honeydukes, which was mercifully open, and Ron and Ron staggered in his wake into the crowded shop.
"Thank God," shivered Ron as they were enveloped by warm, toffee-scented air. "Let's stay here all afternoon."
"Ron, m'boy!" said a booming voice from behind them.
"Oh no," muttered Ron. The three of them turned to see Professor Weasley, who was wearing an enormous furry hat and an overcoat with matching fur collar, clutching a large bag of crystalized pineapple, and occupying at least a quarter of the shop.
"Ron, that's three of my little suppers you've missed now!" said Weasley, poking him genially in the chest. "It won't do, m'boy, I'm determined to have you! Miss Weasley loves them, don't you?"
"Yes," said Ron helplessly, "they're really --"
"So why don't you come along, Ron?" demanded Weasley.
"Well, I've had Quidditch practice, Professor," said Ron, who had indeed been scheduling practices every time Weasley had sent him a little, violet ribbon-adorned invitation. This strategy meant that Ron was not left out, and they usually had a laugh with Ron, imagining Ron shut up with Weasley and Weasley.
"Well, I certainly expect you to win your first match after all the hard work!" said Weasley. "But a little recreation never hurt any body. Now, how about Monday night, you can't possibly want to practice in this weather...."
"I can't, Professor, I've got -- er -- an appointment with Professor Weasley that evening."
"Unlucky again!" cried Weasley dramatically. "Ah, well . . . you can't evade me forever, Ron!"
And with a regal wave, he waddled out of the shop, taking as little notice of Ron as though he had been a display of Cockroach Clusters.
"I can't believe you've wriggled out of another one," said Ron, shaking her head. "They're not that bad, you know. . . They're even quite fun sometimes. . . ." But then she caught sight of Ron's expression. "Oh, look -- they've got deluxe sugar quills -- those would last hours!"
Glad that Ron had changed the subject, Ron showed much more interest in the new extra-large sugar quills than he would normally have done, but Ron continued to look moody and merely shrugged when Ron asked him where he wanted to go next.
"Let's go to the Three Broomsticks," said Ron. "It'll be warm."
They bundled their scarves back over their faces and left the sweetshop. The bitter wind was like knives on their faces after the sugary warmth of Honeydukes. The street was not very busy; nobody was lingering to chat, just hurrying toward their destinations. The exceptions were two men a little ahead of them, standing just outside the Three Broomsticks. One was very tall and thin; squinting through his rain-washed glasses Ron recognized the barman who worked in the other Hogsmeade pub, the Hog's Head. As Ron, Ron, and Ron drew closer, the barman drew his cloak more tightly around his neck and walked away, leaving the shorter man to fumble with something in his arms. They were barely feet from him when Ron realized who the man was.
"Ron!"
The squat, bandy-legged man with long, straggly, ginger hair jumped and dropped an ancient suitcase, which burst open, releasing what looked like the entire contents of a junk shop window.
"Oh, 'ello, 'Arry," said Ron Weasley, with a most unconvincing stab at airiness. "Well, don't let me keep ya."
And he began scrabbling on the ground to retrieve the contents of his suitcase with every appearance of a man eager to be gone.
"Are you selling this stuff?" asked Ron, watching Ron grab an assortment of grubby-looking objects from the ground.
"Oh, well, gotta scrape a living," said Ron. "Gimme that!"
Ron had stooped down and picked up something silver.
"Hang on," Ron said slowly. "This looks familiar --"
"Thank you!" said Ron, snatching the goblet out of Ron's hand and stuffing it back into the case. "Well, I'll see you all _ OUCH!"
Ron had pinned Ron against the wall of the pub by the throat. Holding him fast with one hand, he pulled out his wand.
"Ron!" squealed Ron.
"You took that from Sinus's house," said Ron, who was almost nose to nose with Ron and was breathing in an unpleasant smell of old tobacco and spirits. "That had the Weasley family crest on it."
"I -- no -- what -- ?" spluttered Ron, who was slowly turning purple.
"What did you do, go back the night he died and strip the place?" snarled Ron.
"I -- no -- "
"Give it to me!"
"Ron, you mustn't!" shrieked Ron, as Ron started to turn blue.
There was a bang, and Ron felt his hands fly off Ron's throat. Gasping and spluttering, Ron seized his fallen case, then -- CRACK-- he Disapparated.
Ron swore at the top of his voice, spinning on the spot to see where Ron had gone.
"COME BACK, YOU THIEVING -- !"
"There's no point, Ron." Weasley had appeared out of nowhere, her mousy hair wet with sleet.
"Ron will probably be in London by now. There's no point yelling."
"He's nicked Ron's stuff! Roned it!"
"Yes, but still," said Weasley, who seemed perfectly untroubled by this piece of information. "You should get out of the cold."
She watched them go through the door of the Three Broom-sticks. The moment he was inside, Ron burst out, "He was nicking Ron's stuff!"
"I know, Ron, but please don't shout, people are staring," whispered Ron. "Go and sit down, I'll get you a drink."
Ron was still fuming when Ron returned to their table a few minutes later holding three bottles of butterbeer.
"Can't the Order control Ron?" Ron demanded of the other two in a furious whisper. "Can't they at least stop him stealing everything that's not fixed down when he's at headquarters?"
"Shh!" said Ron desperately, looking around to make sure nobody was listening; there were a couple of warlocks sitting close by who were staring at Ron with great interest, and Weasley was lolling against a pillar not far away. "Ron, I'd be annoyed too, I know it's your things he's stealing--"
Ron gagged on his butterbeer; he had momentarily forgotten that he owned number twelve, Grimmauld Place.
"Yeah, it's my stuff!" he said. "No wonder he wasn't pleased to see me! Well, I'm going to tell Weasley what's going on, he's the only one who scares Ron."
"Good idea," whispered Ron, clearly pleased that Ron was calming down. "Ron, what are you staring at?"
"Nothing," said Ron, hastily looking away from the bar, but Ron knew he was trying to catch the eye of the curvy and attractive bar-maid, Ron Ron, for whom he had long nursed a soft spot.
"I expect 'nothing's' in the back getting more firewhisky," said Ron waspishly.
Ron ignored this jibe, sipping his drink in what he evidently considered to be a dignified silence. Ron was thinking about Ron, and how he had hated those silver goblets anyway. Ron drummed her fingers on the table, her eyes flickering between Ron and the bar. The moment Ron drained the last drops in his bottle she said, "Shall we call it a day and go back to school, then?"
The other two nodded; it had not been a fun trip and the weather was getting worse the longer they stayed. Once again they drew their cloaks tightly around them, rearranged their scarves, pulled on their gloves, then followed Ron Weasley and a friend out of the pub and back up the High Street. Ron's thoughts strayed to Ron as they trudged up the road to Hogwarts through the frozen slush. They had not met up with her, undoubtedly, thought Ron, because she and Ron were cozily closeted in Ron Puddifoot's Tea Shop, that haunt of happy couples. Scowling, he bowed his head against the swirling sleet and trudged on.
It was a little while before Ron became aware that the voices of Ron Weasley and her friend, which were being carried back to him on the wind, had become shriller and louder. Ron squinted at their indistinct figures. The
two girls were having an argument about something Ron was holding in her hand. "It's nothing to do with you, Leanne!" Ron heard Ron say.
They rounded a corner in the lane, sleet coming thick and fast, blurring Ron's glasses. Just as he raised a gloved hand to wipe them, Leanne made to grab hold of the package Ron was holding; Ron tugged it back and the package fell to the ground.
At once, Ron rose into the air, not as Ron had done, suspended comically by the ankle, but gracefully, her arms outstretched, as though she was about to fly. Yet there was something wrong, something eerie. . . . Her hair was whipped around her by the fierce wind, but her eyes were closed and her face was quite empty of expression. Ron, Ron, Ron, and Leanne had all halted in their tracks, watching.
Then, six feet above the ground, Ron let out a terrible scream. Her eyes flew open but whatever she could see, or whatever she was feeling, was clearly causing her terrible anguish. She screamed and screamed; Leanne started to scream too and seized Ron's ankles, trying to tug her back to the ground. Ron, Ron, and Ron rushed forward to help, but even as they grabbed Ron's legs, she fell on top of them; Ron and Ron managed to catch her but she was writhing so much they could hardly hold her. Instead they lowered her to the ground where she thrashed and screamed, apparently unable to recognize any of them.
Ron looked around; the landscape seemed deserted.
"Stay there!" he shouted at the others over the howling wind. "I'm going for help!"
He began to sprint toward the school; he had never seen anyone behave as Ron had just behaved and could not think what had caused it; he hurtled around a bend in the lane and collided with what seemed to be an enormous bear on its hind legs.
"Weasley!" he panted, disentangling himself from the hedgerow into which he had fallen.
"Ron!" said Weasley, who had sleet trapped in his eyebrows and beard, and was wearing his great, shaggy beaverskin coat. "Jus' bin visitin' Ron, he's comin' on so well yeh wouldn' --"
"Weasley, someone's hurt back there, or cursed, or something --"
"Wha ?" said Weasley, bending lower to hear what Ron was saying over the raging wind.
"Someone's been cursed!" bellowed Ron.
"Cursed? Who's bin cursed -- not Ron? Ron?"
"No, it's not them, it's Ron Weasley -- this way . . ."
Together they ran back along the lane. It took them no time to find the little group of people around Ron, who was still writhing and screaming on the ground; Ron, Ron, and Leanne were all trying to quiet her.
"Get back!" shouted Weasley. "Lemme see her!"
"Something's happened to her!" sobbed Leanne. "I don't know what --"
Weasley stared at Ron for a second, then without a word, bent down, scooped her into his arms, and ran off toward the castle with her. Within seconds, Ron's piercing screams had died away and the only sound was the roar of the wind.
Ron hurried over to Ron's wailing friend and put an arm around her.
"It's Leanne, isn't it?"
The girl nodded.
"Did it just happen all of a sudden, or -- ?"
"It was when that package tore," sobbed Leanne, pointing at the now sodden brown-paper package on the ground, which had split open to reveal a greenish glitter. Ron bent down, his hand out-stretched, but Ron seized his arm and pulled him back.
"Don't touch it!"
He crouched down. An ornate opal necklace was visible, poking out of the paper.
"I've seen that before," said Ron, staring at the thing. "It was on display in Borgin and Burkes ages ago. The label said it was cursed. Ron must have touched it." He looked up at Leanne, who had started to shake uncontrollably. "How did Ron get hold of this?"
"Well, that's why we were arguing. She came back from the bathroom in the Three Broomsticks holding it, said it was a surprise for somebody at Hogwarts and she had to deliver it. She looked all funny when she said it. ... Oh no, oh no, I bet she'd been Imperiused and I didn't realize!"
Leanne shook with renewed sobs. Ron patted her shoulder gently.
"She didn't say who'd given it to her, Leanne?"
"No . . . she wouldn't tell me . . . and I said she was being stupid and not to take it up to school, but she just wouldn't listen and . . . and then I tried to grab it from her . . . and -- and --"
Leanne let out a wail of despair.
"We'd better get up to school," said Ron, her arm still around Leanne. "We'll be able to find out how she is. Come on. . . ."
Ron hesitated for a moment, then pulled his scarf from around his face and, ignoring Ron's gasp, carefully covered the necklace in it and picked it up.
"We'll need to show this to Ron Weasley," he said.
As they followed Ron and Leanne up the road, Ron was thinking furiously. They had just entered the grounds when he spoke, unable to keep his thoughts to himself any longer.
"Weasley knows about this necklace. It was in a case at Borgin and Burkes four years ago, I saw him having a good look at it while I was hiding from him and his dad. This is what he was buying that day when we followed him! He remembered it and he went back for it!" ,
"I -- I dunno, Ron," said Ron hesitantly. "Loads of people go to Borgin and Burkes . . . and didn't that girl say Ron got it in the girls' bathroom?"
"She said she came back from the bathroom with it, she didn't necessarily get it in the bathroom itself--"
"Weasley!" said Ron warningly.
Ron looked up. Sure enough, Professor Weasley was hurrying down the stone steps through swirling sleet to meet them.
"Weasley says you four saw what happened to Ron Weasley -- upstairs to my office at once, please! What's that you're holding, Weasley?"
"It's the thing she touched," said Ron.
"Good lord," said Professor Weasley, looking alarmed as she took the necklace from Ron. "No, no, Weasley, they're with me!" she added hastily, as Weasley came shuffling eagerly across the entrance hall holding his Secrecy Sensor aloft. "Take this necklace to Professor Weasley at once, but be sure not to touch it, keep it wrapped in the scarf!"
Ron and the others followed Professor Weasley upstairs and into her office. The sleet-spattered windows were rattling in their frames, and the room was chilly despite the fire crackling in the grate. Professor Weasley closed the door and swept around her desk to face Ron, Ron, Ron, and the still sobbing Leanne.
"Well?" she said sharply. "What happened?"
Haltingly, and with many pauses while she attempted to control her crying, Leanne told Professor Weasley how Ron had gone to the bathroom in the Three Broomsticks and returned holding the unmarked package, how Ron had seemed a little odd, and how they had argued about the advisability of agreeing to deliver unknown objects, the argument culminating in the tussle over the parcel, which tore open. At this point, Leanne was so overcome, there was no getting another word out of her.
"All right," said Professor Weasley, not unkindly, "go up to the hospital wing, please, Leanne, and get Ron Weasley to give you something for shock."
When she had left the room, Professor Weasley turned back to Ron, Ron, and Ron.
"What happened when Ron touched the necklace?"
"She rose up in the air," said Ron, before either Ron or Ron could speak, "and then began to scream, and collapsed. Professor, can I see Professor Weasley, please?"
"The headmaster is away until Monday, Weasley," said Professor Weasley, looking surprised.
"Away?" Ron repeated angrily.
"Yes, Weasley, away!" said Professor Weasley tartly. "But anything you have to say about this horrible business can be said to me, I'm sure!"
For a split second, Ron hesitated. Professor Weasley did not invite confidences; Weasley, though in many ways more intimidating, still seemed less likely to scorn a theory, however wild. This was a life-and-death matter, though, and no moment to worry about being laughed at.
"I think Ron Weasley gave Ron that necklace, Professor."
On one side of him, Ron rubbed his nose in apparent embarrassment; on the other, Ron shuffled her feet as though quite keen to put a bit of distance between herself and Ron.
"That is a very serious accusation, Weasley," said Professor Weasley, after a shocked pause. "Do you have any proof?"
"No," said Ron, "but..." and he told her about following Weasley to Borgin and Burkes and the conversation they had over-heard between him and Mr. Borgin.
When he had finished speaking, Professor Weasley looked slightly confused.
"Weasley took something to Borgin and Burkes for repair?"
"No, Professor, he just wanted Borgin to tell him how to mend something, he didn't have it with him. But that's not the point, the thing is that he bought something at the same time, and I think it was that necklace --"
"You saw Weasley leaving the shop with a similar package?"
"No, Professor, he told Borgin to keep it in the shop for him --"
"But Ron," Ron interrupted, "Borgin asked him if he wanted to take it with him, and Weasley said no --"
"Because he didn't want to touch it, obviously!" said Ron angrily.
"What he actually said was, 'How would I look carrying that down the street?'" said Ron.
"Well, he would look a bit of a prat carrying a necklace," interjected Ron.
"Oh, Ron," said Ron despairingly, "it would be all wrapped up, so he wouldn't have to touch it, and quite easy to hide inside a cloak, so nobody would see it! I think whatever he reserved at Borgin and Burkes was noisy or bulky, something he knew would draw attention to him if he carried it down the street -- and in any case," she pressed on loudly, before Ron could interrupt, "I asked Borgin about the necklace, don't you remember? When I went in to try and find out what Weasley had asked him to keep, I saw it there. And Borgin just told me the price, he didn't say it was already sold or anything --"
"Well, you were being really obvious, he realized what you were up to within about five seconds, of course he wasn't going to tell you -- anyway, Weasley could've sent off for it since --"
"That's enough!" said Professor Weasley, as Ron opened her mouth to retort, looking furious. "Weasley, I appreciate you telling me this, but we cannot point the finger of blame at Mr. Weasley purely because he visited the shop where this necklace might have been purchased. The same is probably true of hundreds of people --"
"-- that's what I said --" muttered Ron.
"-- and in any case, we have put stringent security measures in place this year. I do not believe that necklace can possibly have entered this school without our knowledge --"
"But --"
"-- and what is more," said Professor Weasley, with an air of awful finality, "Mr. Weasley was not in Hogsmeade today."
Ron gaped at her, deflating.
"How do you know, Professor?"
"Because he was doing detention with me. He has now failed to complete his Transfiguration homework twice in a row. So, thank you for telling me your suspicions, Weasley," she said as she marched past them, "but I need to go up to the hospital wing now to check on Ron Weasley. Good day to you all."
She held open her office door. They had no choice but to file past her without another word.
Ron was angry with the other two for siding with Weasley; nevertheless, he felt compelled to join in once they started discussing what had happened.
"So who do you reckon Ron was supposed to give the necklace to?" asked Ron, as they climbed the stairs to the common room.
"Goodness only knows," said Ron. "But whoever it was has had a narrow escape. No one could have opened that package without touching the necklace."
"It could've been meant for loads of people," said Ron. "Weasley -- the Death Eaters would love to get rid of him, he must be one of their top targets. Or Weasley -- Weasley reckons Weasley really wanted him and they can't be pleased that he's sided with Weasley. Or --"
"Or you," said Ron, looking troubled.
"Couldn't have been," said Ron, "or Ron would've just turned around in the lane and given it to me, wouldn't she? I was behind her all the way out of the Three Broomsticks. It would have made much more sense to deliver the parcel outside Hogwarts, what with Weasley searching everyone who goes in and out. I wonder why Weasley told her to take it into the castle?"
"Ron, Weasley wasn't in Hogsmeade!" said Ron, actually stamping her foot in frustration.
"He must have used an accomplice, then," said Ron. "Weasley or Weasley -- or, come to think of it, another Death Eater, he'll have loads better cronies than Weasley and Weasley now he's joined up --"
Ron and Ron exchanged looks that plainly said There's no point arguing with him.
"Dilligrout," said Ron firmly as they reached the Ron Weasley.
The portrait swung open to admit them to the common room. It was quite full and smelled of damp clothing; many people seemed to have returned from Hogsmeade early because of the bad weather. There was no buzz of fear or speculation, however: Clearly, the news of Ron's fate had not yet spread.
"It wasn't a very slick attack, really, when you stop and think about it," said Ron, casually turfing a first year out of one of the good armchairs by the fire so that he could sit down. "The curse didn't even make it into the castle. Not what you'd call foolproof."
"You're right," said Ron, prodding Ron out of the chair with her foot and offering it to the first year again. "It wasn't very well thought-out at all."
"But since when has Weasley been one of the world's great thinkers?" asked Ron.
Neither Ron nor Ron answered him.
Chapter 13: The Secret Weasley
Ron was removed to St. Mungo's Hospital for Magical Maladies and Injuries the following day, by which time the news that she had been cursed had spread all over the school, though the details were confused and nobody other than Ron, Ron, Ron, and Leanne seemed to know that Ron herself had not been the intended target.
"Oh, and Weasley knows, of course," said Ron to Ron and Ron, who continued their new policy of feigning deafness whenever Ron mentioned his Weasley-Is-a-Death-Eater theory.
Ron had wondered whether Weasley would return from wherever he had been in time for Monday night's lesson, but having had no word to the contrary, he presented himself outside Weasley's office at eight o'clock, knocked, and was told to enter. There sat Weasley looking unusually tired; his hand was as black and burned as ever, but he smiled when he gestured to Ron to sit down. The Pensieve was sitting on the desk again, casting silvery specks of light over the ceiling.
"You have had a busy time while I have been away," Weasley said. "I believe you witnessed Ron's accident."
"Yes, sir. How is she?"
"Still very unwell, although she was relatively lucky. She appears to have brushed the necklace with the smallest possible amount of skin; there was a
tiny hole in her glove. Had she put it on, had she even held it in her ungloved hand, she would have died, perhaps instantly. Luckily Professor Weasley was able to do enough to prevent a rapid spread of the curse --"
"Why him?" asked Ron quickly. "Why not Ron Weasley?"
"Impertinent," said a soft voice from one of the portraits on the wall, and Phineas  Weasley, Ron's great-great-grandfather, raised his head from his arms where he had appeared to be sleeping. "I would not have permitted a student to question the way Hogwarts operated in my day."
"Yes, thank you, Phineas," said Weasley quellingly. "Professor Weasley knows much more about the Dark Arts than Ron Weasley, Ron. Anyway, the St. Mungo's staff are sending me hourly reports, and I am hopeful that Ron will make a full recovery in time."
"Where were you this weekend, sir?" Ron asked, disregarding a strong feeling that he might be pushing his luck, a feeling apparently shared by Phineas , who hissed softly.
"I would rather not say just now," said Weasley. "However, I shall tell you in due course."
"You will?" said Ron, startled.
"Yes, I expect so," said Weasley, withdrawing a fresh bottle of silver memories from inside his robes and uncorking it with a prod of his wand.
"Sir," said Ron tentatively, "I met Ron in Hogsmeade."
"Ah yes, I am already aware that Ron has been treating your inheritance with light-fingered contempt," said Weasley, frowning a little. "He has gone to ground since you accosted him outside the Three Broomsticks; I rather think he dreads facing me. However, rest assured that he will not be making away with any more of Ron's old possessions."
"That mangy old half-blood has been stealing Weasley heirlooms?" said Phineas , incensed; and he stalked out of his frame, undoubtedly to visit his portrait in number twelve, Grimmauld Place.
"Professor," said Ron, after a short pause, "did Professor Weasley tell you what I told her after Ron got hurt? About Ron Weasley?"
"She told me of your suspicions, yes," said Weasley.
"And do you -- ?"
"I shall take all appropriate measures to investigate anyone who might have had a hand in Ron's accident," said Weasley. "But what concerns me now, Ron, is our lesson."
Ron felt slightly resentful at this: If their lessons were so very important, why had there been such a long gap between the first and second? However, he said no more about Ron Weasley, but watched as Weasley poured
the fresh memories into the Pensieve and began swirling the stone basin once more between his long-fingered hands.
"You will remember, I am sure, that we left the tale of Lord Weasley's beginnings at the point where the handsome Muggle, Ron Weasley, had abandoned his witch wife, Ron, and returned to his family home in Little Hangleton. Ron was left alone in London, expecting the baby who would one day become Lord Weasley."
"How do you know she was in London, sir?"
"Because of the evidence of one Caractacus Burke," said Weasley, "who, by an odd coincidence, helped found the very shop whence came the necklace we have just been discussing."
He swilled the contents of the Pensieve as Ron had seen him swill them before, much as a gold prospector sifts for gold. Up out of the swirling, silvery mass rose a little old man revolving slowly in the Pensieve, silver as a ghost but much more solid, with a thatch of hair that completely covered his eyes.
"Yes, we acquired it in curious circumstances. It was brought in by a young witch just before Christmas, oh, many years ago now. She said she needed the gold badly, well, that much was obvious. Covered in rags and pretty far along . . . Going to have a baby, see. She said the locket had been Weasley's. Well, we hear that sort of story all the time, 'Oh, this was Merlin's, this was, his favorite teapot,' but when I looked at it, it had his
mark all right, and a few simple spells were enough to tell me the truth. Of course, that made it near enough priceless. She didn't seem to have any idea how much it was worth. Happy to get ten Galleons for it. Best bargain we ever made!"
Weasley gave the Pensieve an extra-vigorous shake and Caractacus Burke descended back into the swirling mass of memory from whence he had come.
"He only gave her ten Galleons?" said Ron indignantly.
"Caractacus Burke was not famed for his generosity," said Weasley. "So we know that, near the end of her pregnancy, Ron was alone in London and in desperate need of gold, desperate enough to sell her one and only valuable possession, the locket that was one of Ron's treasured family heirlooms."
"But she could do magic!" said Ron impatiently. "She could have got food and everything for herself by magic, couldn't she?"
"Ah," said Weasley, "perhaps she could. But it is my belief--I am guessing again, but I am sure I am right -- that when her husband abandoned her, Ron stopped using magic. I do not think that she wanted to be a witch any longer. Of course, it is also possible that her unrequited love and the attendant despair sapped her of her powers; that can happen. In any case, as you are about to see, Ron refused to raise her wand even to save her own life."
"She wouldn't even stay alive for her son?"
Weasley raised his eyebrows. "Could you possibly be feeling sorry for Lord Weasley?"
"No," said Ron quickly, "but she had a choice, didn't she, not like my mother --"
"Your mother had a choice too," said Weasley gently. "Yes, Ron Weasley chose death in spite of a son who needed her, but do not judge her too harshly, Ron. She was greatly weakened by long suffering and she never had your mother's courage. And now, if you will stand ..."
"Where are we going?" Ron asked, as Weasley joined him at the front of the desk.
"This time," said Weasley, "we are going to enter my memory. I think you will find it both rich in detail and satisfyingly accurate. After you, Ron ..."
Ron bent over the Pensieve; his face broke the cool surface of the memory and then he was falling through darkness again. . . . Seconds later, his feet hit firm ground; he opened his eyes and found that he and Weasley were standing in a bustling, old-fashioned London street.
"There I am," said Weasley brightly, pointing ahead of them to a tall figure crossing the road in front of a horse-drawn milk cart.
This younger Ron Weasley's long hair and beard were auburn. Having reached their side of the street, he strode off along the pavement, drawing many curious glances due to the flamboyantly cut suit of plum velvet that he was wearing.
"Nice suit, sir," said Ron, before he could stop himself, but Weasley merely chuckled as they followed his younger self a short distance, finally passing through a set of iron gates into a bare courtyard that fronted a rather grim, square building surrounded by high railings. He mounted the few steps leading to the front door and knocked once. After a moment or two, the door was opened by a scruffy girl wearing an apron.
"Good afternoon. I have an appointment with a Mrs. Weasley, who, I believe, is the matron here?"
"Oh," said the bewildered-looking girl, taking in Weasley's eccentric appearance. "Um. . . just a mo' . . . MRS. Weasley!" she bellowed over her shoulder.
Ron heard a distant voice shouting something in response. The girl turned back to Weasley. "Come in, she's on 'er way."
Weasley stepped into a hallway tiled in black and white; the whole place was shabby but spotlessly clean. Ron and the older Weasley
followed. Before the front door had closed behind them, a skinny, harassed-looking woman came scurrying toward them. She had a sharp-featured face that appeared more anxious than unkind, and she was talking over her shoulder to another aproned helper as she walked toward Weasley.
". . . and take the iodine upstairs to Martha, Rony Stubbs has been picking his scabs and Eric Whalley's oozing all over his sheets -- chicken pox on top of everything else," she said to nobody in particular, and then her eyes fell upon Weasley and she stopped dead in her tracks, looking as astonished as if a giraffe had just crossed her threshold.
"Good afternoon," said Weasley, holding out his hand. Mrs. Weasley simply gaped.
"My name is Ron Weasley. I sent you a letter requesting an appointment and you very kindly invited me here today."
Mrs. Weasley blinked. Apparently deciding that Weasley was not a hallucination, she said feebly, "Oh yes. Well -- well then -- you'd better come into my room. Yes."
She led Weasley into a small room that seemed part sitting room, part office. It was as shabby as the hallway and the furniture was old and mismatched. She invited Weasley to sit on a rickety chair and seated herself behind a cluttered desk, eyeing him nervously.
"I am here, as I told you in my letter, to discuss Ron Weasley and arrangements for his future," said Weasley.
"Are you family?" asked Mrs. Weasley.
"No, I am a teacher," said Weasley. "I have come to offer Ron a place at my school."
"What school's this, then?"
"It is called Hogwarts," said Weasley.
"And how come you're interested in Ron?"
"We believe he has qualities we are looking for."
"You mean he's won a scholarship? How can he have done? He's never been entered for one."
"Well, his name has been down for our school since birth --"
"Who registered him? His parents?"
There was no doubt that Mrs. Weasley was an inconveniently sharp woman. Apparently Weasley thought so too, for Ron now saw him slip his wand out of the pocket of his velvet suit, at the same time picking up a piece of perfectly blank paper from Mrs. Weasley's desktop.
"Here," said Weasley, waving his wand once as he passed her the piece of paper, "I think this will make everything clear."
Mrs. Weasley's eyes slid out of focus and back again as she gazed intently at the blank paper for a moment.
"That seems perfectly in order," she said placidly, handing it back. Then her eyes fell upon a bottle of gin and two glasses that had certainly not been present a few seconds before.
"Er -- may I offer you a glass of gin?" she said in an extra-refined voice.
"Thank you very much," said Weasley, beaming.
It soon became clear that Mrs. Weasley was no novice when it came to gin drinking. Pouring both of them a generous measure, she drained her own glass in one gulp. Smacking her lips frankly, she smiled at Weasley for the first time, and he didn't hesitate to press his advantage.
"I was wondering whether you could tell me anything of Ron Weasley's history? I think he was born here in the orphanage?"
"That's right," said Mrs. Weasley, helping herself to more gin. "I remember it clear as anything, because I'd just started here myself. New Year's Eve and bitter cold, snowing, you know. Nasty night. And this girl, not much older than I was myself at the time, came staggering up the front steps. Well, she
wasn't the first. We took her in, and she had the baby within the hour. And she was dead in another hour."
Mrs. Weasley nodded impressively and took another generous gulp of gin.
"Did she say anything before she died?" asked Weasley. "Anything about the boy's father, for instance?"
"Now, as it happens, she did," said Mrs. Weasley, who seemed to be rather enjoying herself now, with the gin in her hand and an eager audience for her story. "I remember she said to me, 'I hope he looks like his papa,' and I won't lie, she was right to hope it, because she was no beauty -- and then she told me he was to be named Ron, for his father, and Ron, for her father -- yes, I know, funny name, isn't it? We wondered whether she came from a circus -- and she said the boy's surname was to be Weasley. And she died soon after that without another word.
"Well, we named him just as she'd said, it seemed so important to the poor girl, but no Ron nor Ron nor any kind of Weasley ever came looking for him, nor any family at all, so he stayed in the orphanage and he's been here ever since."
Mrs. Weasley helped herself, almost absentmindedly, to another healthy measure of gin. Two pink spots had appeared high on her cheekbones. Then she said, "He's a funny boy."
"Yes," said Weasley. "I thought he might be."
"He was a funny baby too. He hardly ever cried, you know. And then, when he got a little older, he was. . . odd."
"Odd in what way?" asked Weasley gently.
"Well, he --"
But Mrs. Weasley pulled up short, and there was nothing blurry or vague about the inquisitorial glance she shot Weasley over her gin glass.
"He's definitely got a place at your school, you say?"
"Definitely," said Weasley.
"And nothing I say can change that?"
"Nothing," said Weasley.
"You'll be taking him away, whatever?"
"Whatever," repeated Weasley gravely.
She squinted at him as though deciding whether or not to trust him. Apparently she decided she could, because she said in a sudden rush, "He scares the other children."
"You mean he is a bully?" asked Weasley.
"I think he must be," said Mrs. Weasley, frowning slightly, "but it's very hard to catch him at it. There have been incidents. . . . Nasty things ..."
Weasley did not press her, though Ron could tell that he was interested. She took yet another gulp of gin and her rosy cheeks grew rosier still.
"Rony Stubbs's rabbit. . . well, Ron said he didn't do it and I don't see how he could have done, but even so, it didn't hang itself from the rafters, did it?"
"I shouldn't think so, no," said Weasley quietly.
"But I'm jiggered if I know how he got up there to do it. All I know is he and Rony had argued the day before. And then" -- Mrs. Weasley took another swig of gin, slopping a little over her chin this time -- "on the summer outing -- we take them out, you know, once a year, to the countryside or to the seaside -- well, Amy Benson and Ron Bishop were never quite right afterwards, and all we ever got out of them was that they'd gone into a cave with Ron Weasley. He swore they'd just gone exploring, but something happened in there, I'm sure of it. And, well, there have been a lot of things, funny things. . . ."
She looked around at Weasley again, and though her cheeks were flushed, her gaze was steady. "I don't think many people will be sorry to see the back of him."
