"You understand, I'm sure, that we will not be keeping him permanently?" said Weasley. "He will have to return here, at the very least, every summer."
"Oh, well, that's better than a whack on the nose with a rusty poker," said Mrs. Weasley with a slight hiccup. She got to her feet, and Ron was impressed to see that she was quite steady, even though two-thirds of the gin was now gone. "I suppose you'd like to see him?"
"Very much," said Weasley, rising too.
She led him out of her office and up the stone stairs, calling out instructions and admonitions to helpers and children as she passed. The orphans, Ron saw, were all wearing the same kind of grayish tunic. They looked reasonably well-cared for, but there was no denying that this was a grim place in which to grow up.
"Here we are," said Mrs. Weasley, as they turned off the second landing and stopped outside the first door in a long corridor. She knocked twice and entered.
"Ron? You've got a visitor. This is Mr. Dumberton -- sorry, Dunderbore. He's come to tell you -- well, I'll let him do it."
Ron and the two Weasleys entered the room, and Mrs. Weasley closed the door on them. It was a small bare room with nothing in it except an old
wardrobe and an iron bedstead. A boy was sitting on top of the gray blankets, his legs stretched out in front of him, holding a book.
There was no trace of the Weasleys in Ron Weasley's face. Ron had got her dying wish: He was his handsome father in miniature, tall for eleven years old, dark-haired, and pale. His eyes narrowed slightly as he took in Weasley's eccentric appearance. There was a moment's silence.
"How do you do, Ron?" said Weasley, walking forward and holding out his hand.
The boy hesitated, then took it, and they shook hands. Weasley drew up the hard wooden chair beside Weasley, so that the pair of them looked rather like a hospital patient and visitor.
"I am Professor Weasley."
"'Professor'?" repeated Weasley. He looked wary. "Is that like 'doctor'? What are you here for? Did she get you in to have a look at me?"
He was pointing at the door through which Mrs. Weasley had just left.
"No, no," said Weasley, smiling.
"I don't believe you," said Weasley. "She wants me looked at, doesn't she? Tell the truth!"
He spoke the last three words with a ringing force that was almost shocking. It was a command, and it sounded as though he had given it many times before. His eyes had widened and he was glaring at Weasley, who made no response except to continue smiling pleasantly. After a few seconds Weasley stopped glaring, though he looked, if anything, warier still.
"Who are you?"
"I have told you. My name is Professor Weasley and I work at a school called Hogwarts. I have come to offer you a place at my school -- your new school, if you would like to come."
Weasley's reaction to this was most surprising. He leapt from the bed and backed away from Weasley, looking furious.
"You can't kid me! The asylum, that's where you're from, isn't it? 'Professor,' yes, of course -- well, I'm not going, see? That old cat's the one who should be in the asylum. I never did anything to little Amy Benson or Ron Bishop, and you can ask them, they'll tell you!
"I am not from the asylum," said Weasley patiently. "I am a teacher and, if you will sit down calmly, I shall tell you about Hogwarts. Of course, if you would rather not come to the school, nobody will force you --"
"I'd like to see them try," sneered Weasley.
"Hogwarts," Weasley went on, as though he had not heard Weasley's last words, "is a school for people with special abilities --"
"I'm not mad!"
"I know that you are not mad. Hogwarts is not a school for mad people. It is a school of magic."
There was silence. Weasley had frozen, his face expressionless, but his eyes were flickering back and forth between each of Weasley's, as though trying to catch one of them lying.
"Magic?" he repeated in a whisper.
"That's right," said Weasley.
"It's. . . it's magic, what I can do?"
"What is it that you can do?"
"All sorts," breathed Weasley. A flush of excitement was rising up his neck into his hollow cheeks; he looked fevered. "I can make filings move without touching them. I can make animals do what I want them to do, without training them. I can make bad things happen to people who annoy me. I can make them hurt if I want to."
His legs were trembling. He stumbled forward and sat down on the bed again, staring at his hands, his head bowed as though in prayer.
"I knew I was different," he whispered to his own quivering fingers. "I knew I was special. Always, I knew there was something."
"Well, you were quite right," said Weasley, who was no longer smiling, but watching Weasley intently. "You are a wizard."
Weasley lifted his head. His face was transfigured: There was a wild happiness upon it, yet for some reason it did not make him better looking; on the contrary, his finely carved features seemed somehow rougher, his expression almost bestial.
"Are you a wizard too?"
"Yes, I am."
"Prove it," said Weasley at once, in the same commanding tone he had used when he had said, "Tell the truth."
Weasley raised his eyebrows. "If, as I take it, you are accepting your place at Hogwarts--"
"Of course I am!"
"Then you will address me as 'Professor' or 'sir.'"
Weasley's expression hardened for the most fleeting moment before he said, in an unrecognizably polite voice, "I'm sorry, sir. I meant -- please, Professor, could you show me -- ?"
Ron was sure that Weasley was going to refuse, that he would tell Weasley there would be plenty of time for practical demonstrations at Hogwarts, that they were currently in a building full of Muggles and must therefore be cautious. To his great surprise, however, Weasley drew his wand from an inside pocket of his suit jacket, pointed it at the shabby wardrobe in the corner, and gave the wand a casual flick.
The wardrobe burst into flames.
Weasley jumped to his feet; Ron could hardly blame him for howling in shock and rage; all his worldly possessions must be in there. But even as Weasley rounded on Weasley, the flames vanished, leaving the wardrobe completely undamaged.
Weasley stared from the wardrobe to Weasley; then, his expression greedy, he pointed at the wand. "Where can I get one of them?"
"All in good time," said Weasley. "I think there is something trying to get out of your wardrobe."
And sure enough, a faint rattling could be heard from inside it. For the first time, Weasley looked frightened.
"Open the door," said Weasley.
Weasley hesitated, then crossed the room and threw open the wardrobe door. On the topmost shelf, above a rail of threadbare clothes, a small cardboard box was shaking and rattling as though there were several frantic mice trapped inside it.
"Take it out," said Weasley.
Weasley took down the quaking box. He looked unnerved.
"Is there anything in that box that you ought not to have?" asked Weasley.
Weasley threw Weasley a long, clear, calculating look. "Yes, I suppose so, sir," he said finally, in an expressionless voice.
"Open it," said Weasley.
Weasley took off the lid and tipped the contents onto his bed without looking at them. Ron, who had expected something much more exciting, saw a mess of small, everyday objects: a yo-yo, a silver thimble, and a tarnished mouth organ among them. Once free of the box, they stopped quivering and lay quite still upon the thin blankets.
"You will return them to their owners with your apologies," said Weasley calmly, putting his wand back into his jacket. "I shall know whether it has been done. And be warned: Thieving is not tolerated at Hogwarts."
Weasley did not look remotely abashed; he was still staring coldly and appraisingly at Weasley. At last he said in a colorless voice, "Yes, sir."
"At Hogwarts," Weasley went on, "we teach you not only to use magic, but to control it. You have -- inadvertently, I am sure -- been using your powers in a way that is neither taught nor tolerated at our school. You are not the first, nor will you be the last, to allow your magic to run away with you. But you should know that Hogwarts can expel students, and the Ministry of Magic -- yes, there is a Ministry -- will punish lawbreakers still more severely. All new wizards must accept that, in entering our world, they abide by our laws."
"Yes, sir," said Weasley again.
It was impossible to tell what he was thinking; his face remained quite blank as he put the little cache of stolen objects back into the cardboard box. When he had finished, he turned to Weasley and said baldly, "I haven't got any money."
"That is easily remedied," said Weasley, drawing a leather money-pouch from his pocket. "There is a fund at Hogwarts for those who require
assistance to buy books and robes. You might have to buy some of your spellbooks and so on secondhand, but --"
"Where do you buy spellbooks?" interrupted Weasley, who had taken the heavy money bag without thanking Weasley, and was now examining a fat gold Galleon,
"In Diagon Alley," said Weasley. "I have your list of books and school equipment with me. I can help you find everything --"
"You're coming with me?" asked Weasley, looking up.
"Certainly, if you --"
"I don't need you," said Weasley. "I'm used to doing things for myself, I go round London on my own all the time. How do you get to this Diagon Alley -- sir?" he added, catching Weasley's eye.
Ron thought that Weasley would insist upon accompanying Weasley, but once again he was surprised. Weasley handed Weasley the envelope containing his list of equipment, and after telling Weasley exactly how to get to the Leaky Cauldron from the orphanage, he said, "You will be able to see it, although Muggles around you -- non-magical people, that is -- will not. Ask for Ron the barman -- easy enough to remember, as he shares your name --"
Weasley gave an irritable twitch, as though trying to displace an irksome fly.
"You dislike the name 'Ron'?"
"There are a lot of Rons," muttered Weasley. Then, as though he could not suppress the question, as though it burst from him in spite of himself, he asked, "Was my father a wizard? He was called Ron Weasley too, they've told me."
"I'm afraid I don't know," said Weasley, his voice gentle.
"My mother can't have been magic, or she wouldn't have died," said Weasley, more to himself than Weasley. "It must've been him. So -- when I've got all my stuff-- when do I come to this Hogwarts?"
"All the details are on the second piece of parchment in your envelope," said Weasley. "You will leave from King's Cross Station on the first of September. There is a train ticket in there too."
Weasley nodded. Weasley got to his feet and held out his hand again. Taking it, Weasley said, "I can speak to snakes. I found out when we've been to the country on trips -- they find me, they whisper to me. Is that normal for a wizard?"
Ron could tell that he had withheld mention of this strangest power until that moment, determined to impress.
"It is unusual," said Weasley, after a moment's hesitation, "but not unheard of."
His tone was casual but his eyes moved curiously over Weasley's face. They stood for a moment, man and boy, staring at each other. Then the handshake was broken; Weasley was at the door.
"Good-bye, Ron. I shall see you at Hogwarts."
"I think that will do," said the white-haired Weasley at Ron's side, and seconds later, they were soaring weightlessly through darkness once more, before landing squarely in the present-day office.
"Sit down," said Weasley, landing beside Ron.
Ron obeyed, his mind still full of what he had just seen.
"He believed it much quicker than I did -- I mean, when you told him he was a wizard," said Ron. "I didn't believe Weasley at first, when he told me."
"Yes, Weasley was perfectly ready to believe that he was -- to use his word -- 'special,'" said Weasley.
"Did you know -- then?" asked Ron.
"Did I know that I had just met the most dangerous Dark wizard of all time?" said Weasley. "No, I had no idea that he was to grow up to be what he is. However, I was certainly intrigued by him. I returned to Hogwarts intending to keep an eye upon him, something I should have done in any case, given that he was alone and friendless, but which, already, I felt I ought to do for others' sake as much as his.
"His powers, as you heard, were surprisingly well-developed for such a young wizard and -- most interestingly and ominously of all -- he had already discovered that he had some measure of control over them, and begun to use them consciously. And as you saw, they were not the random experiments typical of young wizards: He was already using magic against other people, to frighten, to punish, to control. The little stories of the strangled rabbit and the young boy and girl he lured into a cave were most suggestive. . . . 'I can make them hurt if I want to. . . .'"
"And he was a Parselmouth," interjected Ron.
"Yes, indeed; a rare ability, and one supposedly connected with the Dark Arts, although as we know, there are Parselmouths among the great and the good too. In fact, his ability to speak to serpents did not make me nearly as uneasy as his obvious instincts for cruelty, secrecy, and domination.
"Time is making fools of us again," said Weasley, indicating the dark sky beyond the windows. "But before we part, I want to draw your attention to certain features of the scene we have just witnessed, for they have a great bearing on the matters we shall be discussing in future meetings.
"Firstly, I hope you noticed Weasley's reaction when I mentioned that another shared his first name, 'Ron'?"
Ron nodded.
"There he showed his contempt for anything that tied him to other people, anything that made him ordinary. Even then, he wished to be different, separate, notorious. He shed his name, as you know, within a few short years of that conversation and created the mask of ‘Lord Weasley' behind which he has been hidden for so long.
"I trust that you also noticed that Ron Weasley was already highly self-sufficient, secretive, and, apparently, friendless? He did not want help or companionship on his trip to Diagon Alley. He preferred to operate alone. The adult Weasley is the same. You will hear many of his Death Eaters claiming that they are in his confidence, that they alone are close to him, even understand him. They are deluded. Lord Weasley has never had a friend, nor do I believe that he has ever wanted one.
"And lastly -- I hope you are not too sleepy to pay attention to this, Ron -- the young Ron Weasley liked to collect trophies. You saw the box of stolen articles he had hidden in his room. These were taken from victims of his bullying behavior, souvenirs, if you will, of particularly unpleasant bits of magic. Bear in mind this magpie-like tendency, for this, particularly, will be important later.
"And now, it really is time for bed."
Ron got to his feet. As he walked across the room, his eyes fell I upon the little table on which Ron Weasley's ring had rested last I time, but the ring was no longer there.
"Yes, Ron?" said Weasley, for Ron had come to a halt.
"The ring's gone," said Ron, looking around. "But I thought I you might have the mouth organ or something."
Weasley beamed at him, peering over the top of his halfw moon spectacles.
"Very astute, Ron, but the mouth organ was only ever a mouth organ."
And on that enigmatic note he waved to Ron, who understood himself to be dismissed.
Chapter 14: Felix Felicis
Ron had Herbology first thing the following morning. He had been unable to tell Ron and Ron about his lesson with Weasley over breakfast for fear of being over-heard, but he filled them in as they walked across the vegetable patch toward the greenhouses. The weekend’s brutal wind had died out at last; the weird mist had returned and it took them a little longer than usual to find the correct greenhouse.
"Wow, scary thought, the boy You-Know-Who," said Ron qui-etly, as they took their places around one of the gnarled Snargaluff stumps that formed this terms project, and began pulling on their protective gloves. "But I still don't get why Weasley's showing you all this. I mean, it's really interesting and everything, but what's the point?"
"Dunno," said Ron, inserting a gum shield. "But he says its all important and it'll help me survive."
"I think it's fascinating," said Ron earnestly. "It makes absolute sense to know as much about Weasley as possible. How else will you find out his weaknesses?"
"So how was Weasley's latest party?" Ron asked her thickly through the gum shield.
"Oh, it was quite fun, really," said Ron, now putting on protective goggles. "I mean, he drones on about famous exploits a bit, and he
absolutely fawns on Weasley because he's so well connected, but he gave us some really nice food and he introduced us to Gwenog Jones."
"Gwenog Jones?" said Ron, his eyes widening under his own goggles. "The Gwenog Jones? Captain of the Holyhead Harpies?"
"That's right," said Ron. "Personally, I thought she was a bit full of herself, but --"
"Quite enough chat over here!" said Professor Weasley briskly, bustling over and looking stern. "You're lagging behind, everybody else has started, and Ron's already got his first pod!"
They looked around; sure enough, there sat Ron with a bloody lip and several nasty scratches along the side of his face, but clutching an unpleasantly pulsating green object about the size of a grapefruit.
"Okay, Professor, we're starting now!" said Ron, adding quietly, when she had turned away again, "should ve used Muffliato, Ron."
"No, we shouldn't!" said Ron at once, looking, as she always did, intensely cross at the thought of the Half-Blood Prince and his spells. "Well, come on ... we'd better get going. ..."
She gave the other two an apprehensive look; they all took deep breaths and then dived at the gnarled stump between them.
It sprang to life at once; long, prickly, bramblelike vines flew out of the top and whipped through the air. One tangled itself in Ron's hair, and Ron beat it back with a pair of secateurs; Ron succeeded in trapping a couple of vines and knotting them together; a hole opened in the middle of all the tentaclelike branches; Ron plunged her arm bravely into this hole, which closed like a trap around her elbow; Ron and Ron tugged and wrenched at the vines, forcing the hole to open again, and Hermi-one snatched her arm free, clutching in her fingers a pod just like Ron's. At once, the prickly vines shot back inside, and the gnarled stump sat there looking like an innocently dead lump of wood.
"You know, I don't think I'll be having any of these in my garden when I've got my own place," said Ron, pushing his goggles up onto his forehead and wiping sweat from his face.
"Pass me a bowl," said Ron, holding the pulsating pod at arm's length; Ron handed one over and she dropped the pod into it with a look of disgust on her face.
"Don't be squeamish, squeeze it out, they're best when they're fresh!" called Professor Weasley.
"Anyway," said Ron, continuing their interrupted conver-sation as though a lump of wood had not just attacked them, "Weasley's going to have a Christmas party, Ron, and there's no way you'll be able to wriggle out of this one because he actually asked me to check your free evenings, so he could be sure to have it on a night you can come."
Ron groaned. Meanwhile, Ron, who was attempting to burst the pod in the bowl by putting both hands on it, standing up, and squashing it as hard as he could, said angrily, "And this is another party just for Weasley's favorites, is it?"
"Just for the Slug Club, yes," said Ron.
The pod flew out from under Ron's fingers and hit the green house glass, rebounding onto the back of Professor Weasley's head and knocking off her old, patched hat. Ron went to retrieve the pod; when he got back, Ron was saying, "Look, I didn't make up the name 'Slug Club' --"
"'Slug Club,'"repeated Ron with a sneer worthy of Weasley. "It's pathetic. Well, I hope you enjoy your party. Why don't you try hooking up with Weasley, then Weasley can make you King and Queen Slug --"
"We're allowed to bring guests," said Ron, who for some reason had turned a bright, boiling scarlet, "and I was going to ask you to come, but if you think it's that stupid then I won't bother!"
Ron suddenly wished the pod had flown a little farther, so that he need not have been sitting here with the pair of them. Unno-ticed by either, he seized the bowl that contained the pod and be-gan to try and open it by the noisiest and most energetic means he could think of; unfortunately, he could still hear every word of their conversation.
"You were going to ask me?" asked Ron, in a completely differ-ent voice.
"Yes," said Ron angrily. "But obviously if you'd rather 1 hooked up with Weasley ..."
There was a pause while Ron continued to pound the resilient pod with a trowel.
"No, I wouldn't," said Ron, in a very quiet voice.
Ron missed the pod, hit the bowl, and shattered it.
‘"Reparo,"' he said hastily, poking the pieces with his wand, and the bowl sprang back together again. The crash, however, appeared to have awoken Ron and Ron to Ron's presence. Ron looked flustered and immediately started fussing about for her copy of "Flesh-Eating Trees of the World" to find out the correct way to juice Snargaluff pods; Ron, on the other hand, looked sheepish but also rather pleased with himself.
"Hand that over, Ron," said Ron hurriedly. "It says we're supposed to puncture them with something sharp. . . ."
Ron passed her the pod in the bowl; he and Ron both snapped their goggles back over their eyes and dived, once more, for the stump. It was not as though he was really surprised, thought Ron, as he wrestled with a thorny vine intent upon throttling him; he had had an inkling that this might happen sooner or later. But he was not sure how he felt about it. ... He and
Ron were now too em-barrassed to look at each other, let alone talk to each other; what if Ron and Ron started going out together, then split up? Could their friendship survive it? Ron remembered the few weeks when they had not been talking to each other in the third year; he had not enjoyed trying to bridge the distance between them. And then, what if they didn't split up? What if they became like Ron and Ron, and it became excruciatingly embarrassing to be in their presence, so that he was shut out for good?
"Gotcha!" yelled Ron, pulling a second pod from the stump just as Ron managed to burst the first one open, so that the bowl was full of tubers wriggling like pale green worms.
The rest of the lesson passed without further mention of Weasley's party. Although Ron watched his two friends more closely over the next few days, Ron and Ron did not seem any different except that they were a little politer to each other than usual. Ron supposed he would just have to wait to see what
happened under the influence of butterbeer in Weasley's dimly lit room on the night of the party. In the meantime, however, he had more pressing worries.
Ron Weasley was still in St. Mungo's Hospital with no prospect of leaving, which meant that the promising Weasley team Ron had been training so carefully since September was one Chaser short. He kept putting off replacing Ron in the hope that she would return, but their opening match
against Weasley was loom-ing, and he finally had to accept that she would not be back in time to play.
Ron did not think he could stand another full-House tryout. With a sinking feeling that had little to do with Quidditch, he cor-nered Ron Ron after Transfiguration one day. Most of the class had already left, although several twittering yellow birds were still zooming around the room, all of Ron's creation; nobody else had succeeded in conjuring so much as a feather from thin air.
"Are you still interested in playing Chaser?"
"Wha -- ? Yeah, of course!" said Ron excitedly. Over Ron’s shoulder, Ron saw Ron Finnegan slamming his books into his bag, looking sour. One of the reasons why Ron would have pre-ferred not to have to ask Ron to play was that he knew Ron would not like it. On the other hand, he had to do what was best for the team, and Ron had outflown Ron at the tryouts.

"Right," said Ron. "Cheers, Ron! Blimey, I can't wait to tell Ron!"
He sprinted out of the room, leaving Ron and Ron alone together, an uncomfortable moment made no easier when a bird dropping landed on Ron's head as one of Ron's canaries whizzed over them.
Ron was not the only person disgruntled by the choice of Ron’s substitute. There was much muttering in the common room about the fact that Ron had now chosen two of his class-mates for the team. As Ron had endured much worse mutterings than this in his school career, he was not particularly bothered, but all the same, the pressure was increasing to provide a win in the upcoming match against Weasley. If Weasley won, Ron knew that the whole House would forget that they had criticized him and swear that they had always known it was a great team. If they lost. . . well, Ron thought wryly, he had still endured worse mutterings. . . .
Ron had no reason to regret his choice once he saw Ron fly that evening; he worked well with Ron and Ron. The Beaters, Peakes and Coote, were getting better all the time. The only problem was Ron.
Ron had known all along that Ron was an inconsistent player who suffered from nerves and a lack of confidence, and unfortu-nately, the looming prospect of the opening game of the season seemed to have brought out all his old insecurities. After letting in half a dozen goals, most of them scored by Ron, his technique became wilder and wilder, until he finally punched an oncoming Ron Weasley in the mouth.

"I can fix that," said Ron, landing beside the two girls, pointing his wand at Rons mouth, and saying "Episkey." "And Ron, don't call Ron a prat, you're not the Captain of this team --"
"Well, you seemed too busy to call him a prat and I thought someone should --"
Ron forced himself not to laugh.
"In the air, everyone, let's go. . . ."
Overall it was one of the worst practices they had had all term, though Ron did not feel that honesty was the best policy when they were this close to the match.
"Good work, everyone, I think we'll flatten Weasley," he said bracingly, and the Chasers and Beaters left the changing room looking reasonably happy with themselves.

He kept up a relentless flow of encouragement all the way back to the castle, and by the time they reached the second floor, Ron was looking marginally more cheerful. When Ron pushed open the tapestry to take their usual shortcut up to Weasley Tower, however, they found themselves looking at Ron and Ron, who were locked in a close embrace and kissing fiercely as though glued together.
It was as though something large and scaly erupted into life in Ron's stomach, clawing at his insides: Hot blood seemed to flood his brain, so that all thought was extinguished, replaced by a savage urge to jinx Ron into a jelly. Wrestling with this sudden madness, he heard Ron's voice as though from a great distance away.
"Oi!"

"I don't want to find my own sister snogging people in public!" "This was a deserted corridor till you came butting in!" said Ron.
Ron was looking embarrassed. He gave Ron a shifty grin that Ron did not return, as the newborn monster inside him was roar-ing for Ron's instant dismissal from the team.

Ron had pulled out his wand too; Ron stepped swiftly between them.

No ... he would have to control that particular brotherly feeling. . . .
Ron gave a great grunting snore.
She's Ron's sister, Ron told himself firmly. Ron's sister. She's out-of-bounds. He would not risk his friendship with Ron for anything. He punched his pillow into a more comfortable shape and waited for sleep to come, trying his utmost not to allow his thoughts to stray anywhere near Ron.
Ron awoke next morning feeling slightly dazed and confused by a series of dreams in which Ron had chased him with a Beater’s bat, but by midday he would have happily exchanged the dream Ron for the real one, who was not only cold-shouldering Ron and Ron, but also treating a hurt and bewildered Ron with an icy, sneering indifference. Ron, pull yourself together, you played really well today, Ron . . ." he waited until the rest of the team were out of earshot before saying it, "you're my best mate, but carry on treating the rest of them like this and I'm going to kick you off the team."
He really thought for a moment that Ron might hit him, but then something much worse happened: Ron seemed to sag on his broom. all the fight went out of him and he said, "I resign. I'm pathetic."
"You're not pathetic and you're not resigning!" said Ron fiercely, seizing Ron by the front of his robes. "You can save any-thing when you're on form, it's a mental problem you've got!" "You calling me mental?" "Yeah, maybe I am!"

Nothing Ron said made any difference. He tried boosting Ron's confidence all through dinner, but Ron was too busy being grumpy and surly with Ron to notice. Ron persisted in the common room that evening, but his assertion that the whole team would be devastated if Ron left was somewhat undermined by the fact that the rest of the team was sitting in a huddle in a distant corner, clearly muttering about Ron and casting him nasty looks. Finally Ron tried getting angry again in the hope of provoking Ron into a defiant, and hopefully goal-saving, attitude, but this strategy did not appear to work any better than encouragement; Ron went to bed as dejected and hopeless as ever.
Ron lay awake for a very long time in the darkness. He did not want to lose the upcoming match; not only was it his first as Cap-tain, but he was determined to beat Ron Weasley at Quidditch even if he could not yet prove his suspicions about him. Yet if Ron played as he had done in the last few practices, their chances of winning were very slim. . . .
If only there was something he could do to make Ron pull him-self together . . . make him play at the top of his form . . . some-thing that would ensure that Ron had a really good day. . . .
And the answer came to Ron in one, sudden, glorious stroke of inspiration.
Breakfast was the usual excitable affair next morning; the Weasleys hissed and booed loudly as every member of the Weasley team entered the Great Hall. Ron glanced at the ceiling and saw a clear, pale blue sky: a good omen.

Ron and Ron were already wearing their Quidditch robes and waiting in the changing room.

These words were greeted with jeers and applause from the Weasley end of the pitch. Ron craned around on his broom to look toward the commentator's podium. Chapter 15: The Unbreakable Vow
Snow was swirling against the icy windows once more; Christmas was approaching fast. Weasley had already singlehandedly delivered the usual twelve C hristmas trees to the Great Hall; garlands of holly and tinsel had been twisted around the banisters of the stairs; everlasting candles glowed from inside the helmets of suits of armor and great bunches of mistletoe had been hung at intervals along the corridors. Large groups of girls tended to converge underneath the mistletoe bunches every time Ron went past, which caused blockages in the corridors; fortunat e ly, however, Ron's frequent nighttime wanderings had given him an unusually good knowledge of the castle's secret passageways, so that he was often, without too much difficulty, to naviga t e mistletoe-free routes between classes.
Ron, who might once have found the necessity of these detours excuse for jealousy rather than hilarity, simply roared with laughter about it all. Although Ron much preferred this new laughing, joking Ron to the moody, aggressive model he had been enduring for the last few weeks, the improved Ron came at a heavy price. Firstly, Ron had to put up with the frequent presence of Ron Weasley, who seemed to regard any moment that she was not kissing Ron as a moment wasted; and secondly, Ron found himself once more the best friend of two people who seemed unlikely ever to speak to each other again.
Ron, whose hands and forearms still bore scratches and cuts from Ron's bird attack, was taking a defensive and resentful tone.

There he turned its pages, searching, until he finally found, at the front of the book, the date that it had been pub-lished. It was nearly fifty years old. Neither his father, nor his father's friends, had been at Hogwarts fifty years ago. Feeling disappointed, Ron threw the book back into his trunk, turned off the lamp, and rolled over, thinking of werewolves and Weasley, Ron Weasley and the Half-Blood Prince, and finally falling into an uneasy sleep full of creeping shadows and the cries of bitten children. . . .
"She's got to be joking. . . ."
Ron woke with a start to find a bulging stocking lying over the end of his bed. He put on his glasses and looked around; the tiny window was almost completely obscured with snow and, in front of it, Ron was sitting bolt upright in bed and examining what ap-peared to be a thick gold chain.
"What's chat?" asked Ron. '

honestly think I'd wear ..."

"My sweetheart"

"Stutter at me?" said Ron, grinning. "Come on, would I?"
"How could she think I'd like something like that, though?" Ron demanded of thin air, looking rather shocked.
"Well, think back," said Ron. "Have you ever let it slip that you'd like to go out in public with the words 'My Sweetheart' round your neck?"
"Well... we don't really talk much," said Ron. "It's mainly . . ."
"Snogging," said Ron.
"Well, yeah," said Ron. He hesitated a moment, then said, "Is Ron really going out with Weasley?"
"I dunno," said Ron. "They were at Weasley's party together, but I don't think it went that well."
Ron looked slightly more cheerful as he delved deeper into his stocking.
Rons presents included a sweater with a large Golden Snitch worked onto the front, hand-knitted by Mrs. Weasley, a large box of Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes products from the twins, and a slightly damp, moldy-smelling package that came with a label read-ing To Master, From Ron,
Ron stared at it. "D'you reckon this is safe to open?" he asked. "Can't be anything dangerous, all our mail's still being searched at the Ministry," replied Ron, though he was eyeing the parcel suspiciously.

"Ron would," said Ron. "But let's wait and see what it is before you start feeling guilty."
A moment later, Ron had given a loud yell and leapt out of his camp bed; the package contained a large number of maggots. "Nice," said Ron, roaring with laughter. "Very thoughtful." "I'd rather have them than that necklace," said Ron, which sobered Ron up at once.
Everybody was wearing new sweaters when they all sat down for Christmas lunch, everyone except Ron (on whom, it appeared, Mrs. Weasley had not wanted to waste one) and Mrs. Weasley herself, who was sporting a brand-new midnight blue witch's hat glittering with what looked like tiny starlike diamonds, and a spec-tacular golden necklace.

. In his eagerness to help her, he knocked the gravy boat flying; Ron waved his wand and the gravy soared up in the air and returned meekly to the boat.

She gave Weasley an annoyed look, as though it was all his fault she was getting Ron for a daughter-in-law instead of Weasley, but Ron, glancing across at Ron, who was now feeding Ron bits of turkey off her own fork, thought that Mrs. Weasley was fighting a long-lost battle. He was, however, reminded of a question he had with regard to Weasley, and who better to ask than Weasley, the man who knew all about Patronuses?
"Weasley's Patronus has changed its form," he told him. "Weasley said so anyway. I didn't know that could happen. Why would your Patronus change?"
Weasley took his time chewing his turkey and swallowing before saying slowly, "Sometimes ... a great shock ... an emotional up-heaval ..."
"It looked big, and it had four legs," said Ron, struck by a sud-den thought and lowering his voice. "Hey ... it couldn't be -- ?"
"Ron!" said Mrs. Weasley suddenly. She had risen from her chair; her hand was pressed over her heart and she was staring out of the kitchen window. "Ron -- it's Ron!"
"What?"
Mr. Weasley looked around. Everybody looked quickly at the window; Ron stood up for a better look. There, sure enough, was Ron Weasley, striding across the snowy yard, his horn-rimmed glasses glinting in the sunlight. He was not, however, alone.
"Ron, he's -- he's with the Minister!"
And sure enough, the man Ron had seen in the Daily Prophet was following along in Ron's wake, limping slightly, his mane of graying hair and his black cloak flecked with snow. Ron Weasley paused in the doorway, leaning on his walk-ing stick and smiling as he observed this affecting scene.

The atmosphere around the table changed perceptibly. Every-body looked from Weasley to Ron. Nobody seemed to find Weasley's pretense that he did not know Ron's name convincing, or find it natural that he should be chosen to accompany the Minister around the garden when Ron, Ron, and Ron also had clean plates.
"Yeah, all right," said Ron into the silence.
He was not fooled; for all Weasley's talk that they had just been in the area, that Ron wanted to look up his family, this must be the real reason that they had come, so that Weasley could speak to Ron alone.

Ron walked across the yard toward the Weasleys' overgrown, snow-covered garden, Weasley limping slightly at his side. He had, Ron knew, been Head of the Auror office; he looked tough and battle-scarred, very different from portly Weasley in his bowler hat.
"Charming," said Weasley, stopping at the garden fence and looking out over the snowy lawn and the indistinguishable plants. "Charming."
Ron said nothing. He could tell that Weasley was watching him.

"No," said Ron truthfully. ¦!.

"The rumors that have flown around!" said Weasley. "Well, of course, we both know how these stories get distorted ... all these whispers of a prophecy . . . of you being 'the Ronsen One'. . ."
They were getting near it now, Ron thought, the reason Scrim-geour was here.
"I assume that Weasley has discussed these matters with you?",
Ron deliberated, wondering whether he ought to lie or not. Ron had to mull that one over for a few seconds before re-sponding. "I don't really know what you mean, Minister."
"Well, of course, to you it will matter enormously," said Scrim-geour with a laugh. "But to the Wizarding community at large . . . it's all perception, isn't it? It's what people believe that's important."
Ron said nothing. He thought he saw, dimly, where they were heading, but he was not going to help Weasley get there. The gnome under the rhododendron was now digging for worms at its roots, and Ron kept his eyes fixed upon it.

And I can't help but feel that, once you realize this, you might consider it, well, almost a duty, to stand alongside the Ministry, and give everyone a boost."
The gnome had just managed to get hold of a worm. It was now tugging very hard on it, trying to get it out of the frozen ground. Ron was silent so
long that Weasley said, looking from Ron to the gnome, "Funny little chaps, aren't they? But what say you, Ron?"

Ron Weasley has told me that you cherish an ambition to become an Auror. Well, that could be arranged very easily. ..."
Ron felt anger bubbling in the pit of his stomach: So Ron Weasley was still at the Ministry, was she?
"So basically," he said, as though he just wanted to clarify a few points, "you'd like to give the impression that I'm working for the Ministry?"

"Well," said Weasley, frowning slightly, "well, yes, that's partly why we'd like --"

Weasley did not speak for a moment but his expression hard-ened instantly. "I would not expect you to understand," he said, and he was not as successful at keeping anger out of his voice as Ron had been. "These are dangerous times, and certain measures need to be taken. You are sixteen years old --"

They looked at each other, long and hard. Finally Weasley said, with no pretense at warmth, "I see. You prefer -- like your hero, Weasley -- to disassociate yourself from the Ministry?" "I don't want to be used," said Ron.

did. You never get it right, you people, do you? Either we've got Weasley, pretending everything's lovely while people get murdered right under his nose, or we've got you, chucking the wrong people into jail and trying to pretend you've got 'the Ronsen One' work-ing for you!" ' i
"So you're not 'the Ronsen One'?" said Weasley. '

He raised his right fist. There, shining white on the back of his cold hand, were the scars which Ron Weasley had forced him to carve into his own flesh: I must not tell lies.
"I don't remember you rushing to my defense when I was trying to tell everyone Weasley was back. The Ministry wasn't so keen to be pals last year."
They stood in silence as icy as the ground beneath their feet. The gnome had finally managed to extricate his worm and was now sucking on it happily, leaning against the bottommost branches of the rhododendron bush.
"What is Weasley up to?" said Weasley brusquely. "Where does he go when he is absent from Hogwarts?"
"No idea," said Ron.
"And you wouldn't tell me if you knew," said Weasley, "would you?"
"No, 1 wouldn't," said Ron.
"Well, then, I shall have to see whether I can't find out by other means."
"You can try," said Ron indifferently. "But you seem cleverer than Weasley, so I'd have thought you'd have learned from his mis-takes. He tried interfering at Hogwarts. You might have noticed he's not Minister anymore, but Weasley’s still headmaster. I'd leave Weasley alone, if I were you."
There was a long pause.
"Well, it is clear to me that he has done a very good job on you," said Weasley, his eyes cold and hard behind his wire-rimmed glasses, "Weasley’s man through and through, aren't you, Weasley?"
"Yeah, I am," said Ron. "Glad we straightened that out."
And turning his back on the Minister of Magic, he strode back toward the house.
Chapter 17: A Sluggish Memory
Late in the afternoon, a few days after New Year, Ron, Ron, and Ron lined up beside the kitchen fire to return to Hogwarts. The Ministry had arranged this one-off connection to the Floo Network to return students quickly and safely to the school. Only Mrs. Weasley was there to say good-bye, as Mr. Weasley, Ron, Ron, Ron, and Ron were all at work. Mrs. Weasley dissolved into tears at the moment of parting. Admittedly, it took very little to set her off lately; she had been crying on and off ever since Ron had stormed from the house on Christmas Day with his glasses splattered with mashed parsnip (for which Ron, Ron, and Ron all claimed credit).
"Don't cry, Mum," said Ron, patting her on the back as Mrs. Weasley sobbed into her shoulder. "It's okay. ..."
"Yeah, don't worry about us," said Ron, permitting his mother to plant a very wet kiss on his cheek, "or about Ron. He's such a prat, it's not really a loss, is it?"
Mrs. Weasley sobbed harder than ever as she enfolded Ron in her arms.
"Promise me you'll look after yourself.. .. Stay out of trouble. ..."
"I always do, Mrs. Weasley," said Ron. "I like a quiet life, you know me."
She gave a watery chuckle and stood back. "Be good, then, all of you. ..."
Ron stepped into the emerald fire and shouted "Hogwarts!" He had one last fleeting view of the Weasleys' kitchen and Mrs. Weasley's tearful face before the flames engulfed him; spinning very fast, he caught blurred glimpses of other Wizarding rooms, which were whipped out of sight before he could get a proper look; then he was slowing down, finally stopping squarely in the fireplace in Professor Weasley's office. She barely glanced up from her work as he clambered out over the grate.
"Evening, Weasley. Try not to get too much ash on the carpet."
"No, Professor."
Ron straightened his glasses and flattened his hair as Ron came spinning into view. When Ron had arrived, all three of them trooped out of Weasley's office and off toward Weasley Tower. Ron glanced out of the corridor windows as they passed; the sun was already sinking over grounds carpeted in deeper snow than had lain over the Burrow garden. In the distance, he could see Weasley feeding Ron in front of his cabin.
"Baubles," said Ron confidently, when they reached the Ron Weasley, who was looking rather paler than usual and winced at his loud voice.
"No," she said.
"What d’you mean, ‘no’ ?
"There is a new password," she said. "And please don't shout."
"But we've been away, how're we supposed to -- ?"
"Ron! Ron!"
Ron was hurrying toward them, very pink-faced and wearing a cloak, hat, and gloves.
"I got back a couple of hours ago, I've just been down to visit Weasley and Buck -- I mean Witherwings," she said breathlessly. "Did you have a good Christmas?"
"Yeah," said Ron at once, "pretty eventful, Ron Scrim --" ] "I've got something for you, Ron," said Ron, neither looking at Ron nor giving any sign that she had heard him. "Oh, hang on -- password. Abstinence."
"Precisely," said the Ron Weasley in a feeble voice, and swung forward to reveal the portrait hole.
"What's up with her?" asked Ron.
"Overindulged over Christmas, apparently," said Ron, rolling her eyes as she led the way into the packed common room. "She and her friend
Violet drank their way through all the wine in that picture of drunk monks down by the Charms corridor. Anyway..."
She rummaged in her pocket for a moment, then pulled out a scroll of parchment with Weasley's writing on it.
"Great," said Ron, unrolling it at once to discover that his next lesson with Weasley was scheduled for the following night. "I’ve got loads to tell him -- and you. Let's sit down --"
But at that moment there was a loud squeal of "Won-Won!" and Ron Weasley came hurtling out of nowhere and flung herself into Ron's arms. Several onlookers sniggered; Ron gave a tinkling laugh and said, "There's a cable over here... Coming. Ron?"
"No, thanks, I said I'd meet Ron," said Ron, though Ron could not help noticing that she did not sound very enthusiastic. Leaving Ron and Ron locked in a kind of vertical wrestling, match, Ron led Ron over to the spare table.
"So how was your Christmas?"
"Oh, fine," she shrugged. "Nothing special. How was it at Won-Won's?"
"I'll tell you in a minute," said Ron. "Look, Ron, can't you --"
"No, I can't," she said flatly. "So don't even ask."
"I thought maybe, you know, over Christmas --"
"It was the Ron Weasley who drank a vat of five-hundred-year-old wine, Ron, not me. So what was this important news you wanted to tell me?"
She looked too fierce to argue with at that moment, so Ron dropped the subject of Ron and recounted all that he had overheard between Weasley and Weasley. When he had finished, Ron sat in thought for a moment and then said, "Don't you think -- ?"
"-- he was pretending to offer help so that he could trick Weasley into telling him what he's doing?"
"Well, yes," said Ron.

"No, I can't," she answered slowly.
"And he's acting on Weasley's orders, just like I said!"
"Hmm .. . did either of them actually mention Weasley's name?"

She stared across the room, apparently lost in thought, not even noticing Ron tickling Ron. "How's Weasley?"

And the rest of the evening passed amicably with both of them abusing the Minister of Magic, for Ron, like Ron, thought that after all the Ministry had put Ron through the previous year, they had a great deal of nerve asking him for help now.
The new term started next morning with a pleasant surprise for the sixth years: a large sign had been pinned to the common room notice boards overnight.
APPARITION LESSONS
If you are seventeen years of age, or will turn seventeen on or before the 31st August next, you are eligible for a twelve-week course of Apparition Lessons from a Ministry of Magic Apparition instructor. Please sign below if you would like to participate. Cost: 12 Galleons.
Ron and Ron joined the crowd that was jostling around the notice and taking it in turns to write their names at the bottom. Ron was just taking out his quill to sign after Ron when Ron crept up behind him, slipped her hands over his eyes, and trilled, "Guess who, Won-Won?" Ron turned to see Ron stalking off; he caught up with her, having no wish to stay behind with Ron and Ron, but to his surprise, Ron caught up with them only a little way beyond the portrait hole, his ears bright red and his expression disgruntled. Without a word, Ron sped up to walk with Ron.
"So -- Apparition," said Ron, his tone making it perfectly plain that Ron was not to mention what had just happened. "Should be a laugh, eh?"
"I dunno," said Ron. "Maybe it's better when you do it yourself, I didn’t enjoy it much when Weasley took me along for the ride."
"I forgot you'd already done it. ... I'd better pass my test first
time," said Ron, looking anxious. "Ron and Ron did," "Ron failed, though, didn't he?" "Yeah, but Ron's bigger than me" -- Ron held his arms out from his body as though he was a gorilla -- "so Ron and Ron
didn't go on about it much . . . not to his face anyway . . ." "When can we take the actual test?" "Soon as we're seventeen. That's only March for me!" "Yeah, but you wouldn't be able to Apparate in here, not in the castle . . ."
"Not the point, is it? Everyone would know I could Apparate if I wanted."
Ron was not the only one to be excited at the prospect of Apparition. All that day there was much talk about the forthcoming , lessons; a great deal of store was set by being able to vanish and reappear at will.
"How cool will it be when we can just --" Ron clicked his ringers to indicate disappearance. "Me cousin Fergus does it just to annoy me, you wait till I can do it back. . . He'll never have another peaceful moment. . . ."
Lost in visions of this happy prospect, he flicked his wand a little too enthusiastically, so that instead of producing the fountain of pure water that was the object of today's Charms lesson, he let out a hoselike jet that ricocheted off the ceiling and knocked Professor Weasley flat on his face.
"Ron’s already Apparated," Ron told a slightly abashed Ron, after Professor Weasley had dried himself off with a wave of his wand and set Ron lines: "I am a wizard, not a baboon brandishing a stick." "Dum -- er -- someone took him. Side-Along-Apparition, you know."
"Whoa!" whispered Ron, and he, Ron, and Ron put their heads a little closer to hear what Apparition felt like. For the rest of the day, Ron was besieged with requests from the other sixth years to describe the sensation of Apparition. All of them seemed awed, rather than put off, when he told them how uncomfortable it was, and he was still answering detailed questions at ten to eight that evening, when he was forced to lie and say that he needed to return a book to the library, so as to escape in time for his lesson with Weasley.
The lamps in Weasley’s office were lit, the portraits of previous headmasters were snoring gently in their frames, and the Pen-sieve was ready upon the desk once more. Weasley’s hands lay on either side of it, the right one as blackened and burnt-looking as ever. It did not seem to have healed at all and Ron wondered, for perhaps the hundredth time, what had caused such a distinctive injury, but did not ask; Weasley had said that he would know eventually and there was, in any case, another subject he wanted to discuss. But before Ron could say anything about Weasley and Weasley, Weasley spoke.
"I hear that you met the Minister of Magic over Christmas?" "Yes," said Ron. "He's not very happy with me."
"No," sighed Weasley. "He is not very happy with me either. We must try not to sink beneath our anguish, Ron, but battle on."
Ron grinned.
"He wanted me to tell the Wizarding community that the Ministry's doing a wonderful job.'
Weasley smiled.
"It was Weasley's idea originally, you know. During his last days in office, when he was trying desperately to cling to his post, he sought a meeting with you, hoping that you would give him your
support --"
"After everything Weasley did last year?" said Ron angrily. "After Weasley ?"
"I told Ron there was no chance of it, but the idea did not die when he left: office. Within hours of Weasley's appointment we met and he demanded that I arrange a meeting with you --"
"So that's why you argued!" Ron blurted out. "It was in the Daily Prophet"'
"The Prophet is bound to report the truth occasionally," said Weasley, "if only accidentally. Yes, that was why we argued. Well, it appears that Ron found a way to corner you at last."
"He accused me of being 'Weasley's man through and through.'"
"How very rude of him."
"I told him I was."
Weasley opened his mouth to speak and then closed it again. Behind Ron, Ron the phoenix let out a low, soft, musical cry. To Ron’s intense embarrassment, he suddenly realized
that Weasley's bright blue eyes looked rather watery, ami stared hastily at his own knees. When Weasley spoke, however, his voice was quite steady.
"I am very touched, Ron."
