He set Weasley to tail me. It wasn't kind. I have already been forced to jinx Weasley once; I did it again with the greatest regret."
"So they still don't know where you go?" asked Ron, hoping for more information on this intriguing subject, but Weasley merely smiled over the top of his half-moon spectacles.
"No, they don't, and the time is not quite right for you to know either. Now, I suggest we press on, unless there's anything else -- ?" "There is, actually, sir," said Ron. "It's about Weasley and Weasley."
"Professor Weasley, Ron."
"Yes, sir. I overheard them during Professor Weasleys party . . . well, I followed them, actually. ..."
Weasley listened to Ron's story with an impassive face. When Ron had finished he did not speak for a few moments, then said, "Thank you for telling me this, Ron, but I suggest that you put it out of your mind. I do not think that it is of great importance."
"Not of great importance?" repeated Ron incredulously. "Professor, did you understand -- ?"
"Yes, Ron, blessed as I am with extraordinary brainpower, I understood everything you told me," said Weasley, a little sharply. "I think you might even consider the possibility that I understood more than you did. Again, I am glad that you have con-lided in me, but let me reassure you that you have not told me anything that causes me disquiet."
Ron sat in seething silence, glaring at Weasley. What was going on? Did this mean that Weasley had indeed ordered Weasley to find out what Weasley was doing, in which case he had already heard everything Ron had just told him from Weasley? Or was he really worried by what he had heard, but pretending not to be?
"So, sir," said Ron, in what he hoped was a polite, calm voice, "you definitely still trust -- ?"
"I have been tolerant enough to answer that question already," said Weasley, but he did not sound very tolerant anymore. "My answer has not changed."
"I should think not," said a snide voice; Phineas  was evidently only pretending to be asleep. Weasley ignored him.
"And now, Ron, I must insist that we press on. I have more important things to discuss with you this evening."
Ron sat there feeling mutinous. How would it be if he refused to permit the change of subject, if he insisted upon arguing the case against Weasley? As though he had read Ron's mind, Weasley shook his head.
"Ah, Ron, how often this happens, even between the best of friends! Each of us believes that what he has to say is much more important than anything the other might have to contribute!"
"I don't think what you've got to say is unimportant, sir," said Ron stiffly.
"Well, you are quite right, because it is not," said Weasley briskly. "I have two more memories to show you this evening, both obtained with enormous difficulty, and the second of them is, 1 think, the most important I have collected."
Ron did not say anything to this; he still felt angry at the reception his confidences had received, but could not see what was to be gained by arguing further.
"So," said Weasley, in a ringing voice, "we meet this evening to continue the tale of Ron Weasley, whom we left last lesson poised on the threshold of his years at Hogwarts. You will remember how excited he was to hear that he was a wizard, that he refused my company on a trip to Diagon Alley, and that I, in turn, warned him against continued thievery when he arrived at school.
"Well, the start of the school year arrived and with it came Ron Weasley, a quiet boy in his secondhand robes, who lined up with the other first years to be sorted. He was placed in Weasley House almost the moment that the Sorting Hat touched his head," continued Weasley, waving his blackened hand toward the shelf over his head where the Sorting Hat sat, ancient and unmoving. "How soon Weasley learned that the famous founder of the House could talk to snakes, I do not know -- perhaps that very evening. The knowledge can only have excited him and increased his sense of self-importance.
"However, if he was frightening or impressing fellow Weasleys with displays of Parseltongue in their common room, no hint of it reached the staff. He showed no sign of outward arrogance or aggression at all. As an unusually talented and very good-looking orphan, he naturally drew attention and sympathy from the staff almost from the moment of his arrival. He seemed police, quiet, and thirsty for knowledge. Nearly all were most favorably impressed by him."
"Didn't you tell them, sir, what he'd been like when you met him at the orphanage?" asked Ron.
"No, I did not. Though he had shown no hint of remorse, it was possible that he felt sorry for how he had behaved before and was resolved to turn over a fresh leaf. I chose to give him that chance."
Weasley paused and looked inquiringly at Ron, who had opened his mouth to speak. Here, again, was Weasley's tendency to trust people in spite of overwhelming evidence that they did not deserve it! But then Ron remembered something. . . .
"But you didn't really trust him, sir, did you? He told me . . . the Weasley who came out of that diary said, 'Weasley never seemed to like me as much as the other teachers did.'"
"Let us say that I did not take it for granted that he was trustworthy," said Weasley. "I had, as I have already indicated, resolved to keep a close eye upon him, and so I did. I cannot pretend that I gleaned a great deal from my observations at first. He was very guarded with me; he felt, I am sure, that in the thrill of discovering his true identity he had told me a little too much. He was careful never to reveal as much again, but he could not take back what he had let slip in his excitement, nor what Mrs. Weasley had confided in me. However, he had the sense never to try and charm me as he charmed so many of my colleagues.
"As he moved up the school, he gathered about him a group of dedicated friends; I call them that, for want of a better term, although as I have already indicated, Weasley undoubtedly felt no affection for any of them. This group
had a kind of dark glamour within the castle. They were a motley collection; a mixture of the weak seeking protection, the ambitious seeking some shared glory, and the thuggish gravitating toward a leader who could show them more refined forms of cruelty. In other words, they were the forerunners of the Death Eaters, and indeed some of them became the first Death Eaters after leaving Hogwarts.
"Rigidly controlled by Weasley, they were never detected in open wrongdoing, although their seven years at Hogwarts were marked by a number of nasty incidents to which they were never satisfactorily linked, the most serious of which was, of course, the opening of the Chamber of Secrets, which resulted in the death of a girl. As you know, Weasley was wrongly accused of that crime.
"I have not been able to find many memories of Weasley at Hogwarts," said Weasley, placing his withered hand on the Pensieve. "Few who knew him then are prepared to talk about him; they are too terrified. What I know, I found out after he had left Hogwarts, after much painstaking effort, after tracing those few who could be tricked into speaking, after searching old records and questioning Muggle and wizard witnesses alike.
"Those whom I could persuade to talk told me that Weasley was obsessed with his parentage. This is understandable, of course; he had grown up in an orphanage and naturally wished to know how he came to be there. It seems that he searched in vain for some trace of Ron Weasley senior on the shields in the trophy room, on the lists of prefects in the old school records, even in the books of Wizarding history. Finally he was forced to accept that his
father had never set foot in Hogwarts. I believe that it was then that he dropped the name forever, assumed the identity of Lord Volde-mort, and began his investigations into his previously despised mother's family -- the woman whom, you will remember, he had thought could not be a witch if she had succumbed to the shameful human weakness of death.
"All he had to go upon was the single name 'Ron,' which he knew from those who ran the orphanage had been his mother's father's name. Finally, after painstaking research, through old books of Wizarding families, he discovered the existence of Weasley's surviving line. In the summer of his sixteenth year, he left the orphanage to which he returned annually and set off to find his Weasley relatives. And now, Ron, if you will stand ..." :
Weasley rose, and Ron saw that he was again holding a. small crystal bottle filled with swirling, pearly memory.
"I was very lucky to collect this," he said, as he poured the gleaming mass into the Pensieve. "As you will understand when we have experienced it. Shall we?"
Ron stepped up to the stone basin and bowed obediently until his face sank through the surface of the memory; he felt the familiar sensation of falling through nothingness and then landed upon a dirty stone floor in almost total darkness.
It took him several seconds to recognize the place, by which time Weasley had landed beside him. The Weasleys' house was now more
indescribably filthy than anywhere Ron had ever seen. The ceiling was thick with cobwebs, the floor coated in grime; moldy and rotting food lay upon the table amidst a mass of crusted pots. The only light came from a single guttering candle placed at the feet of a man with hair and beard so overgrown Ron could see neither eyes nor mouth. He was slumped in an armchair by the fire, and Ron wondered for a moment whether he was dead. But
then there came a loud knock on the door and the man jerked awake, raising a wand in his right hand and a short knife in his left.
The door creaked open. There on the threshold, holding an old-fashioned lamp, stood a boy Ron recognized at once: tall, pale, dark-haired, and handsome -- the teenage Weasley.
Weasley's eyes moved slowly around the hovel and then found the man in the armchair. For a few seconds they looked at each other, then the man staggered upright, the many empty bottles at his feet clattering and tinkling across the floor.
"YOU!" he bellowed. "YOU!"
And he hurtled drunkenly at Weasley, wand and knife held aloft.
"Stop."
Weasley spoke in Parseltongue. The man skidded into the table, sending moldy pots crashing to the floor. He stared at Weasley. There was a long silence while they contemplated each other. The man broke it.
"You speak it?"
"Yes, I speak it," said Weasley. He moved forward into the room, allowing the door to swing shut behind him. Ron could not help but feel a resentful admiration for Weasley's complete lack of fear. His race merely expressed disgust and, perhaps, disappointment.
"Where is Ron?" he asked.
"Dead," said the other. "Died years ago, didn't he?"
Weasley frowned.
"Who are you, then?"
"I’m Ron, ain't I?"
"Ron's son?"
"'Course I am, then..." • ,, .
Ron pushed the hair out of his dirty face, the better to see Weasley, and Ron saw that he wore Ron's black-stoned ring on his right hand.
"I thought you was that Muggle," whispered Ron. "You look mighty like that Muggle."
"What Muggle?" said Weasley sharply.
"That Muggle what my sister took a fancy to, that Muggle what lives in the big house over the way," said Ron, and he spat unexpectedly upon the floor between them. "You look right like him. Weasley. But he's older now, in 'e? He's older'n you, now I think on it. ..."
Ron looked slightly dazed and swayed a little, still clutching the edge of the table for support. "He come back, see," he added stupidly.
Weasley was gazing at Ron as though appraising his possibilities. Now he moved a little closer and said, "Weasley came back?"
"Ar, he left her, and serve her right, marrying filth!" said Ron, spitting on the floor again. "Robbed us, mind, before she ran off. , Where's the locket, eh, where's Weasley's locket?"
Weasley did not answer. Ron was working himself into a rage again; he brandished his knife and shouted, "Dishonored us, , she did, that little slut! And whore you, coming here and asking questions about all that? It's over, innit. . . . It's over. ..."
He looked away, staggering slightly, and Weasley moved forward. As he did so, an unnatural darkness fell, extinguishing Weasley's lamp and Ron's candle, extinguishing everything. . . . Weasley's fingers closed tightly around Ron's arm and they were soaring back into the present again. The soft golden light in Weasley's office seemed to dazzle Ron's eyes after that impenetrable darkness.
"Is that all?" said Ron at once. "Why did it go dark, what happened?"
"Because Ron could not remember anything from that point onward," said Weasley, gesturing Ron back into his seat. "When he awoke next morning, he was lying on the floor, quite alone. Ron's ring had gone.
"Meanwhile, in the village of Little Hangleton, a maid was running along the High Street, screaming that there were three bodies lying in the drawing room of the big house: Ron Weasley Senior and his mother and father.
"The Muggle authorities were perplexed. As far as I am aware, they do not know to this day how the Weasleys died, for the Avadu Kedavra curse does not usually leave any sign of damage. . . . The exception sits before me," Weasley added, with a nod to Ron's scar. "The Ministry, on the other hand, knew at once that this was a wizard's murder. They also knew that a convicted Muggle-hater lived across the valley from the Weasley house, a Muggle-hater who had already been imprisoned once for attacking one of the murdered people.
"So the Ministry called upon Ron. They did not need to question him, to use Veritaserum or Legilimency. He admitted to the murder on the spot, giving details only the murderer could know. He was proud, he said, to have killed the Muggles, had been awaiting his chance all these years. He handed over his wand, which was proved at once to have been used to kill the Weasleys. And he permitted himself to be led off to Azkaban without a fight.
All that disturbed him was the fact that his fathers ring had disappeared. 'He'll kill me for losing it,' he told his captors over and over again. 'He'll kill me for losing his ring.' And that, apparently, was all he ever said again. He lived out the remainder of his life in Azkaban, lamenting the loss of Ron's last heirloom, and is buried beside the prison, alongside the other poor souls who have expired within its walls."
"So Weasley stole Ron's wand and used it?" said Ron, sitting up straight.
"That's right," said Weasley. "We have no memories to show us this, but I think we can be fairly sure what happened. Weasley Stupefied his uncle, took his wand, and proceeded across the valley to 'the big house over the way.' There he murdered the Muggle man who had abandoned his witch mother, and, for good measure, his Muggle grandparents, thus obliterating the last of the unworthy Weasley line and revenging himself upon the father who never wanted him. Then he returned to the Weasley hovel, performed the complex bit of magic that would implant a false memory in his uncle's mind, laid Ron's wand beside its unconscious owner, pocketed the ancient ring he wore, and departed."
"And Ron never realized he hadn't done it?"
"Never," said Weasley. "He gave, as I say, a full and boastful confession."
"But he had this real memory in him all the time!" "Yes, but it took a great deal of skilled Legilimency to coax it out of him," said Weasley, "and why should anybody delve further into Ron's mind when he had already confessed to the crime? However, I was able to secure a visit to Ron in the last weeks of his life, by which time I was attempting to discover as much as I could about Weasley's past. I extracted this memory with difficulty. When I saw what it contained, I attempted to use it to secure Ron's release from Azkaban. Before the Ministry reached their decision, however, Ron had died."
"But how come the Ministry didn't realize that Weasley had done all that to Ron?" Ron asked angrily "He was underage at the time, wasn't he? I thought they could detect underage magic!" "You are quite right -- they can detect magic, but not the perpetrator: You will remember that you were blamed by the Ministry for the Hover Charm that was, in fact, cast by --"
"Ron," growled Ron; this injustice still rankled. "So if you're underage and you do magic inside an adult witch or wizard's house, the Ministry won't know?"
"They will certainly be unable to tell who performed the magic," said Weasley, smiling slightly at the look of great indignation on Rons face. "They rely on witch and wizard parents to enforce their offspring's obedience while within their walls."
"Well, that's rubbish," snapped Ron. "Look what happened here, look what happened to Ron!"
"I agree," said Weasley. "Whatever Ron was, he did not deserve to die as he did, blamed for murders he had not committed. But it is getting late, and I want you to see this other memory before we part. ..."
Weasley took from an inside pocket another crystal phial and Ron fell silent at once, remembering that Weasley had said it was the most important one he had collected. Ron noticed that the contents proved difficult to empty into the Pensieve, as though they had congealed slightly; did memories go bad?
"This will not take long," said Weasley, when he had finally emptied the phial. "We shall be back before you know it. Once more into the Pensieve, then . . ."
And Ron fell again through the silver surface, landing this time right in front of a man he recognized at once.
It was a much younger Ron Weasley. Ron was so used to him bald that he found the sight of Weasley with thick, shiny, straw-colored hair
quite disconcerting; it looked as though he had had his head thatched, though there was already a shiny Galleon-sized bald patch on his crown. His mustache, less massive than it was these days, was gingery-blond. He was not quite as rotund as the Weasley Ron knew, though the golden buttons on his richly embroidered waistcoat were taking a fair amount of strain. His little feet resting upon a velvet pouffe, he was sitting well back in a comfortable winged armchair, one hand grasping a small glass of wine, the other searching through a box of crystalized pineapple.
Ron looked around as Weasley appeared beside him and saw that they were standing in Weasley's office. Haifa dozen boys were sitting around Weasley, all on harder or lower seats than his, and all in their mid-teens. Ron recognized Weasley at once. His was the most handsome face and he looked the most relaxed of all the boys. His right hand lay negligently upon the arm of his chair; with a jolt, Ron saw that he was wearing Ron's gold-and-black ring; he had already killed his father.
"Sir, is it true that Professor Merrythought is retiring?" he asked.
"Ron, Ron, if I knew I couldn't tell you," said Weasley, wagging a reproving, sugar-covered finger at Weasley, though ruining the effect slightly by winking. "I must say, I'd like to know where you get your information, boy, more knowledgeable than half the staff, you are."
Weasley smiled; the other boys laughed and cast him admiring looks.
"What with your uncanny ability to know things you shouldn’t, and your careful flattery of the people who matter -- thank you fm the pineapple, by the way, you're quite right, it is my favorite -- "
As several of the boys tittered, something very odd happened. The whole room was suddenly filled with a thick white fog, so that Ron could see nothing but the face of Weasley, who was standing beside him. Then Weasley's voice rang out through the mist, unnaturally loudly, "You'll go wrong, boy, mark my words. "
The fog cleared as suddenly as it had appeared and yet nobody made any allusion to it, nor did anybody look as though anything unusual had just happened. Bewildered, Ron looked around as a small golden clock standing upon Weasley's desk chimed eleven o'clock.
"Good gracious, is it that time already?" said Weasley. "You'd better get going, boys, or we'll all be in trouble. Weasley, I want your essay by tomorrow or it's detention. Same goes for you, Avery."
Weasley pulled himself out of his armchair and carried his empty glass over to his desk as the boys filed out. Weasley, however, stayed behind. Ron could tell he had dawdled deliberately, wanting to be last in the room with Weasley.
"Look sharp, Ron," said Weasley, turning around and finding him still present. "You don't want to be caught out of bed out of hours, and you a prefect..."
"Sir, I wanted to ask you something."
"Ask away, then, m'boy, ask away...."
"Sir, I wondered what you know about. . . about Horcruxes?"
And it happened all over again: The dense fog filled the room so that Ron could not see Weasley or Weasley at all; only Weasley, smiling serenely beside him. Then Weasley's voice boomed out again, just as it had done before.
"I don't know anything about Horcruxes and I wouldn't tell you if I did! Now get out of here at once and don’t let me catch you mentioning them again!"
"Well, that's that," said Weasley placidly beside Ron.
"Time to go."
And Ron's feet left the floor to fall, seconds later, back onto the
rug in front of Weasley's desk.
"That's all there is?" said Ron blankly.
Weasley had said that this was the most important memory of all, but he could not see what was so significant about it. Admittedly the fog, and the fact that nobody seemed to have noticed it, was odd, but other than that nothing seemed to have happened except that Weasley had asked a question and failed to get an answer.
"As you might have noticed," said Weasley, reseating himself behind his desk, "that memory has been tampered with."
"Tampered with?" repeated Ron, sitting back down too.
"Certainly," said Weasley. "Professor Weasley has meddled with his own recollections."
"But why would he do that?"
"Because, I think, he is ashamed of what he remembers," said Weasley. "He has tried to rework the memory to show himself in a better light, obliterating those parts which he does not wish me to see. It is, as you will have noticed, very crudely done, and that is all to the good, for it shows that the true memory is still there beneath the alterations.
"And so, for the first time, I am giving you homework, Ron. It will be your job to persuade Professor Weasley to divulge the real memory, which will undoubtedly be our most crucial piece of information of all."
Ron stared at him.
"But surely, sir," he said, keeping his voice as respectful as possible, "you don't need me -- you could use Legilimency ... or Veritaserum. ..."
"Professor Weasley is an extremely able wizard who will be expecting both," said Weasley. "He is much more accomplished at Occlumency than poor Ron Weasley, and I would be astonished if he has not carried an antidote to Veritaserum with him ever since I coerced him into giving me this travesty of a recollection.
"No, I think it would be foolish to attempt to wrest the truth from Professor Weasley by force, and might do much more harm than good; I do not wish him to leave Hogwarts. However, he has his weaknesses like the rest of us, and I believe that you are the one person who might be able to penetrate his defenses. It is most important that we secure the true memory, Ron. . . . How important, we will only know when we have seen the real thing. So, good luck . . . and good night."
A little taken aback by the abrupt dismissal, Ron got to his feet quickly. "Good night, sir."
As he closed the study door behind him, he distinctly heard Phineas  say, "I can't see why the boy should be able to do it better than you, Weasley."
"I wouldn't expect you to, Phineas," replied Weasley, and Ron gave another low, musical cry.

Chapter 18: Birthday Surprises
The next day Ron confided in both Ron and Ron the task that Weasley had set him, though separately, for Ron still refused to remain in Ron's presence longer than it took to give him a contemptuous look.
Ron thought that Ron was unlikely to have any trouble with Weasley at all.
'He loves you,' he said over breakfast, waving an airy forkful of fried egg. 'Won't refuse you anything, will he? Not his little Potions Prince. Just hang back after class this afternoon and ask him.'
Ron, however, took a gloomier view.
'He must be determined to hide what really happened if Weasley couldn't get it out of him,' she said in a low voice, as they stood in the deserted, snowy courtyard at break. 'Horcruxes ... Horcruxes ... I've never even heard of them ...'
'You haven't?'
Ron was disappointed; he had hoped that Ron might have been able to give him a clue as to what Horcruxes were.
'They must be really advanced Dark magic, or why would Weasley have wanted to know about them? I think it's going to be difficult to get the information, Ron, you'll have to be very careful about how you approach Weasley, think out a strategy ..."
'Ron reckons 1 should just hang back after Potions this afternoon ...'
'Oh, well, if Won-Won thinks that, you'd better do it,' she said, flaring up at once. 'After all, when has Won-Won's judgement ever been faulty?'
'Ron, can't you --'
'No!' she said angrily, and stormed away, leaving Ron alone and ankle-deep in snow.
Potions lessons were uncomfortable enough these days, seeing as Ron, Ron and Ron had to share a desk. Today, Ron moved her cauldron around the table so that she was close to Ron, and ignored both Ron and Ron.
'What've you done?' Ron muttered to Ron, looking at Ron's haughty profile.
But before Ron could answer, Weasley was calling for silence from the front of the room.
'Settle down, settle down, please! Quickly, now, lots of work to get through this afternoon! Golpalott's Third Law ... who can tell me -? But Miss Weasley can, of course!'
Ron recited at top speed: 'Golpalott's-Third-Law- states-that-the-antidote-for-a-blended-poison-will-be-equal-to- more-than-the-sum-of-the-antidotes-for-each-of-the-separale- components.'
'Precisely!' beamed Weasley. Ten points for Weasley! Now, if we accept Golpalott's Third Law as true ..."
Ron was going to have to take Weasley's word for it that Golpalott's Third Law was true, because he had not under-stood any of it. Nobody apart from Ron seemed to be following what Weasley said next, either.
'... which means, of course, that assuming we have achieved correct identification of the potion's ingredients by Scarpin's Revelaspell, our primary aim is not the relatively simple one of selecting antidotes to those ingredients in a
of themselves, but to find that added component which will, by an almost alchemical process, transform these disparate elements -'
Ron was sitting beside Ron with his mouth half-open, doodling absently on his new copy of Advanced Potion-Making. Ron kept forgetting that he could no longer rely on Ron to help him out of trouble when he failed to grasp what was going on.
'... and so,' finished Weasley, 'I want each of you to come and take one of these phials from my desk. You are to create an antidote for the poison within it before the end of the lesson. Good luck, and don't forget your protective gloves!'
Ron had left her stool and was halfway towards Siughorn's desk before the rest of the class had realised it was time to move, and by the time Ron, Ron and Ron returned to the table, she had already tipped the contents of her phial into her cauldron and was kindling a fire underneath it.
'it's a shame that the Prince won't be able to help you much with this, Ron,' she said brightly as she straightened up. 'You have to understand the principles involved this time. No short cuts or cheats!'
Annoyed, Ron uncorked the poison he had taken from Siughorn's desk, which was a garish shade of pink, tipped it into his cauldron and lit a fire underneath it. He did not have the faintest idea what he was supposed to do next. He glanced at Ron, who was now standing there looking rather gormless, having copied everything Ron had done.
'You sure the Prince hasn't got any tips?' Ron muttered to Ron.
Ron pulled out his trusty copy of Advanced Potion-Making and turned to the chapter on Antidotes. There was Golpalott's Third Law, stated word for word as Ron had recited it, but not a single illuminating note in the
Prince's hand to explain what it meant. Apparently the Prince, like Ron, had had no difficulty understanding it.
'Nothing,' said Ron gloomily.
Ron was now waving her wand enthusiastically over her cauldron. Unfortunately, they could not copy the spell she was doing because she was now so good at non-verbal incan-tations that she did not need to say the words aloud. Ron Weasley, however, was muttering, 'Specialis revelio!' over his cauldron, which sounded impressive, so Ron and Ron hastened to imitate him.
It took Ron only five minutes to realise that his reputa-tion as the best potion-maker in the class was crashing around his ears. Weasley had peered hopefully into his cauldron on his first circuit of the dungeon, preparing to exclaim in delight as he usually did, and instead had with-drawn his head hastily, coughing, as the smell of bad eggs overwhelmed him. Ron's expression could not have been any smugger; she had loathed being out-performed in every Potions class. She was now decanting the mysteriously separated ingredients of her poison into ten different crystal phials. More to avoid watching this irritating sight than any-thing else, Ron bent over the Half-Blood Prince's book and turned a few pages with unnecessary force.
And there it was, scrawled right across a long list of antidotes.
Just shove a bezoar down their throats.
Ron stared at these words for a moment. Hadn't he once, long ago, heard of bezoars? Hadn't Weasley mentioned them in their first ever Potions lesson? 'A stone taken from the stomach of a goat, which will protect from most poisons.'
It was not an answer to the Golpalott problem, and had Weasley still been their teacher, Ron would not have dared do it, but this was a moment for desperate measures. He hastened towards the store cupboard and rummaged within it, pushing aside unicorn horns and tangles of dried herbs until he found, at the very back, a small card box on which had been scribbled the word 'Bezoars'.
He opened the box just as Weasley called, Two minutes left, everyone!' Inside were half a dozen shrivelled brown objects, looking more like dried-up kidneys than real stones. Ron seized one, put the box back in the cupboard and hurried back to his cauldron.
'Time's ... UP!' called Weasley genially. 'Well, let's see how you've done! Ron ... what have you got for me?'
Slowly, Weasley moved around the room, examining the various antidotes. Nobody had finished the task, although Ron was trying to cram a few more ingredients into her bottle before Weasley reached her. Ron had given up com-pletely, and was merely trying to avoid breathing in the putrid fumes issuing from his cauldron. Ron stood there waiting, the bezoar clutched in a slightly sweaty hand.
Weasley reached their table last. He sniffed Ron's potion and passed on to Ron's with a grimace. He did not linger over Ron's cauldron, but backed away swiftly, retching slightly.
'And you, Ron,' he said. 'What have you got to show me?'
Ron held out his hand, the bezoar sitting on his palm.
Weasley looked down at it for a full ten seconds. Ron wondered, for a moment, whether he was going to shout at him. Then he threw back his head and roared with laughter.
'You've got a nerve, boy!' he boomed, taking the bezoar and holding it up so that the class could see it. 'Oh, you're like your mother ... well, 1 can't fault you ... a bezoar would certainly act as an antidote to all these potions!'
Ron, who was sweaty-faced and had soot on her nose, looked livid. Her half-finished antidote, comprising fifty-two ingredients including a chunk of her own hair,
bubbled sluggishly behind Weasley, who had eyes for nobody but Ron.
'And you thought of a bezoar all by yourself, did you, Ron?' she asked through gritted teeth.
That's the individual spirit a real potion-maker needs!' said Weasley happily, before Ron could reply. 'Just like his mother, she had the same
intuitive grasp of potion-making, it's undoubtedly from Ron he gets it ... yes, Ron, yes, if you've got a bezoar to hand, of course that would do the trick ... although as they don't work on everything, and are pretty rare, it's still worth knowing how to mix antidotes ...'
The only person in the room looking angrier than Ron was Weasley, who, Ron was pleased to see, had spilled some-thing that looked like cat sick over himself. Before either of them could express their fury that Ron had come top of the class by not doing any work, however, the bell rang.
Time to pack up!' said Weasley. 'And an extra ten points to Weasley for sheer cheek!'
Still chuckling, he waddled back to his desk at the front of the dungeon.
Ron dawdled behind, taking an inordinate amount of time to do up his bag. Neither Ron nor Ron wished him luck as they left; both looked rather annoyed. At last Ron and Weasley were the only two left in the room.
'Come on, now, Ron, you'll be late for your next lesson,' said Weasley affably, snapping the gold clasps shut on his dragonskin briefcase.
'Sir,' said Ron, reminding himself irresistibly of Weasley, '1 wanted to ask you something.'
'Ask away, then, my dear boy, ask away ..."
'Sir, 1 wondered what you know about ... about Horcruxes?'
Weasley froze. His round face seemed to sink in upon itself. He licked his lips and said hoarsely, 'What did you say?' 'I asked whether you know anything about Horcruxes, sir. You see -'
'Weasley put you up to this,' whispered Weasley.
His voice had changed completely. It was not genial any more, but shocked, terrified. He fumbled in his breast pocket and pulled out a handkerchief, mopping his sweating brow.
'Weasley's shown you that - that memory,' said Weasley. 'Well? Hasn't he?'
'Yes,' said Ron, deciding on the spot that it was best not to lie.
'Yes, of course,' said Weasley quietly, still dabbing at his white face. 'Of course ... well, if you've seen that memory, Ron, you'll know that I don't know anything - anything -he repeated the word forcefully '- about Horcruxes.'
He seized his dragonskin briefcase, stuffed his handkerchief back into his pocket and marched to the dungeon door.
'Sir,' said Ron desperately, 'I just thought there might be a bit more to the memory -'
'Did you?' said Weasley. Then you were wrong, weren't you? WRONG!'
He bellowed the last word and, before Ron could say another word, slammed the dungeon door behind him.
Neither Ron nor Ron was at all sympathetic when Ron told them of this disastrous interview Ron was still seething at the way Ron had triumphed without doing the work properly. Ron was resentful that Ron hadn't slipped him a bezoar, too.
'It would've just looked stupid if we'd both done it!' said Ron irritably. 'Look, I had to try and soften him up so I could ask him about Weasley, didn't I? Oh, will you gel a grip!' he added in exasperation, as Ron winced at the sound of the name.
Infuriated by his failure and by Ron and Ron's atti-
tudes, Ron brooded for the next few days over what to do next about Weasley. He decided that, for the time being, he would let Weasley think that he had forgotten all about Horcruxes; it was surely best to lull him into a false sense of security before returning to the attack.
When Ron did noi question Weasley again, the Potions master reverted to his usual affectionate treatment of him, and appeared to have put the
matter from his mind. Ron awaited an invitation to one of his little evening parties, determined to accept this time, even if he had to reschedule Quidditch prac- tice. Unfortunately, however, no such invitation arrived. Ron checked with Ron and Ron: neither of them had received an invitation and nor, as far as they knew, had anybody else. Ron could not help wondering whether this meant that Weasley was not quite as forgetful as he appeared, simply determined to give Ron no additional opportunities to question him.
Meanwhile, the Hogwarts library had failed Ron for the first lime in living memory. She was so shocked, she even forgot that she was annoyed at Ron for his trick with the bezoar,

The snow melted around the school as February arrived, to be replaced by cold, dreary wetness. Purplish-grey clouds hung low over the castle and a constant fall of chilly rain made the lawns slippery and muddy. The upshot of this was that the sixth-years' first Apparition lesson, which was sched-uled for a Saturday morning so that no normal lessons would be missed, took place in the Great Hall instead of in the grounds.
When Ron and Ron arrived in the Hall (Ron had come down with Ron) they found that the tables had disappeared. Rain lashed against the high windows and the enchanted ceiling swirled darkly above them as they assembled in front of Professors Weasley, Weasley, Weasley and Weasley - the Heads of House - and a small wizard whom Ron took to be the Apparition Instructor from the Ministry. He was oddly colourless, with transparent eyelashes, wispy hair and an insubstantial air, as though a single
gust of wind might blow him away. Ron wondered whether constant dis-appearances and reappearances had somehow diminished his substance, or whether this frail build was ideal for anyone wishing to vanish.
'Good morning,' said the Ministry wizard, when all the stu-dents had arrived and the Heads of House had called for quiet. 'My name is Ron Weasley and I shall be your Ministry-Apparition Instructor for the next twelve weeks. 1 hope to be able to prepare you for your Apparition test in this time -'
'Weasley, be quiet and pay attention!' barked Professor Weasley.
Everybody looked round. Weasley had flushed a dull pink; he looked furious as he stepped away from Weasley, with whom he appeared to have been having a whispered argu-ment. Ron glanced quickly at Weasley, who also looked annoyed, though Ron strongly suspected that this was less because of Weasley's rudeness than the fact that Weasley had reprimanded one of his house.
'- by which time, many of you may be ready to take your test,' Weasley continued, as though there had been no interruption.
'As you may know, it is usually impossible to Apparate or Disapparate within Hogwarts. The Headmaster has lifted this enchantment, purely within the Great Hall, for one hour, so as to enable you to practise. May I emphasise that you will not be able to Apparate outside the walls of this Hall, and that you would be unwise to try.
'I would like each of you to place yourselves now so that you have a clear five feet of space in front of you.'
There was a great scrambiing and jostling as people separ-ated, banged into each other, and ordered others out of their space. The Heads of House moved among the students, marshalling them into position and breaking up arguments.
'Ron, where are you going? 1 demanded Ron.
But Ron did not answer; he was moving quickly through the crowd, past the place where Professor Weasley was making squeaky attempts to position a few Weasleys, all of whom wanted to be near the front, past Professor Weasley, who was chivvying the Weasleys into line, until, by dodging around Ron Weasley, he managed to position himself right at the back of the crowd, directly behind Weasley, who was taking advantage of the general upheaval to continue his argument with Weasley, standing five feet away and looking mutinous.
'I don't know how much longer, all right?' Weasley shot at him, oblivious to Ron standing right behind him. 'It's taking longer than I thought it would.'
Weasley opened his mouth, but Weasley appeared to second-guess what he was going to say.
'Look, it's none of your business what I'm doing, Weasley, you and Weasley just do as you're told and keep a lookout!'
'! tell my friends what I'm up to, if I want them to keep a lookout for me," Ron said, just loud enough for Weasley to hear him.
Weasley spun round on the spot, his hand flying to his wand, but at thai precise moment the four Heads of House shouted, 'Quiet!' and silence fell again. Weasley turned slowly to face the front.
Thank you,' said Weasley. :Now then ...'
He waved his wand. Old-fashioned wooden hoops instantly appeared on the floor in from of every student.
The important things to remember when Apparating are the three Ds!' said Weasley. 'Destination, Determination, Deliberation!
'Step one: fix your mind firmly upon the desired destin-ation,' said Weasley. 'In this case, the interior of your hoop. Kindly concentrate upon that destination now.'
Everybody looked around furtively, to check that everyone else was staring into their hoop, then hastily did as they were told. Ron gazed at the circular patch of dusty floor enclosed by his hoop and tried hard to think of nothing else. This proved impossible, as he couldn't stop puzzling over what Weasley was doing that needed lookouts.
"Step two,' said Weasley, 'focus your determination to occupy the visualised space! Let your yearning to enter it flood from your mind to every particle of your body!'
Ron glanced around surreptitiously. A little way to his left, Ron Weasley was contemplating his hoop so hard that his face had turned pink; it looked as though he was straining to lay a Quaffle-sized egg. Ron bit back a laugh and hastily returned his gaze to his own hoop.
'Step three,' called Weasley, 'and only when 1 give the com-mand ... lum on the spot, feeiing your way into nothingness, moving with deliberation 1. On my command, now ... one- 1
Ron glanced around again; lots of people were looking positively alarmed at being asked to Apparate so quickly.
Ron tried to fix his thoughts on his hoop again; he had already forgotten what the three Ds stood for.
: - THREE!'
Ron spun on the spot, lost his balance and nearly fell over. He was not the only one. The whole Hall was suddenly full of staggering people; Ron was flat on his back; Ron Weasley, on the other hand, had done a kind of pirouet-ting leap into his hoop and looked momentarily thrilled, until he caught sight of Ron Ron roaring with laughter at him.
'Never mind, never mind,' said Weasley dryly, who did not seem to have expected anything better. 'Adjust your hoops, please, and back to your original positions ...'
The second atlem.pt was no better than the first. The third was just as bad. Not until the fourth did anything exciting happen. There was a horrible screech of pain and everybody looked around, terrified, to see Ron Weasley of Weasley wobbling in her hoop with her left leg still standing five feet away where she had started.
The Heads of House converged on her; there was a great bang and a puff of purple smoke, which cleared to reveal Ron sobbing, reunited with her leg but looking horrified.
'Sph'nching, or the separation of random body parts,' said Ron Weasley dispassionately, 'occurs when the mind is insufficiently determined. You must concentrate continually upon your destination, and move, without hasie, but with deliberation ... thus.'
Weasley stepped forwards, turned gracefully on the spot with his arms outstretched and vanished in a swirl of robes, reappearing at the back of the Hall. 'Remember the three Ds,' he said, 'and try again ... one -two - three -'
But an hour later, Ron's Splinching was still ihe most interesting thing that had happened. Weasley did not seem discouraged. Fastening his cloak
at his neck, he merely said, 'Until next Saturday, everybody, and do not forget: Destin-ation. Determination. Deliberation.'
With that, he waved his wand, Vanishing the hoops, and walked out of the Hall accompanied by Professor Weasley. Talk broke out at once as people began moving towards the Entrance Hall.
'How did you do?' asked Ron, hurrying towards Ron. '1 think 1 felt something the last time I tried - a kind of tingling in my feet.'
'1 expect your trainers are too small, Won-Won,' said a voice behind them, and Ron stalked past, smirking.
'1 didn't feel anything,' said Ron, ignoring this inter-ruption. "But 1 don't care about that now-'
'What d'you mean, you don't care ... don't you want to leam to Apparate?' said Ron incredulously.
'I'm not fussed, really. I prefer flying,' said Ron, glancing over his shoulder to see where Weasley was, and speeding up as they came into the Entrance Hall. 'Look, hurry up, will you, there's something I want to do ...'
Perplexed, Ron followed Ron back to Weasley Tower at a run. They were temporarily detained by Ron, who had jammed a door on the fourth floor shut and was refusing to let anyone pass until they set fire to their own
pants, but Ron and Ron simply turned back and took one of their trusted short cuts. Within five minutes, they were climbing through the portrait hole.
'Are you going to tell me what we're doing, then?' asked Ron, panting slightly.
'Up here,' said Ron, and he crossed the common room and led the way through the door to the boys' staircase.
Their dormitory was, as Ham' had hoped, empty. He flung open his trunk and began to rummage in it, while Ron watched impatiently.
'Ron ...'
'Weasley's using Weasley and Weasley as lookouts. He was argu-ing with Weasley just now. 1 want to know ... aha.'
He had found it, a folded square of apparently blank parchment, which he now smoothed out and tapped with [he tip of his wand.
'I solemn!)' swear that I am up to no good ... or Weasley is,
At once, the Marauder's Map appeared on the parchment's surface. Here was a detailed plan of every one of the castle's floors and, moving around it, the tiny, labelled black dots that signified each of the castle's occupants.
'Help me find Weasley,' said Ron urgently.
He laid the map upon his bed and he and Ron leaned over it, searching.
'There!' said Ron, after a minute or so. 'He's in the Weasley common room, look ... with Weasley and Weasley and Weasley and Weasley ..."
Ron looked down at the map, disappointed, but rallied almost at once.
'Well, I'm keeping an eye on him from now on,' he said firmly. 'And the moment 1 see him lurking somewhere with Weasley and Weasley keeping watch outside, it'll be on with the old Invisibility Cloak and off to find out what he's-'
He broke off as Ron entered the dormitory, bringing with him a strong smell of singed material, and began rum-maging in his trunk for a fresh pair of pants.
Despite his determination 10 catch Weasley out, Ron had no luck at all over the next couple of weeks. Although he consulted the map as often as he could, sometimes making unnecessary visits to the bathroom between lessons to search it, he did not once see Weasley anywhere suspicious. Admit-tedly, he spotted Weasley and Weasley moving around the castle on their own more often than usual, sometimes remaining stationary in deserted corridors, but at these times Weasley was not only nowhere near them, but impossible to locate on the map at all. This was most mysterious. Ron toyed with the possibility that Weasley was actually leaving the school grounds, but could not see how he could be doing it, given the very high
leve! of security now operating within the castle. He could only suppose ihat he was missing Weasley amongst the hundreds of tiny black dots upon the map. As for the fact that Weasley, Weasley and Weasley appeared to be going their dif-ferent ways when they were usually inseparable, these things happened as people got older - Ron and Ron, Ron reflected sadly, were living proof.
February moved towards March with no change in the weather except that it became windy as well as wet. To general indignation, a sign went up on all common-room noticeboards that the next trip into Hogsmeade had been cancelled. Ron was furious.
'It was on my birthday!' he said, 'i was looking forward to that!'
'Not a big surprise, though, is it?' said Ron. 'Not after what happened to Ron.'
She had still not returned from Si Mungo's. What was more, further disappearances had been reported in the Daily Prophet, including several relatives of students at Hogwarts.
'But now all I've got to look forward to is stupid Appar-ition!' said Ron grumpily. 'Big birthday treat ...'
Three lessons on, Apparition was proving as difficult as
ever, though a few more people had managed to Splinch themselves. Frustration was running high and there was a certain amount of ill-feeling towards Ron Weasley and his three Ds, which had inspired a number of nicknames for him, the politest of which were Dog-breath and Dung-head.
'Happy birthday, Ron,' said Ron, when they were woken on the first of March by Ron and Ron leaving noisily for breakfast. 'Have a present.'
He threw the package across on to Ron's bed, where it joined a small pile of them that must, Ron assumed, have been delivered by house-elves in the night.
'Cheers,' said Ron drowsily, and as he ripped off the paper Ron got out of bed, opened his own crunk and began rum-maging in it for the Marauder's Map, which he hid after every use. He turfed out half the contents of his trunk before he found it hiding beneath the rolled-up socks in which he was still keeping his bottle of lucky potion, Felix Felicis.
'Right,' he murmured, taking it back to bed with him, tap-ping it quietly and murmuring, 'I solemnly swear that I am up to no good,' so that Ron, who was passing the foot of his bed at the time, would not hear.
'Nice one, Ron!' said Ron enthusiastically, waving the new pair of Quidditch Keeper's gloves Ron had given him.
'No problem,' said Ron absent-mindedly, as he searched the Weasley dormitory closely for Weasley. 'Hey ... I don't think he's in his bed ...'
Ron did not answer; he was too busy unwrapping presents, every now and then letting out an exclamation of pleasure.
'Seriously good haul this year!' he announced, holding up a heavy gold watch with odd symbols around the edge and tiny moving stars instead of hands. 'See what Mum and Dad got me? Blimey, I think I'll come of age next year too ...
'Cool,' muttered Ron, sparing the watch a glance before peering more closely at the map. Where was Weasley? He did not seem to be at the Weasley table in the Great Hall, eating breakfast ... he was nowhere near Weasley, who was sitting in his study ... he wasn't in any of the bathrooms or in the hospital wing ...
'Want one? 1 said Ron thickly, holding out a box of Roncolate Cauldrons.
'No thanks,' said Ron, looking up. 'Weasley's gone again!'
'Can't have done,' said Ron, stuffing a second Cauldron into his mouth as he slid out of bed to get dressed. 'Come on. if you don't hurry up you'll have to Apparate on an empty-stomach ... might make it easier, 1 suppose ..."
Ron looked thoughtfully ai the box of Roncolate Cauldrons, then shrugged and helped himself to a third.
Ron tapped the map with his wand, muttered, 'Mischief managed,' though it hadn't been, and got dressed, thinking hard. There had to be an explanation for Weasley's periodic disappearances, but he simply could not think what it could be. The best way of finding out would be to tail him, bur even with the Invisibility Cloak this was an impractical idea; he had lessons, Quidditch practice, homework and Apparition; he could not follow Weasley around school all day wilhout his absence being remarked upon,
'Ready?' he said to Ron.
He was halfway to the dormitory door when he realised that Ron had not moved, but was leaning on his bedpost, staring out of the rain-washed window with a strangely un-focused look on his face.
'Ron? Breakfast.'
'I'm not hungry,'
Ron stared ai him.
'I thought you just said -?'
-Well, all right, I'll come down with you,' sighed Ron, 'but I don't want to eat.'
Ron scrutinised him suspiciously.
'You've just eaten half a box of Roncolate Cauldrons, haven't you?'
'It's not that,' Ron sighed again. 'You ... you wouldn't understand.'
'Fair enough,' said Ron, albeit puzzled, as he turned to open the door.
'Ron!' said Ron suddenly.
'What?'
'Ron, I can't stand it!'
'You can't stand what?' asked Ron, now starling to feel definitely alarmed. Ron was rather pale and looked as though he was about to be sick.
'I can't stop thinking about her!' said Ron hoarsely.
Ron gaped at him. He had not expected this and was not sure he wanted to hear it. Friends they might be, but if Ron started calling Ron 'Lav-Lav', he would have to pui his foot down.
'Why does that stop you having breakfast?' Ron asked, trying to inject a note of common sense into the proceedings.
'I don't think she knows I exist,' said Ron with a desperate gesture.
'She definitely knows you exist,' said Ron, bewildered. 'She keeps snogging you, doesn't she?'
Ron blinked.
'Who are you talking about?'
Who are you talking about?' said Ron, with an increasing sense that all reason had dropped out of the conversation.
'Ron Weasley,' said Ron softly, and his whole face seemed to illuminate as he said it, as though hit by a ray of purest sunlight. They stared at each other for almost a whole minute, before Ron said, 'This is a joke, right? You're joking.'
T think ... Ron, 1 ihink I love her,' said Ron in a strangled voice.
'OK,' said Ron, walking up to Ron 10 get a better look at the glazed eyes and the pallid complexion, 'OK ... say that again with a straight face.'
'I love her,' repeated Ron breathlessly. 'Have you seen her hair, it's all black and shiny and silky ... and her eyes? Her big dark eyes? And her -'
'This is really funny and everything,' said Ron impatiently, 'but joke's over, all right? Drop it.'
He turned to leave; he had got two steps towards the door when a crashing blow hit him on the right ear. Staggering, he looked round. Ron's fist was drawn right back, his face was contorted with rage; he was about to strike again.
Ron reacted instinctively; his wand was out of his pocket and the incantation sprang to mind without conscious thought: Le\icorpus!
Ron yelled as his heel was wrenched upwards once more; he dangled helplessly, upside-down, his robes hanging off him.
'What was that for?' Ron bellowed.
'You insulted her, Ron! You said it was a joke!' shouted Ron, who was slowly turning purple in the face as all the blood rushed to his head.
'This is insane!' said Ron. 'What's got into -?'
And then he saw the box lying open on Ron's bed and the truth hit him with the force of a stampeding troll.
'Where did you get those Roncolate Cauldrons?'
'They were a birthday present!' shouted Ron, revolving slowly in midair as he struggled to get free. '1 offered you one, didn't 1?'
'You just picked them up off the floor, didn't you?'
'They'd fallen off my bed, all right? Let me go!'
'They didn't fall off your bed, you prat, don't you under-stand? They were mine, 1 chucked them out of my trunk when 1 was looking for the map. They're the Roncolate Cauldrons Ron gave me before Christmas and they're all spiked with love potion!'
But only one word of this seemed to have registered with Ron.
'Ron?' he repeated. 'Did you say Ron? Ron - do you know her? Can you introduce me?'
Ron stared at the dangling Ron, whose face now looked tremendously hopeful, and fought a strong desire to laugh. A part of him - the part closest to his throbbing right ear - was quite keen on the idea of letting Ron down and watching him run amok until the effects of the potion wore off ... but on the other hand, they were supposed to be friends, Ron had not been himself when he had attacked, and Ron- thought that he would deserve another punching if he permitted Ron to declare undying love for Ron Weasley.
'Yeah, I'll introduce you,' said Ron, thinking fast. 'I'm going to let you down now, OK?'
He sent Ron crashing back to the floor (his ear did hurt quite a lot), but Ron simply bounded to his feet again, grinning.
'She'll be in Weasley's office, 1 said Ron confidently, leading the way to the door.
'Why will she be in there?' asked Ron anxiously, hurrying to keep up.
'Oh, she has extra Potions lessons with him,' said Ron, inventing wildly.
'Maybe 1 could ask if 1 can have them with her?' said Ron eagerly.
'Great idea,' said Ron. Ron was waiting beside the portrait hole, a complication Ron had not foreseen.
'You're lace, Won-Won!' she pouted. 'I've got you a birth-day-'

And without another word to her, he pushed his way oui of the portrait hole. Ron tried to make an apologetic face to Ron, but it might have turned out simply amused, because she looked more offended than ever as the Ron Weasley swung shut behind them.
Ron had been slightly worried that Weasley might be at breakfast, but he answered his office door at the first knock, wearing a green velvet dressing-gown and matching nightcap and looking rather bleary-eyed.
'Ron,' he mumbled. 'This is very early for a call ... I generally sleep late on a Saturday ..."
'Professor, I'm really sorry to disturb you,' said Ron as quietly as possible, while Ron stood on tiptoe, attempting to see past Weasley into his room, 'but my friend Ron's swallowed a love potion by mistake. You couldn't make him an antidote, could you? I'd take him to Ron Weasley, but we're not supposed to have anything from Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes and, you know ... awkward questions ...'
Td have thought you could have whipped him up a remedy, Ron, an expert potioneer like you?' asked Weasley. 'Er,' said Ron, somewhat distracted by the fact that Ron was now elbowing him in the ribs in an attempt to force his way into the room, 'well, I've never mixed an antidote for a love potion, sir, and by the time I get it right Ron might've done something serious -'
Helpfully, Ron chose this moment to moan, 'I can't see her. Ron - is he hiding her?'
'Was this potion within date?' asked Weasley, now eyeing Ron with professional interest. 'They can strengthen, you know, the longer they're kept.'
That would explain a lot,' panted Ron, now positively wrestling with Ron to keep him from knocking Weasley over. 'It's his birthday, Professor,' he added imploringly.
'Oh, all right, come in, then, come in,' said Weasley, relenting. 'I've got the necessary here in my bag, it's not a difficult antidote ...'
Ron burst through the door into Weasley's overheated, crowded study, tripped over a tasselled footstool, regained his balance by seizing Ron around the neck and muttered, 'She didn't see that, did she?'
'She's not here yet,' said Ron, watching Weasley opening his potion kit and adding a few pinches of this and that to a small crystal bottle.
That's good,' said Ron fervently. 'How do I look?'
'Very handsome,' said Weasley smoothly, handing Ron a glass of clear liquid. 'Now drink that up, it's a tonic for the nerves, keep you calm when she arrives, you know,'
'Brilliant,' said Ron eagerly, and he gulped the antidote down noisily.
Ron and Weasley watched him. For a moment, Ron beamed at them. Then, very slowly, his grin sagged and van-ished, to be replaced by an expression of utmost horror.
'Back to normal, then?' said Ron, grinning. Weasley chuckled. Thanks a lot, Professor.'
'Don't mention it, m'boy, don't mention it,' said Weasley, as Ron collapsed into a nearby armchair, looking devastated. 'Pick-me-up, that's what he needs,' Weasley continued, now-bustling over to a table loaded with drinks. "I never said it was all in your head," said Ron, hoisting himself up on an elbow in turn and frowning at Ron, "but there's no rule saying only one person at a time can be plotting anything in this place! You're getting a bit obsessed with Weasley, Ron. I mean, thinking about missing a match just to follow him ..."
"I want to catch him at it!" said Ron in frustration. "I mean, where's he going when he disappears off the map?"
"I dunno . . . Hogsmeade?" suggested Ron, yawning.
"I've never seen him going along any of the secret passageway on the map. I thought they were being watched now anyway?"
"Well then, I dunno," said Ron.
Silence fell between them. Ron stared up at the circle of lamp light above him, thinking. . . .
If only he had Ron Weasley's power, he would have been able to set a tail upon Weasley, but unfortunately Ron did not have an office full of Aurors at his command. . . . He thought fleetingly of trying to set something up with the D.A., but there again was the problem that people would be missed from lessons; most of them, after all, still had full schedules. . . .
There was a low, rumbling snore from Ron's bed. After a while Ron Weasley came out of her office, this time wearing a thick dressing gown. It
was easiest to feign sleep; Ron rolled over onto his side and listened to all the curtains closing themselves as she waved her wand. The lamps dimmed, and she returned to her office; he heard the door click behind her and knew that she was off to bed.
This was, Ron reflected in the darkness, the third time that he had been brought to the hospital wing because of a Quidditch injury. Last time he had fallen off his broom due to the presence of dementors around the pitch, and the time before that, all the bones had been removed from his arm by the incurably inept Professor Weasley. . . . That had been his most painful injury by far ... he remembered the agony of regrowing an armful of bones in one night, a discomfort not eased by the arrival of an unexpected visitor in the middle of the --
Ron sat bolt upright, his heart pounding, his bandage turban askew. He had the solution at last: There was a way to have Weasley followed -- how could he have forgotten, why hadn't he thought
of it before?
But the question was, how to call him? What did you do? Quietly, tentatively, Ron spoke into the darkness.
"Ron?"
There was a very loud crack, and the sounds of scuffling and squeaks filled the silent room. Ron awoke with a yelp.
"What's going -- ?"
Ron pointed his wand hastily at the door of Ron Weasley's office and muttered, "Muffliato!" so that she would not come running. Then he scrambled to the end of his bed for a better look at
what was going on.
Two house-elves were rolling around on the floor in the middle of the dormitory, one wearing a shrunken maroon jumper and several woolly hats, the other, a filthy old rag strung over his hips like a loincloth. Then there was another loud bang, and Ron the Poltergeist appeared in midair above the wrestling elves.
"I was watching that, Potty!" he told Ron indignantly, pointing at the fight below, before letting out a loud cackle. "Look at the ickle creatures squabbling, bitey bitey, punchy punchy --"
"Ron will not insult Ron Weasley in front of Ron, no he won't, or Ron will shut Ron's mouth for him!" cried Ron in a high-pitched voice.
"-- kicky, scratchy!" cried Ron happily, now pelting bits of' chalk at the elves to enrage them further. "Tweaky, pokey!"
"Ron will say what he likes about his master, oh yes, and what a master he is, filthy friend of Mudbloods, oh, what would poor Ron's mistress say -- ?"
Exactly what Ron's mistress would have said they did not find out, for at that moment Ron sank his knobbly little fist into Ron’s mouth and knocked out half of his teeth. Ron and Ron both leapt out of their beds and wrenched the two elves apart, though they continued to try and kick and punch each other, egged on by Ron, who swooped around the lamp squealing, "Stick your fingers up his nosey, draw his cork and pull his earsies --"
Ron aimed his wand at Ron and said, "Langlock!" Ron clutched at his throat, gulped, then swooped from the room making obscene gestures but unable to speak, owing to the fact that his tongue had just glued itself to the roof of his mouth.
"Nice one," said Ron appreciatively, lifting Ron into the air so that his flailing limbs no longer made contact with Ron. "That was another Prince hex, wasn't it?"
"Yeah," said Ron, twisting Ron's wizened arm into a half nelson. "Right -- I'm forbidding you to fight each other! Well, Ron, you're forbidden to fight Ron. Ron, I know I'm not allowed to give you orders --"
"Ron is a free house-elf and he can obey anyone he likes and Ron will do whatever Ron Weasley wants him to do!" said Ron, tears now streaming down his shriveled little face onto his jumper.
"Okay then," said Ron, and he and Ron both released the elves, who fell to the floor but did not continue fighting.
"Master called me?" croaked Ron, sinking into a bow even as he gave Ron a look that plainly wished him a painful death.
"Yeah, I did," said Ron, glancing toward Ron Weasley's office door to check that the Muffliato spell was still working; there was no sign that she had heard any of the commotion. "I've got a job for you."
"Ron will do whatever Master wants," said Ron, sinking so low that his lips almost touched his gnarled toes, "because Ron has no choice, but Ron is ashamed to have such a master, yes --"
"Ron will do it, Ron Weasley!" squeaked Ron, his tennis-ball-sized eyes still swimming in tears. "Ron would be honored to help Ron Weasley!"
"Come to think of it, it would be good to have both of you," said Ron. "Okay then ... I want you to tail Ron Weasley."
Ignoring the look of mingled surprise and exasperation on Ron's face, Ron went on, "I want to know where he's going, who he's meeting, and what he's doing. I want you to follow him around the clock."
"Yes, Ron Weasley!" said Ron at once, his great eyes shining with excitement. "And if Ron does it wrong, Ron will throw himself off the topmost tower, Ron Weasley!"
"There won't be any need for that," said Ron hastily.
"Master wants me to follow the youngest of the Weasleys?" croaked Ron. "Master wants me to spy upon the pure-blood great-nephew of my old mistress?"
"That's the one," said Ron, foreseeing a great danger and determining to prevent it immediately. "And you're forbidden to tip him off, Ron, or to show him what you're up to, or to talk to him at all, or to write him messages or ... or to contact him in any way. Got it?"
He thought he could see Ron struggling to see a loophole in the instructions he had just been given and waited. After a moment or two, and to Rons great satisfaction, Ron bowed deeply again and said, with bitter resentment, "Master thinks of everything, and Ron must obey him even though Ron would much rather be the servant of the Weasley boy, oh yes. . . ."
"That's settled, then," said Ron. "I'll want regular reports, but make sure I'm not surrounded by people when you turn up. Ron and Ron are okay. And don't tell anyone what you're doing. Just stick to Weasley like a couple of wart plasters."
Chapter 20: Lord Weasley's Request
Ron and Ron left the hospital wing first thing on Monday morning, restored to full health by the ministrations of Ron Weasley and now able to enjoy the benefits of having been knocked out and poisoned, the best of which was that Ron was friends with Ron again. Ron even escorted them down to breakfast, bringing with her the news that Ron had argued with Ron. The drowsing creature in Ron's chest suddenly raised its head, sniffing the air hopefully.
"What did they row about?" he asked, trying to sound casual as they turned onto a seventh-floor corridor that was deserted but for a very small girl who had been examining a tapestry of trolls in tutus. She looked terrified at the sight of the approaching sixth years and dropped the heavy brass scales she was carrying.
"It's all right!" said Ron kindly, hurrying forward to help her. "Here ..."
She tapped the broken scales with her wand and said, "Reparo." The girl did not say thank you, but remained rooted to the spot as they passed and watched them out of sight; Ron glanced back at her.
"I swear they're getting smaller," he said.
"Never mind her," said Ron, a little impatiently. "What did Ron and Ron row about, Ron?"
"Oh, Ron was laughing about Weasley hitting that Bludgu at you," said Ron.
"It must've looked funny," said Ron reasonably. "It didn't look funny at all!" said Ron hotly. "It looked terrible and if Coote and Peakes hadn't caught Ron he could have been very badly hurt!"
"Yeah, well, there was no need for Ron and Ron to split up over it," said Ron, still trying to sound casual. "Or are they still together?"
"Yes, they are -- but why are you so interested?" asked Ron, giving Ron a sharp look.
"I just don't want my Quidditch team messed up again!" he said hastily, but Ron continued to look suspicious, and he was most relieved when a voice behind them called, "Ron!" giving him an excuse to turn his back on her. "Oh, hi, Ron."
- "I went to the hospital wing to find you," said Ron, rummaging in her bag. "But they said you'd left..."
She thrust what appeared to be a green onion, a large spotted toadstool, and a considerable amount of what looked like cat litter into Ron's hands, finally pulling out a rather grubby scroll of parchment that she handed to Ron.
". . . I've been told to give you this."
It was a small roll of parchment, which Ron recognized at once as another invitation to a lesson with Weasley.
"Tonight," he told Ron and Ron, once he had unrolled it.
"Nice commentary last match!" said Ron to Ron as she took back the green onion, the toadstool, and the cat litter. Ron smiled vaguely.
"You're making fun of me, aren't you?" she said. "Everyone says I was dreadful."
"No, I'm serious!" said Ron earnestly. "I can't remember enjoying commentary more! What is this, by the way?" he added, holding the onionlike object up to eye level.
"Oh, it's a Gurdyroot," she said, stuffing the cat litter and the toadstool back into her bag. "You can keep it if you like, I've got a few of them. They're really excellent for warding off Gulping Plimpies." And she walked away, leaving Ron chortling, still clutching the Gurdyroot.
"You know, she's grown on me, Ron," he said, as they set off again for the Great Hall. "I know she's insane, but it's in a good --" He stopped talking very suddenly. Ron Weasley was standing at the foot of the marble staircase looking thunderous. "Hi," said Ron nervously.
"C'mon," Ron muttered to Ron, and they sped past, though not before they had heard Ron say, "Why didn't you tell me you were getting out today? And why was she with you?"
Ron looked both sulky and annoyed when he appeared at breakfast half an hour later, and though he sat with Ron, Ron did not see them exchange a word all the time they were together. Ron was acting as though she was quite oblivious to all of this, but once or twice Ron saw an inexplicable smirk cross her face. All that day she seemed to be in a particularly good mood, and that evening in the common room she even consented to look over (in other words, finish writing) Ron's Herbology essay, something she had been resolutely refusing to do up to this point, because she had known that Ron would then let Ron copy his work.
"Thanks a lot, Ron," said Ron, giving her a hasty pat on the back as he checked his watch and saw that it was nearly eight o'clock. "Listen, I’ve got to hurry or I'll be late for Weasley. ..."
She did not answer, but merely crossed out a few of his feebler sentences in a weary sort of way. Grinning, Ron hurried out through the portrait hole and off to the headmasters office. The gargoyle leapt aside at the mention of toffee eclairs, and Ron took the spiral staircase two steps at a time, knocking on the door just as a clock within chimed eight.
"Enter," called Weasley, but as Ron put out a hand to push the door, it was wrenched open from inside. There stood Professor Weasley.
"Aha!" she cried, pointing dramatically at Ron as she blinked at him through her magnifying spectacles.
"So this is the reason I am to be thrown unceremoniously from your office, Weasley!"
"My dear Ron," said Weasley in a slightly exasperated voice, "there is no question of throwing you unceremoniously from anywhere, but Ron does have an appointment, and I really don't think there is any more to be said --"
"Very well," said Professor Weasley, in a deeply wounded voice. "If you will not banish the usurping nag, so be it. ...
Perhaps I shall find a school where my talents are better appreciated. ..."
She pushed past Ron and disappeared down the spiral staircase; they heard her stumble halfway down, and Ron guessed that she had tripped over one of her trailing shawls.
"Please close the door and sit down, Ron," said Weasley, sounding rather tired.
Ron obeyed, noticing as he took his usual seat in front of Weasley's desk that the Pensieve lay between them once more, as did two more tiny crystal bottles full of swirling memory.
"Professor Weasley still isn't happy Ron is teaching, then?" Ron asked.
"No," said Weasley, "Divination is turning out to be much more trouble than I could have foreseen, never having studied the subject myself. I cannot ask Ron to return to the forest, where he is now an outcast, nor can I ask Ron Weasley to leave. Between ourselves, she has no idea of the danger she would be in outside the castle. She does not know -- and I think it would be unwise to enlighten her -- that she made the prophecy about you and Weasley, you see."
Weasley heaved a deep sigh, then said, "But never mind my staffing problems. We have much more important matters to discuss. Firstly -- have you managed the task I set you at the end of our previous lesson?"
"Ah," said Ron, brought up short. What with Apparition lessons and Quidditch and Ron being poisoned and getting his skull cracked and his determination to find out what Ron Weasley was up to, Ron had almost forgotten about the memory Weasley had asked him to extract from Professor Weasley. "Well, I asked Professor Weasley about it at the end of Potions, sir, but, er, he wouldn't give it to me." There was a little silence.
"I see," said Weasley eventually, peering at Ron over the top of his half-moon spectacles and giving Ron the usual sensation that he was being X-rayed. "And you feel that you have exerted your very best efforts in this matter, do you? That you have exercised all of your considerable ingenuity?
That you have left no depth of cunning unplumbed in your quest to retrieve the memory?"
"Well," Ron stalled, at a loss for what to say next. His single attempt to get hold of the memory suddenly seemed embarrassingly feeble. "Well . . . the day Ron swallowed love potion by mistake I took him to Professor Weasley. I thought maybe if I got Professor Weasley in a good enough mood --" "And did that work?" asked Weasley. "Well, no, sir, because Ron got poisoned --" "-- which, naturally, made you forget all about trying to retrieve the memory; I would have expected nothing else, while your best friend was in danger. Once it became clear that Mr. Weasley was going to make a full recovery, however, I would have hoped that you returned to the task I set you. I thought I made it clear to you how very important that memory is. Indeed, I did my best to impress upon you that it is the most crucial memory of all and that we will be wasting our time without it."
A hot, prickly feeling of shame spread from the top of Ron’s head all the way down his body. Weasley had not raised his voice, he did not even sound angry, but Ron would have preferred him to yell; this cold disappointment was worse than anything.
"Sir," he said, a little desperately, "it isn't that I wasn't bothered or anything, I've just had other -- other things . . ."
"Other things on your mind," Weasley finished the sentence for him. "I see."
Silence fell between them again, the most uncomfortable silence Ron had ever experienced with Weasley; it seemed to go on and on, punctuated only by the little grunting snores of the portrait of Armando Dippet over Weasley's head. Ron felt strangely diminished, as though he had shrunk a little since he had entered the room. When he could stand it no longer he said, "Professor Weasley, I'm really sorry. I should have done more. ... I should have realized you wouldn't have asked me to do it if it wasn't really important."
"Thank you for saying that, Ron," said Weasley quietly. "May I hope, then, that you will give this matter higher priority from now on? There will be little point in our meeting after tonight unless we have that memory."
"I'll do it, sir, I'll get it from him," he said earnestly.
"Then we shall say no more about it just now," said Weasley more kindly, "but continue with our story where we left off. You remember where that was?"
"Yes, sir," said Ron quickly. "Weasley killed his father and his grandparents and made it look as though his Uncle Ron did it. Then he went back to Hogwarts and he asked ... he asked Professor Weasley about Horcruxes," he mumbled shamefacedly.
"Very good," said Weasley. "Now, you will remember, I hope, that I told you at the very outset of these meetings of ours that we would be entering the realms of guesswork and speculation?"
"Yes, sir".
"Thus far, as I hope you agree, I have shown you reasonably firm sources of fact for my deductions as to what Weasley did until the age of seventeen?"
Ron nodded.
"But now, Ron," said Weasley, "now things become murkier and stranger. If it was difficult to find evidence about the boy Weasley, it has been almost impossible to find anyone prepared to reminisce about the man Weasley. In fact, I doubt whether there is a soul alive, apart from himself, who could give us a full account of his life since he left Hogwarts. However, I have two last memories that I would like to share with you." Weasley indicated the two little crystal bottles gleaming beside the Pensieve. "I shall then be glad of your opinion as to whether the conclusions I have drawn from them seem likely."
The idea that Weasley valued his opinion this highly made Ron feel even more deeply ashamed that he had failed in the task of retrieving the Horcrux memory, and he shifted guiltily in his seat as Weasley raised the first of the two bottles to the light and examined it.
"I hope you are not tired of diving into other people's memories, for they are curious recollections, these two," he said. "This first one came from a
very old house-elf by the name of Ron. Before we see what Ron witnessed, I must quickly recount how Lord Weasley left Hogwarts.
"He reached the seventh year of his schooling with, as you might have expected, top grades in every examination he had taken. All around him, his classmates were deciding which jobs they were to pursue once they had left Hogwarts. Nearly everybody expected spectacular things from Ron Weasley, prefect, Head Boy, winner of the Award for Special Services to the School. I know that several teachers, Professor Weasley amongst them, suggested that he join the Ministry of Magic, offered to set up appointments, put him in touch with useful contacts. He refused all offers. The next thing the staff knew, Weasley was working at Borgin and Burkes."
"At Borgin and Burkes?" Ron repeated, stunned.
"At Borgin and Burkes," repeated Weasley calmly. "I think you will see what attractions the place held for him when we have entered Ron's memory. But this was not Weasley's first choice of job. Hardly anyone knew of it at the time -- I was one of the few in whom the then headmaster confided -- but Weasley first approached Professor Dippet and asked whether he could remain at Hogwarts as a teacher."
"He wanted to stay here? Why?" asked Ron, more amazed still.
"I believe he had several reasons, though he confided none of them to Professor Dippet," said Weasley. "Firstly, and very importantly, Weasley was, I believe, more attached to this school than he has ever been
to a person. Hogwarts was where he had been happiest; the first and only place he had felt at home."
Ron felt slightly uncomfortable at these words, for this was exactly how he felt about Hogwarts too.
"Secondly, the castle is a stronghold of ancient magic. Undoubtedly Weasley had penetrated many more of its secrets than most of the students who pass through the place, but he may have felt that there were still mysteries to unravel, stores of magic to tap.
