"And thirdly, as a teacher, he would have had great power and influence over young witches and wizards. Perhaps he had gained the idea from Professor Weasley, the teacher with whom he was on best terms, who had demonstrated how influential a role a teacher can play. I do not imagine for an instant that Weasley envisaged spending the rest of his life at Hogwarts, but I do think that he saw it as a useful recruiting ground, and a place where he might begin to build himself an army."
"But he didn't get the job, sir?"
"No, he did not. Professor Dippet told him that he was too young at eighteen, but invited him to reapply in a few years, if he still wished to teach."
"How did you feel about that, sir?" asked Ron hesitantly. "Deeply uneasy," said Weasley. "I had advised Armando against the appointment
-- I did not give the reasons I have given you, for Professor Dippet was very fond of Weasley and convinced of his honesty. But I did not want Lord Weasley back at this school, and especially not in a position of power."
"Which job did he want, sir? What subject did he want to teach?"
Somehow, Ron knew the answer even before Weasley gave it.
"Defense Against the Dark Arts. It was being taught at the time by an old Professor by the name of Galatea Merrythought, who had been at Hogwarts for nearly fifty years.
"So Weasley went off to Borgin and Burkes, and all the staff who had admired him said what a waste it was, a brilliant young wizard like that, working in a shop. However, Weasley was no mere assistant. Polite and handsome and clever, he was soon given particular jobs of the type that only exist in a place like Borgin and Burkes, which specializes, as you know, Ron, in objects with unusual and powerful properties. Weasley was sent to persuade people to part with their treasures for sale by the partners, and he was, by all accounts, unusually gifted at doing this."
"I'll bet he was," said Ron, unable to contain himself.
"Well, quite," said Weasley, with a faint smile. "And now it is time to hear from Ron the house-elf, who worked for a very old, very rich witch by the name of Ron Weasley."
Weasley tapped a bottle with his wand, the cork flew out, and he tipped the swirling memory into the Pensieve, saying as he did so, "After you, Ron."
Ron got to his feet and bent once more over the rippling silver contents of the stone basin until his face touched them. He tumbled through dark nothingness and landed in a sitting room in front of an immensely fat old lady wearing an elaborate ginger wig and a brilliant pink set of robes that flowed all around her, giving her the look of a melting iced cake. She was looking into a small jeweled mirror and dabbing rouge onto her already scarlet cheeks with a large powder puff, while the tiniest and oldest house-elf Ron had ever seen laced her fleshy feet into tight satin slippers.
"Hurry up, Ron!" said Ron imperiously. "He said he'd come at four, it's only a couple of minutes to and he's never been late yet!"
She tucked away her powder puff as the house-elf straightened up. The top of the elf's head barely reached the seat of Ron's chair, and her papery skin hung off her frame just like the crisp linen sheet she wore draped like a toga.
"How do I look?" said Ron, turning her head to admire the various angles of her face in the mirror.
"Lovely, madam," squeaked Ron.
Ron could only assume that it was down in Ron’s contract that she must lie through her teeth when asked this question, because Ron Weasley looked a long way from lovely in his opinion.
A tinkling doorbell rang and both mistress and elf jumped.
"Quick, quick, he's here, Ron!" cried Ron and the elf scurried out of the room, which was so crammed with objects that it was difficult to see how anybody could navigate their way across it without knocking over at least a dozen things: There were cabinets full of little lacquered boxes, cases full of gold-embossed books, shelves of orbs and celestial globes, and many flourishing potted plants in brass containers. In fact, the room looked like a cross between a magical antique shop and a conservatory.
The house-elf returned within minutes, followed by a tall young man Ron had no difficulty whatsoever in recognizing as Weasley. He was plainly dressed in a black suit; his hair was a little longer than it had been at school and his cheeks were hollowed, but all of this suited him; he looked more handsome than ever. He picked his way through the cramped room with an air that showed he had visited many times before and bowed low over Ron's fat little hand, brushing it with his lips.
"I brought you flowers," he said quietly, producing a bunch of roses from nowhere.
"You naughty boy, you shouldn't have!" squealed old Ron, though Ron noticed that she had an empty vase standing ready on the nearest little
table. "You do spoil this old lady, Ron. ... Sit down, sit down. . . . Where's Ron? Ah ..."
The house-elf had come dashing back into the room carrying a tray of little cakes, which she set at her mistress's elbow.
"Help yourself, Ron," said Ron, "I know how you love my cakes. Now, how are you? You look pale. They overwork you at that shop, I've said it a hundred times. ..."
Weasley smiled mechanically and Ron simpered.
"Well, what's your excuse for visiting this time?" she asked, bat-ring her lashes.
"Mr. Burke would like to make an improved offer for the goblin-made armor," said Weasley. "Five hundred Galleons, he feels it is a more than fair --"
"Now, now, not so fast, or I’ll think you're only here for my trinkets!" pouted Ron.
"I am ordered here because of them," said Weasley quietly. "I am only a poor assistant, madam, who must do as he is told. Mr. Burke wishes me to inquire --"
"Oh, Mr. Burke, phooey!" said Ron, waving a little hand. "I've something to show you that I've never shown Mr. Burke! Can you keep a secret, Ron? Will you promise you won't tell Mr. Burke I've got it? He'd never let me rest if he knew I'd shown it to you, and I'm not selling, not to Burke, not to anyone! But you, Ron, you'll appreciate it for its history, not how many Galleons you can get for it."
"I'd be glad to see anything Miss Ron shows me," said Weasley quietly, and Ron gave another girlish giggle.
"I had Ron bring it out for me . . . Ron, where are you? I want to show Mr. Weasley our finest treasure. ... In fact, bring both, while you're at it. ..."
"Here, madam," squeaked the house-elf, and Ron saw two leather boxes, one on top of the other, moving across the room as if of their own volition, though he knew the tiny elf was holding them over her head as she wended her way between tables, ***pouffes, and footstools.
"Now," said Ron happily, taking the boxes from the elf, laying them in her lap, and preparing to open the topmost one, "I think you'll like this, Ron. . . . Oh, if my family knew I was showing you. . . . They can't wait to get their hands on this!"
She opened the lid. Ron edged forward a little to get a better view and saw what looked like a small golden cup with two finely wrought handles.
"I wonder whether you know what it is, Ron? Pick it up, have a good look!" whispered Ron, and Weasley stretched out a long-fingered hand and lifted the cup by one handle out of its snug silken wrappings. Ron thought he saw a red gleam in his dark eyes. His greedy expression was curiously mirrored on Ron’s face, except that her tiny eyes were fixed upon Weasley's handsome features.
"A badger," murmured Weasley, examining the engraving upon the cup. "Then this was . . . ?"
"Ron Weasley's, as you very well know, you clever boy!" said Ron, leaning forward with a loud creaking of corsets and actually pinching his hollow cheek. "Didn't I tell you I was distantly descended? This has been handed down in the family for years and years. Lovely, isn't it? And all sorts of powers it's supposed to possess too, but I haven't tested them thoroughly, I just keep it nice and safe in here. . . ."
She hooked the cup back off Weasley's long forefinger and restored it gently to its box, too intent upon settling it carefully back into position to notice the shadow that crossed Weasley's face as the cup was taken away.
"Now then," said Ron happily, "where’s Ron? Oh yes, there you are -- take that away now, Ron."
The elf obediently took the boxed cup, and Ron turned her attention to the much flatter box in her lap.
"I think you'll like this even more, Ron," she whispered. "Lean in a little, dear boy, so you can see. . . . Of course, Burke knows I've got this one, I bought it from him, and I daresay he'd love to get it back when I'm gone. ..."
She slid back the fine filigree clasp and flipped open the box. There upon the smooth crimson velvet lay a heavy golden locket.
Weasley reached out his hand, without invitation this time, and held it up to the light, staring at it.
"Weasley's mark," he said quietly, as the light played upon an ornate, serpentine S.
"That's right!" said Ron, delighted, apparently, at the sight of Weasley gazing at her locket, transfixed. "I had to pay an arm and a leg for it, but I couldn't let it pass, not a real treasure like that, had to have it for my collection. Burke bought it, apparently, from a ragged-looking woman who seemed to have stolen it, but had no idea of its true value --"
There was no mistaking it this time: Weasley's eyes flashed scarlet at the words, and Ron saw his knuckles whiten on the locket's chain.
"-- I daresay Burke paid her a pittance but there you are. . . . Pretty, isn't it? And again, all kinds of powers attributed to it, though I just keep it nice and safe. . . ."
She reached out to take the locket back. For a moment, Ron thought Weasley was not going to let go of it, but then it had slid through his fingers and was back in its red velvet cushion.
"So there you are, Ron, clear, and I hope you enjoyed that!"
She looked him full in the face and for the first time, Ron saw her foolish smile falter.
"Are you all right, dear?"
"Oh yes," said Weasley quietly. "Yes, I'm very well. ..."
"I thought -- but a trick of the light, I suppose --" said Ron, looking unnerved, and Ron guessed that she too had seen the momentary red gleam in Weasley's eyes. "Here, Ron, take these away and lock them up again. ... The usual enchantments...
"Time to leave, Ron," said Weasley quietly, and as the in tie elf bobbed away bearing the boxes, Weasley grasped Ron once again above the elbow and together they rose up through oblivion and back to Weasley's office.
"Ron Weasley died two days after that little scene," said Weasley, resuming his seat and indicating that Ron should do the same. "Ron the house-elf was convicted by the Ministry of poisoning her mistress's evening cocoa by accident."
"No way!" said Ron angrily.
"I see we are of one mind," said Weasley. "Certainly, then are many similarities between this death and that of the Weasleys. In both cases, somebody else took the blame, someone who had a clear memory of having caused the death --" "Ron confessed?"
"She remembered putting something in her mistress's cocoa that turned out not to be sugar, but a lethal and little-known poison, said Weasley. "It was concluded that she had not meant to do it, but being old and confused --"
"Weasley modified her memory, just like he did with Ron!" "Yes, that is my conclusion too," said Weasley. "And, just as with Ron, the Ministry was predisposed to suspect Ron --"
"-- because she was a house-elf," said Ron. He had rarely felt more in sympathy with the society Ron had set up, S.P.E.W. "Precisely," said Weasley. "She was old, she admitted to having tampered with the drink, and nobody at the Ministry bothered to inquire further. As in the case of Ron, by the time I traced her and managed to extract this memory, her life was almost over -- but her memory, of course, proves nothing except that Weasley knew of the existence of the cup and the locket.
"By the time Ron was convicted, Ron's family had realized that two of her greatest treasures were missing. It took them a while to be sure of
this, for she had many hiding places, having always guarded her collection most jealously. But before they were sure beyond doubt that the cup and the locket were both gone, the assistant who had worked at Borgin and Burkes, the young man who had visited Ron so regularly and charmed her so well, had resigned his post and vanished. His superiors had no idea where he had gone; they were as surprised as anyone at his disappearance. And that was the last that was seen or heard of Ron Weasley for a very long time.
"Now," said Weasley, "if you don't mind, Ron, I want to pause once more to draw your attention to certain points of our story. Weasley had committed another murder; whether it was his first since he killed the Weasleys, I do not know, but I think it was. This time, as you will have seen, he killed not for revenge, but for gain. He wanted the two fabulous trophies that poor, besotted, old woman showed him. Just as he had once robbed the other children at his orphanage, just as he had stolen his Uncle Ron’s ring, so he ran off now with Rons cup and locket."
"But," said Ron, frowning, "it seems mad. . . . Risking everything, throwing away his job, just for those . . ."
"Mad to you, perhaps, but not to Weasley," said Weasley. "I hope you will understand in due course exactly what those objects meant to him, Ron, but you must admit that it is not difficult to imagine that he saw the locket, at least, as rightfully his." "The locket maybe," said Ron, "but why take the cup as well?"
"It had belonged to another of Hogwarts’s founders," said Weasley. "I think he still felt a great pull toward the school and that he could not resist an object so steeped in Hogwarts history. There were other reasons, I think. ... I hope to be able to demonstrate them to you in due course.
"And now for the very last recollection I have to show you, at least until you manage to retrieve Professor Weasley's memory for us. Ten years separates Ron’s memory and this one, ten years during which we can only guess at what Lord Weasley was doing. . . ." Ron got to his feet once more as Weasley emptied the last memory into the Pensieve.
"Whose memory is it?" he asked. "Mine," said Weasley.
And Ron dived after Weasley through the shifting silver mass, landing in the very office he had just left. There was Ron slumbering happily on his perch, and there behind the desk was Weasley, who looked very similar to the Weasley standing beside Ron, though both hands were whole and undamaged and his face was, perhaps, a little less lined. The one difference between the present-day office and this one was that it was snowing in the past; bluish flecks were drifting past the window in the dark and building up on the outside ledge.
The younger Weasley seemed to be waiting for something, and sure enough, moments after their arrival, there was a knock on the door and he said, "Enter."
Ron let out a hastily stifled gasp. Weasley had entered the room. His features were not those Ron had seen emerge from the great stone cauldron almost two years ago: They were not as snake-like, the eyes were not yet scarlet, the face not yet masklike, and yet he was no longer handsome Ron Weasley. It was as though his features had been burned and blurred; they were waxy and oddly distorted, and the whites of the eyes now had a permanently bloody look, though the pupils were not yet the slits that Ron knew they would become. He was wearing a long black cloak, and his face was as pale as the snow glistening on his shoulders.
The Weasley behind the desk showed no sign of surprise. Evidently this visit had been made by appointment.
"Good evening, Ron," said Weasley easily. "Won't you sit down?"
"Thank you," said Weasley, and he took the seat to which Weasley had gestured -- the very seat, by the looks of it, that Ron had just vacated in the present. "I heard that you had become headmaster," he said, and his voice was slightly higher and colder than it had been. "A worthy choice."
"I am glad you approve," said Weasley, smiling. "May I offer you a drink?"
"That would be welcome," said Weasley. "I have come a long way."
Weasley stood and swept over to the cabinet where he now kept the Pensieve, but which then was full of bottles. Having handed Weasley a
goblet of wine and poured one for himself, he returned to the seat behind his desk. . "So, Ron ... to what do I owe the pleasure?"
Weasley did not answer at once, but merely sipped his wine.
"They do not call me 'Ron' anymore," he said. "These days, 1 am known as --"
"I know what you are known as," said Weasley, smiling, pleasantly. "But to me, I'm afraid, you will always be Ron Weasley. It is one of the irritating things about old teachers. I am afraid that they never quite forget their charges' youthful beginnings."
He raised his glass as though toasting Weasley, whose face remained expressionless. Nevertheless, Ron felt the atmosphere in the room change subtly: Weasley's refusal to use Weasley’s chosen name was a refusal to allow Weasley to dictate the terms of the meeting, and Ron could tell that Weasley took it as such.
"I am surprised you have remained here so long," said Weasley after a short pause. "I always wondered why a wizard such as yourself never wished to leave school."
"Well," said Weasley, still smiling, "to a wizard such as myself, there can be nothing more important than passing on ancient skills, helping hone young minds. If I remember correctly, you once saw the attraction of teaching too."
"I see it still," said Weasley. "I merely wondered why you -- who are so often asked for advice by the Ministry, and who have twice, I think, been offered the post of Minister --"
"Three times at the last count, actually," said Weasley. "But the Ministry never attracted me as a career. Again, something we have in common, I think."
Weasley inclined his head, unsmiling, and took another sip of wine. Weasley did not break the silence that stretched between them now, but waited, with a look of pleasant expectancy, for Weasley to talk first.
"I have returned," he said, after a little while, "later, perhaps, than Professor Dippet expected . . . but I have returned, nevertheless, to request again what he once told me I was too young to have. I have come to you to ask that you permit me to return to this castle, to teach. I think you must know that I have seen and done much since I left this place. I could show and tell your students things they can gain from no other wizard."
Weasley considered Weasley over the top of his own goblet for a while before speaking.
"Yes, I certainly do know that you have seen and done much since leaving us," he said quietly. "Rumors of your doings have reached your old school, Ron. I should be sorry to believe half of them."
Weasley's expression remained impassive as he said, "Greatness inspires envy, envy engenders spite, spite spawns lies. You must know this, Weasley."
"You call it 'greatness,' what you have been doing, do you?" asked Weasley delicately.
"Certainly," said Weasley, and his eyes seemed to burn red. "I have experimented; I have pushed the boundaries of magic further, perhaps, than they have ever been pushed --"
"Of some kinds of magic," Weasley corrected him quietly. "Of some. Of others, you remain . . . forgive me . . . woefully ignorant."
For the first time, Weasley smiled. It was a taut leer, an evil thing, more threatening than a look of rage.
"The old argument," he said softly. "But nothing I have seen in the world has supported your famous pronouncements that love is more powerful than my kind of magic, Weasley."
"Perhaps you have been looking in the wrong places," suggested Weasley.
"Well, then, what better place to start my fresh researches than here, at Hogwarts?" said Weasley. "Will you let me return? Will you let me share
my knowledge with your students? I place myself and my talents at your disposal. I am yours to command."
Weasley raised his eyebrows. "And what will become of those whom you command? What will happen to those who call themselves -- or so rumor has it -- the Death Eaters?"
Ron could tell that Weasley had not expected Weasley to know this name; he saw Weasley’s eyes flash red again and the slitlike nostrils flare.
"My friends," he said, after a moment's pause, "will carry on without me, I am sure."
"I am glad to hear that you consider them friends," said Weasley. "I was under the impression that they are more in the order of servants."
"You are mistaken," said Weasley.
"Then if I were to go to the Hog's Head tonight, I would not find a group of them -- Weasley, Rosier, Muldber, Weasley -- awaiting your return? Devoted friends indeed, to travel this far with you on a snowy night, merely to wish you luck as you attempted to secure a teaching post."
There could be no doubt that Weasley's detailed knowledge of those with whom he was traveling was even less welcome to Weasley; however, he rallied almost at once.
"You are omniscient as ever, Weasley."
"Oh no, merely friendly with the local barmen," said Weasley lightly. "Now, Ron . . ."
Weasley set down his empty glass and drew himself up in his seat, the tips of his fingers together in a very characteristic gesture.
"Let us speak openly. Why have you come here tonight, surrounded by henchmen, to request a job we both know you do not want?"
Weasley looked coldly surprised. "A job I do not want? On the contrary, Weasley, I want it very much."
"Oh, you want to come back to Hogwarts, but you do not want to teach any more than you wanted to when you were eighteen. What is it you're after, Ron? Why not try an open request for once?"
Weasley sneered. "If you do not want to give me a job --"
"Of course I don't," said Weasley. "And I don't think for a moment you expected me to. Nevertheless, you came here, you asked, you must have had a purpose."
Weasley stood up. He looked less like Ron Weasley than ever, his features thick with rage. "This is your final word?"
"It is," said Weasley, also standing.
"Then we have nothing more to say to each other."
"No, nothing," said Weasley, and a great sadness filled his face. "The time is long gone when I could frighten you with a burning wardrobe and force you to make repayment for your crimes. But I wish I could, Ron. ... I wish I could. . . ."
For a second, Ron was on the verge of shouting a pointless warning: He was sure that Weasley's hand had twitched toward his pocket and his wand; but then the moment had passed, Weasley had turned away, the door was closing, and he was gone.
Ron felt Weasley's hand close over his arm again and moments later, they were standing together on almost the same spot, but there was no snow building on the window ledge, and Weasley's hand was blackened and dead-looking once more.
"Why?" said Ron at once, looking up into Weasley's face. "Why did he come back? Did you ever find out?"
"I have ideas," said Weasley, "but no more than that."
"What ideas, sir?"
"I shall tell you, Ron, when you have retrieved that memory from Professor Weasley," said Weasley.
"When you have that last piece of the jigsaw, everything will, I hope, be clear ... to both of us."
Ron was still burning with curiosity and even though Weasley had walked to the door and was holding it open for him, he did not move at once.
"Was he after the Defense Against the Dark Arts job again, sir? He didn't say. ..."
"Oh, he definitely wanted the Defense Against the Dark Arts job," said Weasley. "The aftermath of our little meeting proved that. You see, we have never been able to keep a Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher for longer than a year since I refused the post to Lord Weasley."
Chapter 21: The Unknowable Room
Ron wracked his brains over the next week as to how he was to persuade Weasley to hand over the true memory, but nothing in the nature of a brain wave occurred and he was reduced to doing what he did increasingly these days when at a loss: poring over his Potions book, hoping that the Prince would have scribbled something useful in a margin, as he had done so many times before.
"You won't find anything in there," said Ron firmly, late on Sunday evening.
"Don't start, Ron," said Ron. "If it hadn't been for the Prince, Ron wouldn't be sitting here now."
"He would if you'd just listened to Weasley in our first year," said Ron dismissively.
Ron ignored her. He had just found an incantation "Sectum-sempra!" scrawled in a margin above the intriguing words "For enemies," and was itching to try it out, but thought it best not to in front of Ron. Instead, he surreptitiously folded down the corner of the page. They were sitting beside the fire in the common room; the only other people awake were fellow sixth years. There had been a cer-tain amount of excitement earlier when they had come back from dinner to find a new sign on the notice board that announced the date for their Apparition Test. Those who would be seventeen on or before the first test date, the twenty-first of April, had the
option of signing up for additional practice sessions, which would take place (heavily supervised) in Hogsmeade.
Ron had panicked on reading this notice; he had still not man-aged to Apparate and feared he would not be ready for the test. Ron, who had now achieved Apparition twice, was a little more confident, but Ron, who would not be seventeen for an-other four months, could not take the test whether ready or not.
"At least you can Apparate, though!" said Ron tensely. "You'll have no trouble come July!"
"I've only done it once," Ron reminded him; he had finally managed to disappear and rematerialize inside his hoop during their previous lesson.
Having wasted a lot of time worrying aloud about Apparition, Ron was now struggling to finish a viciously difficult essay for Weasley that Ron and Ron had already completed. Ron fully expected to receive low marks on his, because he had disagreed with Weasley on the best way to tackle dementors, but he did not care: Weasleys memory was the most important thing to him now.
"I'm telling you, the stupid Prince isn't going to be able to help you with this, Ron!" said Ron, more loudly. "There's only one way to force someone to do what you want, and that's the Imperius Curse, which is illegal --"
"Yeah, I know that, thanks," said Ron, not looking up from the book. "That's why I'm looking for something different. Dumbledorf says Veritaserum won't do it, but there might be something else, a potion or a spell. . . ."
"You're going about it the wrong way," said Ron. "Only you can get the memory, Weasley says. That must mean you can persuade Weasley where other people can’t. It's not a question of slipping him a potion, anyone could do that --"
"How do you spell 'belligerent'?" said Ron, shaking his quill very hard while staring at his parchment. "It can't be B -- U -- M --"
"No, it isn't," said Ron, pulling Ron's essay toward her. "And 'augury' doesn't begin O -- R -- G either. What kind of quill are you using?"
"It's one of Ron and Ron's Spell-Check ones, but I think the charm must be wearing off."
"Yes, it must," said Ron, pointing at the title of his essay, "because we were asked how we'd deal with dementors, not 'Dug-bogs', and I don't remember you changing your name to 'Roonil Wazlib’ either."
"Ah no!" said Ron, staring horror-struck at the parchment. "Don't say I'll have to write the whole thing out again!"
"It's okay, we can fix it," said Ron, pulling the essay toward her and taking out her wand.
"I love you, Ron," said Ron, sinking back in his chair, rub-bing his eyes wearily. Ron turned faintly pink, but merely said, "Don't let Ron hear you saying that."
"1 won't," said Ron into his hands. "Or maybe I will, then she'll ditch me."
"Why don't you ditch her if you want to finish it?" asked Ron.
"You haven't ever chucked anyone, have you?" said Ron. "You and Ron just --"
"Sort of fell apart, yeah," said Ron.
"Wish that would happen with me and Ron," said Ron gloomily, watching Ron silently tapping each of his mis-spelled words with the end of her wand, so that they corrected themselves on the page. "But the more I hint I want to finish it, the tighter she holds on. It's like going out with the giant squid."
"There," said Ron, some twenty minutes later, handing back Ron's essay.
"Thanks a million," said Ron. "Can I borrow your quill for the conclusion?" Ron, who had found nothing useful in the Half-Blood
Prince's notes so far, looked around; the three of them were now the only ones left in the common room, Ron having just gone up to bed cursing Weasley and his essay. The only sounds were the crackling of the fire and Ron scratching out one last paragraph on dementors using Ron's quill. Ron had just closed the Half-Blood Prince's book, yawning, when --
Crack!
Ron let out a little shriek; Ron spilled ink all over his freshly completed essay, and Ron said, "Ron!"
The house-elf bowed low and addressed his own gnarled toes. "Master said he wanted regular reports on what the Weasley boy is doing, so Ron has come to give--"
Crack!
Ron appeared alongside Ron, his tea-cozy hat askew. "Ron has been helping too, Ron Weasley!" he squeaked, cast-ing Ron a resentful look. "And Ron ought to tell Ron when he is coming to see Ron Weasley so they can make their re-ports together!"
"What is this?" asked Ron, still looking shocked by these sudden appearances. "What's going on, Ron?" Ron hesitated before answering, because he had not told Her-mione about setting Ron and Ron to tail Weasley; house-elves were always such a touchy subject with her.
"Well. . . they've been following Weasley for me," he said.
"Night and day," croaked Ron.
"Ron has not slept for a week, Ron Weasley!" said Ron proudly, swaying where he stood. Ron looked indignant.
"You haven't slept, Ron? But surely, Ron, you didn't tell him not to --"
"No, of course I didn't," said Ron quickly. "Ron, you can sleep, all right? But has either of you found out anything?" he has-tened to ask, before Ron could intervene again.
"Master Weasley moves with a nobility that befits his pure blood," croaked Ron at once. "His features recall the fine bones of my mistress and his manners are those of--"
"Ron Weasley is a bad boy!" squeaked Ron angrily. "A bad boy who -- who --" He shuddered from the tassel of his tea cozy to the toes of his socks and then ran at the fire, as though about to dive into it. Ron, to whom this was not entirely unexpected, caught him around the middle and held him fast. For a few seconds Ron struggled, then went limp.
"Thank you, Ron Weasley," he panted. "Ron still finds it dif-ficult to speak ill of his old masters." Ron released him; Ron straightened his tea
cozy and said defiantly to Ron, "But Ron should know that Ron Weasley is not a good master to a house-elf!"
"Yeah, we don't need to hear about you being in love with Weasley," Ron told Ron. "Let's fast forward to where he's actually been going."
Ron bowed again, looking furious, and then said, "Master Weasley eats in the Great Hall, he sleeps in a dormitory in the dun-geons, he attends his classes in a variety of--"
"Ron, you tell me," said Ron, cutting across Ron. "Has he been going anywhere he shouldn't have?"
"Ron Weasley, sir," squeaked Ron, his great orblike eyes shining in the firelight, "the Weasley boy is breaking no rules that Ron can discover, but he is still keen to avoid detection. He has been making regular visits to the seventh floor with a variety of other students, who keep watch for him while he enters --"
"The Room of Requirement!" said Ron, smacking himself hard on the forehead with Advanced Potion-Making. Ron and Ron stared at him. "That's where he's been sneaking off to! That's where he's doing... whatever he's doing! And I bet that's why he's been disappearing off the map -- come to think of it, I've never seen the Room of Requirement on there!"
"Maybe the Marauders never knew the room was there," said Ron.
"I think it'll be part of the magic of the room," said Ron. "If you need it to be unplottable, it will be."
"Ron, have you managed to get in to have a look at what Weasley's doing?" said Ron eagerly.
"No, Ron Weasley, that is impossible," said Ron.
"No, it's not," said Ron at once. "Weasley got into our head-quarters there last year, so I'll be able to get in and spy on him, no problem."
"But I don't think you will, Ron," said Ron slowly. "Mal-foy already knew exactly how we were using the room, didn't he, because that stupid Ron had blabbed. He needed the room to become the headquarters of the D.A., so it did. But you don't know what the room becomes when Weasley goes in there, so you don't know what to ask it to transform into."
"There'll be a way around that," said Ron dismissively. "You've done brilliantly, Ron."
"Rons done well too," said Ron kindly; but far from looking grateful, Ron averted his huge, bloodshot eyes and croaked at the ceiling, "The Mudblood is speaking to Ron, Ron will pretend he cannot hear --"
"Get out of it," Ron snapped at him, and Ron made one last deep bow and Disapparated. "You'd better go and get some sleep too, Ron."
"Thank you, Ron Weasley, sir!" squeaked Ron happily, and he too vanished.
"How good is this?" said Ron enthusiastically, turning to Ron and Ron the moment the room was elf-free again. "We know where Weasley's going! We've got him cornered now!"
"Yeah, it's great," said Ron glumly, who was attempting to mop up the sodden mass of ink chat had recently been an almost com-pleted essay. Ron pulled it toward her and began siphoning the ink off with her wand.
"But what's all this about him going up there with a variety of students'?" said Ron. "How many people are in on it? You wouldn't think he'd trust lots of them to know what he's do-ing---"
"Yeah, that is weird," said Ron, frowning. "I heard him telling Weasley it wasn't Weasley's business what he was doing... so what's he telling all these... all these..." Ron's voice tailed away; he was staring at the fire. "God, I've been stupid," he said quietly. "Its obvious, isn't it? There was a great vat of it down in the dungeon. . . . He could’ve nicked some any time during that lesson. . . ."
"Roned what?" said Ron.
"Polyjuice Potion. He stole some of the Polyjuice Potion Slug-horn showed us in our first Potions lesson... There aren't a whole variety of students standing guard for Weasley... it's just Weasley and Weasley as usual. ...Yeah, it all fits!" said Ron, jumping up and starting to pace in front of the fire. "They're stupid enough to do what they're told even if he won't tell them what he's up to, but he doesn't want them to be seen lurking around outside the Room of Requirement, so he's got them taking Polyjuice to make them look like other people... Those two girls I saw him with when he missed Quidditch -- ha! Weasley and Weasley!"
"Do you mean to say," said Ron in a hushed voice, "that that little girl whose scales I repaired -- ?"
"Yeah, of course!" said Ron loudly, staring at her. "Of course! Weasley must've been inside the room at the time, so she -- what am I talking about? -- he dropped the scales to tell Weasley not to corne out, because there was someone there! And there was that girl who dropped the toadspawn too! We've been walking past him all the time and not realizing it!"
"He's got Weasley and Weasley transforming into girls?" guffawed Ron. "Blimey... no wonder they don't look too happy these days. I'm surprised they don't tell him to stuff it."
"Well, they wouldn't, would they, if he's shown them his Dark Mark?" said Ron.
"Hmmm... the Dark Mark we don't know exists," said Hermi-one skeptically, rolling up Ron's dried essay before it could come to any more harm and handing it to him.
"We'll see" said Ron confidently.
"Yes, we will," Ron said, getting to her feet and stretching. "But, Ron, before you get all excited, I still don't think you'll be able to get into the Room of Requirement without knowing what's there first'. And I don't think you should forget" -- she heaved her bag onto her shoulder and gave him a very serious look -- "that what you're supposed to be concentrating on is getting that memory from Weasley. Good night."
Ron watched her go, feeling slightly disgruntled. Once the door to the girls' dormitories had closed behind her he rounded on Ron. "What d'you think?"
"Wish I could Disapparate like a house-elf," said Ron, staring at the spot where Ron had vanished. "I'd have that Apparition Test in the bag."
Ron did not sleep well that night. He lay awake for what felt like hours, wondering how Weasley was using the Room of Requirement and what he, Ron, would see when he went in there the following day, for whatever Ron said, Ron was sure that if Weasley had-=- been able to see the headquarters of the D.A., he would be able to see Weasley's, what could it be? A meeting place? A hideout? A ston room? A workshop? Rons mind worked feverishly and his dreams, when he finally fell asleep, were broken
and disturbed by images of Weasley, who turned into Weasley, who turned into Weasley...
Ron was in a state of great anticipation over breakfast the following morning; he had a free period before Defense Against the Dark Arts and was determined to spend it trying to get into the Room of Requirement. Ron was rather ostentatiously showing no interest in his whispered plans for forcing entry into the room, which irritated Ron, because he thought she might be a lot of help if she wanted to.
"Look," he said quietly, leaning forward and putting a hand on the Daily Prophet, which she had just removed from a post owl, to stop her from opening it and vanishing behind it. "I haven't for-gotten about Weasley, but I haven't got a clue how to get that memory off him, and until I get a brain wave why shouldn't I find out what Weasley's doing?"
"I've already told you, you need to persuade Weasley," said Her-mione. "It's not a question of tricking him or bewitching him, or Weasley could have done it in a second. Instead of messing around outside the Room of Requirement" -- she jerked the Prophet out from under Rons hand and unfolded it to look at the front page -- "you should go and find Weasley and start appeal-ing to his better nature."
"Anyone we know -- ?" asked Ron, as Ron scanned the headlines.
"Yes!" said Ron, causing both Ron and Ron to gag on their breakfast. "But it's all right, he's not dead -- its Ron, he's been
arrested and sent to Azkaban! Something to do with impersonating an Inferius during an attempted burglary, and someone called Octavius Pepper has vanished. Oh, and how horrible, a nine-year-old boy has been arrested for trying to kill his grandparents, they think he was under the Imperius Curse."
They finished their breakfast in silence. Ron set off imme-diately for Ancient Runes; Ron for the common room, where he still had to finish his conclusion on Weasley's dementor essay, and Ron for the corridor on the seventh floor and the stretch of wall opposite the tapestry of Barnabas the Barmy teaching trolls to do ballet.
Ron slipped on his Invisibility Cloak once he had found an empty passage, but he need not have bothered. When he reached his destination he found it deserted. Ron was not sure whether his chances of getting inside the room were better with Weasley in-side it or out, but at least his first attempt was not going to be complicated by the presence of Weasley or Weasley pretending to be an eleven-year-old girl.
He closed his eyes as he approached the place where the Room of Requirement's door was concealed. He knew what he had to do; he had become most accomplished at it last year. Concentrating with all his might he thought, "I need to see what Weasley's doing in here... I need to see what Weasley's doing in here... I need to see what Weasley's doing in here..."
Three times he walked past the door; then, his heart pounding with excitement, he opened his eyes and faced it -- but he was still looking at a
stretch of mundanely blank wall. He moved forward and gave it an experimental push. The stone remained solid and unyielding.
"Okay," said Ron aloud. "Okay... I thought the wrong thing..." He pondered for a moment then set off again, eyes closed, con-centrating as hard as he could. "I need to see the place where Weasley keeps coming secretly... I need to see the place where Weasley keeps coming secretly..." After three walks past, he opened his eyes expectantly.
There was no door.
"Oh, come off it," he told the wall irritably. "That was a clear instruction. Fine." He thought hard for several minutes before striding off once more. "I need you to become the place you become for Ron Weasley..."
He did not immediately open his eyes when he had finished his patrolling; he was listening hard, as though he might hear the door pop into existence. He heard nothing, however, except the distant twittering of birds outside. He opened his eyes.
There was still no door.
Ron swore. Someone screamed. He looked around to see a gaggle of first years running back around the corner, apparently un-der the impression that they had just encountered a particularly foulmouthed ghost.
Ron tried every variation of "I need to see what Ron Weasley is doing inside you" that he could think of for a whole hour, at the end of which he was forced to concede that Ron might have had a point: The room simply did not want to open for him. Frus-trated and annoyed, he set off for Defense Against the Dark Arts, pulling off his Invisibility Cloak and stuffing it into his bag as he went.
"Late again, Weasley," said Weasley coldly, as Ron hurried into the candlelit classroom. "Ten points from Gryfrindor." Ron scowled at Weasley as he flung himself into the seat beside Ron. Half the class were still on their feet, taking out books and orga-nizing their things; he could not be much later than any of them.
"Before we start, I want your dementor essays," said Weasley, wav-ing his wand carelessly, so that twenty-five scrolls of parchment soared into the air and landed in a neat pile on his desk. "And I hope for your sakes they are better than the tripe I had to endure on resisting the Imperius Curse. Now, if you will all open your books to page -- what is it, Mr. Weasley?"
"Sir," said Ron, "I've been wondering, how do you tell the difference between an Inferius and a ghost? Because there was something in the paper about an Inferius --"
"No, there wasn't," said Weasley in a bored voice.
"But sir, I heard people talking --"
"If you had actually read the article in question, Mr. Weasley, you would have known that the so-called Inferius was nothing but a smelly sneak thief by the name of Ron Weasley."
"I thought Weasley and Ron were on the same side," mut-tered Ron to Ron and Ron. "Shouldn't he be upset Mun-dungus has been arrest --"
"But Weasley seems to have a lot to say on the subject," said Weasley, pointing suddenly at the back of the room, his black eyes fixed on Ron. "Let us ask Weasley how we would tell the difference between an Inferius and a ghost."
The whole class looked around at Ron, who hastily tried to recall what Weasley had told him the night that they had gone to visit Weasley. "Er -- well -- ghosts are transparent --" he said.
"Oh, very good," interrupted Weasley, his lip curling. "Yes, it in easy to see that nearly six years of magical education have not been wasted on you, Weasley. 'Ghosts are transparent."'
Ron Weasley let out a high-pitched giggle. Several other peo-ple were smirking. Ron took a deep breath and continued calmly, though his insides were boiling, "Yeah, ghosts are transparent, but Inferi are dead bodies, aren't they? So they'd be solid --"
"A five-year-old could have told us as much," sneered Weasley. "The Inferius is a corpse that has been reanimated by a Dark wiz-ard's spells. It is
not alive, it is merely used like a puppet to do the wizard's bidding. A ghost, as I trust that you are all aware by now, is the imprint of a departed soul left upon the earth, and of course, as Weasley so wisely tells us, transparent. "
"Well, what Ron said is the most useful if we're trying to tell them apart!" said Ron. "When we come face-to-face with one down a dark alley, we're going to be having a look to see if its solid, aren't we, we're not going to be asking, 'Excuse me, are you the imprint of a departed soul?'" There was a ripple of laughter, instantly quelled by the look Weasley gave the class.
"Another ten points from Weasley," said Weasley. "I would ex-pect nothing more sophisticated from you, Ronald Weasley, the boy so solid he cannot Apparate half an inch across a room."
"No!" whispered Ron, grabbing Rons arm as he opened his mouth furiously. "There's no point, you'll just end up in deten-tion again, leave it!"
"Now open your books to page two hundred and thirteen," said Weasley, smirking a little, "and read the first two paragraphs on the Cruciatus Curse."
Ron was very subdued all through the class. When the bell sounded at the end of the lesson, Ron caught up with Ron and Ron (Ron mysteriously melted out of sight as she ap-proached) and abused Weasley hotly for his jibe about Ron's Appari-tion, but this seemed to merely irritate Ron, and he shook her off by making a detour into the boys' bathroom with Ron.
"Weasley's right, though, isn't he?" said Ron, after staring into a cracked mirror for a minute or two. "I dunno whether it's worth me taking the test. I just can't get the hang of Apparition."
"You might as well do the extra practice sessions in Hogsmeade and see where they get you," said Ron reasonably. "It'll be more interesting than trying to get into a stupid hoop anyway. Then, if you're still not -- you know -- as good as you'd like to be, you can postpone the test, do it with me over the summer -- Ron, this is the boys' bathroom!"
The ghost of a girl had risen out of the toilet in a cubicle behind them and was now floating in midair, staring at them through thick, white, round glasses. "Oh," she said glumly. "It's you two."
"Who were you expecting?" said Ron, looking at her in the mirror.
"Nobody," said Ron, picking moodily at a spot on her chin. "He said he'd come back and see me, but then you said you'd pop in and visit me too" -- she gave Ron a reproachful look -- "and I haven't seen you for months and months. I've learned not to ex-pect too much from boys."
"I thought you lived in that girls' bathroom?" said Ron, who had been careful to give the place a wide berth for some years now.
"I do," she said, with a sulky little shrug, "but that doesn't mean I cant visit other places. I came and saw you in your bath once, remember?"
"Vividly," said Ron.
"But I thought he liked me," she said plaintively. "Maybe if you two left, he'd come back again. We had lots in common. I'm sure he felt it."
And she looked hopefully toward the door. "When you say you had lots in common," said Ron, sounding rather amused now, "d'you mean he lives in an S-bend too?"
"No," said Ron defiantly, her voice echoing loudly around the old tiled bathroom. "I mean he's sensitive, people bully him too, and he feels lonely and hasn't got anybody to talk to, and he's not afraid to show his feelings and cry!"
"There's been a boy in here crying?" said Ron curiously. "A young boy?"
"Never you mind!" said Ron, her small, leaky eyes fixed on Ron, who was now definitely grinning. "I promised I wouldn't tell anyone, and I'll take his secret to the --"
"-- not the grave, surely?" said Ron with a snort. "The sewers, maybe." Ron gave a howl of rage and dived back into the toilet, caus-ing water to slop over the sides and onto the floor. Goading Ron seemed to have put fresh heart into Ron. "You're right," he said, swinging his schoolbag back over his shoulder, "I'll do the practice sessions in Hogsmeade before I de-cide about taking the test."
And so the following weekend, Ron joined Ron and the rest of the sixth years who would turn seventeen in time to take the test in a fortnight. Ron felt rather jealous watching them all get ready to go into the village; he missed making trips there, and it was a particularly fine spring day, one of the first clear skies they had seen in a long time. However, he had decided to use the time to attempt another assault on the Room of Requirement.
"You'd do better," said Ron, when he confided this plan to Ron and her in the entrance hall, "to go straight to Weasley's of-fice and try and get that memory from him."
"I've been trying!" said Ron crossly, which was perfectly true. He had lagged behind after every Potions lesson that week in an at-tempt to corner Weasley, but the Potions master always left the dungeon so fast that Ron had not been able to catch him. Twice, Ron had gone to his office and knocked, but received no reply, though on the second occasion he was sure he had heard the quickly stifled sounds of an old gramophone.
"He doesn't want to talk to me, Ron! He can tell I've been trying to get him on his own again, and he's not going to let it happen!"
"Well, you've just got to keep at it, haven't you?"
The short queue of people waiting to file past Weasley, who was do-ing his usual prodding act with the Secrecy Sensor, moved forward a few steps and Ron did not answer in case he was overheard by the caretaker. He wished
Ron and Ron both luck, then turned and climbed the marble staircase again, determined, whatever Her-mione said, to devote an hour or two to the Room of Requirement.
Once out of sight of the entrance hall, Ron pulled the Ma-rauder's Map and his Invisibility Cloak from his bag. Having concealed himself, he tapped the map, murmured, "I solemnly swear that I am up to no good," and scanned it carefully.
As it was Sunday morning, nearly all the students were inside their various common rooms, the Weasleys in one tower, the Weasleys in another, the Weasleys in the dungeons, and the Weasleys in the basement near the kitchens. Here and there a stray person meandered around the library or up a corridor. There were a few people out in the grounds, and there, alone in the seventh-floor corridor, was Ron Weasley. There was no sign of the Room of Requirement, but Ron was not worried about that; if Weasley was standing guard outside it, the room was open, whether the map was aware of it or not. He therefore sprinted up the stairs, slowing down only when he reached the corner into the corridor, when he began to creep, very slowly, toward the very same little girl, clutching her heavy brass scales, that Ron had so kindly helped a fortnight before. He waited until he was right be-hind her before bending very low and whispering, "Hello...you're very pretty, aren't you?"
Weasley gave a high-pitched scream of terror, threw the scales up into the air, and sprinted away, vanishing from sight long before the sound of the scales smashing had stopped echoing around the corri-dor. Laughing, Ron
turned to contemplate the blank wall behind which, he was sure, Ron Weasley was now standing frozen, aware that someone unwelcome was out there, but not daring to make an appearance. It gave Ron a most agreeable feeling of power as he tried to remember what form of words he had not yet tried.
Yet this hopeful mood did not last long. Half an hour later, hav-ing tried many more variations of his request to see what Weasley was up to, the wall was just as doorless as ever. Ron felt frustrated beyond belief-=Weasley might be just feet away from him, and there was still not the tiniest shred of evidence as to what he was doing in there. Losing his patience completely, Ron ran at the wall and kicked it.
"OUCH!"
He thought he might have broken his toe; as he clutched it and hopped on one foot, the Invisibility Cloak slipped off him.
"Ron?"
He spun around, one-legged, and toppled over. There, to his utter astonishment, was Weasley, walking toward him as though she frequently strolled up this corridor.
"What’re you doing here?" he said, scrambling to his feet again; why did she always have to find him lying on the floor?
"I came to see Weasley," said Weasley. Ron thought she looked terrible: thinner than usual, her mouse-colored hair lank.
"His office isn't here," said Ron, "it's round the other side of the castle, behind the gargoyle --"
"I know," said Weasley. "He's not there. Apparently he's gone away again."
"Has he?" said Ron, putting his bruised foot gingerly back on the floor. "Hey -- you don't know where he goes, I suppose?"
"No," said Weasley.
"What did you want to see him about?"
"Nothing in particular," said Weasley, picking, apparently uncon-sciously, at the sleeve of her robe. "I just thought he might know what's going on. I've heard rumors... people getting hurt."
"Yeah, I know, it's all been in the papers," said Ron. "That lit-tle kid trying to kill his --"
"The Prophet's often behind the times," said Weasley, who didn't seem to be listening to him. "You haven't had any letters from any-one in the Order recently?"
"No one from the Order writes to me anymore," said Ron, "not since Ron --" He saw that her eyes had filled with tears.
"I'm sorry," he muttered awkwardly. "I mean... I miss him, as well."
"What?" said Weasley blankly, as though she had not heard him. "Well. I'll see you around, Ron."
And she turned abruptly and walked back down the corridor, leaving Ron to stare after her. After a minute or so, he pulled the Invisibility Cloak on again and resumed his efforts to get into the Room of Requirement, but his heart was not in it. Finally, a hollow feeling in his stomach and the knowledge that Ron and Ron would soon be back for lunch made him abandon the attempt and leave the corridor to Weasley who, hopefully, would be too afraid to leave for some hours to come.
He found Ron and Ron in the Great Hall, already halfway through an early lunch.
"I did it -- well, kind of!" Ron told Ron enthusiastically when he caught sight of him. "I was supposed to be Apparating to out-side Ron Puddifoots Tea Shop and I overshot it a bit, ended up near Scrivenshafts, but at least I moved!"
"Good one," said Ron. "How'd you do, Ron?"
"Oh, she was perfect, obviously," said Ron, before Ron could answer. "Perfect deliberation, divination, and desperation or whatever the hell it is -- we all went for a quick drink in the Three Broomsticks after and you should've heard Weasley going on about her -- I'll be surprised if he doesn't pop the question soon --"
"And what about you?" asked Ron, ignoring Ron. "Have you been up at the Room of Requirement all this time?"
"Yep," said Ron. "And guess who I ran into up there? Weasley!"
"Weasley?" repeated Ron and Ron together, looking surprised.
"Yeah, she said she'd come to visit Weasley."
"If you ask me," said Ron once Ron had finished describing his conversation with Weasley, "she's cracking up a bit. Losing her nerve after what happened at the Ministry."
"It’s a bit odd," said Ron, who for some reason looked very concerned. "She's supposed to be guarding the school, why she suddenly abandoning her post to come and see Weasley when he's not even here?"
"I had a thought," said Ron tentatively. He felt strange about voicing it; this was much more Ron’s territory than his. "You don't think she can have been... you know... in love with Ron?"
Ron stared at him. "What on earth makes you say that?"
"I dunno," said Ron, shrugging, "but she was nearly crying when I mentioned his name, and her Patronus is a big four-legged thing now. I wondered whether it hadn't become... you know... him."
"It's a thought," said Ron slowly. "But I still don't know why she'd be bursting into the castle to see Weasley, if that's re-ally why she was here."
"Goes back to what I said, doesn't it?" said Ron, who was now shoveling mashed potato into his mouth. "She's gone a bit funny. Lost her nerve. Women," he said wisely to Ron, "they're easily upset."
"And yet," said Ron, coming out of her reverie, "I doubt you'd find a woman who sulked for half an hour because Ron Ron didn't laugh at their joke about the hag, the Healer, and the Mimbulus mimbletonia."
Ron scowled.
Chapter 22: After the Burial
Patches of bright blue sky were beginning to appear over the castle turrets, but these signs of approaching summer did not lift Ron's mood. He had been thwarted, both in his attempts to find out what Weasley was doing, and in his efforts to start a conversation with Weasley that might lead, somehow, to Weasley hand-ing over the memory he had apparently suppressed for decades.
"For the last time, just forget about Weasley," Ron told Ron firmly.
They were sitting with Ron in a sunny corner of the courtyard after lunch. Ron and Ron were both clutching a Ministry of Magic leaflet -- Common Apparition Mistakes and How to Avoid Them -- for they were taking their tests that very afternoon, but by and large the leaflets had not proved soothing to the nerves.
Ron gave a start and tried to hide behind Ron as a girl came around the corner.
"It isn't Ron," said Ron wearily.
"Oh, good," said Ron, relaxing.
"Ron Weasley?" said the girl. "I was asked to give you this."
"Thanks..."
Ron's heart sank as he took the small scroll of parchment. Once the girl was out of earshot he said, "Weasley said we wouldn't be having any more lessons until I got the memory!"
"Maybe he wants to check on how you're doing?" suggested Ron, as Ron unrolled the parchment; but rather than finding Weasley's long, narrow, slanted writing he saw an untidy sprawl, very difficult to read due to the presence of large blotches on the parchment where the ink had run.
Dear Ron, Ron and Ron!
Ron died last night. Ron and Ron, you met him and you know how special he was.
Ron, I know you'd have liked him.
It would mean a lot to me if you'd nip down for the burial later this evening.
I'm planning on doing it round dusk, that was his favorite time of day.
I know you're not supposed to be out that late, but you can use the cloak.
Wouldn't ask, but I can't face it alone.
Weasley
"Look at this," said Ron, handing the note to Ron. "Oh, for heaven's sake," she said, scanning it quickly and passing it to Ron, who read it through looking increasingly incredulous. "He's mental" he said furiously. "That thing told its mates to eat Ron and me! Told them to help themselves! And now Weasley ex-pects us to go down there and cry over its horrible hairy body!"
"Its not just that," said Ron. "He's asking us to leave the castle at night and he knows security's a million times tighter and how much trouble we'd be in if we were caught."
"We've been down to see him by night before," said Ron.
"Yes, but for something like this?" said Ron. "We've risked a lot to help Weasley out, but after all -- Ron's dead. If it were a question of saving him --"
"-- I'd want to go even less," said Ron firmly. "You didn't meet him, Ron. Believe me, being dead will have improved him a lot."
Ron took the note back and stared down at all the inky blotches all over it. Tears had clearly fallen thick and fast upon the parchment. . . .
"Ron, you can't be thinking of going," said Ron. "It's such a pointless thing to get detention for."
Ron sighed. "Yeah, I know," he said. "I s'pose Weasley'll have to bury Ron without us."
"Yes, he will," said Ron, looking relieved. "Look, Potions will be almost empty this afternoon, with us all off doing our tests. . . . Try and soften Weasley up a bit then!"
"Fifty-seventh time lucky, you think?" said Ron bitterly.
"Lucky," said Ron suddenly. "Ron, that's it -- get lucky!"
"What d'you mean?"
"Use your lucky potion!"
"Ron, that's -- that's it!" said Ron, sounding stunned. "Of course! Why didn't I think of it?"
Ron stared at them both. "Felix Felicis?" he said. "I dunno . . . I was sort of saving it. ..."
"What for?" demanded Ron incredulously.
"What on earth is more important than this memory, Ron?" asked Ron.
Ron did not answer. The thought of that little golden bottle had hovered on the edges of his imagination for some time; vague and unformulated plans that involved Ron splitting up with Ron, and Ron somehow being happy to see her with a new boyfriend, had been fermenting in the depths of his brain, unacknowledged except during dreams or the twilight time between sleeping and waking. . . .
"Ron? Are you still with us?" asked Ron.
"Wha -- ? Yeah, of course," he said, pulling himself together. "Well. . . okay. If I can't get Weasley to talk this afternoon, I'll take some Felix and have another go this evening."
"That's decided, then," said Ron briskly, getting to her feet and performing a graceful pirouette. "Destination . . . determina-tion . . . deliberation . . ." she murmured.
"Oh, stop that," Ron begged her, "I feel sick enough as it is -- quick, hide me!"
"It isn't Ron!" said Ron impatiently, as another cou-ple of girls appeared in the courtyard and Ron dived behind her.
"Cool," said Ron, peering over Rons shoulder to check. "Blimey, they don't look happy, do they?"
"They're the Montgomery sisters and of course they don't look happy, didn't you hear what happened to their little brother?" said Ron.
"I'm losing track of what's happening to everyone's relatives, to be honest," said Ron.
"Well, their brother was attacked by a werewolf. The rumor is that their mother refused to help the Death Eaters. Anyway, the boy was only five and he died in St. Mungos, they couldn't save him."
"He died?" repeated Ron, shocked. "But surely werewolves don't kill, they just turn you into one of them?"
"They sometimes kill," said Ron, who looked unusually grave now. "I've heard of it happening when the werewolf gets carried away."
"What was the werewolf's name?" said Ron quickly.
"Well, the rumor is that it was that Ron Weasley," said Ron.
"I knew it -- the maniac who likes attacking kids, the one Weasley told me about!" said Ron angrily.
Ron looked at him bleakly.
"Ron, you've got to get that memory," she said. "It's all about stopping Weasley, isn't it? These dreadful things that are hap-pening are all down to him. . . ."
The bell rang overhead in the castle and both Ron and Ron jumped to their feet, looking terrified.
"You'll do fine," Ron told them both, as they headed toward the entrance hall to meet the rest of the people taking their Ap-parition Test. "Good luck."
"And you too!" said Ron with a significant look, as Ron headed off to the dungeons.
There were only three of them in Potions that afternoon: Ron, Ron, and Ron Weasley.
"All too young to Apparate just yet?" said Slughorh genially, "Not turned seventeen yet?"
They shook their heads.
"Ah well," said Weasley cheerily, "as we're so few, we'll do something for fun. I want you all to brew me up something amusing!"
"That sounds good, sir," said Ron sycophantically, rubbing his hands together. Weasley, on the other hand, did not crack a smile. "What do you mean, 'something amusing'?" he said irritably. "Oh, surprise me," said Weasley airily.
Weasley opened his copy of Advanced Potion-Making with a sulky expression. It could not have been plainer that he thought this les-son was a waste of time. Undoubtedly, Ron thought, watching him over the top of his own book, Weasley was begrudging the time he could otherwise be spending in the Room of Requirement.
Was it his imagination, or did Weasley, like Weasley, look thinner! Certainly he looked paler; his skin still had that grayish tinge, probably because he so rarely saw daylight these days. But there was no air of smugness, excitement, or superiority; none of the swagger that he had had on the
Hogwarts Express, when he had boasted openly of the mission he had been given by Weasley. . . . There could be only one conclusion, in Ron's opinion: The mission, whatever it was, was going badly.
Cheered by this thought, Ron skimmed through his copy of Advanced Potion-Making and found a heavily corrected Half-Blood Prince's version of "An Elixir to Induce Euphoria," which seemed not only to meet Weasley's instructions, but which might (Ron's heart leapt as the thought struck him) put Weasley into such a good mood that he would be prepared to hand over that memory if Ron could persuade him to taste some. . . .
"Well, now, this looks absolutely wonderful," said Weasley an hour and a half later, clapping his hands together as he stared down into the sunshine yellow contents of Ron's cauldron. "Euphoria, I take it? And what's that I smell? Mmmm . . . you've added just a sprig of peppermint, haven't you? Unorthodox, but what a stroke of inspiration, Ron, of course, that would tend to counterbalance the occa-sional side effects of excessive singing and nose-tweaking. ... I really don't know where you get these brain waves, my boy . . . unless --"
Ron pushed the Half-Blood Prince's book deeper into his bag with his foot.
"-- it's just your mother's genes coming out in you!"
"Oh . . . yeah, maybe," said Ron, relieved.
Ron was looking rather grumpy; determined to outshine Ron for once, he had most rashly invented his own potion, which had curdled and formed a kind of purple dumpling at the bottom of his cauldron. Weasley was already packing up, sour-faced; Weasley had pronounced his Hiccuping Solution merely "passable."
The bell rang and both Ron and Weasley left at once. "Sir," Ron began, but Weasley immediately glanced over his shoulder; when he saw that the room was empty but for himself and Ron, he hurried away as fast as he could.
"Professor -- Professor, don't you want to taste my po -- ?" called Ron desperately.
But Weasley had gone. Disappointed, Ron emptied the caul-dron, packed up his things, left the dungeon, and walked slowly back upstairs to the common room.
Ron and Ron returned in the late afternoon.
"Ron!" cried Ron as she climbed through the portrait hole. "Ron, I passed!"
"Well done!" he said. "And Ron?"
"He -- he just failed," whispered Ron, as Ron came slouching into the room looking most morose. "It was really unlucky, a tiny thing, the
examiner just spotted that he'd left half an eyebrow behind. . . How did it go with Weasley?"
"No joy," said Ron, as Ron joined them. "Bad luck, mate, but you'll pass next time -- we can take it together."
"Yeah, I s'pose," said Ron grumpily. "But half an eyebrow – like that matters!"
"I know," said Ron soothingly, "it does seem really harsh. ..."
They spent most of their dinner roundly abusing the Apparition examiner, and Ron looked fractionally more cheerful by the time they set off back to the common room, now discussing the continuing problem of Weasley and the memory.
"So, Ron -- you going to use the Felix Felicis or what?" Ron demanded.
"Yeah, I s'pose I'd better," said Ron. "I don't reckon I'll need all of it, not twenty-four hours' worth, it can't take all night.... I'll just take a mouthful. Two or three hours should do it."
"It's a great feeling when you take it," said Ron reminiscently. "Like you can't do anything wrong."
"What are you talking about?" said Ron, laughing. "You've never taken any!"
"Yeah, but I thought I had, didn't I?" said Ron, as though ex-plaining the obvious. "Same difference really ..."
As they had only just seen Weasley enter the Great Hall and knew that he liked to take time over meals, they lingered for a while in the common room, the plan being that Ron should go to Weasley s office once the teacher had had time to get back there. When the sun had sunk to the level of the treetops in the Forbid-den Forest, they decided the moment had come, and after check-ing carefully that Ron, Ron, and Ron were all in the common room, sneaked up to the boys' dormitory.
Ron took out the rolled-up socks at the bottom of his trunk and extracted the tiny, gleaming bottle.
"Well, here goes," said Ron, and he raised the little bottle and look a carefully measured gulp.
"What does it feel like?" whispered Ron.
Ron did not answer for a moment. Then, slowly but surely, an exhilarating sense of infinite opportunity stole through him; he felt as though he could have done anything, anything at all... and getting the memory from Weasley seemed suddenly not only pos-sible, but positively easy. . . .
He got to his feet, smiling, brimming with confidence.
"Excellent," he said. "Really excellent. Right. . . I'm going down to Weasley's."
"What?" said Ron and Ron together, looking aghast.
"No, Ron -- you've got to go and see Weasley, remember?" said Ron.
"No," said Ron confidently. "I'm going to Weasley's, I've got a good feeling about going to Weasley's."
"You've got a good feeling about burying a giant spider?" asked Ron, looking stunned.
"Yeah," said Ron, pulling his Invisibility Cloak out of his bag. "I feel like it's the place to be tonight, you know what I mean?"
"No," said Ron and Ron together, both looking positively alarmed now.
"This is Felix Felicis, I suppose?" said Ron anxiously, holding up the bottle to the light. "You haven't got another little bottle full of-- I don't know --"
"Essence of Insanity?" suggested Ron, as Ron swung his cloak over his shoulders.
Ron laughed, and Ron and Ron looked even more alarmed.
"Trust me," he said. "I know what I'm doing ... or at least" he strolled confidently to the door-- "Felix does."
He pulled the Invisibility Cloak over his head and set off down the stairs, Ron and Ron hurrying along behind him. At the foot of the stairs, Ron slid through the open door.
"What were you doing up there with her!" shrieked Ron Weasley, staring right through Ron at Ron and Ron emerging together from the boys' dormitories. Ron heard Ron splutter-ing behind him as he darted across the room away from them.
Getting through the portrait hole was simple; as he approached it, Ron and Ron came through it, and Ron was able to slip between them. As he did so, he brushed accidentally against Ron.
"Don't push me, please, Ron," she said, sounding annoyed. ; "You're always doing that, I can get through perfectly well on my own. ..."
The portrait swung closed behind Ron, but not before he had heard Ron make an angry retort.. . . His feeling of elation in-creasing, Ron strode off through the castle. He did not have to creep along, for he met nobody on his way, but this did not surprise him in the slightest. This evening, he was the luckiest person at Hogwarts.
Why he knew that going to Weasley's was the right thing to do, he had no idea. It was as though the potion was illuminating a few steps of the path at a time. He could not see the final destination, he could not see where Weasley came in, but he knew that he was going the right way to get that memory. When he reached the en-trance hall he saw that Weasley had forgotten to lock the front door. Beaming, Ron threw it open and breathed in the smell of clean air and grass for a moment before walking down the steps into the dusk.
It was when he reached the bottom step that it occurred to him how very pleasant it would be to pass the vegetable patch on his walk to Weasley's. It was not strictly on the way, but it seemed clear to Ron that this was a whim on which he should act, so he di-rected his feet immediately toward the vegetable patch, where he was pleased, but not altogether surprised, to find Professor Weasley in conversation with Professor Weasley. Ron lurked be-hind a low stone wall, feeling at peace with the world and listening to their conversation.
"I do thank you for taking the time, Ron," Weasley was saying courteously, "most authorities agree that they are at their most efficacious if picked at twilight."
"Oh, I quite agree," said Professor Weasley warmly. "That enough for you?"
"Plenty, plenty," said Weasley, who, Ron saw, was carrying an armful of leafy plants. "This should allow for a few leaves for each of my third
years, and some to spare if anybody over-stews them. . . . Well, good evening to you, and many thanks again!"
Professor Weasley headed off into the gathering darkness in the direction of her greenhouses, and Weasley directed his steps to the spot where Ron stood, invisible.
Seized with an immediate desire to reveal himself, Ron pullet I off the cloak with a flourish.
"Good evening, Professor."
"Merlin’s beard, Ron, you made me jump," said Slughotn, stopping dead in his tracks and looking wary. "How did you get out of the castle?"
"I think Weasley must've forgotten to lock the doors," said Ron cheerfully, and was delighted to see Weasley scowl.
"I'll be reporting that man, he's more concerned about litter than proper security if you ask me. . . . But why are you out then, Ron?"
"Well, sir, it's Weasley," said Ron, who knew that the right thing to do just now was to tell the truth. "He's pretty upset. . . But you won't tell anyone, Professor? I don't want trouble for him. ..."
Weasley's curiosity was evidently aroused. "Well, I can't promise that," he said gruffly. "But I know that Weasley trusts Weasley to the hilt, so I'm sure he can't be up to anything very dreadful. .."
"Well, it's this giant spider, he's had it for years. ... It lived in the forest. ... It could talk and everything --"
"I heard rumors there were acromantulas in the forest," said Weasley softly, looking over at the mass of black trees. "It's true, then?"
"Yes," said Ron. "But this one, Ron, the first one Weasley ever got, it died last night. He's devastated. He wants company while he buries it and I said I'd go."
"Touching, touching," said Weasley absentmindedly, his large droopy eyes fixed upon the distant lights of Weasley's cabin. "But acromantula venom is very valuable ... If the beast only just died it might not yet have dried out. . . . Of course, I wouldn't want to do anything insensitive if Weasley is upset. . . but if there was any way to procure some ... I mean, its almost impossible to get venom from an acromantula while its alive. ..."
Weasley seemed to be talking more to himself than Ron now. ". . . seems an awful waste not to collect it... might get a hun-dred Galleons a pint. ... To be frank, my salary is not large. . . ."
And now Ron saw clearly what was to be done. "Well," he said, with a most convincing hesitancy, "well, if you wanted to come, Professor, Weasley
would probably be really pleased. . . . Give Ron a better send-off, you know ..."
"Yes, of course," said Weasley, his eyes now gleaming with en-thusiasm. "I tell you what, Ron, I'll meet you down there with a bottle or two. . . . We'll drink the poor beast's -- well -- not health -- but we'll send it off in style, anyway, once it's buried. And I'll change my tie, this one is a little exuberant for the occa-sion. . . ."
He bustled back into the castle, and Ron sped off to Weasley's, delighted with himself.
"Yen came," croaked Weasley, when he opened the door and saw Ron emerging from the Invisibility Cloak in front of him.
"Yeah -- Ron and Ron couldn't, though," said Ron. "They're really sorry."
"Don -- don matter . . . Hed've bin touched yeh're here, though, Ron. . ."
Weasley gave a great sob. He had made himself a black armband out of what looked like a rag dipped in boot polish, and his eyes were puffy, red, and swollen. Ron patted him consolingly on the elbow, which was the highest point of Weasley he could easily reach.
"Where are we burying him?" he asked. "The forest?"
"Blimey, no," said Weasley, wiping his streaming eyes on the bot-tom of his shirt. "The other spiders won' let me anywhere near their webs now Ron's gone. Turns out it was only on his orders they didn' eat me! Can yeh believe that, Ron?"
The honest answer was "yes"; Ron recalled with painful ease the scene when he and Ron had come face-to-face with the aero-mantulas. They had been quite clear that Ron was the only thing that stopped them from eating Weasley.
"Never bin an area o' the forest I couldn' go before!" said Weasley, shaking his head. "It wasn' easy, gettin' Ron's body out o' there, I can tell yeh -- they usually eat their dead, see. . . . But I wanted ter give 'im a nice burial... a proper send-off. . ."
He broke into sobs again and Ron resumed the patting of his elbow, saying as he did so (for the potion seemed to indicate that it was the right thing to do), "Professor Weasley met me coming down here, Weasley."
"Not in trouble, are yeh?" said Weasley, looking up, alarmed. "Yeh shouldn’ be outta the castle in the evenin', I know it, it's my fault --"
"No, no, when he heard what I was doing he said he'd like to come and pay his last respects to Ron too," said Ron.
"He's gone to change into something more suitable, I think...and he said he'd bring some bottles so we can drink to Ron's mem-ory..."
"Did he?" said Weasley, looking both astonished and touched. "Tha's -- tha's righ' nice of him, that is, an' not turnin' yeh in ei-ther. I've never really had a lot ter do with Ron Weasley before. .. . Comin' ter see old Ron off, though, eh? Well. . . he’d've liked that, Ron would. . . ."
Ron thought privately that what Ron would have liked most about Weasley was the ample amount of edible flesh he pro-vided, but he merely moved to the rear window of Weasley's hut, where he saw the rather horrible sight of the enormous dead spider lying on its back outside, its legs curled and tangled.
"Are we going to bury him here, Weasley, in your garden?"
"Jus' beyond the pumpkin patch, I thought," said Weasley in a choked voice. "I've already dug the -- yeh know -- grave. Jus' thought we'd say a few nice things over him -- happy memories, yeh know --"
His voice quivered and broke. There was a knock on the door, and he turned to answer it, blowing his nose on his great spotted handkerchief as he did so. Weasley hurried over the threshold, several bottles in his arms, and wearing a somber black cravat.
"Weasley," he said, in a deep, grave voice. "So very sorry to hear of your loss."
"Tha's very nice of yeh," said Weasley. "Thanks a lot. An' thanks fer not givin Ron detention neither. . . ."
"Wouldn't have dreamed of it," said Weasley. "Sad night, sad night. . . Where is the poor creature?"
"Out here," said Weasley in a shaking voice. "Shall we -- shall we do it, then?"
The three of them stepped out into the back garden. The moon was glistening palely through the trees now, and its rays mingled with the light spilling from Weasley's window to illuminate Rons body lying on the edge of a massive pit beside a ten-foot- high mound of freshly dug earth.
"Magnificent," said Weasley, approaching the spiders head, where eight milky eyes stared blankly at the sky and two huge, curved pincers shone, motionless, in the moonlight. Ron thougln he heard the tinkle of bottles as Weasley bent over the pincers, apparently examining the enormous hairy head.
"Its not ev'ryone appreciates how beau'iful they are’ said H grid to Weasley's back, tears leaking from the corners of his crinkled eyes. "I didn' know yeh were interested in creatures like Ron, Ron."
"Interested? My dear Weasley, I revere them," said Weasley, stepping back from the body. Ron saw the glint of a bottle disap-pear beneath his cloak,
though Weasley, mopping his eyes once more, noticed nothing. "Now . . . shall we proceed to the burial?"
Weasley nodded and moved forward. He heaved the gigantic spi-der into his arms and, with an enormous grunt, rolled it into the dark pit. It hit the bottom with a rather horrible, crunchy thud. Weasley started to cry again.
"Of course, it's difficult for you, who knew him best," said Weasley, who like Ron could reach no higher than Weasley's el-bow, but patted it all the same. "Why don't I say a few words?"
He must have got a lot of good quality venom from Ron, Ron thought, for Weasley wore a satisfied smirk as he stepped up to the rim of the pit and said, in a slow, impressive voice, "Farewell, Ron, king of arachnids, whose long and faithful friendship those who knew you won't forget! Though your body will decay, your spirit lingers on in the quiet, web-spun places of your forest home.
