May your many-eyed descendants ever flourish and your human friends find solace for the loss they have sustained."
"Tha was . . . tha was . . . beau'iful!" howled Weasley, and he collapsed onto the compost heap, crying harder than ever.
"There, there," said Weasley, waving his wand so that the huge pile of earth rose up and then fell, with a muffled sort of crash, onto the dead spider, forming a smooth mound. "Lets get inside and have a drink. Get on his other side, Ron. . . . That's it. ... Up you come, Weasley . . . Well done ..."
They deposited Weasley in a chair at the table. Ron, who had been skulking in his basket during the burial, now came padding softly across to them and put his heavy head into Ron's lap as usual. Weasley uncorked one of the bottles of wine he had brought.
"I have had it all tested for poison," he assured Ron, pouring most of the first bottle into one of Weasley's bucket-sized mugs and handing it to Weasley. "Had a house-elf taste every bottle after what happened to your poor friend Rupert."
Ron saw, in his mind's eye, the expression on Ron's face if she ever heard about this abuse of houseelves, and decided never to mention it to her.
"One for Ron . . ." said Weasley, dividing a second bottle be-tween two mugs, ". . . and one for me. Well" -- he raised his mug high -- "to Ron."
"Ron," said Ron and Weasley together. Both Weasley and Weasley drank deeply. Ron, however, with the way ahead illuminated for him by Felix Felicis, knew that he must not drink, so he merely pretended to take a gulp and then set the mug back on the table before him.
"I had him from an egg, yeh know," said Weasley morosely. "'Tiny little thing he was when he hatched. 'Bout the size of a Pekingese"
"Sweet," said Weasley.
"Used ter keep him in a cupboard up at the school until . . . well..."
Weasley's face darkened and Ron knew why: Ron Weasley had contrived to have Weasley thrown out of school, blamed for opening the Chamber of Secrets. Weasley, however, did not seem to be listening; he was looking up at the ceiling, from which a number of brass pots hung, and also a long, silky skein of bright white hair.
"That's not unicorn hair, Weasley?"
"Oh, yeah," said Weasley indifferently. "Gets pulled out of their tails, they catch it on branches an' stuff in the forest, yeh know ..."
"But my dear chap, do you know how much that's worth?"
"I use it fer bindin' on bandages an stuff if a creature gets in jured," said Weasley, shrugging. "It's dead useful. . . very strong."
Weasley took another deep draught from his mug, his eyes moving carefully around the cabin now, looking, Ron knew, for more treasures that he might be able to convert into a plentiful su ply of oak-matured mead, crystalized pineapple, and velvet smok-ing jackets. He refilled Weasley's mug and his own, and questioned him about the creatures that lived in the forest these days and how Weasley was able to look after them all. Weasley, becoming expan-sive under the influence of the drink and Weasley's
flattering in-terest, stopped mopping his eyes and entered happily into a long explanation of bowtruckle husbandry.
The Felix Felicis gave Ron a little nudge at this point, and he noticed that the supply of drink that Weasley had brought was running out fast. Ron had not yet managed to bring off the Re-filling Charm without saying the incantation aloud, but the idea that he might not be able to do it tonight was laughable: Indeed, Ron grinned to himself as, unnoticed by either Weasley or Slug-liorn (now swapping tales of the illegal trade in dragon eggs) he pointed his wand under the table at the emptying bottles and they immediately began to refill.
After an hour or so, Weasley and Weasley began making extravagant toasts: to Hogwarts, to Weasley, to elf-made wine, and to-
"Ron Weasley!" bellowed Weasley, slopping some of his four-teenth bucket of wine down his chin as he drained it.
"Yes, indeed," cried Weasley a little thickly, "Parry Otter, the Ronsen Boy Who -- well -- something of that sort," he mumbled, and drained his mug too.
Not long after this, Weasley became tearful again and pressed the whole unicorn tail upon Weasley, who pocketed it with cries of, "To friendship! To generosity! To ten Galleons a hair!"
And for a while after that, Weasley and Weasley were sitting side by side, arms around each other, singing a slow sad song about a dying wizard called Odo.
"Aaargh, the good die young," muttered Weasley, slumping low onto the table, a little cross-eyed, while Weasley continued to war-ble the refrain. "Me dad was no age ter go ... nor were yer mum' an' dad, Ron . . ."
Great fat tears oozed out of the corners of Weasley's crinkled eyes again; he grasped Ron's arm and shook it
"Bes' wiz and witchard o' their age ... I never knew.. . terrible thing . . . terrible thing ..."
"And Odo the hero, they bore him back home
To the place that he'd known as a lad,"
sang Weasley plaintively.
"They laid him to rest with his hat inside out.
And his wand snapped in two, which was sad."
". . . terrible," Weasley grunted, and his great shaggy head rolled sideways onto his arms and he fell asleep, snoring deeply.
"Sorry," said Weasley with a hiccup. "Can't carry a tune to save my life."
"Weasley wasn't talking about your singing," said Ron quietly. "He was talking about my mum and dad dying."
"Oh," said Weasley, repressing a large belch. "Oh dear. Yes, that was -- was terrible indeed. Terrible . . . terrible ..."
He looked quite at a loss for what to say, and resorted to refilling their mugs.
"I don't -- don't suppose you remember it, Ron?" he asked awkwardly.
"No -- well, I was only one when they died," said Ron, his eyes on the flame of the candle flickering in Weasley's heavy snores. "But I've found out pretty much what happened since. My dad died first. Did you know that?"
"I -- I didn't," said Weasley in a hushed voice.
"Yeah . . . Weasley murdered him and then stepped over his body toward my mum," said Ron.
Weasley gave a great shudder, but he did not seem able to tear his horrified gaze away from Ron's face.
"He told her to get out of the way," said Ron remorselessly. "He told me she needn't have died. He only wanted me. She could have run."
"Oh dear," breathed Weasley. "She could have . . . she needn't . . . That's awful. . . ."
"It is, isn't it?" said Ron, in a voice barely more than a whisper. "But she didn't move. Dad was already dead, but she didn't want me to go too. She tried to plead with Weasley. . . but he just laughed...."
"That's enough!" said Weasley suddenly, raising a shaking hand. "Really, my dear boy, enough . . . I'm an old man ... I don't need to hear ... I don't want to hear ..."
"I forgot," lied Ron, Felix Felicis leading him on. "You liked her, didn't you?"
"Liked her?" said Weasley, his eyes brimming with tears once more. "I don't imagine anyone who met her wouldn't have liked her. . . . Very brave . . . Very funny... It was the most horrible thing. ..."
"But you won't help her son," said Ron. "She gave me her life, but you won't give me a memory."
Weasley's rumbling snores filled the cabin. Ron looked steadily into Weasley's tear-filled eyes. The Potions master seemed unable to look away.
"Don't say that," he whispered. "It isn't a question ... If it were to help you, of course . . . but no purpose can be served . . ."
"It can," said Ron clearly. "Weasley needs information. I need information."
He knew he was safe: Felix was telling him that Weasley would remember nothing of this in the morning. Looking Weasley straight in the eye, Ron leaned forward a little.
"I am the Ronsen One. I have to kill him. I need that memory."
Weasley turned paler than ever; his shiny forehead gleamed with sweat.
"You are the Ronsen One?" . . I.
"Of course I am," said Ron calmly.
"But then . . . my dear boy . . . you're asking a great deal. . . you're asking me, in fact, to aid you in your attempt to destroy-"
"You don't want to get rid of the wizard who killed Ron Weasley?'"
"Ron, Ron, of course I do, but --"
"You're scared he'll find out you helped me?"
Weasley said nothing; he looked terrified.
"Be brave like my mother, Professor. . . ."
Weasley raised a pudgy hand and pressed his shaking fingers to his mouth; he looked for a moment like an enormously overgrown baby.
"I am not proud . . ." he whispered through his fingers. "I am ashamed of what -- of what that memory shows. ... I think I may have done great damage that day. ..."
"You'd cancel out anything you did by giving me the memory," said Ron. "It would be a very brave and noble thing to do."
Weasley twitched in his sleep and snored on. Weasley and Ron stared at each other over the guttering candle. There was a long, long silence, but Felix Felicis told Ron not to break it, to wait. Then, very slowly, Weasley put his hand in his pocket and pulled out his wand. He put his other hand inside his cloak and took out a small, empty bottle. Still looking into Ron's eyes, Weasley touched the tip of his wand to his temple and withdrew it, so that a long, silver thread of memory came away too, clinging to the wand tip. Longer and longer the memory stretched until it broke and swung, silvery bright, from the wand. Weasley lowered it into the bottle where it coiled, then spread, swirling like gas. He corked the bottle with a trembling hand and then passed it across the table to Ron.
"Thank you very much, Professor."
"You're a good boy," said Professor Weasley, tears trickling down his fat cheeks into his walrus mustache. "And you've got her eyes. . . . Just don't think too badly of me once you've seen it. . . ,"
And he too put his head on his arms, gave a deep sigh, and fell asleep.
Chapter 23: Horcruxes
Ron could feel the Felix Felicis wearing off as he creeped back into the castle. The front door had remained un locked for him, but on the third floor he met Ron and only narrowly avoided detection by diving sideways through one of his shortcuts. By the time he got up to the portrait of the Ron Weasley and pulled off his Invisibility Cloak, he was not surprised to find her in a most unhelpful mood.
"What sort of time do you call this?"
"I'm really sorry -- I had to go out for something important --"
"Well, the password changed at midnight, so you'll just have to sleep in the corridor, won't you?"
"You're joking!" said Ron. "Why did it have to change at midnight?"
"That's the way it is," said the Ron Weasley. "If you're angry, go and take it up with the headmaster, he's the one who's tightened security."
"Fantastic," said Ron bitterly, looking around at the hard floor. "Really brilliant. Yeah, I would go and take it up with Weasley if he was here, because he's the one who wanted me to --"
"He is here," said a voice behind Ron. "Professor Weasley returned to the school an hour ago."
Nearly Headless Ron was gliding toward Ron, his head wob-bling as usual upon his ruff.
"I had it from the Ron Weasley, who saw him arrive," said Ron. "He appeared, according to the Weasley, to be in good spirits, though a little tired, of course."
"Where is he?" said Ron, his heart leaping,"
"Oh, groaning and clanking up on the Astronomy Tower, it's a, favorite pastime of his --"
"Not the Ron Weasley -- Weasley!"
"Oh -- in his office," said Ron. "I believe, from what the Weasley said, that he had business to attend to before turning in --"
"Yeah, he has," said Ron, excitement blazing in his chest at the prospect of telling Weasley he had secured the memory. He wheeled about and sprinted off again, ignoring the Ron Weasley who was calling after him.
"Come back! All right, I lied! I was annoyed you woke me up! The password's still 'tapeworm'!"
But Ron was already hurtling back along the corridor and within minutes, he was saying "toffee eclairs" to Weasley's gar-goyle, which leapt aside, permitting Ron entrance onto the spiral staircase.
"Enter," said Weasley when Ron knocked. He sounded exhausted. Ron pushed open the door. There was Weasley's office, looking the same as ever, but with black, star-strewn skies beyond the windows.
"Good gracious, Ron," said Weasley in surprise. "To what do I owe this very late pleasure?"
"Sir -- I've got it. I’ve got the memory from Weasley."
Ron pulled out the tiny glass bottle and showed it to Weasley. For a moment or two, the headmaster looked stunned. Then his face split in a wide smile.
"Ron, this is spectacular news! Very well done indeed! I knew you could do it!"
All thought of the lateness of the hour apparently forgotten, he hurried around his desk, took the bottle with Weasley's memory in his uninjured hand, and strode over to the cabinet where he kepi the Pensieve.
"And now," said Weasley, placing the stone basin upon the desk and emptying the contents of the bottle into it. "Now, at last. we shall see. Ron, quickly . . ."
Ron bowed obediently over the Pensieve and felt his feet leave the office floor. . . . Once again he fell through darkness and landed in Ron Weasley's office many years before. There was the much younger Weasley, with his thick, shiny, straw-colored hair and his gingery-blond mustache, sitting again in the comfortable winged armchair in his office, his feet resting upon a velvet pouffe, a small glass of wine in one hand, the other rummaging in a box of crystallized pineapple. And there were the half dozen teenage boys sitting around Weasley with Ron Weasley in the midst of them, Ron's gold-and-black ring gleaming on his finger.
Weasley landed beside Ron just as Weasley asked, "Sir is it true that Professor Merrythought is retiring?"
"Ron, Ron, if I knew I couldn't tell you," said Weasley, wag-ging his finger reprovingly at Weasley, though winking at the same time. "I must say, I'd like to know where you get your information, boy, more knowledgeable than half the staff, you are."
Weasley smiled; the other boys laughed and cast him admiring looks.
"What with your uncanny ability to know things you shouldn't, and your careful flattery of the people who matter -- thank you for the pineapple, by the way, you're quite right, it is my favorite --" Several of the boys tittered again. "-- I confidently expect you to rise to Minister of Magic within twenty years. Fifteen, if you keep sending me pineapple, I have ex-cellent contacts at the Ministry."
Ron Weasley merely smiled as the others laughed again. Ron noticed that he was by no means the eldest of the group of boys, but that they all seemed to look to him as their leader.
"I don't know that politics would suit me, sir," he said when the laughter had died away. "I don't have the right kind of background, for one thing."
A couple of the boys around him smirked at each other. Ron was sure they were enjoying a private joke, undoubtedly about what they knew, or suspected, regarding their gang leader's famous ancestor.
"Nonsense," said Weasley briskly, "couldn't be plainer you come from decent Wizarding stock, abilities like yours. No, you'll go far, Ron, I've never been wrong about a student yet."
The small golden clock standing upon Weasley's desk chimed eleven o'clock behind him and he looked around.
"Good gracious, is it that time already? You'd better get going boys, or we'll all be in trouble. Weasley, I want your essay by in morrow or it's detention. Same goes for you, Avery."
One by one, the boys filed out of the room. Weasley heaved himself out of his armchair and carried his empty glass over to his desk. A movement behind him made him look around; Weasley was still standing there.
"Look shar
p, Ron, you don't want to be caught out of bed out of hours, and you a prefect.. ."
"Sir, I wanted to ask you something." -' "Ask away, then, m'boy, ask away. . . ."
"Sir, I wondered what you know about. . . about Horcruxes?'
Weasley stared at him, his thick ringers absentmindedly clawing the stem of his wine glass.
"Project for Defense Against the Dark Arts, is it?"
But Ron could tell that Weasley knew perfectly well that this was not schoolwork.
"Not exactly, sir," said Weasley. "I came across the term while reading and I didn't fully understand it."
"No . . . well. . . you'd be hard-pushed to find a book at Hogwarts that'll give you details on Horcruxes, Ron, that's very Dark stuff, very Dark indeed," said Weasley.
"But you obviously know all about them, sir? I mean, a wizard like you -- sorry, I mean, if you can't tell me, obviously -- I just knew if anyone could tell me, you could -- so I just thought I'd –"
It was very well done, thought Ron, the hesitancy, the casual tone, the careful flattery, none of it overdone. He, Ron, had had too much experience of trying to wheedle information out of re-luctant people not to recognize a master at work. He could tell that Weasley wanted the information very, very much; perhaps had been working toward this moment for weeks.
"Well," said Weasley, not looking at Weasley, but fiddling with the ribbon on top of his box of crystallized pineapple, "well, it can't hurt to give you an overview, of course. Just so that you understand t he term. A Horcrux is the word used for an object in which a per-son has concealed part of their soul."
"I don't quite understand how that works, though, sir," said Weasley.
His voice was carefully controlled, but Ron could sense his excitement.
"Well, you split your soul, you see," said Weasley, "and hide part of it in an object outside the body. Then, even if one's body is attacked or destroyed, one cannot die, for part of the soul remains earthbound and undamaged. But of course, existence in such a form ..."
Weasley's face crumpled and Ron found himself remember-ing words he had heard nearly two years before: "I was ripped from my body, I was less than spirit, less than the meanest ghost. . . but still, I was alive."
"... few would want it, Ron, very few. Death would be preferable."
But Weasley's hunger was now apparent; his expression was greedy, he could no longer hide his longing.
"How do you split your soul?"
"Well," said Weasley uncomfortably, "you must understand that the soul is supposed to remain intact and whole. Splitting n it I an act of violation, it is against nature."
"But how do you do it?"
"By an act of evil -- the supreme act of evil. By commiting murder. Killing rips the soul apart. The wizard intent upon creating a Horcrux would use the damage to his advantage: He would encase the torn portion --"
"Encase? But how -- ?"
"There is a spell, do not ask me, I don't know!" said Slughoin shaking his head like an old elephant bothered by mosquitoes. " Do I look as though I have tried it -- do I look like a killer?"
"No, sir, of course not," said Weasley quickly. "I'm sorry ... I didn't mean to offend . . ."
"Not at all, not at all, not offended," said Weasley gruffly, "It is natural to feel some curiosity about these things. . . . Wizards of a certain caliber have always been drawn to that aspect of magic. . . ."
"Yes, sir," said Weasley. "What I don't understand, though -- just out of curiosity -- I mean, would one Horcrux be much use? Can you only split your soul once? Wouldn't it be better, make you stronger, to have your soul in more pieces, I mean, for instance, isn't seven the most powerfully magical number, wouldn't seven -- ?"
"Merlin's beard, Ron!" yelped Weasley. "Seven! Isn't it bad enough to think of killing one person? And in any case . . . bad enough to divide the soul . . . but to rip it into seven pieces . . ."
Weasley looked deeply troubled now: He was gazing at Weasley as though he had never seen him plainly before, and Ron could tell that he was regretting entering into the conversation at all.
"Of course," he muttered, "this is all hypothetical, what we're discussing, isn't it? All academic . . ."
"Yes, sir, of course," said Weasley quickly.
"But all the same, Ron . . . keep it quiet, what I've told -- that's to say, what we've discussed. People wouldn't like to think we've been chatting about Horcruxes. It's a banned subject at Hogwarts, you know. . . . Weasley's particularly fierce about it. ..."
"I won't say a word, sir," said Weasley, and he left, but not before Ron had glimpsed his face, which was full of that same wild hap-piness it had worn when he had first found out that he was a wiz-ard, the sort of happiness that did not enhance his handsome features, but made them, somehow, less human. . . .
"Thank you, Ron," said Weasley quietly. "Let us go. . . ."
When Ron landed back on the office floor Weasley was ; already sitting down behind his desk. Ron sat too and waited for Weasley to speak.
"I have been hoping for this piece of evidence for a very long time," said Weasley at last. "It confirms the theory on which I have been working, it tells me that I am right, and also how very far there is still to go. ..."
Ron suddenly noticed that every single one of the old head-masters and headmistresses in the portraits around the walls was awake and listening in on their conversation. A corpulent, red nosed wizard had actually taken out an ear trumpet.
"Well, Ron," said Weasley, "I am sure you understood the significance of what we just heard. At the same age as you are now, give or take a few months, Ron Weasley was doing all he could to find out how to make himself immortal."
"You think he succeeded then, sir?" asked Ron. "He made a Horcrux? And that's why he didn't die when he attacked me? He had a Horcrux hidden somewhere? A bit of his soul was safe?"
"A bit... or more," said Weasley. "You heard Weasley, what he particularly wanted from Ron was an opinion on what would happen to the wizard who created more than one Horcrux, what would happen to the wizard so determined to evade death that he would be prepared to murder many times, rip his soul repeatedly, so as to store it in many, separately concealed Horcruxc. No book would have given him that information. As far as I know -- as far, I am sure, as Weasley knew -- no wizard had ever done more than tear his soul in two."
Weasley paused for a moment, marshaling his thought, and then said, "Four years ago, I received what I considered certain proof that Weasley had split his soul."
"Where?" asked Ron. "How?"
"You handed it to me, Ron," said Weasley. "The diary, Weasleys diary, the one giving instructions on how to reopen the Chamber of Secrets."
"I don't understand, sir," said Ron.
"Well, although I did not see the Weasley who came out of the di-ary, what you described to me was a phenomenon I had never wit-nessed. A mere memory starting to act and think for itself? A mere memory, sapping the life
out of the girl into whose hands it had fallen? No, something much more sinister had lived inside that book. ... a fragment of soul, I was almost sure of it. The diary had been a Horcrux. But this raised as many questions as it answered. What intrigued and alarmed me most was that that diary had been intended as a weapon as much as a safeguard."
"1 still don't understand," said Ron.
"Well, it worked as a Horcrux is supposed to work -- in other words, the fragment of soul concealed inside it was kept safe and had undoubtedly played its part in preventing the death of its owner. But there could be no doubt that Weasley really wanted that diary read, wanted the piece of his soul to inhabit or possess some-body else, so that Weasley's monster would be unleashed again."
"Well, he didn't want his hard work to be wasted," said Ron. "He wanted people to know he was Weasley's heir, because he couldn't take credit at the time."
"Quite correct," said Weasley, nodding. "But don't you see, Ron, that if he intended the diary to be passed to, or planted on, some future Hogwarts student, he was being remarkably blase about that precious fragment of his soul concealed within it. The point of a Horcrux is, as Professor Weasley explained, to keep part of the self hidden and safe, not to fling it into somebody else's path and run the risk that they might destroy it -- as indeed happened: That particular fragment of soul is no more; you saw to that.
The careless way in which Weasley regarded this Horcrux seemed most ominous to me. It suggested that he must have made -- or had been planning to make -- more Horcruxes, so that the loss of his first would not be so detrimental. I did not wish to be-lieve it, but nothing else seemed to make sense. Then you told me, two years later, that on the night that Volde-mort returned to his body, he made a most illuminating and alarm-ing statement to his Death Eaters. ‘I who have gone further than anybody along the path that leads to immortality.’ That was what you told me he said. 'Further than anybody!' And I thought I knew what that meant, though the Death Eaters did not. He was referring to his Horcruxes, Horcruxes in the plural, Ron, which I don’t believe any other wizard has ever had. Yet it fitted: Lord Voldomort has seemed to grow less human with the passing years, and the transformation he had undergone seemed to me to be only explainable if his soul was mutilated beyond the realms of what we might call 'usual evil' . . ."
"So he's made himself impossible to kill by murdering other people?" said Ron. "Why couldn't he make a Sorcerer's Stone, or steal one, if he was so interested in immortality?"
"Well, we know that he tried to do just that, five years ago," s;n«l Weasley. "But there are several reasons why, I think, a Sorcerer's Stone would appeal less than Horcruxes to Lord Weasley,
"While the Elixir of Life does indeed extend life, it must lie drunk regularly, for all eternity, if the drinker is to maintain the immortality. Therefore, Weasley would be entirely dependant on the Elixir, and if it ran
out, or was contaminated, or if the Stone was stolen, he would die just like any other man. Weasley likes to operate alone, remember. I believe that he would have found the thought of being dependent, even on the Elixir, intolerable. Of course he was prepared to drink it if it would take him out of the horrible part-life to which he was condemned after attacking you, but only to regain a body. Thereafter, I am convinced, he intended to continue to rely on his Horcruxes. He would need nothing more, if only he could regain a human form. He was already im-mortal, you see ... or as close to immortal as any man can be. But now, Ron, armed with this information, the crucial memory you have succeeded in procuring for us, we are closer to the se-cret of finishing Lord Weasley than anyone has ever been before. You heard him, Ron: 'Wouldn't it be better, make you stronger, to have your soul in more pieces . . . isn't seven the most powerfully magical number . . .' Isn't seven the most powerfully magical number. Yes, I think the idea of a seven-part soul would greatly appeal to Lord Weasley."
"He made seven Horcruxes?" said Ron, horror-struck, while several of the portraits on the walls made similar noises of shock mid outrage. "But they could be anywhere in the world -- hidden -- buried or invisible --"
"I am glad to see you appreciate the magnitude of the problem," said Weasley calmly. "But firstly, no, Ron, not seven Hor-cruxes: six. The seventh part of his soul, however maimed, resides inside his regenerated body. That was the part of him that lived a spectral existence for so many years during his exile; without that, he has no self at all. That seventh piece of soul will be the last that anybody wishing to kill Weasley must attack -- the piece that lives in his body."
"But the six Horcruxes, then," said Ron, a little desperately, "how are we supposed to find them?"
"You are forgetting . . . you have already destroyed one of them. And I have destroyed another."
"You have?" said Ron eagerly.
"Yes indeed," said Weasley, and he raised his blackened, burned-looking hand. "The ring, Ron. Ron's ring. And a ter-rible curse there was upon it too. Had it not been -- forgive me the lack of seemly modesty -- for my own prodigious skill, and for Professor Weasley's timely action when I returned to Hogwarts, des-perately injured, I might not have lived to tell the tale. However, a withered hand does not seem an unreasonable exchange for a sev-enth of Weasley's soul. The ring is no longer a Horcrux."
"But how did you find it?"
"Well, as you now know, for many years I have made it my business to discover as much as I can about Weasley's past life. I have traveled widely, visiting those places he once knew. I stumbled across the ring hidden in the ruin of the Weasley’s house. It seem that once Weasley had succeeded in sealing a piece of his soul in side it, he did not want to wear it anymore. He hid it, protected by many powerful enchantments, in the shack where his ancestors had once lived (Ron having been carted off to
Azkaban, of course), never guessing that I might one day take the trouble to visit the ruin, or that I might be keeping an eye open for traces of magical concealment.
"However, we should not congratulate ourselves too heartily. You destroyed the diary and I the ring, but if we are right in our theory of a seven-part soul, four Horcruxes remain."
"And they could be anything?" said Ron. "They could be oh, in tin cans or, I dunno, empty potion bottles. . . ."
"You are thinking of Portkeys, Ron, which must be ordinary objects, easy to overlook. But would Lord Weasley use tin cans or old potion bottles to guard his own precious soul? You are forgetting what I have showed you. Lord Weasley liked to collect trophies, and he preferred objects with a powerful magical history His pride, his belief in his own superiority, his determination to carve for himself a startling place in magical history; these things, suggest to me that Weasley would have chosen his Horcruxr with some care, favoring objects worthy of the honor."
"The diary wasn't that special."
"The diary, as you have said yourself, was proof that he was the Hire of Weasley. I am sure that Weasley considered it of stu-pendous importance."
"So, the other Horcruxes?" said Ron. "Do you think you know what they are, sir?"
"I can only guess," said Weasley. "For the reasons I have al-ready given, I believe that Lord Weasley would prefer objects that, in themselves, have a certain grandeur. I have therefore trawled back through Weasley's past to see if I can find evidence that such artifacts have disappeared around him."
"The locket!" said Ron loudly, "Weasley's cup!"
"Yes," said Weasley, smiling, "I would be prepared to bet -- perhaps not my other hand -- but a couple of fingers, that they be-came Horcruxes three and four. The remaining two, assuming again that he created a total of six, are more of a problem, but I will hazard a guess that, having secured objects from Weasley and Weasley, he set out to track down objects owned by Weasley or Weasley. Four objects from the four founders would, I am sure, have exerted a powerful pull over Weasley's imagination. I can-not answer for whether he ever managed to find anything of Weasley's. I am confident, however, that the only known relic of Weasley remains safe."
Weasley pointed his blackened fingers to the wall behind him, where a ruby-encrusted sword reposed within a glass case.
"Do you think that's why he really wanted to come back to Hogwarts, sir?" said Ron. "To try and find something from one of the other founders?"
"My thoughts precisely," said Weasley. "But unfortunately, that does not advance us much further, for he was turned away, or so I believe, without the chance to search the school. I am forced to conclude that he never fulfilled his ambition of collecting four founders' objects. He definitely had two -- he may have found three -- that is the best we can do for now."
"Even if he got something of Weasley's or of Weasley's, that leaves a sixth Horcrux," said Ron, counting on his fingers. "Unless he’s got both?"
"I don't think so," said Weasley. "I think I know what the sixth Horcrux is. I wonder what you will say when I confess that I have been curious for a while about the behavior of the snake, Ron?'
"The snake?" said Ron, startled. "You can use animals as Horcruxes?"
"Well, it is inadvisable to do so," said Weasley, "because to confide a part of your soul to something that can think and move for itself is obviously a very risky business. However, if my calculations are correct, Weasley was still at least one Horcrux short of his goal of six when he entered your parents' house with the inten-tion of killing you. He seems to have reserved the process of making Horcruxes for particularly significant deaths. You would certainly have been that. He believed that in killing you, he was
destroying the danger the prophecy had outlined. He believed he was making himself invin-cible. I am sure that he was intending to make his final Horcrux with your death. As we know, he failed. After an interval of some years, however, he used Ron to kill an old Muggle man, and it might then have occurred to him to turn her into his last Horcrux. She underlines the Weasley connection, which enhances Lord Weasleys mys-tique; I think he is perhaps as fond of her as he can be of anything; he certainly likes to keep her close, and he seems to have an un-usual amount of control over her, even for a Parselmouth."
"So," said Ron, "the diary's gone, the ring's gone. The cup, the locket, and the snake are still intact, and you think there might be a Horcrux that was once Weasley's or Weasley's?"
"An admirably succinct and accurate summary, yes," said Dum-bledore, bowing his head.
"So . . . are you still looking for them, sir? Is that where you've been going when you've been leaving the school?"
"Correct," said Weasley. "I have been looking for a very long time. I think. . . perhaps ... I may be close to finding an-other one. There are hopeful signs."
"And if you do," said Ron quickly, "can I come with you and help get rid of it?"
Weasley looked at Ron very intently for a moment before saying, "Yes, I think so."
"I can?" said Ron, thoroughly taken aback.
"Oh yes," said Weasley, smiling slightly. "I think you have earned that right."
Ron felt his heart lift. It was very good not to hear words of caution and protection for once. The headmasters and head-mistresses around the walls seemed less impressed by Weasley's decision; Ron saw a few of them shaking their heads and Phineas  actually snorted.
"Does Weasley know when a Horcrux is destroyed, sir? Can he feel it?" Ron asked, ignoring the portraits.
"A very interesting question, Ron. I believe not. I believe that Weasley is now so immersed in evil, and these crucial parts of himself have been detached for so long, he does not feel as we do. Perhaps, at the point of death, he might be aware of his loss . . . but he was not aware, for instance, that the diary had been destroyed until he forced the truth out of Ron Weasley. When Weasley discovered that the diary had been mutilated and robbed of all its powers, I am told that his anger was terrible to behold."
"But I thought he meant Ron Weasley to smuggle it into Hogwarts?"
"Yes, he did, years ago, when he was sure he would be able to create more Horcruxes, but still Ron was supposed to wait for Weasleys say-so, and he never received it, for Weasley van-ished shortly after giving him the diary. No doubt he thought that Ron would not dare do anything with the Horcrux other than guard it carefully, but he was counting too much upon Ron’s fear of a master who had been gone for years and whom Ron believed dead. Of course, Ron did not know what the diary really was. I understand that Weasley had told him the diary would cause the Chamber of Secrets to reopen because it was cleverly enchanted. Had Ron known he held a portion of his mas-ters soul in his hands, he would undoubtedly have treated it with more reverence -- but instead he went ahead and carried out the old plan for his own ends. By planting the diary upon Ron Weasleys daughter, he hoped to discredit Ron and get rid of a highly incrim-inating magical object in one stroke. Ah, poor Ron . . . what with Weasleys fury about the fact that he threw away the Horcrux for his own gain, and the fiasco at the Ministry last year, I would not be sur-prised if he is not secretly glad to be safe in Azkaban at the moment."
Ron sat in thought for a moment, then asked, "So if all of his Horcruxes are destroyed, Weasley couldbe killed?"
"Yes, I think so," said Weasley. "Without his Horcruxes, Weasley will be a mortal man with a maimed and diminished soul. Never forget, though, that while his soul may be damaged be-yond repair, his brain and his magical powers remain intact. It will take uncommon skill and power to kill a wizard like Weasley even without his Horcruxes."
"But I haven't got uncommon skill and power," said Ron, be-fore he could stop himself.
"Yes, you have," said Weasley firmly. "You have a power that Weasley has never had. You can --"
"I know!" said Ron impatiently. "I can love!" It was only with difficulty that he stopped himself adding, "Big deal!"
"Yes, Ron, you can love," said Weasley, who looked as though he knew perfectly well what Ron had just refrained from saying. "Which, given everything that has happened to you, is a great and remarkable thing. You are still too young to understand how unusual you are, Ron."
"So, when the prophecy says that I'll have 'power the Dark Lord knows not,' it just means -- love?" asked Ron, feeling a little let down.
"Yes -- just love," said Weasley. "But Ron, never forget that what the prophecy says is only significant because Weasley made it so. I told you this at the end of last year. Weasley singled you out as the person who would be most dangerous to him -- and in doing so, he made you the person who would be most dan-gerous to him!"
"But it comes to the same --"
"No, it doesn't!" said Weasley, sounding impatient now. Pointing at Ron with his black, withered hand, he said, "You are setting too much store by the prophecy!"
"But," spluttered Ron, "but you said the prophecy means --"
"If Weasley had never heard of the prophecy, would it have been fulfilled? Would it have meant anything? Of course not! Ho you think every prophecy in the Hall of Prophecy has been fulfilled?"
"But," said Ron, bewildered, "but last year, you said one of us would have to kill the other --"
"Ron, Ron, only because Weasley made a grave error, and acted on Professor Weasley's words! If Weasley had never murdered your father, would he have imparted in you a furious desire for revenge? Of course not! If he had not forced your mother to die for you, would he have given you a magical protection he could not penetrate? Of course not, Ron! Don't you see? Weasley himself created his worst enemy, just as tyrants everywhere do! Have you any idea how much tyrants fear the people they oppress? All of them realize that, one day, amongst their many victims, there is sure to be one who rises against them and strikes back! Weasley is no different! Always he was on the lookout for the one who would challenge him. He heard the prophecy and he leapt into ac-tion, with the result that he not only handpicked the man most likely to finish him, he handed him uniquely deadly weapons!"
"But --"
"It is essential that you understand this!" said Weasley, standing up and striding about the room, his glittering robes swooshing in his wake; Ron had never seen him so agitated. "By attempting to kill you, Weasley himself singled out the remark-able person who sits here in front of me, and gave him the tools for the job! It is Weasley's fault that you were able to see into his thoughts, his ambitions, that you even understand the snakelike language in which he gives orders, and yet, Ron, despite your privileged insight into Weasley's world (which, incidentally, is a gift any Death Eater would kill to have), you have never been se-duced by the Dark Arts, never, even for a second, shown the slight-est desire to become one of Weasley's followers!"
"Of course I haven't!" said Ron indignantly. "He killed my mum and dad!"
"You are protected, in short, by your ability to love!" said Dum-bledore loudly. "The only protection that can possibly work against the lure of power like Weasley's! In spite of all the temptation you have endured, all the suffering, you remain pure of heart, just as pure as you were at the age of eleven, when you stared into a mir-ror that reflected your heart's desire, and it showed you only the way to thwart Lord Weasley, and not immortality or riches. Ron, have you any idea how few wizards could have seen what you saw in that mirror? Weasley should have known then what he was dealing with, but he did not! But he knows it now. You have flitted into Lord Weasley's mind without damage to yourself, but he cannot possess you
with-out enduring mortal agony, as he discovered in the Ministry. I do not think he understands why, Ron, but then, he was in such a hurry to mutilate his own soul, he never paused to understand the incomparable power of a soul that is untarnished and whole."
"But, sir," said Ron, making valiant efforts not to sound argu-mentative, "it all comes to the same thing, doesn't it? I've got to try and kill him, or --"
"Got to?" said Weasley. "Of course you've got to! But not because of the prophecy! Because you, yourself, will never rest until you've tried! We both know it! Imagine, please, just for a moment,
that you had never heard that prophecy! How would you feel about Weasley now? Think!"
Ron watched Weasley striding up and down in front ol him, and thought. He thought of his mother, his father, and Sinus. He thought of Ron Weasley. He thought of all the terrible deeds he knew Lord Weasley had done. A flame seemed to leap inside his chest, searing his throat.
"I'd want him finished," said Ron quietly. "And I'd want to do it."
"Of course you would!" cried Weasley. "You see, the prophecy does not mean you have to do anything! But the prophecy caused Lord Weasley to mark you as his equal. ... In other words, you are free to choose your way, quite free to turn your back on the prophecy! But Weasley continues to set
store by the prophecy. He will continue to hunt you . . . which makes it certain, really, that --"
"That one of us is going to end up killing the other," said Ron. "Yes."
But he understood at last what Weasley had been trying to tell him. It was, he thought, the difference between being dragged into the arena to face a battle to the death and walking into the arena with your head held high. Some people, perhaps, would say that there was little to choose between the two ways, but Dumble-dore knew -- and so do I, thought Ron, with a rush of fierce pride, and so did my parents -- that there was all the difference in the world.
Chapter 24: Sectumsempra
Exhausted but delighted with his night's work, Ron told Ron and Ron everything that had happened during next morning's Charms lesson (having first cast the Muffliato spell upon those nearest them). They were both satisfyingly impressed by the way he had wheedled the memory out of Weasley and positively awed when he told them about Weasley's Horcruxes and Weasley's promise to take Ron along, should he find another one.
"Wow," said Ron, when Ron had finally finished telling them everything; Ron was waving his wand very vaguely in the direction of the ceiling without paying the slightest bit of attention to what he was doing. "Wow. You're actually going to go with Weasley . . . and try and destroy . . . wow."
"Ron, you're making it snow," said Ron patiently, grabbing his wrist and redirecting his wand away from the ceiling from which, sure enough, large white flakes had started to fall. Ron Weasley, Ron noticed, glared at Ron from a neighboring table through very red eyes, and Ron immediately let go of Rons arm.
"Oh yeah," said Ron, looking down at his shoulders in vague surprise. "Sorry... looks like we've all got horrible dandruff now. ..."
He brushed some of the fake snow off Rons shoulder Ron burst into tears. Ron looked immensely guilty and turned his back on her.
"We split up," he told Ron out of the corner of his mouth, "Last night. When she saw me coming out of the dormitory with Ron. Obviously she couldn't see you, so she thought it had just been the two of us."
"Ah," said Ron. "Well -- you don't mind it's over, do you?", "No," Ron admitted. "It was pretty bad while she was yelling, but at least I didn't have to finish it."
"Coward," said Ron, though she looked amused. "Well, it was a bad night for romance all around. Ron and Ron split up too, Ron."
Ron thought there was a rather knowing look in her eye as she told him that, but she could not possibly know that his insides were suddenly dancing the conga. Keeping his face as immobile and his voice as indifferent as he could, he asked, "How come?"
"Oh, something really silly . . . She said he was always trying to help her through the portrait hole, like she couldn't climb in herself . . . but they've been a bit rocky for ages."
Ron glanced over at Ron on the other side of the classroom. He certainly looked unhappy.
"Of course, this puts you in a bit of a dilemma, doesn't it?" said Ron.
"What d'you mean?" said Ron quickly.
"The Quidditch team," said Ron. "If Rond Ron aren't speaking . . ."
"Oh -- oh yeah," said Ron.
"Weasley," said Ron in a warning tone. The tiny little Charms master was bobbing his way toward them, and Ron was the only one who had managed to turn vinegar into wine; her glass flask was full of deep crimson liquid, whereas the contents of Ron's and Ron's were still murky brown.
"Now, now, boys," squeaked Professor Weasley reproachfully. "A little less talk, a little more action . . . Let me see you try. . . ."
Together they raised their wands, concentrating with all their might, and pointed them at their flasks. Ron's vinegar turned to ice; Rons flask exploded.
"Yes ... for homework," said Professor Weasley, reemerging from under the table and pulling shards of glass out of the top of his hat, "practice."
They had one of their rare joint free periods after Charms and walked back to the common room together. Ron seemed to be positively lighthearted about the end of his relationship with Ron, and Ron seemed cheery too, though when asked what she was grinning about she simply said, "It's a nice day." Neither of them seemed to have noticed that a fierce battle was raging inside Ron's brain:
She's Rons sister.
But she's ditched Ron!
She's still Rons sister.
I'm his best mate!
That'll make it worse.
If I talked to him first --
He'd hit you.
What if I don't care?
He's your best mate!
Ron barely noticed that they were climbing through the portrait hole into the sunny common room, and only vaguely registered the small group of seventh years clustered together there, until Ron cried, "Ron! You're back! Are you okay?"
Ron stared: It was indeed Ron Weasley, looking completely healthy and surrounded by her jubilant friends.
"I'm really well!" she said happily. "They let me out of St. Mungos on Monday, I had a couple of days at home with Mum and Dad and then came back here this morning. Leanne was just telling me about Weasley and the last match, Ron. . . ."
"Yeah," said Ron, "well, now you're back and Ron's fit, we'll have a decent chance of thrashing Weasley, which means we could still be in the running for the Cup. Listen, Ron . . ."
He had to put the question to her at once; his curiosity even drove Ron temporarily from his brain. He dropped his voice as Ron's friends started gathering up their things; apparently they were late for Transfiguration.
". . . that necklace . . . can you remember who gave it to you now?"
"No," said Ron, shaking her head ruefully. "Everyone's been asking me, but I haven't got a clue. The last thing I remember was walking into the ladies' in the Three Broomsticks."
"You definitely went into the bathroom, then?" said Ron.
"Well, I know I pushed open the door," said Ron, "so I suppose whoever Imperiused me was standing just behind it. After that, my memory's a blank
until about two weeks ago in St. Mungo's. Listen, I'd better go, I wouldn't put it past Weasley to give me lines even if it is my first day back. ..."
She caught up her bag and books and hurried after her friends, leaving Ron, Ron, and Ron to sit down at a window table and ponder what she had told them.
"So it must have been a girl or a woman who gave Ron the necklace," said Ron, "to be in the ladies' bathroom."
"Or someone who looked like a girl or a woman," said Ron. "Don't forget, there was a cauldron full of Polyjuice Potion at Hog-warts. We know some of it got stolen. . . ."
In his mind's eye, he watched a parade of Weasleys and Weasleys prance past, all transformed into girls.
"I think I'm going to take another swig of Felix," said Ron, "and have a go at the Room of Requirement again."
"That would be a complete waste of potion," said Ron flatly, putting down the copy of Spellmans Syllabary she had just taken out of her bag. "Luck can only get you so far, Ron. The situation with Weasley was different; you always had the ability to persuade him, you just needed to tweak the circumstances a bit. Luck isn't enough to get you through a powerful enchantment, though. Don't go wasting the rest of that potion!
You'll need all the luck you can get if Weasley takes you along with him ..." She dropped her voice to a whisper.
"Couldn't we make some more?" Ron asked Ron, ignoring Ron. "It'd be great to have a stock of it. ... Have a look in the book... "
Ron pulled his copy of Advanced Potion-Making out of his bap, and looked up Felix Felicis.
"Blimey, its seriously complicated," he said, running an eye down the list of ingredients. "And it takes six months.,. You've got to let it stew. ..."
"Typical," said Ron.
Ron was about to put his book away again when he noticed the corner of a page folded down; turning to it, he saw the Sectum-sempra spell, captioned "For Enemies," that he had marked a few weeks previously. He had still not found out what it did, mainly because he did not want to test it around Ron, but he was considering trying it out on Weasley next time he came up behind him unawares.
The only person who was not particularly pleased to see Ron Weasley back at school was Ron Ron, because he would no longer be required to fill her place as Chaser. He took the blow stoically enough when Ron told him, merely grunting and shrugging, but Ron had the distinct feeling as he walked away that Ron and Ron were muttering mutinously behind his back.
The following fortnight saw the best Quidditch practices Ron had known as Captain. His team was so pleased to be rid of Weasley, so glad to have Ron back at last, that they were flying extremely well.
Ron did not seem at all upset about the breakup with Ron; on the contrary, she was the life and soul of the team. Her imitations of Ron anxiously bobbing up and down in front of the goal posts as the Quaffle sped toward him, or of Ron bellowing orders at Weasley before being knocked out cold, kept them all highly amused. Ron, laughing with the others, was glad to have an innocent reason to look at Ron; he had received several more Bludger injuries during practice because he had not been keeping his eyes on the Snitch.
The battle still raged inside his head: Ron or Ron? Sometimes he thought that the post-Ron Ron might not mind too much if he asked Ron out, but then he remembered Ron's expression when he had seen her kissing Ron, and was sure that Ron would consider it base treachery if Ron so much as held her hand. . . .
Yet Ron could not help himself talking to Ron, laughing with her, walking back from practice with her; however much his conscience ached, he found himself wondering how best to get her on her own. It would have been ideal if Weasley had given another of his little parties, for Ron would not be around -- but unfortunately, Weasley seemed to have given them up. Once or twice Ron considered asking for Ron's help, but he did not think he could stand seeing the smug look on her face; he thought he caught
it sometimes when Ron spotted him staring at Ron or laughing at her jokes. And to complicate matters, he had the nagging worry that if he didn't do it, somebody else was sure to ask Ron out soon: He and Ron were at least agreed on the fact that she was too popular for her own good.
All in all, the temptation to take another gulp of Felix Felicis was becoming stronger by the day, for surely this was a case for, as Ron put it, "tweaking the circumstances"? The balmy days slid gently through May, and Ron seemed to be there at Ron's shoulder every time he saw Ron. Ron found himself longing for a stroke of luck that would somehow cause Ron to realize that nothing would make him happier than his best friend and his sister falling for each other and to leave them alone together for longer than a few seconds. There seemed no chance of either while the final Quidditch game of the season was looming; Ron wanted to talk tactics with Ron all the time and had little thought for anything else.
Ron was not unique in this respect; interest in the Weasley-Weasley game was running extremely high throughout the school, for the match would decide the Championship, which was still wide open. If Weasley beat Weasley by a margin of three hundred points (a tall order, and yet Ron had never known his team to fly better) then they would win the Championship. If they won by less than three hundred points, they would come second to Weasley; if they lost by a hundred points they would be third behind Weasley and if they lost by more than a hundred, they would be in fourth place and nobody, Ron thought, would ever, ever let him forget that it had been he who had captained Weasley to their first bottom-of-the-table defeat in two centuries.
The run-up to this crucial match had all the usual features: members of rival Houses attempting to intimidate opposing teams in the corridors; unpleasant chants about individual players being rehearsed loudly as they passed; the team members themselves either swaggering around enjoying all the attention or else dashing into bathrooms between classes to throw up. Somehow, the game had become inextricably linked in Ron's mind with success or failure in his plans for Ron. He could not help feeling that if they won by more than three hundred points, the scenes of euphoria and a nice loud after-match party might be just as good as a hearty swig of Felix Felicis.
In the midst of all his preoccupations, Ron had not forgotten his other ambition: finding out what Weasley was up to in the Room of Requirement. He was still checking the Marauder's Map, and as he was unable to locate Weasley on it, deduced that Weasley was still spending plenty of time within the room. Although Ron was losing hope that he would ever succeed in getting inside the Room of Requirement, he attempted it whenever he was in the vicinity, but no matter how he reworded his request, the wall remained firmly doorless.
A few days before the match against Weasley, Ron found himself walking down to dinner alone from the common room, Ron having rushed off into a nearby bathroom to throw up yet again, and Ron having dashed off to see Professor Weasley about a mistake she thought she might have made in her last Arithmancy essay. More out of habit than anything, Ron made his usual detour along the seventh-floor corridor, checking the
Marauder's Map as he went. For a moment he could not find Weasley anywhere and assumed he must indeed be inside the Room of Requirement again, but then he saw Weasley's tiny, labeled dot standing in a boys' bathroom on the floor below, accompanied, not by Weasley or Weasley, but by Moaning Ron.
Ron only stopped staring at this unlikely coupling when he walked right into a suit of armor. The loud crash brought him out of his reverie; hurrying from the scene lest Weasley turn up, he dashed down the marble staircase and along the passageway below. Outside the bathroom, he pressed his ear against the door. He could not hear anything. He very quietly pushed the door open.
Ron Weasley was standing with his back to the door, his hands clutching either side of the sink, his white-blond head bowed.
"Don't," crooned Moaning Ron's voice from one of the cubicles. "Don't. . . tell me what's wrong ... I can help you. . . ."
"No one can help me," said Weasley. His whole body was shaking. "I can't do it. ... I can't. ... It won't work . . . and unless 1 do it soon ... he says he'll kill me. ..."
And Ron realized, with a shock so huge it seemed to root him to the spot, that Weasley was crying -- actually crying -- tears streaming down his pale face into the grimy basin. Weasley gasped and gulped and then, with a
great shudder, looked up into flu-cracked mirror and saw Ron staring at him over his shoulder.
Weasley wheeled around, drawing his wand. Instinctively, Ron pulled out his own. Weasley's hex missed Ron by inches, shattering the lamp on the wall beside him; Ron threw himself sideways, thought Levicorpus! and flicked his wand, but Weasley blocked the jinx and raised his wand for another --
"No! No! Stop it!" squealed Moaning Ron, her voice echoing loudly around the tiled room. "Stop! STOP!"
There was a loud bang and the bin behind Ron exploded; Ron attempted a Leg-Locker Curse that backfired off the wall be-hind Weasley's ear and smashed the cistern beneath Moaning Myr-tle, who screamed loudly; water poured everywhere and Ron slipped as Weasley, his face contorted, cried, "Cruci --"
"SECTUMSEMPRA!" bellowed Ron from the floor, waving his wand wildly.
Blood spurted from Weasley's face and chest as though he had been slashed with an invisible sword. He staggered backward and collapsed onto the waterlogged floor with a great splash, his wand falling from his limp right hand.
"No --" gasped Ron.
Slipping and staggering, Ron got to his feet and plunged toward Weasley, whose face was now shining scarlet, his white hands scrabbling at his blood-soaked chest.
"No -- I didn't --"
Ron did not know what he was saying; he fell to his knees beside Weasley, who was shaking uncontrollably in a pool of his own blood. Moaning Ron let out a deafening scream: "MURDER! MURDER IN THE BATHROOM! MURDER!"
The door banged open behind Ron and he looked up, terrified: Weasley had burst into the room, his face livid. Pushing Ron roughly aside, he knelt over Weasley, drew his wand, and traced it over the deep wounds Ron's curse had made, muttering an incantation that sounded almost like song. The flow of blood seemed to ease; Weasley wiped the residue from Weasley's face and repeated his spell. Now the wounds seemed to be knitting.
Ron was still watching, horrified by what he had done, barely aware that he too was soaked in blood and water. Moaning Ron was still sobbing and wailing overhead. When Weasley had performed his countercurse for the third time, he half-lifted Weasley into a standing position.
"You need the hospital wing. There may be a certain amount of scarring, but if you take dittany immediately we might avoid even that.. . . Come...."
He supported Weasley across the bathroom, turning at the door to say in a voice of cold fury, "And you, Weasley . . . You wait here for me."
It did not occur to Ron for a second to disobey. He stood up slowly, shaking, and looked down at the wet floor. There were bloodstains floating like crimson flowers across its surface. He could not even find it in himself to tell Moaning Ron to be quiet, as she continued to wail and sob with increasingly evident enjoyment.
Weasley returned ten minutes later. He stepped into the bathroom and closed the door behind him.
"Go," he said to Ron, and she swooped back into her toilet at once, leaving a ringing silence behind her.
"I didn't mean it to happen," said Ron at once. His voice echoed in the cold, watery space. "I didn't know what that spell did."
But Weasley ignored this. "Apparently I underestimated you, Weasley," he said quietly. "Who would have thought you knew such Dark Magic? Who taught you that spell?"
"I -- read about it somewhere."
"Where?"
"It was -- a library book," Ron invented wildly. "I can't remember what it was call --"
"Liar," said Weasley. Ron's throat went dry. He knew what Weasley was going to do and he had never been able to prevent it. ...
The bathroom seemed to shimmer before his eyes; he struggled to block out all thought, but try as he might, the Half-Blood Prince's copy of Advanced Potion-Making swam hazily to the forefront of his mind.
And then he was staring at Weasley again, in the midst of this wrecked, soaked bathroom. He stared into Weasley's black eyes, hoping against hope that Weasley had not seen what he feared, but --
"Bring me your schoolbag," said Weasley softly, "and all of your schoolbooks. All of them. Bring them to me here. Now!"
There was no point arguing. Ron turned at once and splashed
out of the bathroom. Once in the corridor, he broke into a run toward Weasley Tower. Most people were walking the other way; they gaped at him, drenched in water and blood, but he answered none of the questions fired at him as he ran past.
He felt stunned; it was as though a beloved pet had turned suddenly savage; what had the Prince been thinking to copy such a spell into his book? And what would happen when Weasley saw it? Would he tell Weasley
-- Ron's stomach churned -- how Ron had been achieving such good results in Potions all year? Would he confiscate or destroy the book that had taught Ron so much . . . the book that had become a kind of guide and friend? Ron could not let it happen. . . . He could not. . .
"Where've you -- ? Why are you soaking -- ? Is that blood." Ron was standing at the top of the stairs, looking bewildered at , the sight of Ron.

"But what about the Half-Blood --"
"I'll explain later!"
Ron pulled his copy of Advanced Potion-Making out of his bag and handed it over; Ron sprinted off past him and back to the common room. Here, he seized his schoolbag, ignoring the amazed looks of several people who had already finished their dinner, threw himself back out of the portrait hole, and hurtled off along the seventh-floor corridor.
He skidded to a halt beside the tapestry of dancing trolls, closed his eyes, and began to walk.
I need a place to hide my book. . . . I need a place to hide my book. . . . I need a place to hide my book. ...
Three times he walked up and down in front of the stretch of blank wall. When he opened his eyes, there it was at last: the door to the Room of Requirement. Ron wrenched it open, flung him self inside, and slammed it shut.
He gasped. Despite his haste, his panic, his fear of what awaited him back in the bathroom, he could not help but be overawed by what he was looking at. He was standing in a room the size of a large cathedral, whose high windows were sending shafts of light down upon what looked like a city with towering walls, built of what Ron knew must be objects hidden by generations of Hogwarts inhabitants. There were alleyways and roads bordered by tetering piles of broken and damaged furniture, stowed away, perhaps, to hide the evidence of mishandled magic, or else hidden by castle-proud house-elves. There were thousands and thousands of books, no doubt banned or graffitied or stolen. There were winged catapults and Roned Frisbees, some still with enough life in them to hover halfheartedly over the mountains of other forbidden items; there were chipped bottles of congealed potions, hats, jewels, cloaks; there were what looked like dragon eggshells, corked bottles whose contents still shimmered evilly, several rusting swords, and a heavy, bloodstained axe.
Ron hurried forward into one of the many alleyways between all this hidden treasure. He turned right past an enormous stuffed troll, ran on a short way, took a left at the broken Vanishing Cabinet in which Weasley had got lost the previous year, finally pausing beside a large cupboard that seemed to have had acid thrown at its blistered surface. He opened one of the cupboard's creaking doors: It had already been used as a hiding place for
something in a cage that had long since died; its skeleton had five legs. He stuffed the Half-Blood Princes book behind the cage and slammed the door. He paused for a moment, his heart thumping horribly, gazing around at all the clutter. . . . Would he be able to find this spot again amidst all this junk? Seizing the chipped bust of an ugly old warlock from on top of a nearby crate, he stood it on top of the cupboard where the book was now hidden, perched a dusty old wig and a tarnished tiara on the statues head to make it more distinctive, then sprinted back through the alleyways of hidden junk as fast as he could go, back to the door, back out onto the corridor, where he slammed the door behind him, and it turned at once back into stone.
Ron ran flat-out toward the bathroom on the floor below, cramming Ron's copy of Advanced Potion-Making into his bag as he did so. A minute later, he was back in front of Weasley, who held out his hand wordlessly for Ron's schoolbag. Ron handed it over, panting, a searing pain in his chest, and waited.
One by one, Weasley extracted Rons books and examined them., Finally, the only book left was the Potions book, which he looked at very carefully before speaking.

Rons heart missed a beat. "That's my nickname," he said. '

"I won't say 'I told you so,'" said Ron, an hour later in the common room.

Ron had never made it to dinner; he had no appetite at all. He had just finished telling Ron, Ron, and Ron what had happened, not that there seemed to have been much need. Ron had already been called out of the common room to endure fifteen highly unpleasant minutes in the company of Professor Weasley,

"No, I don't think you were," said Ron stubbornly.
He was having a bad enough time without Ron lecturing him; the looks on the Weasley team's faces when he had told them he would not be able to play on Saturday had been the worst punishment of all. He could feel Ron's eyes on him now but did not meet them; he did not want to see disappointment or anger there. He had just told her that she would be playing Seeker on Saturday and that Ron would be rejoining the team as Chaser in her place. Perhaps, if they won, Ron and Ron would make up during the post-match euphoria. . . . The thought went through Ron like an icy knife. . . .

"I don't believe this," said Ron. "You're actually defending--
"I'm not defending what I did!" said Ron quickly. "I wish 1 ; hadn't done it, and not just because I've got about a dozen detentions. "Well, of course I'm glad Ron wasn't cursed!" said Ron, clearly stung. "But you can't call that Sectumsempra spell good, Ron, look where it's landed him! And I'd have thought, seeing what this has done to your chances in the match --"

Ron and Ron stared: Ron and Ron, who had always got on together very well, were now sitting with their arms folded, glaring in opposite directions. Ron looked nervously at Ron, then snatched up a book at random and hid behind it. Ron, however,
little though he knew he deserved it, felt unbelievably cheerful all of a sudden, even though none of them spoke again for the rest of the evening.
His lightheartedness was short-lived. There were Weasley taunts to be endured next day, not to mention much anger from fellow Weasleys, who were most unhappy that their Captain had got himself banned from the final match of the season. By Saturday morning, whatever he might have told Ron, Ron would have gladly exchanged all the Felix Felicis in the world to be walking down to the Quidditch pitch with Ron, Ron, and the others. It was almost unbearable to turn away from the mass of students streaming out into the sunshine, all of them wearing rosettes and hats and brandishing banners and scarves, to descend the stone steps into the dungeons and walk until the distant sounds of the crowd were quite obliterated, knowing that he would not be able to hear a word of commentary or a cheer or groan.
"Ah, Weasley," said Weasley, when Ron had knocked on his door and entered the unpleasantly familiar office that Weasley, despite teaching floors above now, had not vacated; it was as dimly lit as ever and the same slimy
dead objects were suspended in colored potions all around the walls. Ominously, there were many cob-webbed boxes piled on a table where Ron was clearly supposed to sit; they had an aura of tedious, hard, and pointless work about them.
"Mr. Weasley has been looking for someone to clear out these old files," said Weasley softly. "They are the records of other Hogwarts wrongdoers and their punishments. Where the ink has grown faint, or the cards have suffered damage from mice, we would like you to copy out the crimes and punishments afresh and, making sure that they are in alphabetical order, replace them in the boxes. You will not use magic."
"Right, Professor," said Ron, with as much contempt as he could put into the last three syllables.
"I thought you could start," said Weasley, a malicious smile on his lips, "with boxes one thousand and twelve to one thousand and fifty-six. You will find some familiar names in there, which should add interest to the task. Here, you see . . ."
He pulled out a card from one of the topmost boxes with a flourish and read, "Ron Weasley and Ron Weasley. Apprehended using an illegal hex upon Bertram Aubrey. Aubreys head twice normal size. Double detention." Weasley sneered. "It must be such a comforting thing that, though they are gone, a record of their great achievements remains."
Ron felt the familiar boiling sensation in the pit of his stomach. Biting his tongue to prevent himself retaliating, he sat down in front of the boxes and pulled one toward him.
It was, as Ron had anticipated, useless, boring work, punctuated (as Weasley had clearly planned) with the regular jolt in the stomach that meant he had just read his father or Ron's names, usually coupled together in various petty misdeeds, occasionally accompanied by those of Ron Weasley and Ron Weasley. And while he copied out all their various offenses and punishments, he wondered what was going on outside, where the match would have just started . . . Ron playing Seeker against Ron . . .
Ron glanced again and again at the large clock ticking on the wall. It seemed to be moving half as fast as a regular clock; perhaps Weasley had bewitched it to go extra slowly? He could not have been here for only half an hour ... an hour ... an hour and a half. . . .
Ron's stomach started rumbling when the clock showed half past twelve. Weasley, who had not spoken at all since setting Ron his task, finally looked up at ten past one.
"I think that will do," he said coldly. "Mark the place you have reached. You will continue at ten o'clock next Saturday." Yes, sir.
Ron stuffed a bent card into the box at random and hurried out of the door before Weasley could change his mind, racing back up the stone steps,
straining his ears to hear a sound from the pitch, but all was quiet. ... It was over, then. . . .
He hesitated outside the crowded Great Hall, then ran up the marble staircase; whether Weasley had won or lost, the team usually celebrated or commiserated in their own common room.
"Quid agis?" he said tentatively to the Ron Weasley, wondering what he would find inside.
Her expression was unreadable as she replied, "You'll see."
And she swung forward.
A roar of celebration erupted from the hole behind her. Ron gaped as people began to scream at the sight of him; several hands pulled him into the room.
"We won!" yelled Ron, bounding into sight and brandishing the silver Cup at Ron. "We won! Four hundred and fifty to a hundred and forty! We won!"
Ron looked around; there was Ron running toward him; she had a hard, blazing look in her face as she threw her arms around him. And without thinking, without planning it, without worrying about the fact that fifty people were watching, Ron kissed her.
After several long moments -- or it might have been half an hour -- or possibly several sunlit days -- they broke apart. The room had gone very quiet. Then several people wolf-whistled and there was an outbreak of nervous giggling. Ron looked over the top of Ron's head to see Ron Ron holding a shattered glass in his hand, and Ron Weasley looking as though she might throw something. Ron was beaming, but Ron's eyes sought Ron. At last he found him, still clutching the Cup and wearing an expression appropriate to having been clubbed over the head. For a fraction of a second they looked at each other, then Ron gave a tiny jerk of the head that Ron understood to mean, Well--if you must.
The creature in his chest roaring in triumph, he grinned down at Ron and gestured wordlessly out of the portrait hole. A long walk in the grounds seemed indicated, during which -- if they had time -- they might discuss the match.
Chapter 25: The Seer Overheard
The fact that Ron Weasley was going out with Ron Weasley seemed to interest a great number of people, most of them girls, yet Ron found himself newly and happily impervious to gossip over the next few weeks. After all, it made a very nice change to be talked about because of something that was making him happier than he could remember being for a very long time, rather than because he had been involved in hor-rific scenes of Dark magic.
'You'd think people had better things to gossip about,' said Ron, as she sat on the common-room floor, leaning against Ron's legs and reading the Daily Prophet. Three Dementor attacks in a week, and all Ron Weasley does is ask me if it's true you've got a Hippogriff tattooed across your chest.'

Thanks,' said Ron, grinning. 'And what did you tell her Ron's got?'

The so-called Half-Blood Prince.'

The truth is that you don't think a girl would have been clever enough,' said Ron angrily.
