The Room of Requirement,' repeated Ron. 'Were you try-ing to get in there?'
'I - well - I didn't know students knew about -'
'Not all of them do,' said Ron. 'But what happened? You screamed ... it sounded as though you were hurt...'
'I - well,' said Professor Weasley, drawing her shawls around her defensively and staring down at him with her vastly magnified eyes. 'I wished to - ah - deposit certain – um - personal items in the Room ...' And she muttered something about 'nasty accusations'.
'Right,' said Ron, glancing down at the sherry bottles. 'But you couldn't get in and hide them?'
He found this very odd; the Room had opened for him, after all, when he had wanted to hide the Half-Blood Prince's book.
'Oh, I got in all right,' said Professor Weasley, glaring at the wall. 'But there was somebody already in there.'
'Somebody in -? Who?' demanded Ron. 'Who was in there?'
' ? have no idea,' said Professor Weasley, looking slightly taken aback at the urgency in Ron's voice. 'I walked into the Room and I heard a voice, which has never happened before in all my years of hiding - of using the Room, I mean.'
'A voice? Saying what?'
'I don't know that it was saying anything,' said Professor Weasley. 'It was ... whooping.'
'Whooping?'
'Gleefully,' she said, nodding.
Ron stared at her.
'Was it male or female?'
' ? would hazard a guess at male,' said Professor Weasley.
'And it sounded happy?'
'Very happy,' said Professor Weasley sniffily.
'As though it was celebrating?'
'Most definitely.'
'And then -?'
'And then I called out, "Who's there?"'
'You couldn't have found out who it was without asking?' Ron asked her, slightly frustrated.
‘The Inner Eye,' said Professor Weasley with dignity, straightening her shawls and many strands of glittering beads, 'was fixed upon matters well outside the mundane realms of whooping voices.'
'Right,' said Ron hastily; he had heard about Professor Weasley's Inner Eye all too often before. 'And did the voice say who was there?'
'No, it did not,' she said. 'Everything went pitch black and the next thing I knew, I was being hurled headfirst out of the Room!'
'And you didn't see that coming?' said Ron, unable to help himself.
'No, I did not, as I say, it was pitch -' She stopped and glared at him suspiciously.
'I think you'd better tell Professor Weasley,' said Ron. 'He ought to know Weasley's celebrating - I mean, that some-one threw you out of the Room.'
To his surprise, Professor Weasley drew herself up at this suggestion, looking haughty.
The Headmaster has intimated that he would prefer fewer visits from me,' she said coldly. I am not one to press my company upon those who do not value it. If Weasley chooses to ignore the warnings the cards show -'
Her bony hand closed suddenly around Ron's wrist.
'Again and again, no matter how I lay them out -'
And she pulled a card dramatically from underneath her shawls.
'- the lightning-struck tower,' she whispered. 'Calamity. Disaster. Coming nearer all the time ...'
'Right,' said Ron again. 'Well ... I still think you should tell Weasley about this voice and everything going dark and being thrown out of the Room ...'
'You think so?' Professor Weasley seemed to consider the matter for a moment, but Ron could tell that she liked the idea of retelling her little adventure.
'I'm going to see him right now,' said Ron. 'I've got a meeting with him. We could go together.'
'Oh, well, in that case,' said Professor Weasley with a smile. She bent down, scooped up her sherry bottles and dumped them unceremoniously in a large blue and white vase standing in a nearby niche.
'I miss having you in my classes, Ron,' she said soulfully, as they set off together. 'You were never much of a Seer ... but you were a wonderful Object...'
Ron did not reply; he had loathed being the Object of Professor Weasley's continual predictions of doom.
'I am afraid,' she went on, 'that the nag - I'm sorry, the centaur - knows nothing of cartomancy. I asked him - one Seer to another - had he not, too, sensed the distant vibra-tions of coming catastrophe? But he seemed to find me almost comical. Yes, comical!'
Her voice rose rather hysterically and Ron caught a powerful whiff of sherry even though the bottles had been left behind.
'Perhaps the horse has heard people say that I have not inherited my great-great-grandmother's gift. Those rumours have been bandied about by the jealous for years. You know what I say to such people, Ron? Would Weasley have let me teach at this great school, put so much trust in me all these years, had I not proved myself to him?'
Ron mumbled something indistinct.
'I well remember my first interview with Weasley,' went on Professor Weasley, in throaty tones. 'He was deeply impressed, of course, deeply impressed ... I was staying at the Hog's Head, which I do not advise, incidentally - bed bugs, dear boy - but funds were low. Weasley did me the courtesy of calling upon me in my room at the inn. He questioned me ... I must confess that, at first, I thought he seemed ill-disposed towards Divination ... and I remember I was starting to feel a little odd, I had not eaten much that day ... but then ...'
And now Ron was paying attention properly for the first time, for he knew what had happened then: Professor Weasley had made the prophecy that had altered the course of his whole life, the prophecy about him and Weasley.
'... but then we were rudely interrupted by Ron Weasley!'
'What?'
'Yes, there was a commotion outside the door and it flew open, and there was that rather uncouth barman standing with Weasley, who was waffling about having come the wrong way up the stairs, although I'm afraid that I myself rather thought he had been apprehended eavesdropping on my interview with Weasley - you see, he himself was seeking a job at the time, and no doubt hoped to pick up tips! Well, after that, you know, Weasley seemed much more dis-posed to give me a job, and I could not help thinking, Ron, that it was because he appreciated the stark contrast between my own unassuming manners and quiet talent, compared to the pushing, thrusting young man who was prepared to listen at keyholes - Ron, dear?'
She looked back over her shoulder, having only just real-ised that Ron was no longer with her; he had stopped walking and they were now ten feet from each other.
'Ron?' she repeated uncertainly.
Perhaps his face was white, to make her look so concerned and frightened. Ron was standing stock-still as waves of shock crashed over him, wave after wave, obliterating every-thing except the information that had been kept from him for so long ...
It was Weasley who had overheard the prophecy. It was Weasley who had carried the news of the prophecy to Weasley. Weasley and Ron Weasley together had sent Weasley hunt-ing after Ron and Ron and their son ...
Nothing else mattered to Ron just now.
'Ron?' said Professor Weasley again. 'Ron - I thought we were going to see the Headmaster together?'
'You stay here,' said Ron through numb lips.
'But, dear ... I was going to tell him how I was assaulted in the Room of-'
'You stay here!' Ron repeated angrily.
She looked alarmed as he ran past her, round the corner into Weasley's corridor, where the lone gargoyle stood sentry. Ron shouted the password at the gargoyle and ran up the moving spiral staircase three steps at a time. He did not knock upon Weasley's door, he hammered; and the calm voice answered 'Enter' after Ron had already flung himself into the room.
Ron the phoenix looked round, his bright black eyes gleaming with reflected gold from the sunset beyond the window. Weasley was standing at the window look-ing out at the grounds, a long, black travelling cloak in his arms.
'Well, Ron, I promised that you could come with me.'
For a moment or two, Ron did not understand; the con-versation with Weasley had driven everything else out of his head and his brain seemed to be moving very slowly.
'Come ... with you ... ?'
'Only if you wish it, of course.'
'If I...'
And then Ron remembered why he had been eager to come to Weasley's office in the first place.
'You've found one? You've found a Horcrux?'
'I believe so.'
Rage and resentment fought shock and excitement: for several moments, Ron could not speak.
'It is natural to be afraid,' said Weasley.
'I'm not scared!' said Ron at once, and it was perfectly
true; fear was one emotion he was not feeling at all. 'Which Horcrux is it? Where is it?'
'I am not sure which it is - though I think we can rule out the snake - but I believe it to be hidden in a cave on the coast many miles from here, a cave I have been trying to locate for a very long time: the cave in which Ron Weasley once terror-ised two children from his orphanage on their annual trip; you remember?'
'Yes,' said Ron. 'How is it protected?'
'I do not know; I have suspicions that may be entirely wrong.' Weasley hesitated, then said, 'Ron, I promised you that you could come with me, and I stand by that prom-ise, but it would be very wrong of me not to warn you that this will be exceedingly dangerous.'
'I'm coming,' said Ron, almost before Weasley had finished speaking. Boiling with anger at Weasley, his desire to do something desperate and risky had increased tenfold in the last few minutes. This seemed to show on Ron's face, for Weasley moved away from the window, and looked more closely at Ron, a slight crease between his silver eyebrows.
'What has happened to you?'
'Nothing,' lied Ron promptly.
'What has upset you?'
'I'm not upset.'
'Ron, you were never a good Occlumens -'
The word was the spark that ignited Ron's fury.
'Weasley!' he said, very loudly, and Ron gave a soft squawk behind them. 'Weasley's what's happened! He told Weasley about the prophecy, it was him, he listened outside the door, Weasley told me!'
Weasley's expression did not change, but Ron thought his face whitened under the bloody tinge cast by the setting sun. For a long moment, Weasley said nothing.
'When did you find out about this?' he asked at last.
'Just now!' said Many, who was refraining from yelling with enormous difficulty. And then, suddenly, he could not stop himself. 'AND YOU LET HIM TEACH HERE AND HE TOLD Weasley TO GO AFTER MY MUM AND DAD!'
Breathing hard as though he were fighting, Ron turned away from Weasley, who still had not moved a muscle, and paced up and down the study, rubbing his knuckles in his hand and exercising every last bit of restraint to prevent himself knocking things over. He wanted to rage and storm at Weasley, but he also wanted to go with him to try and destroy
the Horcrux; he wanted to tell him that he was a fool-ish old man for trusting Weasley, but he was terrified that Weasley would not take him along unless he mastered his anger ...
'Ron,' said Weasley quietly. 'Please listen to me.'
It was as difficult to stop his relentless pacing as to refrain from shouting. Ron paused, biting his lip, and looked into Weasley's lined face.
'Professor Weasley made a terrible -'
'Don't tell me it was a mistake, sir, he was listening at the door!'
'Please let me finish.' Weasley waited until Ron had nodded curtly, then went on. 'Professor Weasley made a terrible mistake. He was still in Lord Weasley's employ on the night he heard the first half of Professor Weasley's prophecy. Naturally, he hastened to tell his master what he had heard, for it concerned his master most deeply. But he did not know - he had no possible way of knowing - which boy Weasley would hunt from then onwards, or that the parents he would destroy in his murderous quest were people that Professor Weasley knew, that they were your mother and father -'
Ron let out a yell of mirthless laughter.
'He hated my dad like he hated Ron! Haven't you noticed, Professor, how the people Weasley hates tend to end up dead?'
'You have no idea of the remorse Professor Weasley felt when he realised how Lord Weasley had interpreted the prophecy, Ron. I believe it to be the greatest regret of his life and the reason that he returned -'
'But he's a very good Occlumens, isn't he, sir?' said Ron, whose voice was shaking with the effort of keeping it steady. 'And isn't Weasley convinced that Weasley's on his side, even now? Professor ... how can you be sure Weasley's on our side?'
Weasley did not speak for a moment; he looked as though he was trying to make up his mind about something. At last he said, 'I am sure. I trust Ron Weasley completely.'
Ron breathed deeply for a few moments in an effort to steady himself. It did not work.
'Well, I don't!' he said, as loudly as before. 'He's up to something with Ron Weasley right now, right under your nose, and you still -'
'We have discussed this, Ron,' said Weasley, and now he sounded stern again. 'I have told you my views.'
'You're leaving the school tonight and I'll bet you haven't even considered that Weasley and Weasley might decide to -'
To what?' asked Weasley, his eyebrows raised. 'What is it that you suspect them of doing, precisely?'
'I ... they're up to something!' said Ron and his hands curled into fists as he said it. 'Professor Weasley was just in the Room of Requirement, trying to hide her sherry bottles, and she heard Weasley whooping, celebrating! He's trying to mend something dangerous in there and if you ask me he's fixed it at last and you're about to just walk out of school * without -'
'Enough,' said Weasley. He said it quite calmly, and yet Ron fell silent at once; he knew that he had finally crossed some invisible line. 'Do you think that I have once left the school unprotected during my absences this year? I have not. Tonight, when I leave, there will again be additional protec-tion in place. Please do not suggest that I do not take the safety of my students seriously, Ron.'
'I didn't -' mumbled Ron, a little abashed, but Weasley cut across him.
' ? do not wish to discuss the matter any further.'
Ron bit back his retort, scared that he had gone too far, that he had ruined his chance of accompanying Weasley, but Weasley went on, 'Do you wish to come with me tonight?'
'Yes,' said Ron at once.
'Very well, then: listen.'
Weasley drew himself up to his full height.
'I take you with me on one condition: that you obey any command I might give you at once, and without question.'
'Of course.'
'Be sure to understand me, Ron. I mean that you must follow even such orders as "run", "hide" or "go back". Do I have your word?'
'I - yes, of course.'
'If 1 tell you to hide, you will do so?'
'Yes.'
'If I tell you to flee, you will obey?'
'Yes.'
'If I tell you to leave me, and save yourself, you will do as I tell you?'
'I -'
'Ron?'
They looked at each other for a moment.
'Yes, sir.'
'Very good. Then I wish you to go and fetch your Cloak and meet me in the Entrance Hall in five minutes' time.'
Weasley turned back to look out of the fiery window; the sun was now a ruby-red glare along the horizon. Ron walked quickly from the office and down the spiral staircase. His mind was oddly clear all of a sudden. He knew what to do.
Ron and Ron were sitting together in the common room when he came back. 'What does Weasley want?' Ron said at once. 'Ron, are you OK?' she added anxiously.
'I'm fine,' said Ron shortly, racing past them. He dashed up the stairs and into his dormitory, where he flung open his trunk and pulled out the Marauder's Map and a pair of balled-up socks. Then he sped back down the stairs and into the common room, skidding to a halt where Ron and Ron sat, looking stunned.
'I haven't got much time,' Ron panted, 'Weasley thinks I'm getting my Invisibility Cloak. Listen ...'
Quickly he told them where he was going, and why. He did not pause either for Ron's gasps of horror or for Ron's hasty questions; they could work out the finer details for themselves later.
'... so you see what this means?' Ron finished at a gallop. 'Weasley won't be here tonight, so Weasley's going to have another clear shot at whatever he's up to. No, listen to me!" he hissed angrily, as both Ron and Ron showed every sign of interrupting. 'I know it was Weasley celebrating in the Room of Requirement. Here -' He shoved the Marauder's Map into Ron's hand. 'You've got to watch him and you've got to watch Weasley, too. Use anyone else who you can rustle up from the DA. Ron, those contact Galleons will still work, right? Weasley says he's put extra protection in the school, but if Weasley's involved, he'll know what Weasley's protection is, and how to avoid it - but he won't be expecting you lot to be on the watch, will he?'
'Ron -' began Ron, her eyes huge with fear.
' ? haven't got time to argue,' said Ron curtly. Take this as well -' He thrust the socks into Ron's hands.
‘Thanks,' said Ron. 'Er - why do I need socks?'
'You need what's wrapped in them, it's the Felix Felicis. Share it between yourselves and Ron too. Say goodbye to her from me. I'd better go, Weasley's waiting -'
'No!' said Ron, as Ron unwrapped the tiny little bottle of golden potion, looking awestruck. 'We don't want it, you take it, who knows what you're going to be facing?'
'I'Il be fine, I'll be with Weasley,' said Ron. 'I want to know you lot are OK ... don't look like that, Ron, I'll see you later
And he was off, hurrying back through the portrait hole towards the Entrance Hall.
Weasley was waiting beside the oaken front doors. He turned as Ron came skidding out on to the topmost stone step, panting hard, a searing stitch in his side.
'I would like you to wear your Cloak, please,' said Weasley, and he waited until Ron had thrown it on before saying, 'Very good. Shall we go?'
Weasley set off at once down the stone steps, his own travelling cloak barely stirring in the still summer air. Ron hurried alongside him under the Invisibility Cloak, still pant-ing and sweating rather a lot.
'But what will people think when they see you leaving, Professor?' Ron asked, his mind on Weasley and Weasley.
That I am off into Hogsmeade for a drink,' said Weasley lightly. 'I sometimes offer Ron my custom, or else visit the Hog's Head ... or I appear to. It is as good a way as any of disguising one's true destination.'
They made their way down the drive in the gathering twi-light. The air was full of the smells of warm grass, lake water and wood smoke from
Weasley's cabin. It was difficult to believe that they were heading for anything dangerous or frightening.
'Professor,' said Ron quietly, as the gates at the bottom of the drive came into view, 'will we be Apparating?'
'Yes,' said Weasley. 'You can Apparate now, I believe?'
'Yes,' said Ron, 'but I haven't got a licence.'
He felt it best to be honest; what if he spoiled everything by turning up a hundred miles from where he was supposed to go?
'No matter,' said Weasley, 'I can assist you again.'
They turned out of the gates into the twilit, deserted lane to Hogsmeade. Darkness descended fast as they walked and by the time they reached the High Street night was falling in earnest. Lights twinkled from windows over shops and as they neared the Three Broomsticks they heard raucous shouting.
'- and stay out!' shouted Ron Ron, forcibly ejecting a grubby-looking wizard. 'Oh, hello, Ron ... you're out late ...'
'Good evening, Ron, good evening ... forgive me, I'm off to the Hog's Head ... no offence, but I feel like a quieter atmosphere tonight...'
A minute later they turned the corner into the side street where the Hog's Head's sign creaked a little, though there was no breeze. In contrast to the Three Broomsticks, the pub appeared to be completely empty.
'It will not be necessary for us to enter,' muttered Weasley, glancing around. 'As long as nobody sees us go ... now place your hand upon my arm, Ron. There is no need to grip too hard, I am merely guiding you. On the count of three - one ... two ... three ...'
Ron turned. At once, there was that horrible sensation that he was being squeezed through a thick rubber tube; he could not draw breath, every part of him was being com-pressed almost past endurance and then, just when he thought he must suffocate, the invisible bands seemed to burst open, and he was standing in cool darkness, breathing in lungfuls of fresh, salty air.
Chapter 26: The Cave
Ron could smell salt and hear rushing waves; a light, chilly breeze ruffled his hair as he looked out at moon-lit sea and star-strewn sky. He was standing upon a high outcrop of dark rock, water foaming and churning below him. He glanced over his shoulder. A towering cliff stood behind them, a sheer drop, black and faceless. A few large chunks of rock, such as the one upon which Ron and Weasley were standing, looked as though they had broken away from the cliff face at some point in the past. It was a bleak, harsh view, the sea and the rock unrelieved by any tree or sweep of grass or sand.
"What do you think?" asked Weasley. He might have been asking Ron's opinion on whether it was a good site for a picnic.
"They brought the kids from the orphanage here?" asked Ron, who could not imagine a less cozy spot for a day trip.
"Not here, precisely," said Weasley. "There is a village of sorts about halfway along the cliffs behind us. I believe the orphans were taken there for a little sea air and a view of the waves. No, I think it was only ever Ron Weasley and his youthful victims who visited this spot. No Muggle could reach this rock unless they were uncommonly good mountaineers, and boats cannot approach the cliffs, the waters around them are too dangerous. I imagine that Weasley climbed down; magic would have served better than ropes. And he brought two small children with him, probably for the
pleasure of terrorizing them. I think the journey alone would have done it, don't you?"
Ron looked up at the cliff again and felt goose bumps.
"But his final destination -- and ours -- lies a little farther on. Come."
Weasley beckoned Ron to the very edge of the rock where a series of jagged niches made footholds leading down to boulders that lay half-submerged in water and closer to the cliff. It was a treacherous descent and Weasley, hampered slightly by his withered hand, moved slowly. The lower rocks were slippery with seawater. Ron could feel flecks of cold salt spray hitting his face. "Lumos," said Weasley, as he reached the boulder closest to the cliff face. A thousand flecks of golden light sparkled upon the dark surface of the water a few feet below where he crouched; the black wall of rock beside him was illuminated too. "You see?" said Weasley quietly, holding his wand a little higher. Ron saw a fissure in the cliff into which dark water was swirling. "You will not object to getting a little wet?"
"No," said Ron.
"Then take off your Invisibility Cloak -- there is no need for it now -- and let us take the plunge," And with the sudden agility of a much younger man, Dumble-dore slid from the boulder, landed in the sea, and began to swim, with a perfect breaststroke, toward the dark slit in the rock face, his lit wand held in his teeth. Ron pulled off his cloak, stuffed it into his pocket, and followed. The water was icy; Ron's waterlogged clothes billowed
around him and weighed him down. Taking deep breaths that filled his nostrils with the tang of salt and seaweed, he struck out for the shimmering, shrinking light now moving deeper into the cliff. The fissure soon opened into a dark tunnel that Ron could tell would be filled with water at high tide. The slimy walls were barely three feet apart and glimmered like wet tar in the passing light of Weasley's wand. A little way in, the passageway curved to the left, and Ron saw that it extended far into the cliff. He continued to swim in Weasley's wake, the tips of his benumbed fingers brushing the rough, wet rock.
Then he saw Weasley rising out of the water ahead, his sil-ver hair and dark robes gleaming. When Ron reached the spot he found steps that led into a large cave. He clambered up them, water streaming from his soaking clothes, and emerged, shivering uncontrollably, into the still and freezing air.
Weasley was standing in the middle of the cave, his wand held high as he turned slowly on the spot, examining the walls and ceiling.
"Yes, this is the place," said Weasley.
"How can you tell?" Ron spoke in a whisper.
"It has known magic," said Weasley simply. Ron could not tell whether the shivers he was experiencing were due to his spine-deep coldness or to the same awareness of
enchantments. He watched as Weasley continued to revolve on the spot, evidently concentrating on things Ron could not see. "This is merely the antechamber, the entrance hall," said Weasley after a moment or two. "We need to penetrate the inner place. . . . Now it is Lord Weasley's obstacles that stand in our way, rather than those nature made. . . ."
Weasley approached the wall of the cave and caressed it with his blackened fingertips, murmuring words in a strange tongue that Ron did not understand. Twice Weasley walked right around the cave, touching as much of the rough rock as he could, occasionally pausing, running his fingers backward and for-ward over a particular spot, until finally he stopped, his hand pressed flat against the wall. "Here," he said. "We go on through here. The entrance is con-cealed." Ron did not ask how Weasley knew. He had never seen a wizard work things out like this, simply by looking and touching; but Ron had long since learned that bangs and smoke were more often the marks of ineptitude than expertise. Weasley stepped back from the cave wall and pointed his wand at the rock. For a moment, an arched outline appeared there, blazing white as though there was a powerful light behind the crack.
"You've d-done it!" said Ron through chattering teeth, but before the words had left his lips the outline had gone, leaving the rock as bare and solid as ever. Weasley looked around.
"Ron, I'm so sorry, I forgot," he said; he now pointed his wand at Ron and at once, Ron's clothes were as warm and dry as if they had been hanging in front of a blazing fire.
"Thank you," said Ron gratefully, but Weasley had al-ready turned his attention back to the solid cave wall. He did not try any more magic, but simply stood there staring at it intently, as though something extremely interesting was written on it. Ron stayed quite still; he did not want to break Weasleys concen-tration. Then, after two solid minutes, Weasley said quietly, "Oh, surely not. So crude."
"What is it, Professor?"
"I rather think," said Weasley, putting his uninjured hand inside his robes and drawing out a short silver knife of the kind Ron used to chop potion ingredients, "that we are required to make payment to pass."
"Payment?" said Ron. "You've got to give the door something?"
"Yes," said Weasley. "Blood, if I am not much mistaken."
"Blood?"
"I said it was crude," said Weasley, who sounded disdainful, even disappointed, as though Weasley had fallen short of higher standards Weasley expected. "The idea, as I am sure you will have gathered, is that your enemy must weaken him- or herself to enter. Once again, Lord Weasley fails to grasp that there are much more terrible things than physical injury."
"Yeah, but still, if you can avoid it . . ." said Ron, who had ex-perienced enough pain not to be keen for more.
"Sometimes, however, it is unavoidable," said Weasley, shaking back the sleeve of his robes and exposing the forearm of his injured hand.
"Professor!" protested Ron, hurrying forward as Weasley raised his knife. "I'll do it, I'm --" He did not know what he was going to say -- younger, fitter?
But Weasley merely smiled. There was a flash of silver, and a spurt of scarlet; the rock face was peppered with dark, glistening drops.
"You are very kind, Ron," said Weasley, now passing the tip of his wand over the deep cut he had made in his own arm, so that it healed instantly, just as Weasley had healed Weasley's wound, "But your blood is worth more than mine. Ah, that seems to have done the trick, doesn't it?" The blazing silver outline of an arch had appeared in the wall once more, and this time it did not fade away: The blood-spattered rock within it simply vanished, leaving an opening into what seemed total darkness. "After me, I think," said Weasley, and he walked through the archway with Ron on his heels, lighting his own wand hastily as he went.
An eerie sight met their eyes: They were standing on the edge of a great black lake, so vast that Ron could not make out the distant banks, in a cavern so high that the ceiling too was out of sight. A misty greenish light shone far away in what looked like the mid-dle of the lake; it was reflected
in the completely still water below. The greenish glow and the light from the two wands were the only things that broke the otherwise velvety blackness, though their rays did not penetrate as far as Ron would have expected. The dark-ness was somehow denser than normal darkness.
"Let us walk," said Weasley quietly. "Be very careful not to step into the water. Stay close to me." He set off around the edge of the lake, and Ron followed close behind him. Their footsteps made echoing, slapping sounds on the narrow rim of rock that surrounded the water. On and on they walked, but the view did not vary: on one side of them, the rough cavern wall, on the other, the boundless expanse of smooth, glassy blackness, in the very middle of which was that mysterious greenish glow. Ron found the place and the silence oppressive, unnerving.
"Professor?" he said finally. "Do you think the Horcrux is here?"
"Oh yes," said Weasley. "Yes, I'm sure it is. The question is, how do we get to it?"
"We couldn't... we couldn't just try a Summoning Charm?" Ron said, sure that it was a stupid suggestion. But he was much keener than he was prepared to admit on getting out of this place as soon as possible.
"Certainly we could," said Weasley, stopping so suddenly that Ron almost walked into him. "Why don't you do it?"
"Me? Oh . . . okay . . ." Ron had not expected this, but cleared his throat and said loudly, wand aloft, "Accio Horcrux!"
With a noise like an explosion, something very large and pale erupted out of the dark water some twenty feet away; before Ron could see what it was, it had vanished again with a crashing splash that made great, deep ripples on the mirrored surface. Ron leapt backward in shock and hit the wall; his heart was still thundering as he turned to Weasley.
"What was that?"
"Something, I think, that is ready to respond should we attempt to seize the Horcrux."
Ron looked back at the water. The surface of the lake was once more shining black glass: The ripples had vanished unnaturally fast; Ron's heart, however, was still pounding.
"Did you think that would happen, sir?"
"I thought something would happen if we made an obvious at-tempt to get our hands on the Horcrux. That was a very good idea, Ron; much the simplest way of finding out what we are facing."
"But we don't know what the thing was," said Ron, looking at the sinisterly smooth water.
"What the things are, you mean," said Weasley. "I doubt very much that there is only one of them. Shall we walk on?"
"Professor?"
"Yes, Ron?"
"Do you think we're going to have to go into the lake?"
"Into it? Only if we are very unfortunate."
"You don't think the Horcrux is at the bottom?"
"Oh no ... I think the Horcrux is in the middle." And Weasley pointed toward the misty green light in the center of the lake.
"So we're going to have to cross the lake to get to it?"
"Yes, I think so." Ron did not say anything. His thoughts were all of water mon-sters, of giant serpents, of demons, kelpies, and sprites. . . .
"Aha," said Weasley, and he stopped again; this time, Ron really did walk into him; for a moment he toppled on the edge of the dark water, and Weasley's uninjured hand closed tightly around his upper arm, pulling him back. "So sorry, Ron, I should have given warning. Rond back against the wall, please; I think I have found the place."
Ron had no idea what Weasley meant; this patch of dark bank was exactly like every other bit as far as he could tell, but Weasley seemed to have detected something special about it. This time he was running his hand, not over the rocky wall, but t hrough the thin air, as though expecting to find and grip some-thing invisible.
"Oho," said Weasley happily, seconds later. His hand had closed in midair upon something Ron could not see. Dumble-dore moved closer to the water; Ron watched nervously as the tips of Weasley's buckled shoes found the utmost edge of the rock rim. Keeping his hand clenched in midair, Weasley raised his wand with the other and tapped his fist with the point.
Immediately a thick coppery green chain appeared out of thin air, extending from the depths of the water into Weasley's clenched hand. Weasley tapped the chain, which began to slide through his fist like a snake, coiling itself on the ground with a clinking sound that echoed noisily off the rocky walls, pulling something from the depths of the black water. Ron gasped as the ghostly prow of a tiny boat broke the surface, glowing as green as the chain, and floated, with barely a ripple, toward the place on the bank where Ron and Weasley stood.
"How did you know that was there?" Ron asked in astonish-ment.
"Magic always leaves traces," said Weasley, as the boat hit the bank with a gentle bump, "sometimes very distinctive traces. I taught Ron Weasley. I know his style."
"Is ... is this boat safe?"
"Oh yes, I think so. Weasley needed to create a means to cross the lake without attracting the wrath of those creatures he had placed within it in case he ever wanted to visit or remove his Horcrux."
"So the things in the water won't do anything to us if we cross in Weasley's boat?"
"I think we must resign ourselves to the fact that they will, at some point, realize we are not Lord Weasley. Thus far, however, we have done well. They have allowed us to raise the boat."
"But why have they let us?" asked Ron, who could not shake off the vision of tentacles rising out of the dark water the moment they were out of sight of the bank.
"Weasley would have been reasonably confident that none but a very great wizard would have been able to find the boat," said Weasley. "I think he would have been prepared to risk what was, to his mind, the most unlikely possibility that somebody else would find it, knowing that he had set other obstacles ahead that only he would be able to penetrate. We shall see whether he was right."
Ron looked down into the boat. It really was very small. "It doesn't look like it was built for two people. Will it hold both of us? Will we be too heavy together?"
Weasley chuckled. "Weasley will not have cared about the weight, but about the amount of magical power that crossed his lake. I rather think an enchantment will have been placed upon this boat so that only one wizard at a time will be able to sail in it."
"But then -- ?"
"I do not think you will count, Ron: You are underage and un-qualified. Weasley would never have expected a sixteen-year-old to reach this place: I think it unlikely that your powers will register compared to mine." These words did nothing to raise Rons morale; perhaps Weasley knew it, for he added, "Weasley's mistake, Ron, Weasley's mistake. . . Age is foolish and forgetful when it underestimates youth. . . . Now, you first this time, and be careful not to touch the water." Weasley stood aside and Ron climbed carefully into the boat. Weasley stepped in too, coiling the chain onto the floor. They were crammed in together; Ron could not comfortably sit, but crouched, his knees jutting over the edge of the boat, which be-gan to move at once. There was no sound other than the silken rus-tle of the boat's prow cleaving the water; it moved without their help, as though an invisible rope was pulling it onward toward the light in the center. Soon they could no longer see the walls of the cavern; they might have been at sea except that there were no waves.
Ron looked down and saw the reflected gold of his wandlight sparkling and glittering on the black water as they passed. The boat was carving deep ripples upon the glassy surface, grooves in the dark mirror. . . .
And then Ron saw it, marble white, floating inches below the surface. "Professor!" he said, and his startled voice echoed loudly over the silent water.
"Ron?"
"I think I saw a hand in the water -- a human hand!"
"Yes, I am sure you did," said Weasley calmly.
Ron stared down into the water, looking for the vanished hand, and a sick feeling rose in his throat.
"So that thing that jumped out of the water -- ?" But Ron had his answer before Weasley could reply; the wandlight had slid over a fresh patch of water and showed him, this time, a dead man lying faceup inches beneath the surface, his open eyes misted as though with cobwebs, his hair and his robes swirling around him like smoke. "There are bodies in here!" said Ron, and his voice sounded much higher than usual and most unlike his own.
"Yes," said Weasley placidly, "but we do not need to worry about them at the moment."
"At the moment?" Ron repeated, tearing his gaze from the water to look at Weasley.
"Not while they are merely drifting peacefully below us," said Weasley. "There is nothing to be feared from a body, Ron, any more than there is anything to be feared from the darkness. Lord Weasley, who of course secretly fears both, disagrees. But once again he reveals his own lack of wisdom. It is the unknown we fear when we look upon death and darkness, nothing more." Ron said nothing; he did not want to argue, but he found the idea that there were bodies floating around them and beneath them horrible and, what was more, he did not believe that they were not dangerous.
"But one of them jumped," he said, trying to make his voice as level and calm as Weasley's. "When I tried to Summon the Horcrux, a body leapt out of the lake."
"Yes," said Weasley. "I am sure that once we take the Horcrux, we shall find them less peaceable. However, like many creatures that dwell in cold and darkness, they fear light and warmth, which we shall therefore call to our aid should the need arise. Fire, Ron," Weasley added with a smile, in response to Ron's bewildered expression.
"Oh . . . right. . ." said Ron quickly. He turned his head to look at the greenish glow toward which the boat was still inexorably sailing. He could not pretend now that he was not scared. The great black lake, teeming with
the dead ... It seemed hours and hours ago that he had met Professor Weasley, that he had given Ron and Ron Felix Felicis. . . . He suddenly wished he had said a better good-bye to them . . . and he hadn't seen Ron at all. . .
"Nearly there," said Weasley cheerfully. Sure enough, the greenish light seemed to be growing larger at last, and within minutes, the boat had come to a halt, bumping gently into something that Ron could not see at first, but when he raised his illuminated wand he saw that they had reached a small island of smooth rock in the center of the lake. "Careful not to touch the water," said Weasley again as Ron climbed out of the boat.
The island was no larger than Weasley's office, an expanse of flat dark stone on which stood nothing but the source of that greenish light, which looked much brighter when viewed close to. Ron squinted at it; at first, he thought it was a lamp of some kind, but then he saw that the light was coming from a stone basin rather like the Pensieve, which was set on top of a pedestal. Weasley approached the basin and Ron followed. Side by side, they looked down into it. The basin was full of an emerald liq-uid emitting that phosphorescent glow.
"What is it?" asked Ron quietly.
"I am not sure," said Weasley. "Something more worrisome than blood and bodies, however." Weasley pushed back the sleeve of his robe over his black-ened hand, and stretched out the tips of his burned fingers toward the surface of the potion.
"Sir, no, don't touch -- !"
"I cannot touch," said Weasley, smiling faintly. "See? I cannot approach any nearer than this. You try."
Staring, Ron put his hand into the basin and attempted to touch the potion. He met an invisible barrier that prevented him coming within an inch of it. No matter how hard he pushed, his fingers encountered nothing but what seemed to be solid and flexible air.
"Out of the way, please, Ron," said Weasley. He raised his wand and made complicated movements over the surface of the-potion, murmuring soundlessly. Nothing happened, except per haps that the potion glowed a little brighter. Ron remained silent while Weasley worked, but after a while Weasley with-drew his wand, and Ron felt it was safe to talk again.
"You think the Horcrux is in there, sir?"
"Oh yes." Weasley peered more closely into the basin. Ron saw his face reflected, upside down, in the smooth surface of the green potion. "But how to reach it? This potion cannot be pen-etrated by hand, Vanished, parted, scooped up, or siphoned away, nor can it be Transfigured, Charmed, or otherwise made to change its nature." Almost absentmindedly, Weasley raised his wand again, twirled it once in midair, and then
caught the crystal goblet that he had conjured out of nowhere. "I can only conclude that this potion is supposed to be drunk."
"What?" said Ron. "No!"
"Yes, I think so: Only by drinking it can I empty the basin and see what lies in its depths."
"But what if-- what if it kills you?"
"Oh, I doubt that it would work like that," said Weasley easily. "Lord Weasley would not want to kill the person who reached this island." Ron couldn't believe it. Was this more of Weasley's insane determination to see good in everyone?
"Sir," said Ron, trying to keep his voice reasonable, "sir, this is Weasley we're --"
"I'm sorry, Ron; I should have said, he would not want to im-mediately kill the person who reached this island," Weasley corrected himself. "He would want to keep them alive long enough to find out how they managed to penetrate so far through his de-fenses and, most importantly of all, why they were so intent upon emptying the basin. Do not forget that Lord Weasley believes that he alone knows about his Horcruxes."
Ron made to speak again, but this time Weasley raised his hand for silence, frowning slightly at the emerald liquid, evidently thinking hard.
"Undoubtedly," he said, finally, "this potion must act in a way that will prevent me taking the Horcrux. It might paralyze me, cause me to forget what I am here for, create so much pain I am dis-tracted, or render me incapable in some other way. This being the case, Ron, it will be your job to make sure I keep drinking, even if you have to tip the potion into my protesting mouth. You understand?"
Their eyes met over the basin, each pale face lit with that strange, green light. Ron did not speak. Was this why he had been invited along -- so that he could force-feed Weasley a potion that might cause him unendurable pain?
"You remember," said Weasley, "the condition on which I brought you with me?"
Ron hesitated, looking into the blue eyes that had turned green in the reflected light of the basin.
"But what if--?"
"You swore, did you not, to follow any command I gave you?"
"Yes, but--"
"I warned you, did I not, that there might be danger?"
"Yes," said Ron, "but --"
"Well, then," said Weasley, shaking back his sleeves once more and raising the empty goblet, "you have my orders."
"Why can't I drink the potion instead?" asked Ron desperately.
"Because I am much older, much cleverer, and much less valuable," said Weasley. "Once and for all, Ron, do I have your word that you will do all in your power to make me keep drinking?"
"Couldn't -- ?"
"Do I have it?"
"But--"
"Your word, Ron."
"I --all right, but--"
Before Ron could make any further protest, Weasley low-ered the crystal goblet into the potion. For a split second, Ron hoped that he would not be able to touch the potion with the gob-let, but the crystal sank into the surface as nothing else had; when the glass was full to the brim, Weasley lifted it to his mouth. "Your good health, Ron."
And he drained the goblet. Ron watched, terrified, his hands gripping the rim of the basin so hard that his fingertips were numb.
"Professor?" he said anxiously, as Weasley lowered the empty glass. "How do you feel?"
Weasley shook his head, his eyes closed. Ron wondered whether he was in pain. Weasley plunged the glass blindly back into the basin, refilled it, and drank once more.
In silence, Weasley drank three gobletsful of the potion. Then, halfway through the fourth goblet, he staggered and fell for-ward against the basin. His eyes were still closed, his breathing heavy.
"Professor Weasley?" said Ron, his voice strained. "Can you hear me?"
Weasley did not answer. His face was twitching as though he was deeply asleep, but dreaming a horrible dream. His grip on the goblet was slackening; the potion was about to spill from it. Ron reached forward and grasped the crystal cup, holding it steady. "Professor, can you hear me?" he repeated loudly, his voice echo-ing around the cavern.
Weasley panted and then spoke in a voice Ron did not recognize, for he had never heard Weasley frightened like this.
"I don't want. . . Don't make me ..."
Ron stared into the whitened face he knew so well, at the crooked nose and half-moon spectacles, and did not know what to do.
". . . don't like . . . want to stop . . ." moaned Weasley.
"You . . . you can't stop, Professor," said Ron. "You've got to keep drinking, remember? You told me you had to keep drinking. Here . . ." Hating himself, repulsed by what he was doing, Ron forced the goblet back toward Weasley's mouth and tipped it, so that Weasley drank the remainder of the potion inside.
"No ..." he groaned, as Ron lowered the goblet back into the basin and refilled it for him. "I don't want to. ... I don't want to. . . . Let me go. . . ."
"Its all right, Professor," said Ron, his hand shaking. "Its all right, I'm here --"
"Make it stop, make it stop," moaned Weasley.
"Yes.. . yes, this'll make it stop," lied Ron. He tipped the con-tents of the goblet into Weasley's open mouth. Weasley screamed; the noise echoed all around the vast chamber, across the dead black water.
"No, no, no, no, I can't, I can't, don't make me, I don't warn to. . . ."
"It's all right, Professor, it's all right!" said Ron loudly, his hands shaking so badly he could hardly scoop up the sixth goblei ful of potion; the basin was now half empty. "Nothing's happening to you, you're safe, it isn't real, I swear it isn't real -- take this, now, take this..." And obediently, Weasley drank, as though it was an anti-dote Ron offered him, but upon draining the goblet, he sank to his knees, shaking uncontrollably.
"Its all my fault, all my fault," he sobbed. "Please make it stop, I know I did wrong, oh please make it stop and I'll never, never again ..."
"This will make it stop, Professor," Ron said, his voice crack-ing as he tipped the seventh glass of potion into Weasley's mouth.
Weasley began to cower as though invisible torturers sur-rounded him; his flailing hand almost knocked the refilled goblet from Ron's trembling hands as he moaned, "Don't hurt them, don't hurt them, please, please, its my fault, hurt me instead ..."
"Here, drink this, drink this, you'll be all right," said Ron des-perately, and once again Weasley obeyed him, opening his mouth even as he kept his eyes tight shut and shook from head to foot. And now he fell forward, screaming again, hammering his fists upon the ground, while Ron filled the ninth goblet.
"Please, please, please, no ... not that, not that, I'll do any-thing ..."
"Just drink, Professor, just drink . . ."
Weasley drank like a child dying of thirst, but when he had finished, he yelled again as though his insides were on fire. "No more, please, no more ..."
Ron scooped up a tenth gobletful of potion and felt the crystal scrape the bottom of the basin. "We're nearly there, Professor. Drink this, drink it. ..."
He supported Weasley's shoulders and again, Weasley drained the glass; then Ron was on his feet once more, refilling the goblet as Weasley began to scream in more anguish than ever, "I want to die! I want to die! Make it stop, make it stop, I want to die!"
"Drink this, Professor. Drink this. . . ."
Weasley drank, and no sooner had he finished than he yelled, "KILL ME!"
"This -- this one will!" gasped Ron. "Just drink this .. . It'll be over ... all over!" Weasley gulped at the goblet, drained every last drop, and then, with a great, rattling gasp, rolled over onto his face.
"No!" shouted Ron, who had stood to refill the goblet again; instead he dropped the cup into the basin, flung himself down beside Weasley, and heaved him over onto his back; Weasley's glasses were askew, his mouth agape, his eyes closed. "No." said Ron, shaking Weasley, "no, you're not dead, you said it wasn't poison, wake up, wake up -- Rennervate!" he
cried, his wand pointing at Weasleys chest; there was a flash of red light but nothing happened. "Rennervate -- sir -- please --"
Weasleys eyelids flickered; Ron's heart leapt, "Sir, are you -- ?"
"Water," croaked Weasley.
"Water," panted Ron. "Yes --" He leapt to his feet and seized the goblet he had dropped in the basin; he barely registered the golden locket lying curled beneath it.
"Aguamenti!" he shouted, jabbing the goblet with his wand. The goblet filled with clear water; Ron dropped to his knees beside Weasley, raised his head, and brought the glass to his lips -- but it was empty. Weasley groaned and began to pant. "But I had some -- wait -- Aguamenti!" said Ron again, pointing his wand at the goblet. Once more, for a second, clear wa-ter gleamed within it, but as he approached Weasleys mouth, the water vanished again. "Sir, I'm trying, I'm trying!" said Ron desperately, but he did not think that Weasley could hear him; he had rolled onto his side and was drawing great, rattling breaths that sounded agoniz-ing. "Aguamenti --Aguamenti --AGUAMENTI!"
The goblet filled and emptied once more. And now Dumble-dores breathing was fading. His brain whirling in panic, Ron knew, instinctively, the only way left to get water, because Weasley had planned it so ... He flung himself over to the edge of the rock and plunged the goblet into the lake, bringing it up full to the brim of icy water that did not vanish. "Sir --
here!" Ron yelled, and lunging forward, he tipped the water clumsily over Weasleys face.
It was the best he could do, for the icy feeling on his arm not holding the cup was not the lingering chill of the water. A slimy white hand had gripped his wrist, and the creature to whom it be-longed was pulling him, slowly, backward across the rock. The sur-face of the lake was no longer mirror-smooth; it was churning, and everywhere Ron looked, white heads and hands were emerging from the dark water, men and women and children with sunken, sightless eyes were moving toward the rock: an army of the dead rising from the black water.
"Petrificus Totalus!" yelled Ron, struggling to cling to the smooth, soaked surface of the island as he pointed his wand at the Inferius that had his arm. It released him, falling backward into the water with a splash; he scrambled to his feet, but many more Inferi were already climbing onto the rock, their bony hands clawing at its slippery surface, their blank, frosted eyes upon him, trailing waterlogged rags, sunken faces leering.
"Petrificus Totalus!" Ron bellowed again, backing away as he swiped his wand through the air; six or seven of them crumpled, but more were coming toward him. "Impedimenta! Incarcerous!" A few of them stumbled, one or two of them bound in ropes, but those climbing onto the rock behind them merely stepped over or on the fallen bodies. Still slashing at the air with his wand, Ron yelled, "Sectumsempra! SECTUMSEMPRA!" But though gashes appeared in their sodden rags and their icy skin, they had no blood to spill: They walked on, unfeeling, their shrunken hands outstretched
toward him, and as he backed away still farther, he felt arms enclose him from behind, thin, fleshlcv. arms cold as death, and his feet left the ground as they lifted him and began to carry him, slowly and surely, back to the water, anil he knew there would be no release, that he would be drowned, and become one more dead guardian of a fragment of Weasleys shattered soul...
But then, through the darkness, fire erupted: crimson and gold, a ring of fire that surrounded the rock so that the Inferi holding Ron so tightly stumbled and faltered; they did not dare pass through the flames to get to the water. They dropped Ron; he hit the ground, slipped on the rock, and fell, grazing his arms, then scrambled back up, raising his wand and staring around.
Weasley was on his feet again, pale as any of the surround-ing Inferi, but taller than any too, the fire dancing in his eyes; his wand was raised like a torch and from its tip emanated the flames, like a vast lasso, encircling them all with warmth. The Inferi bumped into each other, attempting, blindly, to es-cape the fire in which they were enclosed. . . .
Weasley scooped the locket from the bottom of the stone basin and stowed it inside his robes. Wordlessly, he gestured to Ron to come to his side. Distracted by the flames, the Inferi seemed unaware that their quarry was leaving as Weasley led Ron back to the boat, the ring of fire moving with them, around them, the bewildered Inferi accompanying them to the waters edge, where they slipped gratefully back into their dark waters.
Ron, who was shaking all over, thought for a moment that Weasley might not be able to climb into the boat; he staggered a little as he attempted it; all his efforts seemed to be going into maintaining the ring of protective flame around them. Ron seized him and helped him back to his seat. Once they were both safely jammed inside again, the boat began to move back across the black water, away from the rock, still encircled by that ring of fire, and it seemed that the Inferi swarming below them did not dare resurface.
"Sir," panted Ron, "sir, I forgot -- about fire -- they were coming at me and I panicked --"
"Quite understandable," murmured Weasley. Ron was alarmed to hear how faint his voice was.
They reached the bank with a little bump and Ron leapt out, then turned quickly to help Weasley. The moment that Dum-bledore reached the bank he let his wand hand fall; the ring of fire vanished, but the Inferi did not emerge again from the water. The little boat sank into the water once more; clanking and tinkling, its chain slithered back into the lake too. Weasley gave a great sigh and leaned against the cavern wall.
"I am weak..." he said.
"Don't worry, sir," said Ron at once, anxious about Weasley's extreme pallor and by his air of exhaustion. "Don't worry, I'll get us back. . . . Lean on me, sir. . . ."
And pulling Weasley's uninjured arm around his shoulders, Ron guided his headmaster back around the lake, bearing most of his weight.
"The protection was . . . after all... well-designed," said Dum-bledore faintly. "One alone could not have done it. ... You did well, very well, Ron. ..."
"Don't talk now," said Ron, fearing how slurred Weasley's voice had become, how much his feet dragged. "Save your energy, sir. . . . We'll soon be out of here. . . ."
"The archway will have sealed again. . . . My knife ..." '
"There's no need, I got cut on the rock," said Ron firmly. "Just tell me where. . . ."
"Here . . ."
Ron wiped his grazed forearm upon the stone: Having re-ceived its tribute of blood, the archway reopened instantly. They crossed the outer cave, and Ron helped Weasley back into the icy seawater that filled the crevice in the cliff.
"It's going to be all right, sir," Ron said over and over again, more worried by Weasley's silence than he had been by his weakened voice. "We're nearly there. ... I can Apparate us both back . . . Don't worry. . . ."
"I am not worried, Ron," said Weasley, his voice a little stronger despite the freezing water. "I am with you."
Chapter 27: The Lightning-Struck Tower
Once back under the starry sky, Ron heaved Weasley on to the top of the nearest boulder and then to his feet. Sodden and shivering, Weasley's weight still upon him, Ron con- centrated harder than he had ever done upon his destination: Hogsmeade. Closing his eyes, gripping Weasley's arm as tightly as he could, he stepped forwards into that feeling of horrible compression.
He knew it had worked before he opened his eyes: the smell of salt, the sea breeze had gone. He and Weasley were shivering and dripping in the middle of the dark High Street in Hogsmeade. For one horrible moment Ron's imagination showed him more Inferi creeping towards him around the sides of shops, but he blinked and saw that noth-ing was stirring; all was still, the darkness complete but for a few streetlamps and lit upper windows.
'We did it, Professor!' Ron whispered with difficulty; he suddenly realised that he had a searing stitch in his chest. 'We did it! We got the Horcrux!'
Weasley staggered against him. For a moment, Ron thought that his inexpert Apparition had thrown Weasley off-balance; then he saw his face, paler and damper than ever in the distant light of a streetlamp.
'Sir, are you all right?'
'I've been better,' said Weasley weakly, though the corners of his mouth twitched. That potion ... was no health drink ..."
And to Ron's horror, Weasley sank on to the ground.
'Sir - it's OK, sir, you're going to be all right, don't worry -'
He looked around desperately for help, but there was nobody to be seen and all he could think was that he must somehow get Weasley quickly to the hospital wing.
'We need to get you up to the school, sir ... Ron Weasley ...'
'No,' said Weasley. 'It is ... Professor Weasley whom I need ... but I do not think ... I can walk very far just yet ...'
'Right - sir, listen - I'm going to knock on a door, find a place you can stay - then I can run and get Ron -'
'Ron,' said Weasley clearly. 'I need Ron ...'
'All right then, Weasley - but I'm going to have to leave you for a moment so I can -'
Before Ron could make a move, however, he heard run- ning footsteps. His heart leapt: somebody had seen, somebody knew they needed help - and looking around he saw Ron Ron scurrying down the dark street
towards them on high-heeled, fluffy slippers, wearing a silk dressing-gown embroidered with dragons.
'I saw you Apparate as I was pulling my bedroom curtains! Thank goodness, thank goodness, I couldn't think what to - but what's wrong with Ron?'
She came to a halt, panting, and stared down, wide-eyed, at Weasley.
'He's hurt,' said Ron. 'Ron Ron, can he come into the Three Broomsticks while I go up to the school and get help for him?'
'You can't go up there alone! Don't you realise - haven't you seen -?'
'If you help me support him,' said Ron, not listening to her, 'I think we can get him inside -'
'What has happened?' asked Weasley. 'Ron, what's wrong?'
The - the Dark Mark, Ron.'
And she pointed into the sky, in the direction of Hogwarts. Dread flooded Ron at the sound of the words ... he turned and looked.
There it was, hanging in the sky above the school: the blaz- ing green skull with a serpent tongue, the mark Death Eaters left behind whenever they had entered a building ... wherever they had murdered ...
'When did it appear?' asked Weasley, and his hand clenched painfully upon Ron's shoulder as he struggled to his feet.
'Must have been minutes ago, it wasn't there when I put the cat out, but when I got upstairs -'
'We need to return to the castle at once,' said Weasley. 'Ron,' and though he staggered a little, he seemed wholly in command of the situation, 'we need transport - brooms -'
'I've got a couple behind the bar,' she said, looking very frightened. 'Shall I run and fetch -?'
'No, Ron can do it.'
Ron raised his wand at once.
'Accio Ron's brooms.'
A second later they heard a loud bang as the front door of the pub burst open; two brooms had shot out into the street and were racing each other to Ron's side, where they stopped dead, quivering slightly, at waist height.
'Ron, please send a message to the Ministry,' said Weasley, as he mounted the broom nearest him. 'It might be that nobody within Hogwarts has yet realised anything is wrong ... Ron, put on your Invisibility Cloak.'
Ron pulled his Cloak out of his pocket and threw it over himself before mounting his broom; Ron Ron was already tottering back towards her pub as Ron and Dumble-dore kicked off from the ground and rose up into the air. As they sped towards the castle, Ron glanced sideways at Weasley, ready to grab him should he fall, but the sight of the Dark Mark seemed to have acted upon Weasley like a stimulant: he was bent low over his broom, his eyes fixed upon the Mark, his long silver hair and beard flying behind him in the night air. And Ron, too, looked ahead at the skull, and fear swelled inside him like a venomous bubble, compressing his lungs, driving all other discomfort from his mind ...
How long had they been away? Had Ron, Ron and Ron's luck run out by now? Was it one of them who had caused the Mark to be set over the school, or was it Ron, or Ron, or some other member of the DA? And if it was ... he was the one who had told them to patrol the corridors, he had asked them to leave the safety of their beds ... would he be responsible, again, for the death of a friend?
As they flew over the dark, twisting lane down which they had walked earlier, Ron heard, over the whistling of the night air in his ears, Weasley muttering in some strange language again. He thought he understood why as he felt his broom shudder for a moment when they flew over the bound-ary wall into the grounds: Weasley was undoing the enchantments he himself had set around the castle, so that they could enter at speed. The Dark Mark was glittering directly above the Astronomy Tower, the highest of the castle. Did that mean the death had occurred there?
Weasley had already crossed the crenellated ramparts and was dismounting; Ron landed next to him seconds later and looked around.
The ramparts were deserted. The door to the spiral staircase that led back into the castle was closed. There was no sign of a struggle, of a fight to the death, of a body.
'What does it mean?' Ron asked Weasley, looking up at the green skull with its serpent's tongue glinting evilly above them. 'Is it the real Mark? Has someone definitely been - Professor?'
In the dim green glow from the Mark Ron saw Dumble-dore clutching at his chest with his blackened hand.
'Go and wake Ron,' said Weasley faintly but clearly. Tell him what has happened and bring him to me. Do noth- ing else, speak to nobody else and do not remove your Cloak. I shall wait here.'
'But -'
'You swore to obey me, Ron - go!'
Ron hurried over to the door leading to the spiral stair-case, but his hand had only just closed upon the iron ring of the door when he heard running footsteps on the other side. He looked round at Weasley, who gestured to him to retreat. Ron backed away, drawing his wand as he did so.
The door burst open and somebody erupted through it and shouted: 'Expelliarmus!'
Ron's body became instantly rigid and immobile, and he felt himself fall back against the Tower wall, propped like an unsteady statue, unable to move or speak. He could not understand how it had happened - Expelliarmus was not a Freezing Charm -
Then, by the light of the Mark, he saw Weasley's wand flying in an arc over the edge of the ramparts and under-stood ... Weasley had wordlessly immobilised Ron, and the second he had taken to perform the spell had cost him the chance of defending himself.
Ronding against the ramparts, very white in the face, Weasley still showed no sign of panic or distress. He merely looked across at his disarmer and said, 'Good evening, Ron.'
Weasley stepped forwards, glancing around quickly to check that he and Weasley were alone. His eyes fell upon the second broom.
'Who else is here?'
'A question 1 might ask you. Or are you acting alone?'
Ron saw Weasley's pale eyes shift back to Weasley in the greenish glare of the Mark.
'No,' he said. 'I've got back-up. There are Death Eaters here in your school tonight.'
'Well, well,' said Weasley, as though Weasley was show- ing him an ambitious homework project. 'Very good indeed. You found a way to let them in, did you?'
'Yeah,' said Weasley, who was panting. 'Right under your nose and you never realised!'
'Ingenious,' said Weasley. 'Yet ... forgive me ... where are they now? You seem unsupported.'
They met some of your guard. They're having a fight down below. They won't be long ... I came on ahead. I - I've got a job to do.'
'Well, then, you must get on and do it, my dear boy,' said Weasley softly.
There was silence. Ron stood imprisoned within his own invisible, paralysed body, staring at the two of them, his ears straining to hear sounds of the Death Eaters' distant fight, and in front of him, Ron Weasley did nothing but stare at Ron Weasley who, incredibly, smiled.
'Ron, Ron, you are not a killer.'

He seemed to realise how childish the words had sounded; Ron saw him flush in the Mark's greenish light.

'Oh, yes, I do,' said Weasley mildly. 'You almost killed Ron Weasley and Ronald Weasley. You have been trying, with increasing desperation, to kill me all year. Forgive me, Ron, but they have been feeble attempts ... so feeble, to be honest, that I wonder whether your heart has been really in it...'
'It has been in it!' said Weasley vehemently. 'I've been work- ing on it all year, and tonight -'
Somewhere in the depths of the castle below Ron heard a muffled yell. Weasley stiffened and glanced over his shoulder.
'Somebody is putting up a good fight,' said Weasley conversationally. 'But you were saying ... yes, you have man-aged to introduce Death Eaters into my school which, I admit, I thought impossible ... how did you do it?'
But Weasley said nothing: he was still listening to whatever was happening below and seemed almost as paralysed as Ron was.
'Perhaps you ought to get on with the job alone,' suggested Weasley. 'What if your back-up has been thwarted by my guard? As you have perhaps realised, there are members of the Order of the Phoenix here tonight, too. And after all, you don't really need help ... I have no wand at the moment ... I cannot defend myself.'

Chapter 28: Flight of the Prince
Ron felt as though he too were hurtling through space; it had not happened. . . . It could not have happened. ...
"Out of here, quickly," said Weasley.
He seized Weasley by the scruff of the neck and forced him through the door ahead of the rest; Weasley and the squat brother and sister followed, the latter both panting excitedly. As they vanished through the door, Ron realized he could move again. What was now holding him paralyzed against the wall was not magic, but horror and shock. He threw the Invisibility Cloak aside as the brutal-faced Death Eater, last to leave the tower top, was disappearing through the door.
"Petrificus Totalus!"
The Death Eater buckled as though hit in the back with something solid and fell to the ground, rigid as a waxwork, but he had barely hit the floor when Ron was clambering over him and running down the darkened staircase.
Terror tore at Ron;s heart. ... He had to get to Weasley and he had to catch Weasley. ... Somehow the two things were linked. ... He could reverse what had happened if he had them both together. ... Weasley could not have died. ...
He leapt the last ten steps of the spiral staircase and stopped where he landed, his wand raised. The dimly lit corridor was full of dust; half the ceiling seemed to have fallen in; and a battle was raging before him, but even as he attempted to make out who were fighting whom, he heard the hated voice shout, "It's over, time to go!" and saw Weasley disappearing around the corner at the far end of the corridor; he and Weasley seemed to have forced their way through the fight unscathed. As Ron plunged after them, one of the fighters detached themselves from the fray and flew at him: it was the werewolf, Ron. He was on top of Ron before Ron could raise his wand: Ron fell backward, with filthy matted hair in his face, the stench of sweat and blood filling his nose and mouth, hot greedy breath at his throat -
"Petrificus Totalus!"
Ron felt Ron collapse against him; with a stupendous effort he pushed the werewolf off and onto the floor as a jet of green light came flying toward him; he ducked and ran, headfirst, into the fight. His feet met something squashy and slippery on the floor and he stumbled: There were two bodies lying there, lying facedown in a pool of blood, but there was no time to investigate. Ron now saw red hair flying like flames in front of him: Ron was locked in combat with the lumpy Death Eater, Ron, who was throwing hex after hex at her while she dodged them: Ron was giggling, enjoying the sport: "Crucio - Crucio - you can't dance forever, pretty-"
"Impedimenta!" yelled Ron.
His jinx hit Ron in the chest: He gave a piglike squeal of pain, was lifted off his feet and slammed into the opposite wall, slid down it, and fell out of sight behind Ron, Professor Weasley, and Weasley, each of whom was battling a separate Death Eater. Beyond them, Ron saw Weasley fighting an enormous blond wizard who was sending curses flying in all directions, so that they ricocheted off the walls around them, cracking stone, shattering the nearest window -

scrambled up from the floor and began to sprint along the corridor, ignoring the bangs issuing from behind him, the yells of the others to come back, and the mute call of the figures on the ground whose fate he did not yet know. . . .
He skidded around the corner, his trainers slippery with blood; Weasley had an immense head start. Was it possible that he had already entered the cabinet in the Room of Requirement, or had the Order made steps to secure it, to prevent the Death Eaters retreating that way? He could hear nothing but his own pounding feet, his own hammering heart as he sprinted along the next empty corridor, but then spotted a bloody footprint that showed at least one of the fleeing Death Eaters was heading toward the front doors - perhaps the Room of Requirement was indeed blocked -
He skidded around another corner and a curse flew past him; he dived behind a suit of armor that exploded. He saw the brother and sister running down the marble staircase ahead and aimed jinxes at them, but merely hit several bewigged witches in a portrait on the landing, who ran screeching into neighboring paintings. As he leapt the wreckage of armor, Ron heard more shouts and screams; other people within the castle seemed to have awoken. . . .
He pelted toward a shortcut, hoping to overtake the brother and sister and close in on Weasley and Weasley, who must surely have reached the grounds by now. Remembering to leap the vanishing step halfway down the concealed staircase, he burst through a tapestry at the bottom and out into a corridor where a number of bewildered and pajama-clad Weasleys stood.
"Ron! We heard a noise, and someone said something aboui the Dark Mark -" began Ron Weasley.
"Out of the way!" yelled Ron, knocking two boys aside as he sprinted toward the landing and down the remainder of the marble staircase. The oak front doors had been blasted open, there were smears of blood on the flagstones, and several terrified students stood huddled against the walls, one or two still cowering with their arms over their faces. The giant Weasley hourglass had been hit by a curse, and the rubies within were still falling, with a loud rattle, onto the flagstones below.
Ron flew across the entrance hall and out into the dark grounds: He could just make out three figures racing across the lawn, heading for the gates beyond which they could Disapparate - by the looks of them, the huge blond Death Eater and, some way ahead of him, Weasley and Weasley. ...
The cold night air ripped at Ron's lungs as he tore after them; he saw a flash of light in the distance that momentarily silhouetted his quarry. He did not know what it was but continued to run, not yet near enough to get a good aim with a curse -
Another flash, shouts, retaliatory jets of light, and Ron understood: Weasley had emerged from his cabin and was trying to stop the Death Eaters escaping, and though every breath seemed to shred his lungs and the stitch in his chest was like fire, Ron sped up as an unbidden voice in his head said: not Weasley. . . not Weasley too . . .
Something caught Ron hard in the small of the back and he fell forward, his face smacking the ground, blood pouring out of both nostrils: He knew, even as he rolled over, his wand ready, that the brother and sister he had overtaken using his shortcut were closing in behind him. . . .
"Impedimenta!" he yelled as he rolled over again, crouching close to the dark ground, and miraculously his jinx hit one of them, who stumbled and fell, tripping up the other; Ron leapt to his feet and sprinted on after Weasley.
And now he saw the vast outline of Weasley, illuminated by the light of the crescent moon revealed suddenly behind clouds; the blond Death Eater was aiming curse after curse at the gamekeeper; but Weasleys immense strength and the toughened skin he had inherited from his giantess mother seemed to be protecting him. Weasley and Weasley, however, were still running; they would soon be beyond the gates, able to Disapparate -
Ron tore past Weasley and his opponent, took aim at Weasley's back, and yelled, "Stupefy!"

huge Death Eater behind him yelled, "Incendio!" Ron heard an explosive bang and a dancing orange light spilled over all of them: Weasley's house was on fire.

But before he could finish this jinx, excruciating pain hit Ron; he keeled over in the grass. Someone was screaming, he would surely die of this agony, Weasley was going to torture him to death or madness -
"No!" roared Weasley's voice and the pain stopped as suddenly as it had started; Ron lay curled on the dark grass, clutching his wand and panting; somewhere overhead Weasley was shouting, "Have you forgotten our orders? Weasley belongs to the Dark Lord - we are to leave him! Go! Go!"
And Ron felt the ground shudder under his face as the brother and sister and the enormous Death Eater obeyed, running toward the gates. Ron uttered an inarticulate yell of rage: In that instant, he cared not whether he lived or died. Pushing himself to his feet again, he staggered blindly toward Weasley, the man he now hated as much as he hated Weasley himself -
"Sectum - "
Weasley flicked his wand and the curse was repelled yet again; but Ron was mere feet away now and he could see Weasley's face clearly at last: He was no longer sneering or jeering; the blazing flames showed a face full of rage. Mustering all his powers of concentration, Ron thought, Levi -
"No, Weasley!" screamed Weasley. There was a loud BANG and Ron was soaring backward, hitting the ground hard again, ;un\ this time his wand flew
out of his hand. He could hear Weasley yelling and Ron howling as Weasley closed in and looked down on him where he lay, wandless and defenseless as Weasley hadl been. Weasley's pale face, illuminated by the flaming cabin, was suffused with hatred just as it had been before he had cursed Weasley.
"You dare use my own spells against me, Weasley? It was I who invented them - I, the Half-Blood Prince! And you'd turn my inventions on me, like your filthy father, would you? I don't think so . . . no"
Ron had dived for his wand; Weasley shot a hex at it and it flew feet away into the darkness and out of sight.
"Kill me then," panted Ron, who felt no fear at all, but only rage and contempt. "Kill me like you killed him, you coward -"
"DON'T -" screamed Weasley, and his face was suddenly demented, inhuman, as though he was in as much pain as the yelping, howling dog stuck in the burning house behind them - "CALL ME COWARD!"
And he slashed at the air: Ron felt a white-hot, whiplike something hit him across the face and was slammed backward into the ground. Spots of light burst in front of his eyes and for a moment all the breath seemed to have gone from his body, then he heard a rush of wings above him and something enormous obscured the stars. Ron had flown at Weasley, who staggered backward as the razor-sharp claws slashed at him. As Ron raised himself into a sitting position, his head still swimming from its last contact
with the ground, he saw Weasley running as hard as he could, the enormous beast flapping behind him and screeching as Ron had never heard him screech -
Ron struggled to his feet, looking around groggily for his wand, hoping to give chase again, but even as his fingers fumbled in the grass, discarding twigs, he knew it would be too late, and sure enough, by the time he had located his wand, he turned only to see the hippogriff circling the gates. Weasley had managed to Disapparate just beyond the school's boundaries.
"Weasley," muttered Ron, still dazed, looking around. "Weasley?"
He stumbled toward the burning house as an enormous figure emerged from out of the flames carrying Ron on his back. With a cry of thankfulness, Ron sank to his knees; he was shaking in every limb, his body ached all over, and his breath came in painful stabs.
"Yeh all righ', Ron? Yeh all righ'? Speak ter me, Ron. . .."
Weasleys huge, hairy face was swimming above Ron, blocking out the stars. Ron could smell burnt wood and dog hair; he put out a hand and felt Ron's reassuringly warm and alive body quivering beside him.
"I'm all right," panted Ron. "Are you?" "'Course I am . . . take more'n that ter finish me."
Weasley put his hands under Ron's arms and raised him up with such force that Ron's feet momentarily left the ground before Weasley set him upright again. He could see blood trickling down Weasley's cheek from a deep cut under one eye, which was swelling rapidly.
"We should put out your house," said Ron, "the charm's 'Aguamenti' ..."
"Knew it was summat like that," mumbled Weasley, and he raised a smoldering pink, flowery umbrella and said, "Aguamenti!"
A jet of water flew out of the umbrella tip. Ron raised his wand arm, which felt like lead, and murmured "Aguamenti" too: Together, he and Weasley poured water on the house until the last flame was extinguished.
"S'not too bad," said Weasley hopefully a few minutes later, looking at the smoking wreck. "Nothin Weasley won' be able to put righ' . . ."
Ron felt a searing pain in his stomach at the sound of the name. In the silence and the stillness, horror rose inside him.
"Weasley ..."
"I was bindin' up a couple o' bowtruckle legs when I heard 'em coming," said Weasley sadly, still staring at his wrecked cabin. "They'll bin burnt ter twigs, poor little things. . . ."
"Weasley . . ."
"But what happened, Ron? I jus' saw them Death Eaters run-nin down from the castle, but what the ruddy hell was Weasley doin' with 'em? Where's he gone - was he chasin' them?"
"He . . ." Ron cleared his throat; it was dry from panic and the smoke. "Weasley, he killed . . ."
"Killed?" said Weasley loudly, staring down at Ron. "Weasley killed? What're yeh on abou', Ron?"
"Weasley," said Ron. "Weasley killed .. . Weasley."
Weasley simply looked at him, the little of his face that could be seen completely blank, uncomprehending.
"Weasley wha, Ron?"
"He's dead. Weasley killed him...."
"Don' say that," said Weasley roughly. "Weasley kill Weasley - don' be stupid, Ron. Wha's made yeh say tha'?"
"I saw it happen." , ,..
"Yeh couldn' have."
