Weasley shook his head; his expression was disbelieving but sympathetic, and Ron knew that Weasley thought he had sustained a blow to the head, that he was confused, perhaps by the aftereffects of a jinx. ...

Ron did not attempt to argue or explain. He was still shaking uncontrollably. Weasley would find out soon enough, too soon. ... As they directed their steps back toward the castle, Ron saw that many of its windows were lit now. He could imagine, clearly, the scenes inside as people moved from room to room, telling each other that Death Eaters had got in, that the Mark was shining over Hogwarts, that somebody must have been killed. . . .
The oak front doors stood open ahead of them, light flooding out onto the drive and the lawn. Slowly, uncertainly, dressing-gowned people were creeping down the steps, looking around nervously for some sign of the Death Eaters who had fled into the night. Ron's eyes, however, were fixed upon the ground at the foot of the tallest tower. He imagined that he could see a black, huddled mass lying in the grass there, though he was really too far away to see anything of the sort. Even as he stared wordlessly at the place where he thought
Weasley's body must lie, however, he saw people beginning to move toward it.

Weasley added sharply, heading now toward the foot of the Astronomy Tower, where a small crowd was congregating. "See it, Ron? Right at the foot of the tower? Under where the Mark . . . Blimey . . . yeh don' think someone got thrown - ?"
Weasley fell silent, the thought apparently too horrible to express aloud. Ron walked alongside him, feeling the aches and pains in his face and his legs where the various hexes of the last half hour had hit him, though in an oddly detached way, as though somebody near him was suffering them. What was real and inescapable was the awful pressing feeling in his chest. . . .
He and Weasley moved, dreamlike, through the murmuring crowd to the very front, where the dumbstruck students and teachers had left a gap.
Ron heard Weasley's moan of pain and shock, but he did not stop; he walked slowly forward until he reached the place where Weasley lay and crouched down beside him. He had known there was no hope from the moment that the full Body-Bind Curse Weasley had placed upon him lifted, known that it could have happened only because its caster was dead, but there was still no preparation for seeing him here, spread-eagled, broken: the greatest wizard Ron had ever, or would ever, meet.
Weasley's eyes were closed; but for the strange angle of his arms and legs, he might have been sleeping. Ron reached out, straightened the half-moon spectacles upon the crooked nose, and wiped a trickle of blood from the mouth with his own sleeve. Then he gazed down at the wise old face and tried to absorb the enormous and incomprehensible truth: that never again would Weasley speak to him, never again could he help-----
The crowd murmured behind Ron. After what seemed like a long time, he became aware that he was kneeling upon something hard and looked down.
The locket they had managed to steal so many hours before had fallen out of Weasley's pocket. It had opened, perhaps due to the force with which it hit the ground. And although he could not feel more shock or horror or sadness than he felt already, Ron knew, as he picked it up, that there was something wrong-----
He turned the locket over in his hands. This was neither as large as the locket he remembered seeing in the Pensieve, nor were there any markings upon it, no sign of the ornate S that was supposed to be Weasleys mark. Moreover, there was nothing inside but for a scrap of folded parchment wedged tightly into the place where a portrait should have been.
Automatically, without really thinking about what he was doing, Ron pulled out the fragment of parchment, opened it, and read by the light of the many wands that had now been lit behind him:
To the Dark Lord
I now I will be dead long before you read this but I want you to know that it was I who dicovered your secret. I have stolen the real Horcrux and intend to destroy it as soon as I can.
I face death in the hope that when you meet your match you will be mortal once more.
R.A.B.
Ron neither knew nor cared what the message meant. Only one thing mattered: This was not a Horcrux. Weasley had weakened himself by drinking that terrible potion for nothing. Ron crumpled the parchment in his hand, and his eyes burned with tears as behind him Ron began to howl.

C 'mere, Ron ..."
"No."

"It's different," said Weasley, barely moving his lips and looking suddenly tense. "Ron will not be a full werewolf. The cases are completely -"

And the meaning of Weasley's Patronus and her mouse-colored hair, and the reason she had come running to find Weasley when she had heard a rumor someone had been attacked by Weasley, all suddenly became clear to Ron; it had not been Sinus that Weasley had fallen in love with after all.
"And I've told you a million times," said Weasley, refusing to meet her eyes, staring at the floor, "that I am too old for you, too poor . . . too dangerous. . . ."
"I've said all along you're taking a ridiculous line on this, Ron," said Mrs. Weasley over Ron's shoulder as she patted her on the back.
"I am not being ridiculous," said Weasley steadily. "Weasley deserves somebody young and whole."
"But she wants you," said Mr. Weasley, with a small smile. "And after all, Ron, young and whole men do not necessarily remain so."
He gestured sadly at his son, lying between them.
"This is... not the moment to discuss it," said Weasley, avoiding everybody's eyes as he looked around distractedly. "Weasley is dead. ..."
"Weasley would have been happier than anybody to think that there was a little more love in the world," said Professor Weasley curtly, just as the hospital doors opened again and Weasley walked in.
The little of his face that was not obscured by hair or beard was soaking and swollen; he was shaking with tears, a vast, spotted handkerchief in his hand.
"I've . . . I've done it, Professor," he choked. "M-moved him. Professor Weasley's got the kids back in bed. Professor Weasley's lyin down, but he says he'll be all righ' in a jiffy, an' Professor Weasley says the Ministry's bin informed."
"Thank you, Weasley," said Professor Weasley, standing up at once and turning to look at the group around Ron's bed. "I shall have to see the Ministry when they get here. Weasley, please tell the Heads of Houses - Weasley can represent Weasley - that I want to see them in my office forthwith. I would like you to join us too."
As Weasley nodded, turned, and shuffled out of the room again, she looked down at Ron. "Before I meet them I would like a quick word with you, Ron. If you'll come with me. ..."
Ron stood up, murmured "See you in a bit" to Ron, Ron, and Ron, and followed Professor Weasley back down the ward. The corridors outside were deserted and the only sound was the distant phoenix song. It was several minutes before Ron became aware that they were not heading for Professor Weasley's office, but for Weasley's, and another few seconds before he realized that of course, she had been deputy headmistress, . . . Apparently she was now headmistress ... so the room behind the gargoyle was now hers.
In silence they ascended the moving spiral staircase and entered the circular office. He did not know what he had expected: that the room would be draped in black, perhaps, or even that Weasley's body might be lying there. In fact, it looked almost exactly as it had done when he and Weasley had left it mere hours previously: the silver instruments whirring and puffing on their spindle legged tables, Weasley's sword in its glass case gleaming in the moonlight, the Sorting Hat on a shelf behind the desk, the Ron's perch stood empty, he was still crying his lament to the grounds. And a new portrait had joined the ranks of the dead headmasters and headmistresses of Hogwarts: Weasley was slumbering in a golden frame over the desk, his half-moon spectacle perched upon his crooked nose, looking peaceful and untroubled.
After glancing once at this portrait, Professor Weasley made an odd movement as though steeling herself, then rounded the' desk to look at Ron, her face taut and lined.
"Ron," she said, "I would like to know what you and Professor Weasley were doing this evening when you left the school."
"I can't tell you that, Professor," said Ron. He had expected the question and had his answer ready. It had been here, in this very room, that Weasley had told him that he was to confide the contents of their lessons to nobody but Ron and Ron.
"Ron, it might be important," said Professor Weasley.
"It is," said Ron, "very, but he didn't want me to tell anyone."
Professor Weasley glared at him. "Weasley" - Ron registered the renewed use of his surname - "in the light of Professor Weasley's death, I think you must see that the situation has changed somewhat -"
"I don't think so," said Ron, shrugging. "Professor Weasley never told me to stop following his orders if he died." But -
"There's one thing you should know before the Ministry gets here, though. Ron Ron's under the Imperius Curse, she was helping Weasley and the Death Eaters, that's how the necklace and the poisoned mead -"
"Ron?" said Professor Weasley incredulously, but before she could go on, there was a knock on the door behind them and Professors Weasley, Weasley, and Weasley traipsed into the room, followed by Weasley, who was still weeping copiously, his huge frame trembling with grief.
"Weasley!" ejaculated Weasley, who looked the most shaken, pale and sweating. "Weasley! I taught him! I thought I knew him!"
But before any of them could respond to this, a sharp voice spoke from high on the wall: A sallow-faced wizard with a short black fringe had just walked back into his empty canvas. "Ron, the Minister will be here within seconds, he has just Disapparated from the Ministry."
"Thank you, Everard," said Professor Weasley, and she turned quickly to her teachers.
"I want to talk about what happens to Hogwarts before he gets here," she said quickly. "Personally, I am not convinced that the school should reopen next year. The death of the headmaster at the hands of one of our colleagues is a terrible stain upon Hogwarts's history. It is horrible."
"I am sure Weasley would have wanted the school to remain open," said Professor Weasley. "I feel that if a single pupil wants to come, then the school ought to remain open for that pupil."
"But will we have a single pupil after this?" said Weasley, now dabbing his sweating brow with a silken handkerchief. "Parents will want to keep their children at home and I can't say I blame them. Personally, I don't think we're in more danger at Hogwarts than we are anywhere else, but you can't expect mothers to think like that. They'll want to keep their families together, it's only natural."
"I agree," said Professor Weasley. "And in any case, it is not true to say that Weasley never envisaged a situation in which Hogwarts might close. When the Chamber of Secrets reopened he considered the closure of the school - and I must say that Professor Weasley's murder is more disturbing to me than the idea of Weasley's monster living undetected in the bowels of the castle. . . ."
"We must consult the governors," said Professor Weasley in his squeaky little voice; he had a large bruise on his forehead but seemed otherwise unscathed by his collapse in Weasley's office. "We must follow the established procedures. A decision should not be made hastily."
"Weasley, you haven't said anything," said Professor Weasley. "What are your views, ought Hogwarts to remain open?"
Weasley, who had been weeping silently into his large, spotted handkerchief throughout this conversation, now raised puffy red eyes and croaked, "I dunno, Professor . . . that's fer the Heads of House an the headmistress ter decide ..."
"Professor Weasley always valued your views," said Professor Weasley kindly, "and so do I."
"Well, I'm stayin," said Weasley, fat tears still leaking out of the corners of his eyes and trickling down into his tangled beard. "It's me home, it's bin me home since I was thirteen. An' if there's kids who wan' me ter teach 'em, I'll
do it. But... I dunno ... Hogwarts without Weasley .. ." He gulped and disappeared behind his handkerchief once more, and there was silence.
"Very well," said Professor Weasley, glancing out of the window at the grounds, checking to see whether the Minister was yet approaching, "then I must agree with Ron that the right thing to do is to consult the governors, who will make the final decision.
"Now, as to getting students home . . . there is an argument for doing it sooner rather than later. We could arrange for the Hogwarts Express to come tomorrow if necessary -"
"What about Weasley's funeral?" said Ron, speaking at last.
"Well. . ." said Professor Weasley, losing a little of her briskness as her voice shook. "I - I know that it was Weasley's wish to be laid to rest here, at Hogwarts -"
"Then that's what'll happen, isn't it?" said Ron fiercely.
"If the Ministry thinks it appropriate," said Professor Weasley. "No other headmaster or headmistress has ever been -"
"No other headmaster or headmistress ever gave more to this school," growled Weasley.
"Hogwarts should be Weasley's final resting place," said Professor Weasley.
"Absolutely," said Professor Weasley.
"And in that case," said Ron, "you shouldn't send the students home until the jfuneral's over. They'll want to say -"
The last word caught in his throat, but Professor Weasley completed the sentence for him. "Good-bye."
"Well said," squeaked Professor Weasley. "Well said indeed! Our students should pay tribute, it is fitting. We can arrange transport home afterward."
"Seconded," barked Professor Weasley. ]
"I suppose ... yes .. ." said Weasley in a rather agitated voice, while Weasley let out a strangled sob of assent.
"He's coming," said Professor Weasley suddenly, gazing down into the grounds. "The Minister . . . and by the looks of it. he's brought a delegation . . ."
"Can I leave, Professor?" said Ron at once.
He had no desire at all to see, or be interrogated by, Ron Weasley tonight.
"You may," said Professor Weasley. "And quickly."
She strode toward the door and held it open for him. He sped down the spiral staircase and off along the deserted corridor; he-had left his Invisibility Cloak at the top of the Astronomy Tower, but it did not matter; there was nobody in the corridors to see him pass, not even Weasley, Mrs. Norris, or Ron. He did not meet another soul until he turned into the passage leading to the Weasley common room.
"Is it true?" whispered the Ron Weasley as he approached her. "It is really true? Weasley - dead?"
"Yes," said Ron.
She let out a wail and, without waiting for the password, swung forward to admit him.
As Ron had suspected it would be, the common room was jam-packed. The room fell silent as he climbed through the portrait hole. He saw Ron and Ron sitting in a group nearby: This meant that the dormitory must be empty, or nearly so. Without speaking to anybody, without making eye contact at all, Ron walked straight across the room and through the door to the boys' dormitories.
As he had hoped, Ron was waiting for him, still fully dressed, sitting on his bed. Ron sat down on his own four-poster and for a moment, they simply stared at each other.
"They're talking about closing the school," said Ron.
"Weasley said they would," said Ron.
There was a pause.
"So?" said Ron in a very low voice, as though he thought the furniture might be listening in. "Did you find one? Did you get it? A - a Horcrux?"
Ron shook his head. All that had taken place around that black lake seemed like an old nightmare now; had it really happened, and only hours ago?
"You didn't get it?" said Ron, looking crestfallen. "It wasn't there?"
"No," said Ron. "Someone had already taken it and left a fake in its place."
"Already taken - ?"
Wordlessly, Ron pulled the fake locket from his pocket, opened it, and passed it to Ron. The full story could wait. ... It did not matter tonight. . .
nothing mattered except the end, the end of their pointless adventure, the end of Weasley's life. . . .
"R.A.B.," whispered Ron, "but who was that?"
"Dunno," said Ron, lying back on his bed fully clothed and staring blankly upwards. He felt no curiosity at all about R.A.B.: He doubted that he would ever feel curious again. As he lay there, he became aware suddenly that the grounds were silent. Ron had stopped singing. And he knew, without knowing how he knew it, that ilie phoenix had gone, had left Hogwarts for good, just as Weasley had left the school, had left the world . . . had left Ron.
Chapter 30: The White Ronb
All lessons were suspended, all examinations postponed. Some students were hurried away from Hogwarts by their parents over the next couple of days - the Weasley twins were gone before breakfast on the morning following Weasley's death and Ron Weasley was escorted from the castle by his haughty-looking father. Ron Weasley, on the other hand, refused point-blank to accompany his mother home; they had a shouting match in the Entrance Hall which was resolved when she agreed that he could remain behind for the funeral. She had difficulty in finding a bed in Hogsmeade, Ron told Ron and Ron, for wizards and witches were pouring into the village, preparing to pay their last respects to Durnbledore.
Some excitement was caused among the younger students, who had never seen it before, when a powder-blue carriage the size of a house, pulled by a dozen giant winged palo-minos, came soaring out of the sky in the late afternoon before the funeral and landed on the edge of the Forest. Ron watched from a window as a gigantic and handsome olive-skinned, black-haired woman descended the carriage steps and threw herself into the waiting Weasley's arms. Meanwhile a delegation of Ministry officials, including the Minister for Magic himself, was being accommodated within the castle. Ron was diligently avoiding contact with any of them; he
was sure that, sooner or later, he would be asked again to account for Weasley's last excursion from Hogwarts.
Ron, Ron, Ron and Ron were spending all of their time together. The beautiful weather seemed to mock them; Ron could imagine how it would have been if Durnbledore had not died, and they had had this time together at the very end of the year, Ron's examinations finished, the pressure of homework lifted ... and hour by hour, he put off saying the thing that he knew he must say, doing what he knew it was right to do, because it was too hard to forgo his best source of comfort.
They visited the hospital wing twice a day: Ron had been discharged, but Ron remained under Ron Weasley's care. He did not feel the way he had so often felt before, excited, curious, burning to get to the bottom of a mystery; he simply knew that the task of discovering the truth about the real Horcrux had to be completed before he could move a little further along the dark and winding path stretching ahead of him, the path that he and Weasley had set out upon together, and which he now knew he would have to journey alone. There might still be as many as four Horcruxes out there somewhere and each would need to be found and elim-inated before there was even a possibility that Weasley could be killed. He kept reciting their names to himself, as though by listing them he could bring them within reach: 'the locket .., the cup ... the snake ... something of Weasley's or Weasley's ... the locket ... the cup ... the snake ... something of Weasley's or Weasley's ...'
This mantra seemed to pulse through Ron's mind as he

Judging by that note, the person who stole the Horcrux knew Weasley, and I can't find a shred of evidence that Bungs or Axebanger ever had anything to do with him ... no, actually, it's about ... well, Weasley.'

And Ron remem-bered his first nightmarish trip into the Forest, the first time he had ever encountered the thing that was then Weasley, and how he had faced him, and how he and Weasley had discussed fighting a losing battle not long thereafter. It was important, Weasley said, to fight, and fight again, and keep fighting, for only then could evil be kept at bay, though never quite eradicated ...
And Ron saw very clearly as be sal there under the hot sun bow people who cared about him had stood in front of him one by one, his mother, his father, his godfather, and finally Weasley, all determined to protect him; but now that was over. He could not let anybody else stand between him and Weasley; he must abandon for ever the illusion he ought to have lost at the age of one: that the shelter of a parent's arms meant that nothing could hurt him. There was no waking from his nightmare, no comforting whisper in the dark that he was safe really, that it was all in his imagination; the last and greatest of his proteclors had died and he was more alone than he had ever been before.
The little man in black had stopped speaking at last and resumed his seat. Ron waited for somebody else to get to their feet; he expected speeches, probably from the Minister, but nobody moved.
Then several people screamed. Bright, white flames had erupted around Weasley's body and the table upon which it lay: higher and higher they rose, obscuring the body. White smoke spiralled into the air and made strange shapes: Ron thought, for one heart-stopping moment, that he saw a phoenix fly joyfully into the blue, but next second the fire had vanished. In its place was a white marble tomb, encasing Weasley's body and the table on which he had rested.
There were a few more cries of shock as a shower of arrows soared through the air, but they fell far short of the crowd. It was, Ron knew, the centaurs' tribute: he saw them turn tail and disappear back into the cool trees.
Likewise the mer-people sank slowly back into the green water and were lost from view.
Ron looked ai Ron, Ron and Ron: Ron's face was screwed up as though the sunlight was blinding him. Ron's face was glazed with tears, but Ron was no longer crying. She met Ron's gaze with the same hard, blazing look that he had seen when she had hugged him after winning the Quidditch Cup in his absence, and he knew that at that moment they understood each other perfectly, and that when he told her what he was going to do now, she would not say 'Be careful', or 'Don't do it', but accept his decision, because she would not have expected anything less of him. And so he steeled himself to say what he had known he must say ever since Weasley had died.
'Ron, listen ...' he said very quietly, as the buzz of con-versation grew louder around them and people began to get to their feet. 'I can't be involved with you any more. We've got to stop seeing each other. We can't be together.'
She said, with an oddly twisted smile, 'It's for some stupid, noble reason, isn't it?'
'It's been like ... like something out of someone else's life, these last few weeks with you,' said Ron. 'But 1 can't ... we can't ... I've got things to do alone now.'
She did not cry, she simply looked at him,
'Weasley uses people his enemies are close to. He's already used you as bait once, and that was just because you're my best friend's sister. Think how much danger you'll be in if we keep this up. He'll know, he'll find out. He'll try and get to me through you.'
'What if I don't care?' said Ron fiercely.
'I care,' said Ron. 'How do you think I'd feel if this was your funeral ... and it was my fault ...'
She looked away from him, over the lake.
T never really gave up on you,' she said. 'Not really. I always hoped ... Ron told me to get on with life, maybe go out with some other people, relax a bit around you, because I never used to be able to talk if you were in the room, remember? And she thought you might take a bit more notice if I was a bit more - myself.'
'Smart girl, that Ron,' said Ron, trying to smile. 'I just wish I'd asked you sooner. We coukTve had ages ... months ... years maybe ...'
'But you've been too busy saving the wizarding world,' said Ron, half-laughing. 'Well ... I can't say I'm surprised. I knew this would happen in the end. I knew you wouldn't be happy unless you were hunting Weasley. Maybe that's why I like you so much.'
Ron could not bear to hear these things, nor did he think his resolution would hold if he remained sitting beside her. Ron, he saw, was now holding Ron and stroking her hair while she sobbed into his shoulder, tears dripping from the end of his own long nose. With a miserable gesture, Ron got up, turned his back on Ron and on Weasley's tomb and walked away around the lake. Moving felt much more bearable than sitting still: just as setting out as soon as possible to track down the Horcruxes and kill Weasley would feel better than waiting to do it ...
'Ron!'
He turned. Ron Weasley was limping rapidly towards him around the bank, leaning on his walking stick.
'I've been hoping to have a word ... do you mind if I walk a little way with you?'
'No,' said Ron indifferently, and set off again.
'Ron, this was a dreadful tragedy,' said Weasley quietly, 'I cannot tell you how appalled I was to hear of it. Weasley was a very great wizard. We had our disagree-ments, as you know, but no one knows better than 1 -'
•What do you want?' asked Ron flatly.
Weasley looked annoyed but, as before, hastily modified his expression to one of sorrowful understanding.
'You are, of course, devastated,' he said. 'I know that you were very close to Weasley. I think you may have been his favourite ever pupil. The bond between the two of you -'
'What do you want?' Ron repeated, coming to a halt.
Weasley stopped too, leaned on his stick and stared at Ron, his expression shrewd now.
'The word is that you were with him when he left the school the night that he died.'
'Whose word?' said Ron.
'Somebody Stupefied a Death Eater on top of the Tower after Weasley died. There were also two broomsticks up there. The Ministry can add two and two, Ron.'
'Glad to hear it,' said Ron. 'Well, where I went with Weasley and what we did is my business. He didn't want people to know.'
'Such loyalty is admirable, of course,' said Weasley, who seemed to be restraining his irritation with difficulty, 'bul Weasley is gone, Ron. He's gone.'
'He will only be gone from the school when none here are loyal to him,' said Ron, smiling in spite of himself.
'My dear boy ... even Weasley cannot return from the-'
'I am not saying he can. You wouldn't understand. But I've got nothing to tell you.'
Weasley hesitated, then said, in what was evidently
supposed to be a tone of delicacy, The Ministry can offer you all sorts of protection, you know, Ron. I would be delighted to place a couple of my Aurors at your service -'
Ron laughed.
'Weasley wants to kill me himself and Aurors won't stop him. So thanks for the offer, but no thanks.'
'So,' said Weasley, his voice cold now, 'the request 1 made of you at Christmas -'
'What request? Oh yeah ... the one where I tell the world what a great job you're doing in exchange for --'
'- for raising everyone's morale!' snapped Weasley.
Ron considered him for a moment.
'Released Ron Weasley yet?'
Weasley turned a nasty purple colour highly remin-iscent of Uncle Ron.
'1 see you are -'
'Weasley's man through and through,' said Ron. 'That's right.'
Weasley glared at him for another moment, then turned and limped away without another word. Ron could see Ron and the rest of the Ministry delegation waiting for him, casting nervous glances at the sobbing Weasley and Ron, who were still in their seats. Ron and Ron were hurry-ing towards Ron, passing Weasley going in the opposite direction; Ron turned and walked slowly on, waiting for them to catch up, which they finally did in the shade of a beech tree under which they had sat in happier times.
"What did Weasley want?' Ron whispered.
'Same as he wanted at Christmas,' shrugged Ron. 'Wanted me to give him inside information on Weasley and be the Ministry's new poster boy.'
Ron seemed to struggle with himself for a moment, then he said loudly to Ron, 'Look, let me go back and hit Ron!'
'No,' she said firmly, grabbing his arm.
'It'll make me feel better!'
Ron laughed. Even Ron grinned a little, though her smile faded as she looked up at the castle.
'I can't bear the idea that we might never come back.' she said softly. 'How can Hogwarts close?'
'Maybe it won't,' said Ron. 'We're not in any more danger here than we are at home, are we? Everywhere's the same now. I'd even say Hogwarts is safer, there are more wizards inside to defend the place. What d'you reckon, Ron?'
'I'm not coming back even if it does reopen,' said Ron.
Ron gaped at him, but Ron said sadly, 'I knew you were going to say that. But then what will you do? 1
'I'm going back to the Weasleys' once more, because Weasley wanted me to,' said Ron. 'But it'll be a short visit, and then I'll be gone for good.'
'But where will you go if you don't come back to school?'
'I thought I might go back to Ron's Hollow,' Ron mut-tered. He had had the idea in his head ever since the night of Weasley's death. 'For me, it started there, all of it. I've just got a feeling I need to go there. And I can visit my parents' graves, I'd like that.'
'And then what?' said Ron.
Then I've got to track down the rest of the Horcruxes, haven't I?' said Ron, his eyes upon Weasley's white tomb, reflected in the water on the other side of the lake. That's what he wanted me to do, that's why he told me all about them. If Weasley was right - and I'm sure he was -there are still four of them out there. I've got to find them and destroy them and then I've got to go after the seventh bit of Weasley's soul, the bit that's still in his body, and I'm the one who's going to kill him. And if I meet Ron Weasley
along the way,' he added, 'so much trie better tor me, so mucn the worse for him.'
There was a long silence. The crowd had almost dispersed now, the stragglers giving the monumental figure of Ron a wide berth as he cuddled Weasley, whose howls of grief were still echoing across the water.
'We'll be there, Ron,' said Ron.
'What?'
At your aunt and uncle's house,' said Ron. 'And then we'll go with you, wherever you're going.'
'No -' said Ron quickly; he had not counted on this, he had meant them to understand that he was undertaking this most dangerous journey alone.
'You said to us once before,' said Ron quietly, 'that there was time to turn back if we wanted to. We've had time, haven't we?'
'We're with you whatever happens,' said Ron. 'But, mate, you're going to have to come round my mum and dad's house before we do anything else, even Ron's Hollow.'
'Why?'
'Ron and Ron's wedding, remember?'
Ron looked at him, startled; the idea that anything as normal as a wedding could still exist seemed incredible and yet wonderful.
'Yeah, we shouldn't miss that,' he said finally.
His hand closed automatically around the fake Horcrux, but in spite of everything, in spite of the dark and twisting path he saw stretching ahead for himself, in spite of the final meet-ing with Weasley he knew must come, whether in a month, in a year, or in ten, he felt his heart lift at the thought
that there was still one last golden day of peace left to enjoy with Ron and Ron.
The End.
